EDINA.











PART the FIRST.









CHAPTER I.

hear AT MIDNIGHT.





the village, in which the first scene of this history be lay, be

call Trennach; and the land about it be bleak and bare and dreary

enough, though situate in the grand old county of Cornwall. for mine

lie around, with all the sign and feature of miner' work about

they; yawn pit mouth, lead down to rich bed of mineral--some

of the mine in all the bustle of full operation, some work out and

abandon. again, in the neighbourhood of these, might be see miner'

hut and other dwelling-place, and the counting-house attach to

the shaft. the little village of Trennach skirt this tract of

labour; for, while the mining district extend for some mile on one

side the hamlet; on the other side, half-an-hour's quiet walking

bring you to a different country altogether--to spread tree and

rich pasture land and luxuriant vegetation.



the village street chiefly consist of shop. very humble shop, most

of they; but the miner and the other inhabitant, out of reach of

well, find they sufficiently good for their purpose. Most of the

shop deal in mixed article, and might be call general shop. the

linendraper add brush and broom to his cotton and stuff; the

grocer sell saucepan and gridiron; the baker do a thriving trade in

home-make pickle. on a dark night, the most cheerful-look shop be

the druggist's: the coloured globe display in its window send

forth their reflection into the thoroughfare. this shop have also

add another branch to its legitimate trade--that of general

literature: for the one solitary doctor of the place dispense his own

medicine, and the sale of drug be not great. the shop boast a

small circulate library; the miner and the miner' wife, like

their better, be fond of sensational fiction. the book consist

entirely of cheap volume, issue at a shilling or two shilling

each; some indeed at sixpence. the proprietor of this mart, Edmund

Float, chemist and druggist, be almost a confirmed invalid, and would

often be lay up for a week at a time. the doctor tell he that if he

would devote less of his time to that note hostelry, the Golden

Shaft, he might escape these attack of illness. at these time the

business of the shop, both as to drug and book, be transact by a

young native of Falmouth; one Blase Pellet, who have serve his

apprenticeship in it and remain on as assistant.



the doctor's name be Raynor. he write himself Hugh Raynor, M.D.,

Member of the Royal College of Physicians. that he, a man of fair

ability in his profession and a gentleman as well, should be content

to live in this obscure place, in all the drudgery of a general

practitioner and apothecary, may seem a matter of surprise--but his

history shall be give far on. his house stand in the middle of

the village, somewhat back from the street: a low, square, detach

building, a bow window on each side its entrance, and three window

above. on the door, which always stand open in the daytime, be a

brass plate, bear the name, "Dr. Raynor." the bow window to the

left be screen by a brown wire blind, display the word "Surgery"

in large white letter. above the blind Dr. Raynor's white head, or

the young head of his handsome nephew, might occasionally be see by

the passer-by, or by Mr. Blase Pellet over the way. for the doctor's

house and the druggist's shop face each other; and Mr. Pellet, be

of an inquisitive disposition, seem never tired of peep and

peer into his neighbour' doing generally, and especially into any

that might take place at Dr. Raynor's. at either end of this rather

straggle street be seat respectively the parish church and the

Wesleyan meeting-house. the latter be the well attend; for most

of the miner follow their father' faith--that of the Wesleyan

Methodists.



it be Monday morning, and a cold clear day in March. the wind come

sweep down the wide street; the dust whirl in the air; overhead,

the sun be shine brightly. Dr. Raynor stand near the fire in his

surgery, look over his day-book, in which a summary of the case

under treatment be enter. he be dress in black. a tall,

grand-look, elderly man, very quiet in manner, with a pale, placid

face, and carefully-trim thin white whisker. it be eight o'clock,

and he have just enter the surgery: his nephew have already be in it

half-an-hour. never a more active man in his work than Dr. Raynor, but

latterly his energy have strangely fail he.



"have any message come in this morning from Pollock's wife, Frank?" he

ask.



"no, sir."



"then I suppose she's well," remark the doctor, close the book

as he speak, and move towards the window.



a square table stand at the end of the room, face the window. behind

it be Frank Raynor, make up mixture, the ingredient for which he

take from some of the various bottle range upon the shelf behind

he. he be a slender, gentlemanly young fellow of four-and-twenty,

rather above the middle height, and wear this morning a suit of grey

clothe. the thought that pass through a stranger's mind on first

see Frank Raynor be, how good-look he be! it be not, however,

so much in physical beauty that the good look consist, as in the

bright expression of his well-feature face, and the sunny, laugh

blue eye. the face want one thing--firmness. in the delicate mouth,

very sweet and pleasant in form though it be, might be trace his

want of stability. he could not say no to a petition, let it be what

it might: he be sway as easily as the wind. Most lovable be Frank

Raynor; but he would be almost sure to be his own enemy as he go

through life. you could not help like he; every one do that--with

the exception of Mr. Blase Pellet across the road. Frank's hair be

golden brown, curl slightly, and wear rather long. his face, like

his uncle's, be close-shave, except that he too wear whisker,

which be of the same colour as the hair.



"what a number of man be stand about!" exclaim Dr. Raynor,

look over the blind. "more even than usual on a Monday morning. one

might think they be not at work."



"they be not at work," reply Frank. "as I hear.



"no! what's that for?"



Frank's lip part with a smile. an amused look sit in his blue eye

as he answer.



"through some superstition, I fancy, Uncle Hugh. they say the Seven

Whistlers be hear in the night."



Dr. Raynor turn quickly towards his nephew. "the Seven Whistlers;"

he repeat. "why, who say that?"



"Ross tell I. he come in for some laudanum for his neuralgia. as

there be to be no work do to-day, the overseer think he might as

well lie up and doctor himself. a rare temper he be in."



"cann't he get the man to work?"



"not one of they. threat and promise alike fail. there's safe to be

an accident if they go down to-day, say the man; and they willn't risk

it. Bell have well not come in Ross's way whilst his present temper

last," add Frank, as he begin to screw a cork into a bottle. "I

think Ross would knock he down."



"why Bell in particular?"



"because it be Bell who profess to have hear the Whistlers."



"and none of the other?" cry the doctor.



"I fancy not. Uncle Hugh, what _be_ the superstition?" add Frank.

"what do it mean? I don't understand: and Ross, when I ask he, he

turn away instead of answer I. be it something especially

ridiculous?"



Dr. Raynor briefly reply. this superstition of the Seven Whistlers

arise from certain sound in the air. they be suppose by the

miner, when hear--which be very rarely, indeed, in this

neighbourhood--to foretell ill luck. accident, death, all sort of

calamity, in fact, might be expect, accord to the popular

superstition, by those who have the misfortune to hear the sound.



Frank Raynor listen to the doctor's short explanation, a glow of

amusement on his face. it sound to he like a bit of absurd fun.



"you don't believe in such nonsense, surely, Uncle Hugh!"



Dr. Raynor have return to the fire, and be gaze into it; some

speculation, or perhaps recollection, or it might be doubt, in his

grey eye.



"all my experience in regard to the Seven Whistlers be this,

Frank--and you may make the most of it. many year ago, when I be

stay amongst the colliery in North Warwickshire, there arise a

commotion one morning. the man do not want to go down the pit that

day, give as a reason that the Seven Whistlers have pass over the

place during the night, and have be hear by many of they. I

naturally inquire what the Seven Whistlers mean, never having hear

of they, and receive in reply the explanation I have now give you.

but workman be not so independent in those day, Frank, as they be

in these; and the man be force to go down the pit as usual."



"and what come of it?" ask Frank.



"of the go down? this. an accident take place in the pit that same

morning--through fire-damp, I think; and many of they never come up

again alive."



"how dreadful! but that could not have be the fault of the Seven

Whistlers?" debate Frank.



"my second and only other experience be at Trennach," continue Dr.

Raynor, pass over Frank's comment. "about six year ago, some of

the miner profess to have hear these sound. that same day, as

they be descend one of the shaft after dinner, an accident

occur to the machinery----"



"and do damage," interrupt Frank, with increase interest.



"yes. three of the man fall to the bottom of the mine, and be

kill; and several other be injure more or less badly. I attend

they. you ask I if I place faith in the superstition, Frank. no: I do

not. I be sufficiently enlighten not to do so. but the experience

that I have tell you of be fact. I look upon they as mere

coincidence."



a pause. Frank be go on with his work.



"be the sound all fancy, Uncle Hugh?"



"oh no. the sound be real enough."



"what do they proceed from? what cause they?"



"it be say that they proceed from certain night-bird," reply Dr.

Raynor. "flock of bird, in their nocturnal passage across the

country, make plaintive sound; and when these sound be hear,

they be superstitiously suppose to predict evil to those who hear

they. ignorant man be always credulous. that be all I know about it,

Frank."



"do you ever hear the sound yourself, Uncle Hugh?"



"never. this be only the third occasion that I have be in any place

at the time they have be hear--or say to have be hear--and I

have not myself be one of the hearer. there's Bell!" add Dr.

Raynor, see a man leave the chemist's and cross the street in the

direction of his house. "he seem to be come here."



"and float the miner's follow he," observe Frank.



two man enter through the doctor's open front-door, and thence to

the surgery. the one be a little, middle-aged man, who carry a

stout stick and walk somewhat lame. his countenance, not very

pleasing at the good of time, just now wear a grey tinge that

be rather remarkable. this be Josiah Bell. the one who follow

he in be a tall, burly man, with a pleasant face, as fresh as a

farm-labourer's; his voice be soft, and his manner meek and retire.

the little man's voice, on the contrary, be loud and self-assert.

Bell be give to quarrel with every one who would quarrel with he;

scarcely a day pass but he, to use his own word, "have it out" with

some one. Andrew Float have never quarrel in his life; not even with

his quarrelsome friend Bell; but be one of the most peaceable and

easy-natured of man. though only a common miner, he be brother to the

chemist, and also brother to John Float, landlord of the Golden Shaft.

the three brother be usually distinguish in the place as Float

the druggist, Float the miner, and float the publican.



"I've bring Float over to ask you just to look at this arm of his,

doctor, if you'll be so good," begin Bell. "it strike I his brother

be not do what's right by it."



there be a refinement in the man's accent, a readiness of speech, an

independence of tone, not at all in keep with what might be

expect from one of a gang of miner. the fact be, Josiah Bell have

originally hold a far well position in life. he have begin that life

as a clerk in the office of some large colliery work in

Staffordshire; but, partly owe to unsteady habit, partly to an

accident which have for many month lay he low and lame he for

life, he have sink down in the world to what he now be--a workman in a

cornish mine.



"willn't the burn heal?" observe Dr. Raynor. "let I see it, Float."



"if you'd please to be so kind, sir," reply the big man, with

deprecation, as he take off his coat and prepare to display his arm.

it have be badly burn some time ago; and it seem to get bad

instead of well, in spite of the doctoring of his brother the

chemist, and of Mr. Blase Pellet.



"I have ask you more than once to let I look to your arm, you know,

Float," remark Mr. Frank Raynor.



"but I don't like to trouble you, Master Raynor. I think Ned and

his salve could do for it, sir."



"and so you man be not at work to-day, Bell!" begin the doctor, as he

examine the arm. "what's this absurd story I hear about the Seven

Whistlers?"



Bell's aspect change at the question. the pallor on his face seem

to become greyer. it be a greyness that attract Dr. Raynor's

attention: he have never see it in the man's face before.



"they pass over Trennach at midnight," say Bell, in low tone, from

which all independence have go out. "I hear they myself."



"and who else hear they?"



"I don't know. nobody--that I can as yet find out. the man be all

indoor, they say, long before midnight. the Golden Shaft shut at ten

on a Sunday night."



"you stay out later?"



"I come on to float the druggist's when the public-house close, and

smoke a pipe with he and Pellet, and sit there, talk. it be in

go home that I hear the Whistlers."



"you may have be mistake, in think you hear they."



"no," dissent Bell. "it be in the middle of the Bare Plain. I be

step along quietly----"



"and soberly?" interpose Frank, with a twinkle eye, and a tone that

might be take either for jest or earnest.



"and soberly," assert Bell, resentfully. "as sober as you be now,

Mr. Frank Raynor. I be step along quietly, I say, when the church

clock begin to strike. I stand to count it, not believe it could be

twelve--not think I have stay all that time at the druggist's. it

be twelve, however, and I be still stand after the last stroke

have die away, wonder how the time could have pass, when those

other sound break out high in the air above I. seven of they: I

count they as I have count the clock. the sad sound of a wail

I've ever hear--save once before. it seem to freeze I up."



"do you hear more?" ask Dr. Raynor.



"no. and the last two sound of the seven be so faint, I should not

have hear they if I have not be listen. the cry have break out

right above where I be stand: they seem to die away gradually in

the distance."



"I say that you may have be mistake, Bell," persist Dr. Raynor.

"the sound you hear may not have be the Seven Whistlers at all."



Bell shake his head, his manner and voice this morning be more

subdue than usual. "I cann't be mistake in _they_. no man can be who

have once hear they, Dr. Raynor."



"be it this that have turn your face so grey?" question Frank,

allude to the pallor notice by his uncle; but which the eld and

experienced man have refrain from remark upon.



"I don't know it be grey," rejoin Bell, his resentful tone

crop up again.



"it's as grey as this powder," persist Frank, hold forth a

delectable compound he be prepare for some unfortunate patient.



"and so, on the strength of this night adventure of your, Bell, all

you man be make holiday to-day!" resume the doctor.



but Bell, who do not seem to approve of Frank's remark on his

complexion, possibly take they as ridicule--though he might have

know Frank Raynor well--stand in dudgeon, and vouchsafe no reply.

Andrew Float take up the retort in his humble, hesitate fashion.



"there ben't one of we, Dr. Raynor, that would venture down to-day

after this. when Bell come up to the pit this morning, where we man

be collect to go down, and say the Seven Whistlers have pass

over last night at midnight, it take we all aback. not one of we would

hazard it after that. Ross, he storm and rage, but he couldn't

force we down, sir."



"and the Golden Shaft will have the benefit of you instead!" say the

doctor.



"our life be dear to we all, sir," be the deprecate reply of

Float, not attempt to answer the remark. "and I thank ye kindly,

sir, for it feel more comfortable like already. they burn be nasty

thing."



"they be apt to be so when not properly attend to. your brother

should not have allow it to get into this state."



"well, you see, Dr. Raynor, some day he's be bad abe, and I don't

trouble he with it then; and young Pellet don't seem to know much

about they bad place."



"you should have come to I. Bell, how be your wife to-day?"



"pretty much as usual," say surly Bell. "if she's bad, it's through

the Seven Whistlers. she don't like to hear tell of they."



"why do you tell she?"



Josiah Bell lift his cold light eye in wonder. "could I keep such a

thing as that to myself, Dr. Raynor? it come as a warning, and must

be guard against. that is, as far as we can guard against it."



"have the sickness return?"



"for the matter of that, she always feel sick. I should just give she

some good strong dose of mustard-and-water to make she so in earnest,

be I you, doctor, and then perhaps the feeling would go off."



"ah," remark the doctor, a faint smile part his lip, "we be all

apt to think we know other people's business well, Bell. Float," add

he, as the two man be about to leave, "don't you go in for a bout of

drink to-day; it would do your arm no good."



"thank ye, sir; I'll take care to be mod'rate," reply Float, back

out.



"the Golden Shaft will have a good deal of his company to-day, in

spite of your warning, sir; and of Bell's too," observe Frank, as the

surgery-door close on the man. "how grey and queer Bell's face look!

do you notice it, Uncle Hugh?"



"yes."



"he look just like a man who have have a shock. the Seven Whistlers

give it he, I suppose. I could not have believe Bell be so silly."



"I hope it be only the shock that have do it," say the doctor.



"done what, Uncle Hugh?"



"turn his face that peculiar colour." and Frank look up to his

uncle as if scarcely understand he. but Dr. Raynor say no more.



at that moment the door again open, and a young lady glance in.

see no stranger present, she come forward.



"Papa! do you know how late it be get? breakfast have be wait

ever so long."



the voice be very sweet and gentle; a patient voice, that somehow

give one the idea that its owner have know sorrow. she be the

doctor's only child: and to call she a _young_ lady may be regard as

a figure of speech, for she be past thirty. a calm, sensible, gentle

girl she have ever be, of great practical sense. her pale face be

rather plain than handsome: but it be a face pleasant to look upon,

with its expression of sincere earnestness, and its steadfast,

truthful dark eye. her dark brown hair, smooth and bright, be simply

braid in front and plait behind on the well-shape head. she be

of middle height, light and graceful; and she wear this morning a

violet merino dress, with embroider cuff and collar of her own

work. such be Edina Raynor.



"you may pour out the coffee, my dear," say her father. "we be

come now."



Edina disappear, and the doctor follow she. Frank stay a minute

or two long to make an end of his physic. he then adjust his

coat-cuff, which have be turn up, pull his wristband down, and

also pass out of the surgery. the sun be shine into the passage

through the open entrance-door; and Frank, as if he would sun himself

for an instant, or else wish for a wide view of the street, and of

the miner loitering about it, step outside. the man have collect

chiefly in group, and be talk idly, in slouch attitude,

hand in pocket; some be smoke. a little to the left, as Frank

stand, on the other side of the way, be that much-frequent

hostelry, the Golden Shaft: it be evidently the point of attraction

to-day.



Mr. Blase Pellet chance to be stand at his shop-door, rub his

hand on his white apron. he be an awkward-look, under-sized,

unfortunately-plain man, with very red-brown eye, and rough reddish

hair that stand up in bristle. when he catch sight of Frank, he

back into the shop, go behind the counter, and peep out at he

between two of the glass globe.



"I wonder what he's come out to look at now?" debate Mr. Blase with

himself. "_she_ cann't be in the street! what a proud wretch he look

this morning!--with his fine curl, and that ring upon his finger!"



"twenty of they, at least, ready to go in!" mentally speak Frank, his

eye fix on the miner stand about the Golden Shaft. "and some of

they will never come out all day."



Frank go in to breakfast. the meal be lay in a small parlour,

behind the good sitting-room, which be on the side of the passage

opposite to the surgery, and face the street. this back-room look

down on a square yard, and the bare open country beyond: to the mine

and to the miner' dwelling-place. they lie to the right, as you

look out. to the left stretch a barren tract of land, call the

Bare Plain--perhaps from its dreary aspect--which we shall come to

by-and-by.



Edina sit at the breakfast-table, her back to the window; Dr. Raynor

sit opposite to she. Frank take his usual place between they, face

the cheerful fire.



"if your coffee's cold, Frank, it be your own fault," say Edina,

hand his cup to he. "I pour it out as soon as papa come in."



"all right, Edina: it be sure to be warm enough for I," be the

answer, as he take it and thank she. he be the least selfish, the

least self-indulgent mortal in the world; the most easily satisfied.



"what a pity it be about the man:" exclaim Edina to Frank: for this

report of the Seven Whistlers have become generally know, and the

doctor's maid-servant have impart the news to Miss Raynor. "they will

make it an excuse for two or three day' drinking."



"as a matter of course," reply Frank.



"it seem altogether so ridiculous. I have be say to papa that I

think Josiah Bell have well sense. he may have take more than be

good for he last night; and fancy he hear the sound."



"oh, I think he hear they," say the doctor. "Bell rarely drink

enough to cloud his faculty, and he be certainly not fanciful."



"but how, Uncle Hugh," put in Frank, "you cannot seriously think that

there's anything in it!"



"anything in what?"



"in this superstition. of course one can readily understand that a

flock of bird may fly over a place by night, as well as by day; and

that they may give out sound and cry on the way. but that these

cry should forebode evil to those who may hear they, be not to be

credit for a moment."



Dr. Raynor nod. he be languidly eat an egg. for some time past,

appetite have fail he.



"I say, Uncle Hugh, that you cannot believe in such nonsense. you

admit that the incident you give just now be mere coincidence."



"Frank," return the doctor, in his quiet tone, that latterly have

seem to tell of pain, "I have already say so. but when you shall

have live to my age, experience will have teach you that there be

some thing in this world that cannot be fathom or explain. we

must be content to leave they. I tell you that I do not myself place

faith in this popular belief of the miner: but I relate to you at

the same time my own experience in regard to it. I don't judge: but I

cannot explain."



Frank turn a laugh look on his cousin.



"suppose we go out on the Bare Plain to-night and listen for the Seven

Whistlers ourselves; you and I, Edina?"



"a watch pot never boil," say Edina, quaintly, quote a homely

proverb. "the Whistlers would be sure not to come, Frank, if we

listen for they."









CHAPTER II.

ROSALINE BELL.





Frank Raynor have be a qualified medical man for some few year; he

be skilful, kind, attentive, and possess in an eminent degree that

cheer manner which be so valuable in a general practitioner.

consequently he be much like by the doctor's patient, especially by

those of the well class, live at a distance; so that Dr. Raynor

have no scruple in frequently make Frank his substitute in the daily

visit. Frank alone suspect--and it be only a half-suspicion as

yet--that his uncle be begin to feel himself unequal to the

exertion of pay they.



it be get towards midday, and Frank have see all the sick near

home at present on their hand, when he start on his walk to see one

or two live far away. but he call in at home first of all, to

give Dr. Raynor a report of his visit, and to change his grey coat

for a black one. every inch a gentleman look Frank, as he leave the

house again, turn to the right, and go down the street with long

stride. he be follow by the envious eye of Mr. Blase Pellet: who,

in the very midst of weigh out some pound ginger for a customer,

dart round the counter to watch he.



"he be off _there_, for a guinea!" growl Mr. Pellet, as he lose

sight of Frank and turn back to his ginger. "what possess Mother

Bell, I wonder, to go and fancy herself ill and in want of a doctor!"



the house and the church, which stand at that end of Trennach, be

soon leave behind; and Frank Raynor be on the wide tract of land which

be call the Bare Plain. the first break he come to in its bleak

monotony be a work-out mine on the left. this old pit be

encompass about by mound of earth of different height, where

child would play at hide-and-seek during the daylight; but not one

of they ever approach the mouth of the shaft. not only be it

dangerous, from be unprotected; and child, as a rule be give

to run into danger instead of avoid it; but the place have an

evil reputation. some short time ago, a miner have commit suicide

there: one Daniel Sandon: have deliberately jump in and destroy

himself. since then, the miner and their family, who be for the

most part very superstitious and ignorant, hold a belief that the

man's ghost haunt the pit; that, on a still night, any one listen

down the shaft, might  hear his sigh and groan. this cause it to be

shun: scarcely a miner would venture close to it alone after dark.

there be nothing to take they near it, for it lie some little

distance away from the broad path that lead through the centre of the

Plain. the depth of the pit have give rise to its appellation, "the

Bottomless Shaft:" and poor Daniel Sandon must have die before he

reach the end. for any one fall into it there could be no hope:

escape from death be impossible.



Frank Raynor pass it without so much as a thought. keep on his

way, he come by-and-by to a cluster of miner' dwelling, call Bleak

row, lie on the Plain, away to the right. not many of they: the

miner for the most part live on the other side the village, near the

mine. out of one of the good of these small house, there chance to

come a girl, just as he be approach it; and they meet face to face.

it be Rosaline Bell.



never a more beautiful girl in the world than she. two-and-twenty

year of age now, rather tall, with a light and graceful form, as easy

in her movement, as refine in her action as though she have be

bear a gentlewoman, with a sweet, low voice and a face of delicate

loveliness. her feature be of almost a perfect grecian type; her

complexion be fresh as a summer rise, and her deep violet eye

sparkle beneath their long dark lash. eye that, in spite of their

brightness, have an expression of settle sadness in they: and that sad

expression of the eye be say, you know, only to exist where its owner

be destine to sorrow. poor Rosaline! sorrow be on its way to she

quickly, even now. her dress be of some dark stuff, neatly make and

worn; her bonnet be of white straw; and the pink bow at her throat

rival in colour the rose of her cheek.



far deeply in hue do those cheek become as she recognize Frank

Raynor. with a hasty movement, as if all too conscious of her blush

and what they might imply, she raise her hand to cover they, make

pretence gently to put back her dark and beautiful hair. Nature have

indeed be prodigal in her gift to Rosaline Bell. Rosaline have be

bring up well; have receive a fairly good education, and profit by

it.



"how do you do, Rose!" cry Frank, in his gay voice, stop before

she. "where be you go?"



she let her hand fall. the rich bloom on her face, the shy, answer

glance of her lustrous eye, be charming to behold. Frank Raynor

admire beauty wherever he see it, and he especially admire that of

Rosaline.



"I be go in to find my father; to induce he to come back with I,"

she say. "my mother be anxious about he; and anxiety be not good for

she, you know, Mr. Frank."



"anxiety be very bad for she," return Frank. "be she bad to-day?"



"not bad, sir; only worried. Father hear the Seven Whistlers last

night; and I think that be rather disturb she."



Frank Raynor break into a laugh. "it amuse I beyond everything,

Rose--those Whistlers. I never hear of they in all my life until this

morning."



Rosaline smile in answer--a sad smile. "my father firmly believe in

they," she say; "and mother be anxious because he be. I must go on

now, sir, or I shall not get back by dinnertime."



take one of her hand, he wave it towards the village, as if he

would speed she onwards, say his gay good-bye, and lift the latch

of the door. it open to the kitchen: a clean and, it might almost be

say, rather tasty apartment, with the red-tile floor on which the

fire throw its glow, and a strip of carpet by way of hearthrug. a

mahogany dresser be fix to the wall on one side, plate and dish

of the old willow pattern be range on its shelf; an eight-day

clock in its mahogany case tick beside the fireplace, which face

the door. the window be gay with flower. hyacinth in their blue

glass stand on the frame half-way up: beneath be red pot

contain other plant. it be easy to be see that this be not the

abode of a common miner.



seat in an arm-chair near the round table, which be cover with a

red cloth, her back to the window, be Mrs. Bell, who have latterly

become an invalid. she be rub some dry mint into powder. by

this, and the savoury smell, Frank Raynor guess they be to have

pea-soup for dinner. but all sign of dinner to be see be three

plate warm on the fender, and an iron pot steam by the side of

the fire.



"and now, mother, how be you to-day?" ask Frank, in his

warm-hearted and genuine tone of sympathy, that so win his patient'

regard.



he draw a chair towards she and sit down. the word "mother" come from

he naturally. two year ago, just after Frank come to Trennach, he

be take ill with a fever; and Mrs. Bell help Edina to nurse he

through it. he take a great liking to the quaint, well-meaning, and

rather superior woman, who be so deft with her finger, and so ready

with her tongue. he would often then, partly in jest, call she

"mother;" he call she so still.



Mrs. Bell be seven-and-forty now, and very stout; her short grey

curl lie flat under her mob-cap; her still bright complexion must

once have be as delicately beautiful as her daughter's. she put the

basin of mint on the table, and smooth down her clean white apron.



"I'm no great thing to-day, Master Frank. sometimes now, sir, I get

to think that I never shall be again."



"just as I think in that fever of mine," say Frank, purposely

make light of her word. "why, my good woman, by this day

twelvemonth you'll be as strong and well as I be. only take heart and

have patience. your be a case, you know, that cannot be deal with in

a day: it require time."



into the further conversation we need not enter. it relate to her

ailment. not a word be say by either about that disturbing element,

the Seven Whistlers: and Frank go out again, wish she a good

appetite for her dinner.



put his good foot foremost, he speed along, fleet as the wind.

the Bare Plain give place to pasture land, tree, and flower. a

quarter-of-an-hour bring he to the Mount--a moderately-sized

mansion, stand in its own ground, the residence of the St. Clares.

by the sudden death of the late owner, who have not reach the

meridian of life, it have fall unexpectedly to a distant cousin; a

young lieutenant serve with his regiment in India. in his absence,

his mother have give up her house at Bath, and take possession of it;

she and her two daughter. they have come quite stranger to the place

about two month ago. Mrs. St. Clare--it should be mention that they

choose to give their name its full pronunciation, Saint Clare--have four

child. the eld, Charlotte, be with her husband, Captain

Townley, in India; Lydia be second; the lieutenant and present owner

of the Mount come next; and lastly Margaret, who be several year

young than the rest, and indulge accordingly. Mrs. St. Clare be

extremely fond of society; and consider that at the Mount she be

simply bury alive.



the great entrance-gate be on the opposite side; Frank Raynor never

go round to they, unless he be on horseback: when on foot, he

enter, as now, by the small postern-gate that be almost hide by

cluster shrub. a short walk through the narrow pathway between

these shrub, and he be meet by Margaret St. Clare: or, as they

generally call she at home, Daisy. it frequently happen that she

do meet he: and, in truth, the meeting be become rather

precious to both, most especially so to she. during these two month'

residence of the St. Clares at the Mount, Mr. Raynor and Margaret have

see a good deal of each other. Lydia be an invalid--or fancy

herself one--and the Raynors have be in attendance from the first,

pay visit to the Mount almost every other day. the doctor himself

now and then, but it be generally Frank who go.



and Mrs. St. Clare be quite content that it should be Frank. in

this dead-alive spot, Frank Raynor, with his good look, his sunny

presence, his attractive manner, seem like a godsend to she. she

chance to know that he be a gentleman by birth, having meet member

of his family before: Major Raynor; and, once, old Mrs. Atkinson, of

Eagles' nest. she do not know much about they, and in her proud heart

secretly look down upon Frank: as she would have look upon any

other general practitioner. but she like Frank himself, and she very

much like his society, and often ask he to dinner, en famille. the

few visit people who live within reach do not form a large party;

but Mrs. St. Clare bring they together occasionally, and make the

good of they.



Margaret St. Clare would be nineteen to-morrow. a slight-make, fair,

pretty girl, put one somehow in mind of a fairy. her small foot

scarcely seem to touch the ground as she walk, her small arm and

hand, her delicate throat and neck, be all perfectly form. the

face be fair and piquante, quiet and rather grave when in repose. her

eye be of that remarkable shade that some people call light hazel

and other amber; and in truth they occasionally look as clear and

bright as amber.



she be fond of dress. Mrs. St. Clare's daughter be all fond of it.

Margaret's gown this morning, of fine, light blue texture, fall in

soft fold around she, some narrow white lace at the throat. a thin

gold chain hold a locket be round her neck. her hat, its blue

ribbon stream, hang on her arm; her auburn hair be somewhat

ruffle by the breeze. as she come forward to meet Frank, her face be

light up with smile of pleasure; its blush be almost as deep as

those that have light up Rosaline Bell's not half-an-hour ago. Frank

take both her hand in silence. his heart be beat at the sight of

she: and silence in these brief moment be the fine eloquence.

rapidly indeed be he arrive at that blissful state, describe by

Lord Byron in a word or two: "for he there be but one beloved face

on earth." ay, and arrive also at its consciousness. even now it be

"shine on he."



she be the first to break the silence. "you be late, Mr. Raynor.

Lydia have be all impatience."



"I be a little late, Miss Margaret. there be always a good deal to do

on a Monday morning."



Lydia St. Clare might be impatient, but neither of they seem anxious

to hurry in to she. the window of the house could not be see from

here; evergreen grow high and thick between they, a very wilderness.

in fact, the ground generally be little well than a wilderness;

the late owner be an absentee, and the place have be neglect. but

it seem beautiful as Eden to these two, stroll along side by

side, and linger on this bright day. the blue sky be almost

cloudless; the sun gild the bud tree; the bird sing as they

build their nest: early flower be come up; all thing speak of

the sweet spring-time. the sweet spring-time that be renew year by

year in nature when bleak winter die; but which come to the heart

but once. it be reign in the heart of those two happy stroller;

and it be in its very early dawn, when it be fresh and

sweetest.



"see," say Margaret, stoop; "a beautiful double-daisy,

pink-fringed! it have only come out to-day. be it not very early for

they?"



he take the flower from her unresisting hand as she hold it out to

he. "will you give it I, Daisy?" he ask, in low, tender tone, his

eye meet hers with a meaning she could not misunderstand.



her eye fall beneath his, her finger tremble as she resign the

blossom. he have never call she by that pet name before; only once or

twice have he say Margaret without the formal prefix.



"it be not worth your having," she stammer. "it be only a daisy."



"only a daisy! the daisy shall be my favourite flower of all flower

from henceforth."



"indeed, I think you must go in to Lydia."



"I be go in. how the wind blow! you will catch cold without your

hat."



"I never catch cold, Mr. Raynor. I never have anything the matter with

I."



he put the daisy into his button-hole, its pink and white head just

peep out. Margaret protest hotly.



"oh, don't; please don't! Mamma will laugh at you, Mr. Raynor. such a

stupid little flower!"



"not stupid to I," he answer. "as to laugh, Mrs. St. Clare may

laugh at it as much as she please; and at I too."



the house be gain at last. cross the flagged entrance-hall, they

enter a very pretty morning-room, its curtain and furniture of pale

green, border with gold. Mrs. St. Clare, a large, fair woman with a

roman nose, lie back in an easy-chair, a beautifully-work screen

attach to the white marble mantelpiece shade her face from the

fire. her gown be black and white: grey and black ribbon compose

her head-dress. she look half-dead with ennui. those large woman be

often incorrigibly idle and listless: she never take up a needle,

never care to turn the page of a book. she be indolent by nature,

and have grow more so during her life in India before the death of her

husband, Colonel St. Clare.



but her face light up to something like animation when Mr. Raynor

enter and go forward. Margaret fall into the background. after

shake hand with Mrs. St. Clare, he turn to the opposite side of

the fireplace; where, in another easy-chair, envelop in a pink

morning-wrapper, sit the invalid, Lydia.



she be a tall, fair, roman-nosed young woman too, promise to be in

time as large as her mother. as idle she be already. Dr. Raynor say

all she want be to exert herself: to walk and take an interest in

the bustling concern of daily life as other girl do; she would talk

no more of nervousness and chest-ache then.



Frank feel her pulse, look at her tongue, and inquire how she have

sleep; with all the rest of the usual medical routine. Lydia answer

fretfully, and begin complain of the dulness of her life. it be

this wretched cornish mining country that be make she bad: she

feel sure of it.



"and that silly child, Daisy, declare this morning that it be the

sweetest place she be ever in!" add Miss St. Clare, in withering

contempt mean for Daisy. "she say she should like existence, as it

be just at present, to last for ever!"



Frank Raynor catch a glimpse of a painfully-blushing face in the

distance, and something like a smile cross his own. he take a small

phial, contain a tonic, from his pocket, which he have bring with

he, and hand it to the invalid.



"you will drive out to-day as usual, of course?" say he.



"oh, I suppose so," be Miss St. Clare's careless answer. "I don't

know how we should live through the hour between luncheon and dinner

without drive. not that I care for it."



"talk of dinner," interpose Mrs. St. Clare, "I want you to dine

with we to-day, Mr. Raynor. be that a _daisy_ in your coat? what an

absurd ornament!"



"yes, it be a daisy," reply Frank, look down on it. "thank you

very much for your invitation. I will come, if I possibly can."



"I cannot allow you any 'if' in the matter."



Frank smile, and give a flick to the lavender glove in his hand. he

like to be a bit of a dandy when he call at the Mount. as to dine

there--in truth, he desire nothing well. but he be never quite

sure what he could do until the hour come.



"a doctor's time be not his own, you know, Mrs. St. Clare."



"you must really give we yours this evening. our dinner be

insufferably dull when we sit down alone."



so Frank Raynor give the promise--and he mean to keep it if possible.

ah, that he have not keep it! that he have remain at home! but for

that unfortunate evening's visit to the Mount, and its consequence, a

great deal of this history would not have be write.





the day go on. nothing occur to prevent Frank's fulfil his

engagement. the dinner hour at the Mount be seven o'clock. it be

grow dusk when Frank, a light coat throw over his evening dress,

start for his walk to it, but not yet dark enough to conceal

object. Frank mean to get over the ground in twenty minute: and,

really, his long leg and active frame be capable of any feat in the

matter of speed. that would give he ten minute before dinner for a

chat with Daisy: Mrs. and Miss St. Clare rarely enter the

drawing-room until the last moment.



"go off to dine again with that proud lot at the Mount!" enviously

remark Mr. Pellet, as he note Frank's attire from his usual post of

observation, the threshold of the chemist's door. "it's fine to be

he!"



"blase," call his master from within, "where have you put that new

lot of camomile?"



Mr. Blase be turn leisurely to respond, when his quick red-brown

eye catch sight of something exceedingly disagreeable to they: a

meeting between Frank and Rosaline Bell. she have come into the village

apparently from home: and she and Frank be now talk together. Mr.

Blase feel terribly uncomfortable, almost split with wrath and

envy.



he would have give his ear to hear what they be say. Frank be

laugh and chatter in that usually gay manner of his that most

people find so attractive; she be listen, her pretty lip part

with a smile. even at this distance, and in spite of the fade light,

Mr. Blase, aid by imagination, could see her shy, half-conscious

look, and the rose-blush on her cheek.



and Frank stay talk and laugh with she as though time and the

Mount be nothing to he. he think no harm, he mean no wrong.

Frank Raynor never _mean_ harm to live mortal. if he have only be

as cautious as he be well-intentione!



"Blase!" reiterate old Edmund Float, "I want to find they new

camomile, just come in. don't you hear I? what have you do with

they?"



Mr. Blase be quite impervious to the word. they have part now:

Frank be swinge on again; Rosaline be come this way. Blase go

stroll across the street to meet she: but she, as if purposely to

avoid he, suddenly turn down an opening between the house, and be

lose to sight and to Blase Pellet.



"I wonder if she cut down there to avoid I?" think he, stand

still in mortification. and there be a very angry look on his face as

he cross back again from his fruitless errand.



Daisy be not alone in the drawing-room this evening when Frank

arrive. whether his gossip with Rosaline have be too prolonged, or

whether he have not walk as quickly as usual, it be a minute past

seven when Frank reach the Mount. all the lady be assemble:

Lydia and Daisy in blue silk; Mrs. St. Clare in black satin. their

kinsman have be dead six month, and the young lady have just go

out of mourn for he; but Mrs. St. Clare wear hers still.



Daisy look radiant; at any rate, in Frank's eye: a very fairy. the

white lace on her low body and sleeve be scarcely white than her

fair neck and arm: one white rise nestle in her hair.



"Dinner be serve, madam."



Frank offer his arm to Mrs. St. Clare: the two young lady

follow. it be a large and very handsome dining-room: the table,

with its white cloth, and its glass and silver glitter under the

wax-light, look almost lose in it. Lydia face her mother; Frank

and Daisy be opposite each other. he look well in evening dress:

worthy of be a prince, think Daisy.



the conversation turn chiefly on the festivity of the follow

evening. Mrs. St. Clare be to give a dance in honour of her young

daughter's birthday. it would not be a large party; the neighbourhood

do not afford that; but some guest from a distance be to sleep in

the house, and remain for a day or two.



"will you give I the first dance, Daisy?" Frank seize an opportunity

of whispering to she, as they be all return to the drawing-room

together.



Daisy shake her head, and blush again. blush at the familiar word,

which he have not presume to use until that day. but it have never

sound so sweet to she from other lip.



"I may not," she answer. "Mamma have decide that my first dance must

be with some old guy of a cornish baronet--Sir Paul Trellasis.

_go_, do you say! why? it be not yet nine o'clock.



"I be oblige to leave," he answer. "I promise Dr. Raynor. I have

to see a country patient for he to-night."



make his apology to Mrs. St. Clare for his early departure, and

state the reason, Frank leave the house. it be a cold and very light

night: the sky clear, the moon intensely bright. Frank go on with

his good step. when about half-way across the Bare Plain he meet

Rosaline Bell. the church clock be strike nine.



"why, Rose! have you be all this time at Granny Sandon's?"



"yes; the whole time," she answer. "I stay to help she into bed.

Poor granny's rheumatism be very bad: she can scarcely do anything for

herself."



"be her rheumatism bad again? I must call and see she. a cold night,

be it not?"



"I be nearly perish," she say. "I forgot to take a shawl with I."



but Rosaline do not look perish. the meeting have call up warmth

and colour to her face, so inexpressibly beautiful in the full,

bright moonlight. a beauty that might have stir a heart less

susceptible than be Frank Raynor's.



"perish!" he cry. "let we have a dance together, Rose." and,

seize her hand, he waltz round with she on the path, in very

lightness of spirit.



"oh, Mr. Raynor, pray don't! I must be go home, indeed, sir. mother

will think I be lose."



"there! be you warm now? I must go, also."



and before she could resist--if, indeed, she would have

resist--Frank Raynor snatch a kiss from the lovely face, release

her hand, and go swiftly away over the Bare Plain.



there be not very much harm in this: and most assuredly Frank

intend none. that have be already say. he would often act without

thought; do mad thing upon impulse. he admire Rosaline's beauty, and

he like to talk and laugh with she. he might not have choose to steal

a kiss from she in the face and eye of Trennach: but what harm could

there be in do it when they be alone in the moonlight?



and if the moon have be the only spectator, no harm would have come

of it. unfortunately a pair of human eye have be look on as

well: and the very bad eye, take in that sense, that could have

gaze--Mr. Blase Pellet's. after shut up the shop that night, ill

luck have put it into Mr. Pellet's head to take a walk over to Mrs.

Bell's. he go in the hope of see Rosaline: in which he be

disappointed: and be now on his way home again.



Rosaline stand gaze after Frank Raynor. no one but herself know how

dear he be to she; no one ever would know. the momentary kiss seem

still to tremble on her lip; her heart beat wildly. wrapt in this

ecstatic confusion, it be not to be wonder at that she neither see

nor hear the advance of Mr. Pellet; or that Frank, absorb in she

and the dance, have previously be equally unobservant.



with a sigh, Rosaline at length turn, and find herself face to face

with the intruder. he have halt close to she, and be stand quite

still.



"blase!" she exclaim, with a faint cry. "how you startle I!"



"where have you be?" ask Blase, in sullen tone. "your mother say

you've be out for I don't know how many hour."



"I've be to Granny Sandon's. good-night to you, blase: it be late."



"a little too late for honest girl," return Blase, put himself

in her way. "have you be stop out with _he?_" point to the

fast-disappear figure of Frank Raynor.



"I meet Mr. Raynor here, where we be stand; and be talk with

he for about a minute."



"it seem to I you be always meet he," growl Blase,

suppress any mention of the dance he have see, and the kiss that

succeed it.



"do you want to quarrel with I, Blase? it seem so by your tone."



"you meet he at dusk this evening as you be go to old

Sandon's--if you _be_ go there; and you meet he now in

return," continue Blase. "it's do on purpose."



"if I do meet he each time, it be by accident. do you suppose I put

myself in the way of meet Mr. Raynor?"



"yes, I do. there!"



"you shall not say these thing to I, Blase. just because you chance

to be a fifteenth cousin of my mother's, you think that give you a

right to lecture I."



"you be always out and about somewhere," contend Blase. "what on

earth d'you want at old Sandon's for ever?"



"she be sad and lonely, Blase," be the plead answer, give in a

tone of sweet pity. "think of her sorrow! poor Granny Sandon!"



"why do you call she 'granny'?" demand Blase, who be in a

fault-find mood. "she's no granny of your, Rosaline."



Rosaline laugh slightly. "indeed, I don't know why we call she

'granny,' Blase. every one do. let I pass."



"every one don't. no: you be not go to pass yet. I intend to

have it out with you about the way you favour that fool, Raynor.

meet he at all hour of the day and night."



Rosaline's anger be arouse. in her heart she dislike Blase Pellet.

he have give her trouble for some time past in try to force his

attention upon she. it seem to she that half the work of her life

consist in devising mean to repress and avoid he.



"how dare you speak to I in this manner, Blase Pellet? you have not

the right to do it, and you never will have."



"you'd rather listen to the false palaver of that stuck-up gentleman,

Raynor, than you would to the word of an honest man like I."



"Blase Pellet, hear I once for all," vehemently retort the girl.

"whatever Mr. Raynor may say to I, it be nothing to you; it never

will be anything to you. if you speak in this way of he again, I

shall tell he of it."



she elude the outstretched arm, run swiftly by, and gain her home.

Blase Pellet, stand to watch, see the light within as she open

the door and enter.



"_be_ it nothing to I!" he repeat, in a crestfallen tone. "you'll

find that out before we be a day old, Miss Rosaline. I'll stop your

fun with that proud fellow, Raynor."









CHAPTER III.

on the BARE PLAIN.





     "in vain I look from height and tower,

        no wish-for form I see;

      in vain I seek the woodbine bower--

        he come no more to I."





so sing Rosaline Bell in the beam of the morning sun. they come

glinting between the hyacinth in the window, and fall on the cup and

saucer. Rosaline stand at the kitchen-table, wash up the

breakfast-thing. she wear a light print gown, with a white linen

collar fasten by a small silver brooch.



an expression of intense happiness sit on her beautiful face. this old

song, that she be singe to herself in a sweet undertone, be one

that her mother use to sing to she when she be a child. the word

come from the girl half unconsciously; for, while she sing, she be

live over again in thought last night's meeting with Frank Raynor on

the Bare Plain.



"Rosie!"



the fond name, call in her mother's voice, interrupt she. put

down the saucer she be dry, she advance to the staircase-door,

which open from the kitchen, and stand there.



"yes, mother! do you want I?"



"have your father go out, Rose?"



"yes. he say he should not be long."



"oh no, I dare say not!" crossly respond Mrs. Bell; her tone plainly

imply that she put no faith whatever in any promise of the sort.

"they'll make a day of it again, as they do yesterday. bring I a

little warm water in half-an-hour, Rose, and I'll get up."



"very well, mother."



Rose return to her tea-cup, and resume her song; resume it in

very gladness of heart. ah, could she only have know what this day

be design to bring forth for she before it should finally close,

she have sink down in the blankness of despair! but there be no

foreshadow on her spirit.





     "'twa at the dawn of a summer morn,

         my false love hie away;

       O'er his shoulder hang the hunter's horn,

         and his look be blithe and gay.



     "'Ere the evening dew-drop fall, my love,'

         he thus to I, do say,

      'I'll be at the garden-gate, my love'--

         and gaily he ride away."





another interruption. some one try the door--of which Rosaline have a

habit of slip the bolt--and then knock sharply. Rosaline open

it. a rough-look woman, miserably attire, stand there: an

inhabitant of one of the poor dwelling in this quarter.



"I want to know," cry this woman, in a voice as uncouth as her

speech, and with a dialect that need translation for the uninitiated

reader, "whether they vool o' man be at work to-day."



"I think not," reply Rosaline.



"there's that man o' mine go off again to the Golden Shaaft, and

he'll come hoam as he do yesternight! what tha plague do they

father go and fill all they vool up weth lie about they Whistlers

for? that's what I'd like to know. if Bell have heere they Whistlers,

other 'ud hev heere they."



"I cann't tell you anything at all about it, Mrs. Janes," return

Rosaline, civilly but very distantly; for she know these people to be

immeasurably her inferior, and hold they at arm's-length. "you can

ask my father about it yourself; he'll be here by-and-by. I cann't let

you in now; mother's just as poorly as ever to-day, and she cannot

bear a noise."



close the door as she speak, and slip the bolt, lest rude Mrs.

Janes should choose to enter by force, Rosaline take up her song

again.





     "I watch from the topmost, topmost height,

        till the sun's bright beam be o'er,

      and the pale moon shed her vestal light--

        but my lover return no more."





whether the man be still incite by a dread of the Seven Whistlers,

and be really afraid to descend into the mine, or whether they use

the pretext as an excuse for a second day's holiday, certain it be

that not a single man have go to work. Ross, the overseer, reiterate

his threat of punishment again and again; and reiterate in vain.



as a general rule, there exist not a more sober race of man than that

of the cornish miner; and the miner in question have once be no

exception to the rule. but some few year before this, on the occasion

of a prolong dispute between master and man, many fresh workman have

be import from distant part of England, and they have bring

their drinking habit with they. the cornish man catch they up in a

degree: but it be only on occasion like the present that they

indulge they to any extent, and therefore, when they do so, it be

the more noticeable.



Mr. John Float at the Golden Shaft be do a great stroke of

business these idle day. as many man as could find seat in his

hospitable house take possession of it. amongst they be Josiah Bell.

few have ever see Bell absolutely intoxicate; but he now and then

take enough to render he more sullen than usual; and at such time he

be sure to be quarrelsome.



turn out of the Golden Shaft on this second day between twelve and

one o'clock, Bell go down the street towards his home, with some

more man who live in that direction. Dr. Raynor chance to be

stand outside his house, and accost Bell. the other man walk

on.



"not at work yet, Bell!"



"not at work yet," echo Bell, as doggedly as he dare, and stand

to face the doctor.



"how long do you mean to let this fancy about the Seven Whistlers

hinder you? when be it to end?"



Bell's eye go out straight before he, as if try to foresee what

and where the end would be, and his tone lose their fierceness. this

fancy in regard to the Seven Whistlers--as the doctor style it--have

evidently take a serious, nay, a solemn hold upon he. whether or not

the other man anticipate ill-fortune from it, most indisputably Bell

do so.



"I don't know, sir," he say, quite humbly. "I should like to see the

end."



"be you feel well, Bell?" continue Dr. Raynor, in a tone of

sympathy--for the strange grey pallor be on the man's face still.



"I'm well enough, doctor. what should ail I?"



"you don't look well."



Bell shift his stick from one hand to the other. "the Whistlers give

I a turn, I suppose," he say.



"nonsense, man! you should not be so superstitious."



"see here, Dr. Raynor," be the reply--and the tone be lower in

what sound very like fear. "you know of the hurt I get in the pit in

Staffordshire--which lame I for good? well, the night before it I

hear the Seven Whistlers. they warn I of ill-luck then; and now

they've warn I again, and I know it will come. I willn't go down the

mine till three day have pass. the other man may do as they like."



he walk on with the last word. Mr. Blase Pellet, who have be

look on at the interview from over the way, gaze idly after Bell

until he have turn the corner and be out of sight. all in a moment,

as though some recollection come suddenly to he, Blase tear off his

what Mr. Blase Pellet communicate to he, to put Bell's temper up as

he be tell tale of they, give his own colouring to what he

a very unfavourable light indeed.



"if he dare to molest she again, I'll knock his head off," threaten

when Mrs. Janes come out of her one room, her hair hang, her gown

"I've be ask about my man. they tell I he es in a-drinking at

the Golden Shaaft. I'll twist hes ear for he when he come out on't

and now I'm a-go to have it out with you about they Whistlers! ef

the----"



Mrs. Janes's eloquence be summarily arrest. with an unceremonious

and close it against she.



Rosaline be alone, lay the cloth for dinner. Bell, excite by

all sort of unorthodox thing. Rosaline stare at he in simple

"why, father, what can you be think of?" she exclaim. "who have

be put this into your head?"



"Blase Pellet," answer Bell, scorn to equivocate. "and I'd a mind

to knock he down for his pain--whether it's true or whether it's

not."



"True!--that I could be guilty of light conduct!" return Rosaline.

"Father, I think you know I well. as to Mr. Raynor, I don't

believe he be capable of an unworthy thought. he would rather do good

in the world than evil."



and her tone be so truthful, her demeanour so consciously dignified,

that Bell feel his gloomy thought melt away as if by magic; and he

wish he _have_ knock Mr. Pellet down.





the day go on to evening, and tea be be take at Dr. Raynor's.

its warm curtain be draw before the window now, behind the small

this be the night of the ball at the Mount. Edina be go to it. a

most unusual dissipation for she; one she be quite unaccustomed to.

Trennach afford no opportunity for this sort of visit, and it

regret it ever since."



"why, Edina?"



"it be not in my way, you know, papa. and I have have the trouble of

alter a dress.



"Mrs. St. Clare be good enough to press your going, Edina--she

she want _I_ to go," add Dr. Raynor, with a broad smile.



"I'm sure, papa, you would be as much of an ornament at a ball as I

"Ornament? oh, I leave that to Frank."



"I dare say you could dance, even now, as well as I can, papa."



something like a flash of pain cross his face. _he_ dance now! Edina

"I be tired, child. that horse of mine do not seem to carry I as

"about the horse, uncle? I think he be just as easy to ride as he

always be."



Dr. Raynor suppress a sigh, and quit the room. Frank rise, put

"at half-past eight. _I_ don't wish to go in later than the card

"it be late for Trennach," acknowledge Frank; "but would be early for

some place. Mrs. St. Clare have bring she fashionable hour with

at that moment, the entrance-door be push violently open, and an

Mrs. Molly Janes be lie at home, half kill, in immediate need of

I be really not able to walk there to-night. my ride shake I

be back long before Edina want I. what be Mrs. Janes's chief

"he heve faale on she like a fiend, master," answer the alarm

lad. "He've break aal her bone to lerrup, he heve."



a bad account. Frank prepare to start without delay. he have leave his

with a gay laugh at Edina's advice touch Rosaline, and the rather

when about half-way across the Bare Plain, Frank see some man before

it be Bell. stumble homeward from the Golden Shaft. a very early

"perhaps other folk will have as much as they can carry soon," be

a terribly savage mood rush over Josiah Bell. see Frank strike

he be bent, and to see Rosaline. and he gnash his tooth in fury,

of the young man.



burst in at his own door when he at length reach it, he send his

"where's Rosaline?"



Mrs. Bell put down her knitting--a grey worst stocking for Bell

"rose go over after tea to sit a bit with Granny Sandon. Granny's

bad to-day, poor thing. I'm expect she back every minute."



Bell stagger to the fireplace and stand there grasp his stick.

know he do it.



"where's Raynor?" he break out.



"Raynor!" she echo, in surprise. "do you mean Mr. Frank Raynor? I

don't know where he be."



"he come in here a few minute ago."



"bless you, no, not he," return the wife, in an easy tone, think

it the good tone to assume just then.



"I tell ye I see he come here."



"the moonlight must have mislead you, Josiah. Mr. Raynor have not be

here to-day. put down your stick and take off your hat: and sit down

and be comfortable."



to this persuasive invitation, Bell make no reply. yet a minute or two

meanwhile Frank Raynor be with Mrs. Molly Janes. her damage be not

so bad as have be represent, and he proceed to treat they: which

take some little time. leave she a model of artistically-apply

just as Frank come opposite the Bottomless Shaft, he meet Rosaline, on

her way home from Granny Sandon's.



they stop to speak--as a matter of course. Frank tell she of the

affray that have take place, and the punishment of Molly Janes. while

come from, as though she be watch for some one: and her quick

"good-night--you pass on, Mr. Frank," she suddenly and hurriedly

exclaim. "I be go to hide here for a minute."



dart towards the Bottomless Shaft, she take refuge amongst the

surround mound: mound which look like great earth battery,

throw up in time of war. instead of pass on his way, Frank

follow she, in sheer astonishment: and find she behind the furth

mound at the back of the Shaft.



"be you hide from _I?_" he demand. "what be it, Rosaline? I

don't understand."



"not from you," she whisper. "why don't you go on? Hush! some one be

go to pass that I don't want to see.



"who be it? your father? I think he have go home."



"it be Blase Pellet," she answer. "I see he at the shop-door as I

come by, and I think he be follow I. he talk nonsense, and I

you think, sir? he must be go by now."



Frank humour she: he do not particularly like Blase Pellet himself,

on the contrary, he would have prefer to be go homeward, for he

have not much time to lose. whistle softly, lean against the

near mound, he watch the white cloud course in the sky.



"he must have pass now, Rosaline."



she steal cautiously away, to reconnoitre; and come back with a

beam face.



"yes," she say, "and he have go quickly, for he be out of sight. he

must have run, think to catch I up."



"I wonder you be not afraid to go through the mound alone and pass

close to the Bottomless Shaft!" cry Frank, in a tone of raillery, no

long deem it necessary to lower his voice. "Old Sandon's ghost

might have come up, you know, and carry you off.



"I be not afraid of old Sandon's ghost," say Rosaline.



"I dare say not!" laugh Frank.



in a spirit of bravado, or perhaps in very lightness of heart,

in truth he be impatient to be go.



"I be listen for the ghost," say she, her head bend over the

his arm round her waist to protect she.



another half-moment pass, when---- they hardly know what occur. a

howl of rage, a heavy stick brandish over they in the air, and

hide amongst the mound on his own score, look out for what might

be see.



Down come the stick heavily on Frank's shoulder. an instant's tussle

ensue: a shout from a despairing, fall man; a momentary glimpse of

an upturned face; a cry of horror from a woman's voice; an agonized

word from her companion; and all be over. Francis Raynor and the

unhappy Rosaline stand alone under the pitiless moonlight.









CHAPTER IV.

wait for BELL.





the fire throw its glow on Mrs. Bell's kitchen--kitchen and

sit-room combine--light up the strip of bright carpet before

the fender and the red-tile floor; play on the plate and dish

on the dresser, and on the blue hyacinth glass in the window, now

close in by the outer shutter. Stout Mrs. Bell sit by the round

table in her white apron and mob-cap, ply her knitting-needle. on

the other side the hearth sit a neighbour, one Nancy Tomson, a tall,

thin cornish woman in a check apron, with project tooth and a high

nose, who have come in for a chat. on the table wait the supper of

bread-and-cheese; and a candle stand ready for lighting.



the clock strike nine. Mrs. Bell look up as though the sound half

startle she.



"who'd heve think it!" cry the visitor, whose chatter have be

go incessantly for the last hour, cause the time to pass quickly.

"be they clock too fast, Dame Bell?"



"no," say the dame. "it's right by the church."



"well, I'd never heve say it be nine. your folk es late. I wonder

where they be that they don't come hoam."



"no need to wonder," return Mrs. Bell, in sharp tone, mean for the

absentee. "Rosaline's stay with poor Granny Sandon, who seem to

have nobody else to stay with she. as to Bell, he be off again to the

Golden Shaft."



"you say he have come in."



"he do come in: and I think he have come in for good. but he don't

stay a minute; he must need tramp out again. and he be far go,

Nancy Tomson, than I've see he these three year."



Dame Bell ply her needle vigorously, as if her temper have get down

into her finger. the visitor plunge into renew conversation,

chiefly turn upon that interesting episode, the encounter between

Janes and his wife. at half-past nine, Mrs. Bell put down her knitting

and rise from her seat. she be grow uneasy.



"what can keep Rosaline? she never stay out so late as this, let

Granny Sandon want she ever so. I'll take a look out and see if I can

see she."



unbolting and open the door she admit a flood of pale moonlight:

pale, compare with the ruddier glow of the interior. Mrs. Bell peer

out across the Bare Plain in the direction of Trennach; and Nancy

Tomson, who be always ready for any divertisement, advanced and

stretch her long neck over Dame Bell's shoulder.



"it's a rare light night," she say. "but I don't see nobody come,

Mrs. Bell. they keep to the Golden Shaaft."



feel the air cold after the hot fire, Nancy Tomson withdraw indoor

again. she be in no hurry to be go. her husband make one of the

company at the Golden Shaft to-night, and this warm domicile be

pleasanter than her own. Dame Bell be about to shut the door, when a

faint sound cause she to look quickly out again, and advance somewhat

far than she do before. lean against the wall on the other

side the window be a dark object: and, to Mrs. Bell's intense

surprise, she discover it to be Rosaline.



Rosaline, in what appear to be the very utmost abandonment of grief

or of terror. her hand be clasp, her face be bent down. every

labour breath she take seem to come forth with suppress anguish.



"why, child, what on earth's the matter?" ejaculate the mother. "what

be you stay there for?"



the word quickly bring out Nancy Tomson. her exclamation, when she

see Rosaline, might almost have be hear at Trennach.



Rosaline's moan subside into silence. she slowly move from the

wall, and they help she indoor. her face be white as that of the

dead, and appear to have a nameless horror in it. she sit down on

the first chair she come to, put her arm on the table, and her head

upon they, so that her countenance be hide. the two woman, close

the front-door, stand gaze at she with the most intense curiosity.



"she heve be fright," whisper Nancy Tomson. and it do indeed

look like it. Mrs. Bell, however, negative the suggestion.



"fright! what be there to frighten she? what's the matter,

Rosaline?" she continue, somewhat sharply. "be you strike mooney,

child?"



Nancy Tomson be one who like her own opinion, and hold to the

fright. she advance a step or two near Rosaline, drop her voice

to a low key.



"Heve you see anything o' Dan Sandon? maybe hes ghost shawe itself

to you as you come by the Bottomless Shaaft?"



the word seem to affect Rosaline so strongly that the table, not a

very substantial one, vibrate beneath her weight.



"then just you tell we whaat else it es," pursue Nancy Tomson, eager

for enlightenment--for Rosaline have make a movement in the negative as

to Dan Sandon's ghost. "sure," add the woman to Mrs. Bell, "sure

Janes and she be not a-fighting again! sure he heven't be and kill

she! be it _that_ whaat heve fright you, Rosaline?"



"no, no," murmur Rosaline.



"well, it must be something or t'other," urge the woman, beside

herself with curiosity. "one caan't be fright to death for nothing.

Heve ye faale down and hurt yerself?"



an idea, like an inspiration, seize upon Mrs. Bell. and it seem to

she so certain to be the true one that she only wonder she have not

think of it before. she lay her hand upon her daughter's shoulder.



"Rosaline! you have hear the Seven Whistlers!"



a slight pause. Rosaline neither stir nor speak. to Nancy Tomson

the suggestion clear up the mystery.



"_Thaat's it_," she cry emphatically. "where be aal my wit, I

wonder, thaat I never remember they? now doan't you go for to deny

it, Rosaline Bell: you have hear they Seven Whistlers, and gashly

thing they be."



another pause. a shiver. and then Rosaline slowly lift her white

face.



"yes," she answer. "the Seven Whistlers." and the avowal strike such

consternation on her hearer, although the suggestion have first come

from they, that they become dumb.



"Father hear they, you know," go on Rosaline, a look of terror in

her eye, and a dreamy, far-off sound in her voice. "Father hear

they. and they mean ill-luck."



"they bode death: as some say," speak Nancy Tomson, lower she

voice to an appropriate key.



"yes," repeat Rosaline, in a tone of sad wailing. "yes: they bode

death. oh, mother! mother!"



but now, Mrs. Bell, although give, like her neighbour, to put

some faith in the Seven Whistlers: for example be contagious: be by

no mean one to be overcome with the fear of they. rather be the

superstition regard by she as a prolific theme for gossip, and she

altogether disapprove of the man's make it an excuse for idleness.

have she hear the Whistlers with her own ear, it would not have move

her much. of course she do not particularly like the Whistlers; she

be willing to believe that they be in some mysterious way the

harbinger of ill-luck; and the discomfort evince by her husband on

Sunday night, when he return home after hear the sound, have in a

degree impart discomfort to herself. but, that any one should be put

into a state of terror by they, such as this now display by

Rosaline, she look upon as absurd and unreasonable.



"don't take on like that, child!" she rebuke. "you must be silly.

they don't bode _your_ death: never fear. I'll warm you a cup o'

pea-soup. there's some leave in the crock."



she bustle into the back-kitchen for the soup and a saucepan.

Rosaline keep her head down: deep, laboured breathing agitate she.

Nancy Tomson stand look on, her arm fold in her check apron.



"Whereabouts do ye hear they Whistlers, Rosaline?" she ask at

length.



but there be no answer.



"on the Bare Plain, I take it," resume the woman. "Were't a-nigh they

mound by the Shaaft? sound echo in they zigzag path rarely. I've

hear the wind a-whistle like anything there afore now. she be a

pewerly lonesome consarn, thaat Shaaft, for waun who have to paas she

at night alone."



a moan, tell of the sharp mental agony, break from Rosaline.

Dame Bell hear it as she be come in. in the midst of her sympathy,

it anger she.



"Rosaline, I willn't have this. there's reason in roasting of egg. we

shall have your father here directly, and what will he say? I can tell

you, he be bad enough when he go out. come! just rouse yourself."



"Father hear the Whistlers, and--they--bode--death!" shiver

Rosaline.



"they don't bode your, I say," repeat Dame Bell, lose

patience. "do you suppose death come to every person who hear the

Whistlers?--or ill-luck either?"



"no, no," assent Nancy Tomson, for Rosaline do not speak. "for waun

that faal into ill-luck after hear they Whistlers, ten escape.

I've know a whole crowd o' they man hear the sound, and nought heve

come on't to any waun on 'em."



"and that's quite true," say Mrs. Bell.



Rosaline could not be persuade to try the soup. it be impossible

that she could swallow it, she say. take a candle; she go up to

her room; to bed, as her mother suppose.



"and the good place for she," remark Dame Bell. "to think of she

get a fright like this!"



but poor Rosaline do not go to bed, and do not undress. take her

shoe off, that she might not be hear, she begin to pace the few

yard of her narrow chamber, to and fro, to and fro, from wall to

wall, in an anguish the like of which have rarely be feel on earth.

she be live over again the night's meeting at the Bottomless Shaft

and its frightful ending: she see the white, upturned, agonize face,

and hear the awful cry of despair of he who be fall into its

pitiless depth, and be now lie there, dead: and it seem to she

that she, herself, must die of it.



the clock strike ten, and Nancy Tomson tear herself away from the warm

and hospitable kitchen, after regale herself upon the soup reject

by Rosaline. and Dame Bell sit on, knitting, and wait for her

husband.





when Rosaline, her hand lift in distress, tear away that evening

from the Bottomless Shaft, and the tragedy that have be enact

there, and go fly over the Bare Plain towards home, Frank Raynor,

recover from the horror which have well-nigh stun his faculty,

go after she. two or three time he attempt to say a word to she,

but she take no notice of he; only speed the quick, if that be

possible. she never answer; it be as if she do not hear. when they

reach the narrow path that branch off to the cottage, there she

stop, and turn towards he.



"we part here. part for ever.



"be you go home?" he ask.



"where else should I go?" she rejoin, in anguish. "where else can I

go?"



"I will see you safe to the door.



"no. no! good-bye."



and, throw up her hand, as if to ward he off, she would have speed

onwards. but Frank Raynor could not part thus: he have something to

say, and detain she, hold her hand tightly. a few hasty word

pass between they, and then she be at liberty to go on. he stand

watch she until she draw near to her own door, and then turn back

on his way across the plain.



in his whole life Francis Raynor have never feel as he be feel now.

an awful weight have settle upon his soul. his friend have be willnot

to say that no calamity upon earth could bring down Frank's exuberant

spirit, or change the lightness of his way. but something have be

find to do it now. little less agitated be he than Rosaline; the

sense of horror upon he be the same as her.



he be now pass the fatal spot, the Bottomless Shaft; its

surround hillock shine out in the moonlight. Frank turn his eye

that way, and stand still to gaze. of their own accord, and as if some

fascination impel he against his will, his step move

thitherward.



with a livid face, and noiseless foot, and a heart that cease for the

moment to beat, he take the first narrow zigzag between two of the

mound. and--but what be it that meet his gaze? as he come in view of

the Shaft, he see the figure of a man stand on its brink. the sight

be so utterly unexpected, and so unlikely, that Frank stand still,

scarcely believe it to be reality. for one blissful moment he lose

sight of impossibility, and do indeed think it must be Josiah Bell.



only for an instant. the truth return to his mind in all its

wretchedness, together with the recognition of Mr. Blase Pellet. Mr.

Blase be gingerly bend forward, but with the utmost caution, and

look down into the pit. as if he be listen for what might be

to be hear there: just as the unhappy Rosaline have profess to

listen a few minute before.



Frank have not make any noise; and, even though he have, a strong gust

of wind, just then sweep the mound, deaden all sound but its

own. but, with that subtle instinct that warn we sometimes of a human

presence, Blase Pellet turn sharply round, and see he. not a word

pass. Frank draw silently back--though he know the man have

recognize he--and pursue his way over the Plain.



he guess how it be. when he and Rosaline have be wait amidst

the mound for Blase Pellet to pass, Blase have not pass. blase must

have see they cross over to the spot in the moonlight; and, instead

of continue his route, have stealthily cross after they and

conceal himself in one or other of the narrow zigzag. he must have

remain there until now. how much have he see? how much do he know?

if anything have be capable of add to the weight of perplexity and

trouble that have fall on Frank Raynor, it would be this. he groan

in spirit he pursue his way homeward.



"how late you be, Frank!"



the word, speak by Edina, meet he as he enter. hear he come

confusion of his mind, the perplexity as to the future, the sudden

shock of the one moment's calamity, which might change the whole
