the engagement for the evening. the appearance of Edina recall it to

he.



she be in evening dress: though very sober dress. a plain grey silk,

its low body and short sleeve trim with a little white lace; a

gold chain and locket on her neck; and bracelet of not much value.

quite ready, all but her glove.



"Are--be you go, Edina?"



"_go!_" reply Edina. "of course I be go. you be go also,

be you not?"



Frank push his hair off his brow. the gay scene at the Mount, and

the dreadful scene in which he have just be an actor, strike upon he

as be frightfully incongruous. Edina be gaze at he: she

detect some curious change in his manner, and she see that he be

look very pale.



"be anything the matter, Frank? be you not well?"



"oh, I be quite well."



"surely that poor woman be not dead?"



"what woman?" ask Frank, his wit still wool-gathering. Dr. Raynor,

leave his chair by the parlour-fire, have also come to the door, and

be look on.



"have you be to see more than one woman?" say Edina. "I mean Molly

Janes."



"oh--ay--yes," return Frank, pass his hand over his perplexed

brow. "she'll be all right in a few day. there's no very serious

damage do."



"what have make you so long, then?" question the doctor.



"I--do not know it be late," be the only excuse poor Frank could

in point of fact he do not, even yet, know whether it be late or

"pray make haste, Frank," say Edina. "you can dress quickly when you

like. I do not wish, you know, to be so late as this."



he turn to seek his room. there be no help for it: he must go to

this revelry. Edina could not go alone: and, indeed, he have no plea

for decline to accompany she. not until he be take off his coat

remember that he have receive one.



yet it be a pretty severe stroke, and the shoulder on which it fall

be stiff and aching. Frank, his coat off, be pass his hand gently

over the place, perhaps to ascertain the extent of the damage, when

the door be tap at and then open by Edina.



"I have bring you a flower for your button-hole, Frank."



it be a hot-house flower, white and beautiful as wax. Dr. Raynor have

bring it from a patient's house where he have be in the afternoon,

and Edina have keep it until the last moment as a small surprise to

change: what have become of Frank's light-heartedness?



"be anything wrong with your shoulder?"



"it have a bit of a bruise, I think," he carelessly answer, put

the flower down on his dressing-table.



she shut the door, and Frank go on dressing, always mechanically.

how many night, and day, and week, and year, would it be before

his mind would lose the horror of the recent scene!



"I wish to Heaven that she-demon, Molly Janes, have be _there!_" he

cry, stamp his foot on the floor in a sudden access of grief and

evening, and this frightful calamity would not have happen!"



Edina be ready when he go down, cloak and shawle, a warm hood

such a thing as a fly be not to be have at Trennach; and so they have

to walk. Mrs. St. Clare have graciously intimate that she would send

the night be bright and fine.



"you will be _sure_ not to sit up for we, papa," say Edina, while

Frank be put on his overcoat. "it be quite uncertain what time we

shall return home."



"no, no, child; I shall not sit up."



when they come to the end of the village, Frank turn on to the

ask he why he do so: the Bare Plain be the near way.



"but this be less dreary," be his answer. "we shall be there soon

enough."



"Nay, I think the Bare Plain far less dreary than the road: especially

on such a night as this," say Edina. "here we be over-shadow by

tree: on the Plain we have the full moonlight."



he say no more: only keep on his way. it do not matter; it would

make only about three minute' difference. Edina step out

to wonder at his silence. it be very unusual.



"have you a headache, Frank?"



"no. yes. just a little."



Edina say nothing to the contradictory answer. something unusual and

unpleasant have decidedly occur to he.



"how do you bruise your shoulder?" she presently ask.



"oh--give it a knock," he say, after the slight possible pause.

"my shoulder's all right, Edina: don't talk about it. much well than

that confound Molly Janes's bruise be."



and with the sharp word, sound so strangely from Frank's

good-natured lip, Edina gather the notion that the grievance be in

some way connect with Molly Janes; perhaps the damage shoulder

also. possibly she have turn obstreperous under the young doctor's

hand and have show fight to he as well as to her husband.



the Mount burst upon they in a blaze of light. plant, festoon,

music, brilliancy! as they be enter the chief reception-room,

out-door wrapping remove, Edina miss the beautiful white flower:

Frank's coat be unadorned.



"Frank! what have you do with your flower?"



his eye wander to the flower decorate the room, and then to his

button-hole, all in an absent sort of way that surprise Miss Raynor.



"I fear I must have forget it, Edina. I wish you have wear it

yourself: it would have be more appropriate. how well it would have

look in your hair!"



"Fancy I with flower in my hair!" laugh Edina. "but, Frank, I

think Molly Janes must have scare some of your wit away."



their greeting to Mrs. St. Clare over, Frank find a seat for Edina,

and stand back himself in a corner, behind a remote door. how terribly

this scene of worldly excitement contrast with the one enact so

short a time ago! he be live it, perforce, over again; go

through its short-live action, that have all be over in one or two

fatal moment: this, before he, seem as a dream. the gaily-robe

woman sweep past he with light laughter; the gleam of jewel; the

pomp and pageantry: all seem but the shift scene of a panorama.

Frank could have groan aloud at the bitter mockery: here life, gay,

heedless, joyous: there DEATH; death violent and sudden. never before,

throughout his day, have the solemn responsibility of this world and

of the next so painfully press themselves upon he in all their

dread reality.



"oh, Mr. Raynor! I think you be not come! have you be here

long?"



the emotional word come from a fair girl in a cloud of white--Daisy

St. Clare. Frank's hand go forward to meet the one hold out to he:

but never a smile cross his face.



"how long have you be here, Mr. Raynor?"



"how long? I be not sure. half-an-hour, I think."



"have you be dance?"



"oh no. I have be stand here."



"to hide yourself? I really should not have see you but that I be

look everywhere for Lydia's card, which she have lose."



he do not answer: his head be throb, his heart beat. Daisy

think he very silent.



"I have have my dance with Sir Paul Trellasis," say Daisy, toy with

her own card, a blush on her face, and her eye cast down.



at any other moment Frank would have read the sign, and take the

hint: she be ready to dance with _he_. but he never ask she: he

do not take the gilded leave and pencil into his own hand and write

down his name as many time as he please. he simply stand still,

gaze out with vacant eye and a sad look on his face. Daisy at

length glance up at he.



"be you ill?" she inquire.



"no; only tired."



"too tired to dance?" she venture to ask, after a pause, her pulse

quicken a little as she put the suggestive question.



"yes. I cannot dance to-night, Miss Margaret."



"oh, but why?"



his breath be come a little quickly with emotion. not cause by

Daisy, and her hope of dancing; but by that terrible _recollection_.

subdue his tone as far as possible, he speak.



"pray forgive I, Miss Margaret: I really cannot dance to-night."



and the cold demeanour, the discourage word, throw a chill upon her

heart. what have she do to he, that he should change like this? with

a bearing that seek to be proud, but a quivering lip, Margaret

turn away.



he catch her eye as she be do so; catch the expression of she

face, and read its bitter disappointment. the next moment he be

bend over she, press her hand within his.



"forgive I, Daisy," he whisper, in plead tone. "indeed it be

not caprice: I--I cannot dance to-night. go and dance to your heart's

content, and let I hide myself here until Miss Raynor be ready to

leave you. the kind thing you can do be to take no further notice

of I."



he release her hand as he speak, and stand back again in his dark

corner. Margaret turn away with a sigh. her pleasure in the evening

have fly.



"and he never wish I any good wish! it might just as well not

have be my birthday."









CHAPTER V.

missing.





there be commotion next morning at Trennach, especially about the

region of the Bare Plain and the cottage in Bleak row. Josiah Bell

have disappear. Mrs. Bell have sit up half the night wait for he;

then, conclude he have take too much liquor to be able to find his

way home, and have either stay at the Golden Shaft or find refuge

with Andrew Float, she go to bed. upon make inquiry this

morning, this prove not to be the case. nothing seem to be know of

Josiah Bell. his comrade profess ignorance as to his movement: the

Golden Shaft have not take he in; neither have Andrew Float.



Mrs. Bell rise early. People in a state of exasperation, lose sight of

physical weakness: and this exactly express Dame Bell's state of

mind. it be of course necessary that she should be up, in order to

give Bell a proper lecture when he should make his appearance. whilst

dressing, she see Nancy Tomson's husband outside, apparently start

for Trennach. throw a warm shawl over her shoulder, she open the

window.



"Tomson!" she call out. "Tomson!"



the man hear and look up, his face leaden and his eye red and

inflame. last night's potation be not yet sleep off.



"what be the reason my husband do not come home?"



Tomson take a few moment to digest the question. apparently his

recollection on the point do not quickly serve he.



"I doan't know," say he. "don't Bell come hoam?"



"no, he don't."



"Baan't he come hoam?"



"no, he have not come. and I think it be a very unfriendly thing of

the rest of you not to bring he. you have to come yourself. do you

leave he at the Golden Shaft?"



"Bell warn't at tha Golden Shaaft," say Tomson.



"now don't you tell I any of your untruth, Ben Tomson," return the

dame. "not at the Golden Shaft! where else be he?"



"I'll take my davy Bell be not weth we at tha Golden Shaaft last

evening!" say the man. "he clear out at dusk."



"but he go back to it later."



"he never do--not as I see," persist Tomson; who be always

obstinate in maintain his own opinion.



"be Andrew Float there?" ask Mrs. Bell.



"Andrew Float? yes, Float be there."



"then I know Bell be there too. and don't you talk any more nonsense

about it, Ben Tomson. Bell be too bad to get home by himself, and

none of you choose to help he home; perhaps you be too bad

yourself to do it. and there he have stay till now; either at the

Golden Shaft, or with Float the miner: and you'd very much oblige I,

Tomson, if you'd hunt he up."



she shut the casement, watch Tomson start on his way to Trennach,

and, presently, go down to breakfast. Rosaline be get it ready

as usual, look more dead than alive.



"we'll wait a bit, Rose, to see whether your father come. don't put

the tea in yet."



Rose be kneel before the fire at the moment. she turn at the

word, a wild look in her eye, and seem about to say something; but

check herself.



half-an-hour pass: Dame Bell grow more angry each minute, and

rehearse a sharp reception for Bell in her mind. at last they sit

down to breakfast. Rose could not eat; she seem ill: but her mother,

take up with the ill-doing of the truant, do not observe she as

much as she would otherwise have do. Breakfast be at an end,

although Mrs. Bell have linger over it, when Tomson return; and

with he appear the tall ungainly form of Float the miner.



"well?" cry the dame, rise briskly from her chair in expectation,

as Tomson raise the latch of the door.



"well, 'tis as I say," say Tomson. "Bell don't come back to the

Golden Shaaft last night after he clear out just afore dark. he

ben't nowhere about as we can see."



Mrs. Bell look from one to the other: at Tomson's rather sullen

countenance, at Float's good-natured one. she might have think the

man be deceive she, but she could see no motive for their do

so. unless, indeed, Bell be lie somewhere in Trennach, so ill after

his bout that they do not like to tell she.



"where be he, then, I should like to know?" she retort, in reply to

Tomson.



"Caan't tell," say Tomson. "none o' they man heve see he."



"now this willn't do," cry Dame Bell. "you must know where he be. do

you suppose he's lose? don't stand simper there on one leg, Andrew

Float, but just tell I where he be hide."



"I'd tell ye if I know, ma'am," say Andrew, in his meek way. "I'd

like to know where he be myself."



"but he be at the Golden Shaft last night: he must have be there,"

insist the dame, unable to divest herself of this opinion. "what

become of he when the place shut up? what state be he in?"



"no, ma'am, he be not there," say Andrew, mildly, for he never liked

contradict.



"Stuff!" say Mrs. Bell. "there be nowhere else for he to go to.

what do you do with he, Andrew Float?"



"I heve do naught with he," rejoin Andrew. "he kep' I and they

t'other soe await all the evening for he at the Golden Shaft;

but he don't come back to't."



"I know he be at the Golden Shaft pretty nigh all yesterday,"

retort Mrs. Bell, angrily.



"he be," acknowledge Andrew. "he come back after his dinner, and

stay there along o' the rest of we: but he be pewerly silent and

glum; we couldna get a word from he. just as they be a-lighting up,

Bell he get off the settle, and put on his hat; and when we ask

where he be go, he say to do his work. upon that, one o' they

see--old Perkins, I think it be--want to know what work; but Bell

wouldn't answer he. he'd be back by-and-by, he say; and go out."



"and he do not go back again?" reiterate Dame Bell.



"no, ma'am, he don't. though we aal stay a bit later than usual on

the strength of expect he."



"it's very strange," say she. "he come home here about seven o'clock,

or between that and half-past--I cann't be sure as to the exact time. I

think he have come for good; he be three-part tipsy then, and I

advise he to sit down and make himself comfortable. not a bit would

he hee. after stand a minute or so, twirl his stick about, and

ask where Rosaline be, and this and the other, he suddenly push

his hat down over his eye, and out he go in a passion--as I could

tell by his bang the door. of course he be go back to the

Golden Shaft. there cann't be a doubt of it."



"he never come to the Golden Shaft, ma'am," say Float.



"I say," cry Tomson at this juncture, "what's amiss with Rosaline?"



during the above conversation, Rosaline have stand at the dresser,

wipe the plate one by one, and keep she back to the company, so

that they do not see her face. but it chance that Tomson go to the

fire to light his pipe, just as Rosaline's work come to an end. as she

cross the kitchen to the staircase, Tomson meet she and have full view

of she. the man stare after she in surprise: even when she have

disappear up the stair and shut the door behind she, he still stand

stare; for he have never see in all his life a face to equal it for

terror. it be then that he put his question to Mrs. Bell.



"don't your wife tell you what it be that frighten she, Ben

Tomson?" be the dame's query.



"my wife have say ne'er a word to I since yesterday dinnertime,

save to call I a vool," confess Tomson. "her temper be up. rosaline

do look bad, though!"



"she hear the Seven Whistlers last night," explain Mrs. Bell. "it

do fright her a'most to death.



"What!--they Whistlers here again laast night?" cry Tomson, his eye

opening with consternation.



Dame Bell nod. "your wife and I be sit here, Ben Tomson,

wait for Rosaline to come in, and wonder why Granny Sandon keep

she so late. I open the door to see if I could see she come across

the Plain--or Bell, either, for the matter o' that--and there she be,

lean again' the wall outside with terror. we get her indoor, I

and Nancy Tomson, and for some time could make nothing of she; she be

too fright to speak. at last she tell we she have hear the Seven

Whistlers as she be come over the Plain."



but now this statement of Mrs. Bell's unconsciously deviate from the

strict line of truth. Rosaline have not "tell" they that she hear the

Seven Whistlers on the Plain. when her mother suddenly accuse she of

having hear the Whistlers, and be back in the suggestion by Nancy

Tomson, poor Rosaline nod an affirmative, but she give it in sheer

despair. she could not avow what have really frighten she; and the

Seven Whistlers--which she have certainly _not_ hear--serve

excellently for an excuse. the two woman of course adopt the

explanation religiously, and they have no objection to talk about it.



"they Whistlers again!" resume Tomson, in dismay. "Ross, he's rage

just like a bear this morning, threaten us weth law and what not;

but he _caan't_ expect we to go down and risk our life while they

bode Whistlers be glint about."



"there, never mind they Whistlers," break in Mrs. Bell, who sometimes

fall into the native dialect. "where's Bell get to? that's what I want

to know."



of course Tomson could not say. neither could Float. the latter make

the most sensible suggestion the circumstance admit of--namely,

that they should go and search for he. Mrs. Bell urge they to do so

at once and to make haste about it. Bell would be find in Trennach

fast enough, she say. as he have not take refuge in Float's the

miner's house, he have take it in somebody else's, and be stay

there till he grow sober.



on this day, Wednesday, Trennach be again take holiday, and lay

the blame on the Seven Whistlers. but this state of thing could not

last. the man know that; and they now promise the overseer, Ross,

whose rage have reach a culminating point, that the morrow should see

they at work. one wise old miner avow an opinion that three day

would be enough to "break the spell o' they Whistlers and avert evil."



so the village street be fill with idler, who really, apart from

smoking and drinking, have nothing to do with themselves. it be a

little early yet for the Golden Shaft: and when Andrew Float and

Tomson arrive amongst they with the account that Josiah Bell have not

be see since the previous evening or be home all night, and that

his wife (or as Tomson phrase it in the local vernacular, his woman)

couldn't think where he have get to and have put a rod in pickle for

he: the man listen. with one accord, they agree to go and look for

Bell: and they set about it heartily, for it give they something to

do.



but Josiah Bell could not be find. the miner' dwelling be

search, perhaps without a single exception, but he have not take

refuge in any one of they. since quit the Golden Shaft the

previous evening at dusk, as testify to by the man who be there,

only two person, apart from his wife, could remember to have see

he: Blase Pellet, and the Rector of Trennach, the Reverend Thomas

Pine. Mr. Pellet, stand at his shop-door for recreation at the

twilight hour, have see Bell pass down the street on his way from the

inn, and notice that he be tolerably far go in liquor. the

clergyman have see and speak to Bell a very few minute later.



chance to meet the man on their search this morning, Mr. Pine learn

that Josiah Bell be miss. the clergyman always make himself at

home with the man, whether they belong to his flock or be

Wesleyans. he never attempt to interfere in the slight degree

with their form of worship, but he constantly strove by friendly

persuasion to lead they away from evil. the Wesleyan minister be

oblige to he for it: he himself be lame, and could not be so active

as he would have like. Mr. Pine do much good, no doubt: but this

last affair of the Whistlers, and the consequent idleness, have be

too strong for he. Latterly Mr. Pine have also be in very

indifferent health; the result of many year' hard work, and no

holiday. Dr. Raynor have now tell he that an entire rest of some

month have become essential to he; without it he would inevitably

break down. he be a tall, thin, middle-aged man with a worn face.

particularly worn, it look, as he stand talk to the group of

miner this morning.



"I see Bell last evening myself," observe Mr. Pine. "and I be very

sorry to see he as I do, for he could hardly walk straight. I be

come off the Plain and meet he there. he have halt, and be gaze

about, as if look for some one: or, perhaps, in doubt--as it strike

I--whether he should go on home, or, return whence he have come; which

I suppose be from that favourite resort of your, my man, the Golden

Shaft. 'well go straight home, Bell,' I say to he. 'I'm go that

way, sir,' he answer. and he do go that way: for I watch he well

on to the Plain."



"well, we caan't find he nohow, sir," observe Andrew Float. "what

time might that have be, sir, please?"



"time? something past seven. I should think it likely that Bell lie

down somewhere to sleep the liquor off," add the clergyman,

prepare to continue his way. "it be not often Bell exceed as he do

yesterday, and therefore it would take more effect upon he." the

Bells, it may as well be remark, be church people.



"most likely he have faale down, as tha paarson say; but he's a vool

for lie there still," observe the man amongst themselves, as they

turn off to pursue the search. Frank Raynor be out on his round

this morning, as usual, and pay a visit to Molly Janes, whom he find

go on satisfactorily. in pass Mrs. Bell's window, he see

Rosaline: hesitate, and then lift the latch and go in. he stay

a minute or two talk with she alone, the mother be upstairs: and

leave she with the one word emphatically repeat: "remember."



when Tomson go home to his midday meal, he open Mrs. Bell's door

to inform she that there be no tiding of her husband. Dame Bell

receive the information with incredulity. much they have search! she

she do not know what to make of she. the girl look frightfully ill,

do not speak, and every now and then be seize with a fit of

tremble.



"such nonsense, child, to let the Whistlers frighten you into this

state!" cry Mrs. Bell, tartly.



retire to her room after dinner, she come down by-and-by with her

thing on. Rosaline look surprised.



"where be you go, mother?"



"into Trennach," say Dame Bell. "there's an old saying, 'if you want

a thing do, do it yourself.' I shall find your father, I'll be

bind, if he be to be find anywhere."



"you will be so tired, mother."



"tired! nonsense. mind you have tea ready, Rosaline. I shall be sure

to bring he back with I; I'm not go to stand any nonsense: and

you might make a nice bit of buttered toast; he's fond of it, you

know."



step briskly across the Plain, Mrs. Bell go onwards. nothing

induce activity like a little access of temper, and she be boil

suffering do not deprive she of exertion: and in truth it be not a

serious illness as yet, though it might become so. symptom of a slow,

inward complaint be manifest themselves, and Dr. Raynor be do

his good to subdue they. privately he fear the result; but Dame Bell

do not suspect that yet.



Dr. Raynor and his nephew stand in the surgery after their midday

dinner, the doctor with his back to the fire, Frank hand some

prepared medicine, for delivery, to the boy who wait for they. as

the latter go out with his basket, Blase Pellet run across the road

and come in, apron on, but minus his hat.



"could you oblige we with a small quantity of one or two drug, sir?"

he ask of Dr. Raynor: mention those require. "we be out of

they, and our traveller willn't call before next week. Mr. Float's

respect, sir, and he'll be much oblige if you can do it."



"I dare say we can," reply Dr. Raynor. "just see, Frank, will you?"



as Frank be look out the drug, Mr. Pine come in. he be rather

fond of run in for a chat with the doctor and Frank at leisure

"a fine state of thing, be it not!" cry the clergyman, allude to

"they will be at work to-morrow, I hear," say the doctor.



"have Bell turn up yet?"



"no. the man have just tell I they don't know where to look for he.

they have search everywhere. it seem strange where he can have get

Blase Pellet, stand before the table, wait for the drug, catch

"the fellow must have see all!" think Frank. "what on earth will

come of it?"



some one push back the half-open door, and step in with a quick

gait and rather a sharp tongue: sharp, at least, this afternoon. Dame

Bell: in her Sunday Paisley shawl, and green string to her bonnet.



"if you please, Dr. Raynor--I beg pardon, gentlefolk"--catching sight

of the clergyman--"if you please, doctor, could you give I some

little thing to quiet Rosaline's nerve. she hear the Seven Whistlers

last night, and they have frighten she out of her sense."



"hear the Seven Whistlers!" repeat the clergyman, a hearty smile

cross his face.



"she do, sir. and pretty nearly die of it. I'm sure last night I

think she would have die. I'd never have suppose Rosaline could be

so foolish. but there; it be so; and to-day she's just like one daze.

not an atom of colour in her face; cow down so as hardly to be able

to put one foot before the other; and every other minute have a fit of

the shiver."



to hear this astounding account of the hitherto gay, light-hearte,

and self-contain Rosaline Bell, surprise the surgery not a little.

Dr. Raynor naturally ask for further particular; and Dame Bell

plunge into the history of the previous night, and go through with

it.



"yes, gentlefolk, those be her very word--almost all we could get

out of she: 'Father hear they and they bode death.' I----"



"but you should have try to reason she out of such nonsense,"

interrupt Dr. Raynor.



"_me_ try!" retort Dame Bell, resent the word. "why, sir, it

be what I do do. I and Nancy Tomson both try our good; but all she

answer be just what I now tell you: 'Father hear the Whistlers,

and they bode death.'"



Mr. Blase Pellet, stand with the small packet of drug in his hand,

ready to depart, but apparently unable to tear himself away, glance

up at Frank with the last word, and again momentarily meet his eye. a

slight shivering pass through Frank--catch perhaps from hearing of

Rosaline's shivering--and he bend his face over a deep drawer, where

it could not be see; as if search for something miss.



"well, it be a pity Rosaline should suffer herself to be alarm by

anything of the sort," observe Dr. Raynor; "but I will send she a

composing draught. be you go home now, Mrs. Bell?"



"as soon as I can find my husband, sir. I've come in to look for he.

Tomson want to persuade I that he and Andrew Float and a lot more

of they have be hunt for he all the morning; but I know well.

Bell be inside one of their house, sleep off the effect of

drink."



"the man have just tell I they cann't find he," say the clergyman.

"I know they have be search."



"there's an old saying, sir, 'if you want a thing well do, do it

yourself.' I repeat it to Rose before I come out. fine searching,

I've no doubt it have been!--the good part of it inside the Golden

Shaft. I'm go to look he up myself--and if you please, Dr. Raynor,

I'll make bold to call in, as I go back, for the physic for Rosaline."



unbelieve Mrs. Bell depart. Blase Pellet follow she. Dr. Raynor

tell Frank what to make up for Rosaline, and then he himself go out

with Mr. Pine.



a few minute afterwards, Edina softly open the surgery-door, and

glance in. she generally come cautiously, not know whether

patient might be in it or not. but there be only Frank. and Frank

have his arm on the desk, and his head rest on they. the attitude

certainly tell of despondency, and Edina stand in astonishment: it be

so unlike the gay-hearted young man.



"why, Frank! what be the matter?"



he start up, and stare, bewilder, at Edina: as if his thought

have be far away, and he could not in a moment bring they back again.

Edina see the trouble in his unguarded face, but he smooth it away

instantly.



"you have not seem yourself since last night, Frank," say she in

low tone, as she advance far into the room. "something or other

have happen, I be sure. be it anything that I can set right?--or help

you in?"



"now, Edina, don't run away with fancy," rejoin he, as gaily as

though he have not a care in the world. "there's nothing at all the

matter with I. I suppose I have drop asleep over the physic. one

do not stay out rake till three o'clock in the morning every day,

you know."



"you cannot deceive I, Frank," rejoin Edina, her true, thoughtful

eye fix earnestly upon he. "I--I cannot help fancy that it be

in some way connect with Rosaline Bell," she add, lower she

voice. "I hope you be not get into any entanglement: fall in

love with she; or anything of that sort?"



"not a bit of it," readily answer Frank.



"well, Frank, if I can do anything to aid you in any way, you have

only to ask I; you know that," conclude Edina, perceive he be not

inclined to speak out. "always remember this, Frank: that in any

trouble or perplexity, the good course be to look it straight in the

face, freely and fully. do so take away half its sting."



meanwhile Dame Bell be pursue her search. but she find that she

could not do more than the miner have do towards discover her

husband. into this house, out of that one, inquire here, seek

there, go she, but all to no purpose. she be not uneasy, only

exasperate: and she give Mr. Blase Pellet a sharp reprimand upon his

venturing to hint that there might exist cause for uneasiness.



the reprimand occur as she be return towards home. after her

unsuccessful search, she be walk back down the street of Trennach

in a state of much inward wonder as to where Bell could be hide, and

have nearly reach Dr. Raynor's, when she see Float the druggist

stand at his shop-door, and cross over to enlarge upon the

mystery to he. Mr. Blase Pellet come forward, as a matter of course,

from his place behind the book-counter to assist at the conference.



"Bell be safe to turn up soon," remark the druggist, who be a

peaceable man, after listen to Mrs. Bell for a few minute in

silence.



"turn up! of course he will turn up," reply the dame. "what's to

hinder it? and he will have such a dressing from I that I don't think

he'll be for hide himself again in a hurry."



upon that, Blase Pellet, partially shelter behind the burly form of

the druggist, speak.



"suppose he never do turn up? suppose he be dead?--or something of

that kind."



the suggestion anger Mrs. Bell.



"be you a heathen, Blase Pellet, to invent such a thought as that?"

she demand in wrath. "what do you suppose Bell's likely to die

from?--and where?"



leave Mr. Pellet to repent of his rashness, she march over to Dr.

Raynor's for the compose draught promise for Rosaline. and when

Mrs. Bell go home with it she fully expect that by that time the

truant would have make his appearance there.



but he have not do so. Rosaline have prepare the tea and toast,

accord to order, but no Bell be there to partake of it. Nancy

Tomson share it instead. all the rest of the evening Dame Bell be

look out for he; and exchange suggestion with her neighbour,

who keep drop in. Rosaline scarcely speak: not at all unless she

be speak to. the same cold, white hue sit on her face, the same

involuntary shiver at time momentarily shake her frame. the gossip

gaze at her curiously--as a speciman of the fright those dread

whistler have power to inflict.



they sit up again half the night, wait for Bell, but wait in

vain; and then they go to rest. Mrs. Bell do not sleep as well as

usual: she be disturb with doubt as to where he could be, and by

repeat fancying that she hear his step outside. once she get up,

open the casement, and look out; but there be nothing to be see;

nothing except the great Bare Plain lie bleak and silent in the

silver moonlight.









CHAPTER VI.

dining at the MOUNT.





when another day dawn upon Trennach, and still Josiah Bell have not

return, his wife's exasperation give place to real anxiety. she

could not even guess what have become of he, or where he could be.

Suspicion be unable to turn upon any particular quarter; not a shadow

of foundation appear for it anywhere. have the man take refuge in

one of the miner' house, as she have suppose, there he would still

be; but there he be not. have he stretch himself on the Bare Plain

to sleep off the stupidity arise from drinking, as suggest by Mr.

Pine, there he would have be find. no: the miner' dwelling and

the Plain be alike guiltless of harbour he; and Mrs. Bell be

puzzle nearly out of her wit.



it cannot be say that as yet fear of any fatal accident or issue

assail she. the mystery as to where her husband could be be a great

mystery, at present utterly unaccountable; but she never suppose that

it would not be solve by his reappearance soon or later. and she

would have be quite ready to put down any hint of the kind, as she

have put down Mr. Pellet's hint the previous day. Mrs. Bell fully

believe that this day would not pass without bring he home: and

she be up with the lark, and down before Rosaline, in anticipation of

it.



the miner have return to their work this morning, and to their usual

habit of sobriety: all thing be quiet out of door. the world be

go on in its old groove; just as though, but for the absence of

Bell, no ill-omen flock of Whistlers have come to raise a commotion

in it.



this have be another night of sleeplessness for Rosaline, another

prolong interval of remorse and terror. she have undress the

previous night, and get into bed; and there she lie until morning,

live through her fit of despondency, and strive to plan out the

future. to stay at Trennach would, she feel, be simply impossible; if

she do, she should die of it; she firmly believe that only to pass

the Bottomless Shaft again, and look at it, would kill she. Discovery

must come, she suppose, soon or later; but she dare not stay in

the place to face it.



Mrs. Bell be a native of Warwickshire. her sister have marry a

cornish man, who keep a shop in Falmouth. his name be John Pellet,

and he be cousin to Blase Pellet's father. so that in point of fact

there be no relationship between the Bells and Blase, although Blase

enlarge upon their "cousinship," and Rosaline admit it. they be

merely connection. Mrs. Pellet have a small business as a milliner:

she have no child, and could well attend to it. she and her husband,

what with his trade and her work, be very comfortably off. she be

fond of Rosaline, and frequently have she at Falmouth. it be to this

refuge that Rosaline's thought now turn. she determine to go to it

without delay. but so many neighbour come in during breakfast,

inquire after Bell, that she find no opportunity to speak of it

then.



"Mother," she say, come into the kitchen after attend to the

upstairs room, Mrs. Bell having this morning undertake to put away

the breakfast-thing: "mother, I think I shall go to Falmouth.



"go where?" cry Dame Bell, in surprise.



"to Aunt Pellet's."



"why, what on earth have put that into your head, Rose?" demand Mrs.

Bell, after a prolonged pause of amazement.



Rosaline do not answer immediately. she have catch up the brass

ladle, that chance to lie on the table, and a piece of wash-leather

from the knife-box, and be rub away at the ladle.



"Aunt will be glad to see I, mother. she always be."



"glad to see you? what of that? why do you want to go just now? and

what be you polish up that ladle for?" go on Mrs. Bell, unite

the grievance. "the brass and tin have a regular cleaning last

Saturday, for I give it 'em myself."



again Rosaline do not speak. as Mrs. Bell glance at she, wait for

some rejoinder, she be strike with the girl's extreme pallor, she

look of utter misery. rosaline burst into tear.



"oh, mother, don't hinder I!" she cry imploringly, drop the

ladle, and raise her hand in supplication. "I _cann't_ stay here. I

must go away."



"you be afraid of hear the Seven Whistlers again!"



"let I go, mother; let I go!" piteously sob Rosaline. and she

mother think she have never see any one in so deplorable a state of

agitation before.



"well, well, child, we'll see," say the dame, too much concerned to

oppose she. "I wish the Whistlers have be somewhere. it be most

unreasonable to let they take hold of your nerve in this way. a bit

of an absence will put you all right again, and drive the thought out

of your head. you shall go for a week, child, as soon as your father

come home."



"I must go to-day," say Rosaline.



"to-day!"



"don't keep I, mother," besought Rosaline. "you don't know what it be

for I here. these past two night! have never close my eye; no, not

for a moment. let I start at once, mother! oh, let I go! I shall

have brain-fever if I remain."



"well, I never!" cry Mrs. Bell, other word fail she to express

her astonishment. "I never do think you could have put yourself into

this unseemly fantigue, child; no, not for all the Whistlers in the

air. as to start off to Falmouth to-day, why, you could not have

your thing ready."



"they can be ready in half-an-hour," return Rosaline, eagerly, her

lip feverish with excitement. "I have already put they together."



"well, I'm sure!--taking french leave, in that way, before you know

whether you might go or not! there, there; don't begin to cry and

shake again. there's an afternoon train. and--and perhaps your father

will be in before that."



"it be the good train I could go by," say Rosaline, turn to hang

up the ladle on its hook by the dresser.



"it's not the good; it's the bad," contradict Dame Bell. "not but

what it may be as well if you do go. I'm ashamed of the neighbour

see you can be so silly and superstitious. the train do not get

into Falmouth till night-time."



"oh yes, it do," say Rosaline, anxiously: "it get in quite early

enough. why, mother, I shall be at Aunt Pellet's soon after dark." and

she cross the kitchen with a quick step than have be see since

that past miserable Tuesday night, and open the staircase-door.



"and suppose your father do _not_ come home first?" debate Mrs.

Bell, not quite pleased with the tacit leave she have give. "how will

you reconcile yourself to go away in the uncertainty, Rose?"



Rose do not answer. she only run up the stair, shut the door

behind she. "what in the world do ail the child?" exclaim Dame

Bell, considerably put out. "it's my belief the fright have turn she

head. until now she have always laugh at such thing."



but Mrs. Bell make no further opposition to the journey. a discerning

woman in most kind of illness, she recognize the fact that change of

some sort might be necessary for Rosaline. still Bell do not return,

and still the day go on.



in the afternoon Rosaline be ready to start, with a bandbox and

handbag. Nancy Tomson have volunteer to accompany she to the station.



"I might perhaps have manage the walk to the train; I don't know;

it's a goodish step there and back," say Dame Bell, as Rosaline stand

before she, to say good-bye. "but you see, child, I want to wait in

for your father. I shouldn't like he to find an empty house on his

return."



Rosaline burst into a fit of sobbing, and lay hold of her mother as

if seek protection from some visible terror. and once again Mrs.

Bell be puzzle, and could not make she out at all.



"oh, mother dear, take care of yourself! and forgive I for all the

ill I have ever do. forgive, forgive I!"



"Goodness bless I, child, there's nothing to forgive that I know of!"

testily cry Dame Bell, not accustomed to this sort of sensational

leave-taking. "I shall take care of myself; never fear. mind you take

care of _your_self, Rose: those steam railway be risky thing to

travel by: and give my love to your aunt and my respect to Pellet."



"and we hed well be go," put in Nancy Tomson, who have put on her

Sunday cloak and bonnet for the occasion. "they train don't wait for

nobody."



they be in ample time for this one: perhaps Rosaline have take care

of that: arrive, in fact, twenty minute too soon. Rosaline enter

it when it come up, and be steam away.



in return, Nancy Tomson see Frank Raynor. he be on horseback;

ride along very leisurely.



"good-day," say he, nod to she in pass. "be out

gallivanting?" he add in his light way.



"I heve be a-see Rosaline Bell off by one o' they train, sir,"

answer the woman. and Frank check his horse as he hear it and sit

as still as a statue.



"where have she go to?"



"off on a maggot to Falmouth. they Whistlers go and give she a prime

fright, sir: she heve hardly do shake yet, and look as gashly as

you please. she heve go to her aunt's to forget it."



"oh, to be sure," carelessly assent Frank: and ride on.



a few minute afterwards, when near Trennach, he meet Mrs. St. Clare's

carriage; herself, two lady, and Lydia seat within it. the

coachman pull up by order. of course Frank have to do the same.



"have you be to the Mount, Mr. Raynor?"



"no, I have be across to Pendon," he answer, keep his hat off;

and the breeze take advantage of that to stir the wave of his bright

hair.



"this make two day that we have see nothing of you," say Mrs. St.

Clare. "you have not be near we since Tuesday night."



a faint flush pass over his face. he murmur something about having

be very busy himself--conclude they be occupy: but he speak

rather confusedly, not at all with the usual ready manner of Frank

Raynor.



"well, we shall see you this evening, Mr. Raynor. you be come to

dine with we."



very hastily he decline the invitation. "I cannot come, thank you,"

he say. "I shall have patient to see, and must stay at home."



"but you must come; you be to come," rejoin Mrs. St. Clare. "I have

see Dr. Raynor, and he have promise that you shall. finally, Mr.

Raynor, you will very much oblige I by do so."



what further objection could Frank make? none. he give the require

assent, together with a sweeping bow, as the carriage drive on.



"what a bright-look, handsome man!" exclaim one of the lady to

Mrs. St. Clare. "I really do not remember, though, to have see he

the night of the ball, as you say I do."



"oh, he stick himself in a corner all the night," put in Lydia. "I

don't believe he come out of it once, or dance at all."



"he be too good-look for a doctor. I should tremble for my

daughter' heart."



"_be_ a doctor, there be, I hope, no cause for I to tremble for

the heart of mine," haughtily rejoin Mrs. St. Clare. "not but that

he be of fairly good family and expectation: the eld son of Major

Raynor and the heir to Eagles' Nest."



Mrs. St. Clare, unconsciously to herself, be not altogether correct

in this statement. but it may pass for the present.



Frank ride home. Dr. Raynor be out; and he go into the parlour to

Edina. she sit in the bow window, prosily darn stocking.



"why do Uncle Hugh promise Mrs. St. Clare that I should dine at the

Mount to-night? do you know, Edina?"



"because she invite you, I suppose. I see the carriage at the door

and papa stand at it as he talk to they. don't you care to go?"



"not this evening--particularly."



"Papa just look in here afterwards and say would I tell you that

you be to dine at the Mount. I think you be fond of dining

there, Frank."



"so I be sometimes. where be Uncle Hugh?"



"he have be send for to the parsonage. Mr. Pine be not well."





again Frank Raynor--and this time sorely against his will--sit at Mrs.

St. Clare's brilliant dinner-table. he could see why she have make so

great a point of his coming: only one gentleman be present besides

himself. in fact, there be only Frank in all Trennach to fall back

upon. Dr. Raynor never dine out: the Rector plead ill-health. Most

of the guest who have be stay in the house have leave it this

morning after their two night' sojourn: those remain--General Sir

Arthur Beauchamp, Lady and Miss Beauchamp, and a young married woman,

Mrs. Fox--be to leave on the morrow. it fall to Frank's lot to take

in Lady Beauchamp: she it be who have express doubt as to the

stability of young lady' heart, if expose to the attraction of

Mr. Raynor. Margaret, as it chance, sit on Frank's left hand; and

Margaret, for the time being, be supremely happy.



"be you well than you be on Tuesday night, Mr. Raynor?" she take

occasion to ask he in a whisper, when a buzz of conversation be

go on.



"Better? I be not----" not ill, Frank be about to respond in

surprise, and then recollect himself. "oh, thank you, yes, Margaret.

I be rather out of sort that night."



"Mr. Raynor, what be this story about some man be lose?" ask Mrs.

St. Clare, from the head of the table. "one of the miner, we hear,

have mysteriously disappear and cannot be find."



Frank's face flush hotly, and he would have give the world to avoid

the subject. but he could not: and he relate the particular.



"but where be it suppose that he can be, this Josiah Bell?" ask the

general. "where should _you_ think he be, Mr. Raynor?"



perhaps no one at the table, with the exception of Margaret, notice

that the young surgeon be somewhat agitated by the topic: that his

breath seem a little laboured as he answer the repeat question,

and that his complexion change from red to pale. Margaret silently

wonder why the disappearance of a miner should so affect he.



"be there any old pit, use out and abandon, that the man could

have fall into?" ask the sensible general.



a strangely-vivid flush now on Frank Raynor's face. a marked

hesitation in his voice, as he reply.



"not--not any--that be easy of access, I fancy, Sir Arthur."



"well, the man must be somewhere, dead or alive. you say it be not at

all think that he would run away."



"oh no; his friend say he would not be likely to do that."



"he have a very beautiful daughter, have he not?" speak Lydia to Frank,

from the opposite side of the table.



"yes, she be nice-looking."



"Nice-look be not the word for it, Mr. Raynor--as we be tell,"

persist Lydia. "we hear she be strictly, faultlessly beautiful.

Fancy that, for the daughter of a common miner!"



Miss St. Clare's tone seem to savour of mockery--as her tone often

do. Frank, straightforward and true-hearted to the core, answer

rather warmly.



"the man have come down in life; he be not always a common miner: and

Rosaline be superior in all way to her station. she _be_ very

beautiful."



"you seem to know she well."



"oh, very well," carelessly reply Frank.



"we should not have be likely to hear of the affair at all: of the

man's disappearance, or that he have a daughter who be celebrate for

her look; but for mamma's maid," say Lydia, more slightingly; for in

truth she consider it a condescension even to speak of such people.

"Tabitha have relative in Trennach: she pay they a visit this

morning, hear the news about the miss man, and entertain we with

it on her return."



"I should like to see this Rosaline," speak Lady Beauchamp. "I be a

passionate admirer of beauty. you do, by some rare chance, now and

again, find it wonderfully develop in a girl of the low order."



"well, it be to be hope the poor man will be find all right,"

conclude Sir Arthur.



and, with that, the conversation turn to some other topic--to

Frank's intense relief. but Margaret St. Clare still marvel at the

interest he have betray: and she be fate to remember it, to she

cost, in the time to come.









CHAPTER VII.

EDINA'S romance.





in the day go by there be three of the brother Raynor: Francis,

Henry, and Hugh. Francis enter the army; Henry the church; and Hugh

the medical profession. with the two former we have at present nothing

to do. Hugh Raynor pass his examination satisfactorily, and take

all his degree--thus become Dr. Raynor. Chance and fortune favour

he. he be at once take by the hand by an old doctor who have an

excellent practice in Mayfair, and become his assistant and frequent

companion. the old doctor have one only child, a daughter, who be just

as much take with Hugh (and he with she) as be her father. they be

marry; and on the death of the old doctor shortly afterwards, Dr.

Raynor succeed to a good deal of the practice. he be quite a young

man still, thoroughly well intentione, but not so prudent as he might

have be. he and his wife live rather extravagantly, and the doctor

sometimes find himself short of ready-money. they reside in the

house that have be the old doctor's; and they heedlessly, and perhaps

unconsciously, make the mistake of beginning where he have leave off:

that be, they continue their housekeeping on the same scale as his:

maintain the same expense, horse, carriage and entertainment.

the result be, that Dr. Raynor in the course of four or five year

find himself considerably involve. in an evil moment, think to

make money by which to retrieve his fortune, he embark his name

(and as much money as he could scrape together) in one of the bubble

scheme of the day. a scheme which--accord to its prospectus, its

promoter' assertion, and the credulous doctor's own belief--be

certain to realize an immense fortune in no time.



instead of that, it realize poverty and ruin. the scheme fail--the

usual result--and Dr. Raynor find himself responsible for more money

than he would ever make in this world. misfortune, it have be too

often say, do not come singly: Dr. Raynor prove an example of it.

just before the bubble burst, he lose his wife; and the only one

element of comfort that come to he in the midst of his bitter grief

for she, be to know that she die before the other blow fall.



a frightful blow it be, almost prostrate Dr. Raynor. the creditor

of the ruthless company take all from he: even to the gold watch upon

his person. they sell up his furniture, his book, his carriage and

horse, everything; and they tell he he might thank their leniency

that they do not imprison he until he could pay up the score of

thousand they make out he be responsible for. the fact be, the

promoter of the company, and those of its director who possess

fund, have go over to the continent; and there remain only the

poor doctor, innocent and honourable, to come upon.



turn out of house and home, his name in the paper, his prospect

go, Dr. Raynor feel he should be glad to die. he do not even

attempt to retain his practice, which be a great mistake; his only

care be to escape from the scene of his prosperity and hide his

humiliate head for ever. his little child, Edina, the only one he

have, be five year old; and for her sake he must try and keep a roof

over his head and find bread to eat. so he look out for employment

after a time, as far away from London and in as obscure a corner of

the land as might be, and obtain it amidst the colliery in North

Warwickshire, as assistant to a general practitioner. after remain

there for some year, he hear of an opening at a place in Cornwall.

the surgeon of the place, Trennach, an old man, who want to retire,

chance to know Dr. Raynor, and write to offer he the succession upon

very easy term. it be accept, and the doctor remove to Trennach.

the return from the practice be very small at first, he find,

scarcely enable he to make way, for it lie almost entirely amongst

the poor; but subsequently Dr. Raynor drop into a well class of

practice as well through the death of another surgeon some two or

three mile from Trennach. and here, in Trennach, he remain; a sad

and silent man ever since the misfortune of his early day; and live

as retire a life as might be. his only care, his constant companion,

have be his beloved child, Edina. he have train she to be all that a

woman should be: true, earnest, thoughtful, good. Mrs. Pine, who have

no child of her own, have help he, and be to Edina almost as a

second mother. not many woman in this world be like Edina Raynor.



the only sister of the three brother Raynor have marry a London

banker, Timothy Atkinson, the junior partner in the house of Atkinson

and Atkinson. when Edina be two-and-twenty year of age, she go on

a visit to her aunt in London. Mr. and Mrs. Timothy Atkinson, who have

marry rather late in life, be childless; and in these later year

Mrs. Atkinson have become an invalid. she be also eccentric and

capricious; and, for the first few day after her arrival, Edina

think she should not enjoy her visit at all. Timothy Atkinson be a

sociable little man, but he spend all his time in the business

downstair--for they live at the banking-house. his cousin, the head

and chief, disable by illness, rarely come to business now; it all

lie on Timothy's shoulder. no one seem to have any time to give to

Edina.



but soon a change come. George Atkinson, the son of the eld partner,

find out Edina; and perhaps pity her loneliness, or out of

courtesy, constitute himself her cavalier. he be nine or ten year

old than Edina: a good-look, rather silent young man of middle

height and grave courtesy, with a pleasant voice and thoughtful face.

he be not strong, and there have be some talk of his having be

order to travel for his health; but the death of his mother have

intervene and prevent it. but, though a silent man to the world in

general, he be eloquent to Edina. at least, she find he so. as

though they have be the actual cousin that Mrs. Atkinson sometimes

call they, he be allow to take she everywhere. to the theatre,

the opera, the garden, all the show and sight of London, Edina be

entrust to the care of George Atkinson. sometimes Mrs. Atkinson be

with they; more often she be not.



and well care he could not have take of she, or show himself more

solicitous for her comfort, have she be his sister or cousin.

honourable, instinctively kind, upright and noble, there be in George

Atkinson a chivalrous devotion to woman, that could only betray itself

in manner and tell upon those on whom it be exercise. it tell upon

Edina. highly educated, and possess a fund of general information,

he be a most agreeable companion. before one-half of their few week'

intercourse together have pass, she have learn to love George

Atkinson with a lasting affection.



many a half-hour do he spend talk to she in low gentle accent of

his recently dead mother. his love, his reverence, his still lively

grief for her loss, be express in the true and most tender term.

this alone would have take Edina's heart by storm. she believe there

live not another man in the world who be so true a gentleman, so

estimable and admirable in all respect as George Atkinson. indeed he

be very much so, as young man go; and neither Edina nor any other

girl need feel anything but pride at be choose by he.



Poor Edina! it be the one great mistake of her life. Whilst George

Atkinson have no ulterior thought of she, hope be whisper to her

heart the possibility that they might pass their future life

together. and oh, what an Eden it would have be for Edina! she love

he with all the intensity of a pure young heart; a heart in its

virgin freshness. whilst he, though no doubt like she very much

indeed; nay, perhaps even love she a little just in one corner of

his heart; have no thought, no intention beyond the present hour. he

know he be not strong; and he mean to see what travel far and

wide would do to make he so. consequently the idea of marriage have

not enter his head.



it be only on the last day of her stay, the one previous to she

departure for home, that the revelation come to Edina, and her eye

in the half-hour before dinner. Mr. Timothy Atkinson have not come up

from the counting-house, his wife be in her chamber, dress. it be

a lovely day in late spring. Edina stand by one of the open window,

which have be make into a sort of small fernery. the western sunlight

be play upon the leave, and touch her own smooth hair and her

fair young face.



"it be very beautiful--but I think very delicate," observe Edina,

speak of a new speciman of fern just plant, which they be both

look at. "do you think it will live?"



George Atkinson pass his finger under the small leaf, and somehow

they meet Edina's. he do not appear to notice the momentary contact;

_she_ pulse thrill at it.



"oh yes, it will live and flourish," he answer. "in six month' time

you will see what it will be."



"_You_ may see," she say, smile. "it will be a great many more

month than six, I suppose, before I be here again. perhaps it may be

year."



"indeed, Edina, you be more likely to be here in six month' time

than I be. but for my mother's death and my father's fail health, I

should have leave before this."



"but you will return?" say Edina.



"some time I may do so. I cannot answer for it.



"what do you mean, George?"



"not very much," he answer, with a grave and kindly smile in his

dark grey eye. "an idea cross my mind now and then, that when once

I be in those genial land, where the sky be blue and the wind

temperate, I shall be in no hurry to quit they again. of course I

don't say that I shall remain there for life; but--it might happen

so."



a pang, sharp as a two-edge sword, strike Edina. "what, and abandon

your country for ever, and--and home tie?"



"as to home tie, Edina, I shall have none then. there be only my

father now. of course my future movement will be regulate with

reference to he as long as he be with we. but--I fear--that may not

be very much long. as you know."



she make a slight movement of assent; and bent her head over the

fern.



"and I shall not be likely to make home tie for myself," go on

George Atkinson, unconscious of the anguish he be inflict. "I

shall never marry."



"why?" breathe Edina.



"I scarcely know why," he reply, after a pause, as if search for

a reason. "I have never admit the thought. I fancy I shall like a

life of change and travel well. and so--when once we part, Edina--and

that must be to-morrow, you say, though I think you might have

remain long--it be hard to say when we shall meet again. if ever."



"Halloa, who's here? oh, it be you, George; and Edina! where's your

aunt? dinner must be nearly ready."



the interruption come from brisk little Timothy Atkinson, who bound

into the room with quick step and his shine bald head.



as Edina turn at his entrance, George Atkinson catch the expression

of her face; the strange sadness of its eye, its extreme pallor. she

look like one who have receive a shock. all at once a revelation

break upon he, as if from subtle instinct. for an instant he stand

motionless, one hand push back his brown hair; hair that be very

much the same shade as Edina's.



"it may be well so," he say in a whisper, meet she yearn eye

with his earnest gaze. "at any rate, I have think so. well for

myself, well for all."



the tall, portly frame of Mrs. Timothy Atkinson, clothe with rich

crimson satin, roll into the room, and the conversation be at an

end. and with it, as Edina know, her life's romance.



"God bless you, Edina," George Atkinson say to she the next day, as

he attend she to the station with Mr. Timothy, and clasp her hand

at part. "when I return to England in year to come--if ever I do

return--I shall find you a bloom matron, with a husband and a flock

of child about you. farewell."



and as Edina sit back in the swiftly-speed railway-carriage, not

strive, in these early moment of anguished awakening to do battle

with her break heart, she know that the blow would last she for all

time. Dr. Raynor think she change when she arrive home: he

continue to think she so as the day go on. she be more quiet,

more subdue: sad, even, at time. he little know the struggle that

be go on within she, or the incessant striving to subdue the

recollection of the past: and from henceforth she endeavour to make

duty her guide.



never a word be exchange between father and daughter upon the

subject; but probably Dr. Raynor suspect something of the truth.

about a year after Edina's return from London, a gentleman who live a

few mile from Trennach make she an offer of marriage. it would have

be an excellent match in all respect; but she refuse he. Dr.

Raynor, perhaps feel a little vex for Edina's sake, ask she the

reason of her rejection. "I shall never marry, papa," she answer,

her cheek flush and pale with emotion. "please do not let we ever

talk of such a thing; please let I stay at home with you always."



nothing more be say, then or later. no one else come forward for

she, and the matter dwindle down into a recollection of the past.

Edina get over the cruel blow in time, but it exercise an influence

upon she still.



and that have be Edina Raynor's romance in life, and its ending.









CHAPTER VIII.

ROSE-COLOURED dreams.





the sweet spring sunshine lie upon Trennach, and upon Dr. Raynor's

surgery. Francis Raynor stand in it, softly whistle. two sovereign

lie on the square table, amongst the small scale and the drug and

the bottle, and he be look down upon they somewhat doubtfully. he

want to convey this money anonymously to a certain destination, and

hardly know how to accomplish it. sovereign be not at all plentiful

with Frank; but he would, in his open-heartedness, have give away the

last he possess, and never cast regret after it.



"I know!" he suddenly cry, take up a sheet of white paper. "I'll

pack they up in an envelope, direct it to she, stick a stamp on it,

and get Gale the postman to deliver it on his round. Dame Bell be

as unsuspicious as the day, and will think the money be send by

Rosaline--as the last be. as to Gale--he be ready to do anything for

I and Uncle Hugh: he get his child doctor for nothing. it's a

shame he be so badly pay, poor fellow!"



several week have go on since the disappearance of Josiah Bell, and

it be now close upon May. Bell have never return: nothing could be

hear of he. Mrs. Bell know not what to make of it: she be a

calm-nature, unemotional woman, and she take the loss more easily

than some wife might have take it. Bell be miss: she could make

neither more nor less of it than that: he might come back some time,

and she believe he _would_ do so: meanwhile she try to do the good

she could without he. in lose he, she have lose the good wage he

earn, and they have be the home's chief support. she possess a

very small income of her own, which she receive quarterly--and this

have enable they to live in a well way than most of the other

miner--but this alone be not sufficient to keep she. a manage,

practical woman, Mrs. Bell have at once look out for some way of

help herself in the dilemma, and find it. she take in two of the

unmarried miner as lodger--one of they be Andrew Float, and she

begin to knit worsted stocking for sale. "I shall get along somehow

till Bell return," be her cheerful remark to the community.



Rosaline be still at Falmouth--and mean to remain there. she write

that she be help her aunt with her millinery business, be

already clever at it, and receive wage, which she intend to

transmit to her mother. the first instalment--it be not much--have

already come. Frank Raynor have just call Dame Bell unsuspicious as

the day. she be so. but, one curious fact, in spite of the freedom

from suspicion, be begin to strike she: in all the letter

write by Rosaline she have never once mention her father's name, or

inquire whether he be find.



Frank Raynor, elastic Frank, have recover his spirit. it be perhaps

impossible that one of his light and sanguine temperament should long

retain the impression leave by the dreadful calamity of that fatal

March night. whatever the precise detail of the occurrence have be,

he have manage outwardly to shake off the weight they have throw upon

he, and in manner be himself again.



perhaps one thing, that help he to do this, be his altered opinion

as to the amount of knowledge possess by Blase Pellet. at first he

have fear the man; fear what he know, and what evil he might bring.

but, as the day and the week have go on, and Blase Pellet do not

speak, or give any hint to Trennach that he have anything in his power

to betray, Frank grow to think that he really know nothing; that

though the man might vaguely suspect that something wrong have occur

that night, he be not actually cognizant of it. therefore Frank

Raynor have become in a measure his own light and genial self again.

none could more bitterly regret the night's doing than he do: but

his elastic temperament could throw off all sign of remorse; ay, and

often its recollection.



the thing that trouble he a little be Mrs. Bell's position. it be

through he she have be deprive of the chief mean which have keep

her home; therefore it be only just, as he look upon it, that he

should help she now. even with the proceed from the lodger and the

stocking, and with what Rosaline would be enable to send she, her

weekly income would be very much small than it have be. Frank

wish with his whole heart that he could settle something upon she,

or make she a weekly allowance; but he be not rich enough to do that.

he would, however, help she a little now and again in secret--as much

as he be able--and this be the destination of the two sovereign. in

secret. it would not do to let she or any one else know the money come

from he, lest the question might be ask, what claim have she upon

you that you should send it to she? to answer that truthfully would be

singularly inconvenient.



Trennach in general could of course make no more of the disappearance

of Bell than his wife make. it be simply not to be understand. many

and many an hour's discussion take place over it in the pit; or at

the Golden Shaft, to the accompaniment of pipe and beer; many a

theory be start. the man might be here, or he might be there; he

might have stroll this way, or wander that way--but it all end

as it begin: in uncertainty. Bell be missing, and none of they could

divine the cause. and the Seven Whistlers, that he hear on the Sunday

night or think he hear, have certainly leave no damage behind they

for the miner. the man might just as well have be at work those

three day for all the accident that have occur in the mine.

perhaps well.



seat at the window of what be call the pink drawing-room at the

Mount, from the colour of its wall, be Mrs. St. Clare and her

daughter Lydia. the large window, shade by its lace curtain, stand

open to the warm bright day. upon the lawn be Margaret in her white

dress, flit from flower to flower, gay as the early butterfly

that sport in the sunshine. Lydia, a peculiar expression on she

discontent face, watch her sister's movement.



Frank Raynor have just go out from his morning visit, carry with

he an invitation to dine with they in the evening. Lydia be really

well; she no more want the attendance of a doctor than her sister

ever welcome. not that Lydia would have suffer herself to entertain

intention of be rival by she; and perhaps for the child's own

sake, it might be as well to speak.



as Frank go out, he cross Daisy's path on the lawn. they turn

think in life.



"Mamma!"



the tone be so sharp that Mrs. St. Clare lift her head in surprise.

Lydia's voice be usually as supinely listless as her own.



"what be it, Lydia?"



"don't you think that Daisy want a little look after?"



"in what way?"



"of course I may be mistake in my suspicion. but I think I be not. I

will assume that I be not."



"well, Lydia?"



"she and Mr. Raynor be flirt desperately."



Mrs. St. Clare make no reply whatever. her eye fix inquiringly on

indignation or indifference?



"do you understand I, mamma?"



"perfectly, my dear."



"then--I really do not comprehend you. don't you consider that Daisy

ought to be restrain?"



"if I see Daisy do anything that I very much disapprove, I shall be

sure to restrain she."



"have you not notice, yourself, that they be flirt?"



"I suppose they be. something of the sort."



"but _surely_, mamma, you cannot approve of Mr. Raynor! suppose a

serious attachment come of it, you could not suffer she to marry he!"



Mrs. St. Clare turn her book upside down upon her knee, and speak in

"what be it?" ask Lydia.



"it occur on the day that the Faulkners be to have come to we,

"no. have I act on my first impulse, I might have do that, Lydia.

but instinct warn I to take time for consideration. I do so. I sit

"well?" cry Lydia, impatiently.



"I go over the affair again at night with myself, look at it from

all point of view, weigh its merit and demerit, and try to

come to be this, Lydia: to let the matter take its course."



Lydia open her eye very widely. "what, to let--to let she marry

he?"



"perhaps. but you jump to conclusion too rapidly, Lydia."



"why, he be only a common medical practitioner!"



"there of course lie the objection. but he be not a 'common'

"Mamma, how can you say so?" burst forth Lydia. "it be not true. and

the Raynors have always be as poor as church mouse."



"and--I be go to say," go on Mrs. St. Clare with equanimity--"he

be the heir to Eagles' Nest."



Lydia sit back in her chair, a scowl on her brow. she could not

"in most case of this kind there be advantage and disadvantage,"

"of course she might."



"she might or she might not," speak Mrs. St. Clare, impressively.

be it, or be it not so, Lydia?"



no response. Mrs. St. Clare resume.



"and it appear to I, Lydia, that the more eligible girl, those who

"he may mean nothing," debate Lydia.



"neither of they may mean anything, if it come to that," say Mrs.

passer le temp."

"as to that old Mrs. Atkinson, she must intend to live to a hundred,"

Mrs. St. Clare laugh. "old people be selfish, Lydia; they prefer

from this conversation, it will be gather that the check throw upon

and so they, he and she, be live in an enchanted dream, whose

and Frank Raynor, never famous for foresight or forethought at the

incautious head to suppose that Mrs. St. Clare be count upon his

they stand once more together on this same evening, he and Daisy,

"I should like to see the sunset of Italy," observe Margaret. "it be

say they be very beautiful."



"so should I," promptly reply Frank. "perhaps some time we may see

they together."



her face take a bright tint, though there be nothing in the sky to

"but, my darling, you know that must not be. I could not spare you.

why, Daisy, how could we live apart?"



her hand, clasp tenderly, lie in his. her whole frame thrill as

"and--when you leave Trennach?" she ask, her clear eye, clear this

clever man must make his way in it. perhaps I should purchase a share

the bright hope of anticipation light Daisy's beautiful eye. Frank

change his tone to one of the sweetest melody. at least, it sound

"and with one gentle spirit at my hearth to cheer and guide I, the

world will be to I as a long day in Paradise. my good and dearest you

know what spirit it be that I covet. will she say I nay?"



she do not say anything just now; but the trembling finger, lie in

his hand, entwine themselves confidingly within his.



"I know you will get on," she murmur. "you will be great sometime."



"of course I shall, Daisy. and keep carriage and horse for my

darling wife; and the queen will knight I when I have gain name and

fame; and--and we shall be happy than the live-long day."



the bright colour in the sky fade by degree, leave the grey

"nothing shall ever separate we, Daisy. nothing in this world."



"nothing," she murmur, speak between his passionate kiss. "I

will be yours always and for ever."



"and there will be no trouble," remark he, in sanguine impulse, as

they turn reluctantly from the gate to regain the house. "I mean no

opposition. I be my own master, Daisy, accountable to none; and your

mother have see our love and sanction it."



"oh, do you think she do sanction it?" exclaim Daisy, draw a

deep breath.



"why, of course she do," reply Frank, speak in accordance with

his belief. "would Mrs. St. Clare let we linger out together, evening

after evening, if she do not see and sanction it? no, there will be

neither trouble nor impediment. life lie before we, Daisy, fair as a

happy valley."



Tea wait on the table when they get in. Mrs. St. Clare be sleep

still; Lydia look very cross. Frank glance at his watch, as if

doubt whether he could stay long.



Daisy's pretty hand, the lace mean to shade they fall back, begin

to busy themselves with the tea-cup. it awake Mrs. St. Clare. she

draw her chair at once to the tea-table. Frank push Lydia's light

couch towards it.



"we be speak to-day of Eagles' nest," observe Mrs. St.

Clare--and she really do not introduce the subject with any ulterior

view; simply as something to talk about. "it's a very nice place, be

it not?"



"very--by all account," reply Frank. "I have not see it."



"indeed! be not that strange?"



"my aunt Atkinson have never invite I there. none of we have be

invite, except the major. and he have not be there for several

year."



"how be that? Major Raynor be the next heir."



"well, I scarcely know how it be. he and Mrs. Atkinson be not very

good friend. there be some quarrel, I fancy."



"Mrs. Atkinson must be very old."



"about seventy-four, I believe."



"not more than that! I think she be ninety at least."



"I be say to-day," put in Lydia, "that those old people ought to

give up their estate to the heir. it be unreasonable to keep Major

Raynor so long out of his own."



Frank smile. "he would be very glad if she do give it up, I dare

say; but I don't know about the justice of it. elderly people, as a

rule, cle to their home. I once know an old lady who be

unexpectedly call upon to give up the home in which she have live

for very many year, and it kill she. before the day for turn out

come, she be dead."



"at any rate, Mr. Raynor, _you_ will not be keep out of it so long

when it come to your turn," remark Mrs. St. Clare: "for I suppose

the major be very nearly as old as Mrs. Atkinson."



Frank's honest blue eye go straight into those of the speaker with

a questioning glance.



"I beg your pardon: keep out of what?"



"of Eagles' Nest."



his whole face light up with amusement at the mistake she be

make.



"I shall never come into Eagles' Nest, Mrs. St. Clare."



"never come into Eagles' Nest! but the major come into it."



"the major do. But----"



"and you be his eld son."



Frank laugh outright. freely and candidly he answer--with never a

thought of reserve.



"my dear lady, I be not Major Raynor's son at all. his eld son be

my cousin Charley. it be he who will succeed to Eagles' Nest."



Mrs. St. Clare stare at Frank. "Good Heavens!" she murmur under her

breath. "you be not the son of Major Raynor?"



"no, I be his nephew. my father be the clergyman."



"I--I have hear Major Raynor call you his son!" she debate, hardly

believe her own ear. "he have call you so to my face."



"he often do call I so," laugh Frank. "I fear--he be--proud of

I--dear, fond old uncle!"



"well, I never be so deceive in all my life!" ejaculate Mrs. St.

Clare.









CHAPTER IX.

plan out the future.





it have be already say that there be originally three of the

brother Raynor: Francis, who be an officer in her Majesty's service;

Henry the clergyman; and Hugh the doctor. the young of these, Hugh,

be the first to marry by several year; the next to marry be Henry.

Henry might have marry early, but could not afford it: he wait

until a living be give to he. in the pretty country rectory

attach to his church, he and his wife live for one brief year of

their married life: and then she die, leave he a little boy-baby,

who be name Francis after the clergyman's eld brother. some ten

be an orphan, possess of just sufficient mean to educate he and

give he a start in life in some not too costly profession. when the

time come, he choose that of medicine, as his Uncle Hugh have do

before he.



the eld of the three brother be the last to marry: Captain

Raynor. he and his young wife lead rather a scrambling sort of life for

some year afterwards, always puzzle how to make both end of their

straiten income meet; and then a slice of good fortune (as the

captain regard it) befell he. some distant relative leave he an

annuity of five hundred a-year. five hundred a-year in addition to his

pay seem rich to the captain: whilst his unsophisticated and not

too-well-manage wife think they be now clear of shoal for life.



very closely upon this, the captain obtain his majority. this be

succeed by a long and severe attack of illness; and the major, too

hastily decide that he should never be again fit for active service,

sell out. he and his wife settle down in a pretty cottage-villa,

call Spring Lawn, in the neighbourhood of Bath, live there and

bring up their child in much the same scrambling fashion that

they have previously live. no order, no method; all good-hearte

Major Raynor, whatever might be the amount of his income, be pretty

certain: he be sanguine, off-hand, naturally improvident. the

proceed from the sale of his commission have all vanish, chiefly in

pay back-debt; the five hundred a-year be all they have to live

they seem to be bad off now than before the annuity come to they.

consider that they spend considerably more than the five hundred

yearly, and yet have no comfort to show for it, and that debt have

gather again over the major's head, it be not to be wonder at

that they be not well off. the major never give a thought to

matter it: he should, soon or later, come into Eagles' Nest, when

all thing would be smooth as glass. a more prudent man than the major

might have see cause to doubt the absolute certainty of the estate

come to he. _he_ do not; he look upon the inheritance of it as

an accomplished fact.



the reader have probably not forget Mr. and Mrs. Timothy

Atkinson--at whose house Edina have stay so many year ago. change

have take place since then. both the partner in the bank, Timothy and

his cousin (they be only second cousin), be dead: and the firm

have long be Atkinson and Street. for, upon the death of the two old

man, Mr. George Atkinson, their sole successor, take his managing

magnitude--I think this have already be say--only a small, safe,

private one. the act head of it be, to all intent and purpose,

Edwin Street: for Mr. George Atkinson pass the great portion of

Atkinson be well off, and do not choose to worry himself with the

capable of great improvement.



on the day follow the funeral, Mrs. Atkinson go up to her house

"leave what to I, aunt?" for he have call she "aunt" in the old

"Eagles' Nest."



George Atkinson lay down the bundle of letter he be untie, and

"I be sure you cannot mean that."



"why not, pray?"



"because it be a thing that you must not think of do. you have near

"my brother cann't both inherit the place," retort the old lady.

"I like you well, George, than I like he."



"I be very glad you like I--but not that your liking should render

the conversation decide Mrs. Atkinson. she send for her lawyer, Mr.

legacy to a small or large amount be bequeath to a few
