the will be make in duplicate; Mrs. Atkinson desire her solicitor to

she affix her own seal to the envelope in which the will be

"I don't know how to thank you, Ann, for this," say the major, tear

but now, as ill-fortune have it, Major Raynor have chance, since that

the one exception be this. when his eld son, Charles, have attain

the major and his wife be both incredibly improvident; he in

he take the road home over the Bare Plain. never, since the night of

a smile cross the more experienced doctor's lip. young man be

"I know that, sir: but there be two way in which it may be do. one

way be, to purchase a share in an establish practice; another, to

"oh, of course," acquiesce Frank, lightly, as though money be the

"I have not save anything: I be never a pound to the good," answer

"just so, Frank. as it be, your plan must be somewhat like castle in

"ah, Frank," smile the doctor, "people be not so chivalrously

"suppose I go to some old-establish man in London who, from some

"but then, you see, to do this, I ought to know the exact sum that be

"and so I would tell you if I know it, Frank. I do not think Francis

"no, he do not. and I do not like to ask he."



there be a pause. Dr. Raynor begin put the paper straight on the

"Uncle Hugh," he cry, briskly, lift his head, his face glow

Frank's eager eye, raise to the doctor's face, fall at the question.

"there's no harm, sir, in try to plan out one's future."



"none in the world, Frank. I only ask the reason for your setting

"well--you know, Uncle Hugh--i--I may be marry some time."



"and you have be fix on the lady, I see, Frank!"



a broad smile now shine upon Frank's face. he be send the

"and if I be, sir?"



"then--I fear--that there may be trouble in store for you," say the

"not in word. but she see how much I and Daisy be together, and she

"Daisy? let I see? oh, that be the young one: she be a nice little

"well, well; if so, I be glad to hear it. nevertheless it surprise

"I shall not be likely to talk of it," reply Dr. Raynor.









CHAPTER X.

MAJOR and MRS. RAYNOR.





the window of Spring Lawn stand open to the afternoon sun. it be a

small, pretty white house, half cottage, half villa, situate about

three mile from Bath. a latticed portico, over which creep the white

clematis, lead into a miniature hall: Major Raynor could just turn

round in it. on either side be a small sitting-room, the dining-room

on the left, the drawing-room on the right.



the scramble midday dinner be over. somehow all the meal seem to

be scramble at the major's, from the utter want of order, and of

proper attendance. only two servant be keep, a cook and a nurse:

and _they_ could not always get their wage pay. when Edina be

there, she strove to bring a little comfort out of the chaos: but that

be only a chance event; a brief and rare occasion, occur at long

interval in life. some wine stand on the old table-cover, with a

plate of biscuit. on one side of the table sit the major; a tall and

very portly man, with a bald head and a white moustache, look every

day of his nine and-sixty year. he have be get on for fifty when

he marry his young wife; who be not quite eight-and-thirty yet: a

delicate, fragile-look woman, with a small fair face and gentle

voice, mild blue eye, a pink colour, and thin light brown hair

quietly braid back from it. Mrs. Raynor look what she be: a

gentle, yielding, amiable, helpless woman; one who could never be

strong-minded in any emergency whatever, but somehow one to be love

at first sight.



she sit half turn from the table--as indeed do the major opposite,

their face towards the window--her foot on a footstool, and her hand

busy with work, apparently a new frock she be make for one of her

young child. she wear a fade muslin gown, green its predominant

colour; a score of pin, belong to the work in process, in she

waistband.



they be talk of the weather. the major be generally in a state

of heat. that morning he have walk into Bath and back again, and get

in late for dinner, puffing and steaming, for it be an up-hill walk.

he like to have a fly one way at least; but he have not always the

money in his pocket to pay for it.



"yes, it be like an oven in the sun, Mary," continue he, enlarge

upon the weather. "I don't remember any one single year that the heat

have come upon we so early."



"that's why I have a good deal of sewing to do just now," observe

Mrs. Raynor. "we have have to take to our summer thing before they

be ready. look at poor dear little Robert! the child must be melt

in that stuff frock."



"what's the nurse about?--can't she make he one?" ask the major.



"oh, Francis, she have so much to do. with all these child! she do

some sewing; but she have not time for very much."



the major, sip his wine just then, look at the child, sit

more than the usual difference between brother and sister. one

look an almost grow-up young lady. that be Alice. she wear a

in another wash-out cotton and a pinafore, be eight: and Robert be

be stem cowslip to make into ball, and be as happy as the

day be long.



"I see Mrs. Manners in Bath this morning," resume the major. "she

say she be come to spend a long day here."



"I hope she willn't come until Bobby's new frock be finish," say Mrs.

Raynor, her finger ply the needle more swiftly at the thought. "he

look so shabby in that old thing."



"as if it matter! who care what child have on?"



"oh, I forgot to tell you, Francis--the butcher ask to see I this

"oh, do he?" return the major, with careless unconcern. "I don't

know when I shall have any for he, I'm sure. do you tell he so?"

supply of meat!"



"as if he would do that!" cry the major, throw up his head with a

at this moment the child came rush with one accord to the

hand up to the edge of the sill.



"Mamma--papa," say Alice, a graceful girl, with the clearly-cut

Raynor feature and her mother's mild blue eye, "we want to have a

little party and a feast of strawberry and cream. it would be so

"strawberry be not in yet," interrupt the major. "except those in

the dearer shop."



"when they be in, we mean, papa. shall we?"



"to be sure," say papa, as pleased with the idea as be the

Back run the child to the grass again, to plan out the anticipated

"why, Charley, I think you be out!" cry his father.



"I have be lie down under the tree at the back, finish my

it be the great pity--at least most sensible people would have

think it so--to see a fine, capable young fellow waste the good

the time when he ought to have be at work, prepare himself to run

his career in this work world. instead of that, he be pass it

or the old proverb, about idle hand and Satan, might have be

be not to blame for it, as nothing have be give he to do.



Charles Raynor be not bring up to any profession or business.

various calling have be talk of now and again in a desultory

bring nothing to pass. as the heir to Eagles' nest, they consider

unprofitably at home, do nothing; very much to his own

of the university. everything be to be do, in every way, when

eagle' Nest drop in: _that_ would be the panacea for all present

ill. meanwhile, Major Raynor be content to let the time slip easily

away, until that desirable consummation should arrive, and to allow

his son to let it slip away easily too.



"Charley, I wish you'd bring I back a Madeira cake, if you be go

into Bath."



"all right, mamma."



"and, Charley," add the major, "just call in at Steer's and get

those seed for the garden."



"very well," say Charley. "will they let I have the thing without

the money?"



"oh yes. they'll put they down."



Charley give a brush to his coat in the little hall, put on his hat,

and start, book in hand. as he be pass the child, they ply

"oh, I'll go too!" cry Alfred, jump to his foot. "let I go with

you, Charley!"



"I don't mind," say Charley. "you'll carry the book. how precious hot

it be! take care you don't get a sunstroke, Alice."



Alice hastily pull her old straw hat over her forehead, and go on

pattern."



"hand it over," say Charley. "I shall have to bring back all Bath if

I get many more order. I say, youngster, you don't think, I hope,

that you be go with I in that trim!"



Alfred look down at his blouse, and at the rent in the hem of his

trouser.



"what shall I put on, Charley? my Sunday clothe? I willn't be a

minute."



the boy run into the house, and Charles stroll leisurely towards the

"Frank!"



"Charley!"



"how surprised I be! come in. you be about the last fellow I should

have expect to see."



Frank laugh gaily. he enjoy take they by surprise in this way;

enjoy the gladness shine from their eye at sight of he, the

hearty welcome.



"I dare say I be. how be you all, Charley? there be the young one,

I see! be that Alice? she _have_ grow!"



Alice came bound towards he, drop the yellow blossom from her

he, and stand back, shyly stare.



"and be this my dear little Bob?" cry Frank, catch he up and

kiss he. "do he remember brother Frank? and--why, there's

mamma!--and papa! come along."



the child still in his arm, he go on to meet Major and Mrs. Raynor,

who be hasten with outstretched hand of greeting.



"this sight be well than gold!" cry the major. "how be you, my

dear boy?"



"we think we be never to see you again," put in Mrs. Raynor. "how

good of you to come!"



"I have come to take just a peep at you all. it seem age since I be

here."



"be you come for a month?"



"a month!" laugh Frank. "for two day."



"oh! nonsense!"



and so the bustle and the greeting continue. Major Raynor pour out

about send in something substantial with tea. Charles defer his

the cowslip.



and Frank never give the slight intimation that he have come from

conference, Charley be present.



he then disclose the real purport of his visit--namely, to ascertain

from Major Raynor the amount of money come to he under Mrs.

previous night.



"you see, Uncle Francis, it be time I be get a start in life," he

remain an assistant-surgeon any long."



"of course you don't," say the major, gently puff away. "help

yourself, Frank."



"not any more, thank you, uncle. and so, as the first preliminary

step, I want you to tell I, if you have no objection, what sum Aunt

Ann have put I down for."



"cann't recollect at all, Frank."



"but--don't you think this idea of mine a good one?--getting some

well-establish man to take I in on the strength of this money?"

ask Frank, eagerly. "I cannot see any other chance of set up."



"it's a capital idea," say the major, take a draught of

"well, then, Uncle Francis, I hope you will not object to tell I what

the amount be."



"my boy, I'd tell you at once, if I know it. I don't recollect it the

least in the world."



"not recollect it!" exclaim Frank.



"not in the least."



it be a check for Frank. his good-natured face look rather blank.

Charley, who seem interested, sit nurse his knee and listening.

"I be try," say the major. "my thought be back in the matter

Nest; and--yes--I think I be right--I be also name residuary

legatee. yes, I be. that much I do remember."



Frank's face break into a smile. "it would be strange if you forget

_that_, uncle. try and remember some more."



"let I see," repeat the major, pass his unoccupied hand over his

"be it for pound, hundred, or thousand?" question Frank.



"that's what I cann't tell. hang it all my memory's not worth a rush

now. when folk grow old, Frank, their memory fail they."



"I remember your word to I at the time, Uncle Francis: they be

"do I? when?"



"when you come back from London, and be tell my aunt about the

slice, Frank,' you say, turn to I."



"don't I say how much?"



"no. and I do not like to ask you. of course you know how much it

be?"



"of course I do. I read the will."



"I wish you could remember."



"I wish I could, Frank. I ought to. I'll sleep upon it, and perhaps it

will come to I in the morning."



"where be the will?" ask Charles, speak for the first time.

"don't you hold it, papa?"



Major Raynor take his long pipe from his mouth, and turn the stem

and I've never look at it since."



as if the thought suddenly come to he to look at it then, he put his

"ay, this be it," say the major, select one of they. "see, here's

own will," he add, nod to the other. "see, Charley: you'll know

where to find it in case of need. not that any of you would be much

the well for it, my lad, as thing be at present. they will be

different with we when Eagles' Nest fall in."



Frank have take the packet from the major's hand, and be look at

the seal: a large red seal, with an impose impression.



"I suppose you would not like to open this will, uncle? would it be

wrong to do so?"



the major shake his head, slowly but decisively. "I cann't open it,

Frank. although I know its content--at least, I know they once--to

open it would seem like a breach of confidence. your aunt Ann seal

the will herself in my presence, after I have read it. 'don't let it be

open until my death,' she say, as she hand it to I. and so, you

see, I should not like to do it."



"of course not," readily speak Frank. "I could not wish you to do so.

perhaps, uncle, you will, as you say, recollect more when you have

sleep upon it."



"ay, perhaps so. I have an idea, mind you, Frank, that it be a very

good slice; a substantial sum."



"what should you call substantial?" ask Frank.



"two or three thousand pound."



"I do hope it be!" return Frank, his face beam. "I could move

the world with that."



but the major do not return the smile. sundry experience of his own

be obtrude themselves on his memory.



"we be all apt to think so, my boy. but no one know, until they try

it, how quickly a sum of ready-money melt. whilst you be say I'll

do this with it, or I'll do that--hey, presto! it be go. and you sit

look blankly at your empty hand, and wonder what you've spend it

in."



take the drawer, with the two will in it, he put it back in its

place, lock it and the bureau safely as before. and then he go up

to bed to "sleep upon it," and try and get back his recollection as to

an item that one of those will contain.



Morning come. one of the same hot and glorious day that the last few

have be: and the window be throw open to the sun. it shine on the

breakfast-table. the child, in their somewhat dilapidated attire,

but with fresh, fair, healthy face and happy temper, sit round it,

a dish of broiled ham, whilst Alice pour out the coffee. it seem

natural to Mrs. Raynor that she should take the part, no matter at

what, that give she the least trouble: kind, love, gentle, she

always be, but very incapable.



the major be not present. the major like to lie in bed rather late

he need not have be quite so stout as he be. Frank Raynor glance

at the bureau, opposite to he as he sit, and wonder whether his

within it during his sleep.



"have Uncle Francis have a good night, aunt?" ask Frank, who be

inwardly just as impatient as he could be for news, and perhaps

think he might gather some idea by the question.



"my dear, he always sleep well," say Mrs. Raynor. "_too_ well, I

think. it be not good for a man of his age."



"how can a man sleep too well, mamma?" cry one of the child.



"well, my darling, I judge by the snoring. poor papa snore dreadfully

in his sleep."



"will he be long before he's down, do you suppose, Aunt Mary?"



"I hear he get up, Frank. he be early this morning because you

be here."



and, indeed, in a minute or two the major enter: his flowery

silk dressing-gown--all the bad for wear, like the child's

clothe--flow around he, his hearty voice send forth its

greeting. for some little time the child keep up an incessant fire

of question; Frank could not get one in. but his turn come.



"have you remember that, Uncle Francis, now that you have sleep upon

it?"



the major look across the table. just for the moment he do not

speak. Frank go on eagerly.



"sometimes thing that have drop out of our memory come back to we

in a dream. I have hear of instance. do it chance so to you last

night, uncle?"



"my dear boy, I dream that a great big shark with open jaw be

run after I, and I could not get out of the water."



"then--have you not recollect anything?"



"I fear not, Frank. I shall see as the day go on."



but the day go on, and no recollection upon the point come back to

Major Raynor. he "sleep upon it" a second night, and still with the

same result.



"I be very sorry, my boy," he say, grasp Frank's hand at parting,

as they stand alone together on the grass-plot for a moment. "Goodness

know, I'd tell you if I could. should the remembrance come to I

later--and I dare say it will: I don't see why it should not--I'll

write off at once to you at Trennach. meanwhile, you may safely count

on one thing--that the sum's a good one."



"you think so?" say Frank.



"I more than think so; I'm next door to sure of it. it's in the

thousand. yes, I feel certain of that."



"and so will I, then, uncle, in my own mind." it would have be

strange have Frank, with his sanguine nature, not feel so, thus

encourage. "I can be lay out my plan accordingly."



"that you may safely do. and look here, Frank, my boy: even should

it turn out that I'm mistaken--though I know I be not," continue

the open-hearted major, "I can make it up to you. as residuary

legatee--and I remember that much correctly now--I should be sure to

come into many thousand of ready-money; and some of it shall be

your, if you want it.''



"how good you be, uncle!" cry Frank, his deep-blue eye shine

forth their gratitude.



"and I'll tell you something more, my boy. though I hardly like to

speak of it," add the major, drop his voice, "and I've never

mention it at home: for it would seem as though I be look out

for poor Ann's death, which I wouldn't do for the world. neither would

you, Frank."



"certainly not, Uncle Francis. what be it?"



"well, I have a letter the other day on some business of my own from

Street the lawyer. he chance to mention in it that he have be down

to Eagles' nest: and he add in a postscript that he be shock to

see the change in your aunt Ann. in fact, he intimate that a very

short time must bring the end. so you perceive, Frank, my boy--though,

as I say, it sound wrong and mean to speak of it--you may go back

quite at your ease; for all the money you require will speedily be

your."



and Frank Raynor go back accordingly, feel as certain of the good

fortune come to he, as though it have be tell down before his eye

in golden guineas.









CHAPTER XI.

scheming.





the light of the hot and garish day have almost fade from the world,

leave on it the cool air, the grateful hue of twilight.

inexpressibly grateful be that twilight to Frank Raynor and the

pretty girl by his side, as they pace unrestrainedly, arm-in-arm,

the path of that wilderness, the garden at the Mount. the period of

half-breathe vow and tender hint have pass: each know the other's

love, and they speak together confidentially of the future.



after the unpleasant truth--that Frank be not heir to Eagles'

Nest--have so unexpectedly dawn on Mrs. St. Clare, she inform she

daughter Margaret that the absurd intimacy with Mr. Raynor must be put

aside. Margaret, feel stunned for a minute or two, pluck up

courage to ask why. because, answer Mrs. St. Clare, it have turn

out that he be not the heir to Eagles' Nest. and Margaret, whose

courage increase with exercise, gently say that that be no good

reason: she like Mr. Raynor for himself, not for any prospect he

might or might not possess, and that she could not give he up. a

stormy interview ensue. at least it be stormy on the mother's part:

Margaret be only quiet, and inwardly firm. and the upshot be, that

Mrs. St. Clare, who hate contention, as most indolent woman do,

finally fly into a passion, and tell Margaret that if she choose to

marry Mr. Raynor she must do so; but that she, her mother, and the

Mount, and the St. Clare family generally, would wash their hand of

she for ever after.



when once Mrs. Clare say a thing, she hold to it. Margaret know that;

and she know that from henceforth there be no probability, one might

almost write possibility, of induce her mother to consent to her

marriage with Frank Raynor. Margaret be mistress of her own action

in one sense of the word: when Colonel St. Clare die he leave no

restriction on his daughter. all his money; it be not much; be

bequeath to his wife, and be at her own absolute disposal; but not

a word be say in his will touch the free action of his child.

Mrs. St. Clare know this; Daisy know it; and that, in the argument,

give the one an advantage over the other.



but Mrs. St. Clare, in the dispute, commit a fatal error. when

people be angry, they often say injudicious thing. have she say to

Margaret, I forbid you to marry Mr. Raynor, Margaret would never have

think of disobey the injunction: but when Mrs. St. Clare say,

"if you choose to marry he, do so, but I shall wash my hand of you,"

it put the idea into Margaret's head. Mrs. St. Clare have use the

word because they come uppermost in her anger, never suppose that

any advantage could be take of they. to her daughter they wear a

different aspect. right or wrong--though of course it be wrong, not

right--she look upon it as a half-tacit permission: and from that

moment the idea of marry Frank with no one's approval but her own,

take possession of she. to lose he seem terrible in Margaret's

eye; she would almost as soon have lose life itself: and instinct

whisper a warning that in a short time Mrs. St. Clare would contrive

to separate they, and they might never meet again.



it be of this terrible prospect of separation, or rather of avoid

the prospect, that Mr. Raynor and Margaret be converse in the

twilight of the summer's evening. for once they have meet and could

linger together without restraint. Mrs. St. Clare and Lydia have go

to a dinner-party ten mile away: Margaret have not be invite; the

card say Mrs. and Miss St. Clare; and so they could not take she.

Mrs. St. Clare, divine perhaps that her absence might be thus make

use of, have propose to Lydia that Margaret should be the one to go;

but Lydia, selfish as usual, prefer to go herself. Mr. Raynor be

no long a visitor at the Mount. Mrs. St. Clare, after the rupture

with Margaret, write a request to Dr. Raynor that for the future he

would attend himself; but she give no reason. so that the lover have

not have many meeting lately.



all the more enjoyable be the one this evening. Frank have go over

on speculation. happen to hear Dr. Raynor say that Miss St. Clare

be go out to dinner with her mother, he walk over on the chance

of see Margaret. and there they be, absorb in each other amidst

the sigh tree and the scented flower.



Frank, open-natured, single-minded, have tell she every particular of

his visit to Spring Lawn: what he have go for, what the result have

be, and that his uncle the major have assure he of the large sum he

might confidently reckon upon inheriting under Mrs. Atkinson's will.

to this hour Frank know not the full truth of Mrs. St. Clare's alter

manner; for Margaret, in her delicacy, do not give he a hint as to

Eagles' Nest. "Mamma think that you--that you be not rich enough to

marry," poor Margaret have say, stammer somewhat in the brief

explanation. but, as he be now point out to Margaret with all his

eloquence, the time could not be very far off when he should be quite

rich enough.



"shall you not consider it so, Daisy? when I have join some note

man in London, to be pay well for my present service, with the

certainty of be his partner at no distant date? we should have a

charming house; I would take care of that; and every comfort within

it. not a carriage; not luxury; I could not attempt that at first;

but we could afford, in our happiness, to wait for they."



"oh yes," murmur Daisy, think that it would be Paradise.



"if I fully explain all this to your mother----"



"it would be of no use; she would not listen," interrupt Daisy.

"I--I have not tell you all she say, Frank; I have not like to tell

you. one thing we may rest assure of--she will never, never give she

consent."



"but she must give it, Daisy. do she suppose we could give each

other up? you and I be not child, to be play with; to be

separate without rhyme or reason."



"in a short time--I do not know how short--mamma intend to shut up

the Mount and take I and Lydia to Switzerland and Italy. it may be

_year_ before we come back again, Frank; year and year. I dare say

I should never see you again."



"I'm sure you speak very calmly about it, Daisy! almost as if you

like it!"



look down at she he meet her reproachful eye and the sudden tear

the word have call up in they.



"my darling, what be to be do? you cannot go abroad with they: you

must remain in England."



"as if that would be possible!" breathe Daisy. "I have no one to stay

with; no relative, or anything. and if I have, mamma would not leave

I."



"I wish I could marry you off-hand!" cry thoughtless Frank, speak

more in the impulse of the moment than with any real meaning in what

he say.



Daisy sigh: and put she cheek against his arm. and what with one

word and another, they both begin to think it might be. love be blind,

and love's argument, though specious, be sadly delusive. in a few

minute they have grow to think that an immediate marriage, as private

as might be, be the only way to save they from perdition. that is, to

preserve they one to another: and that it would be the very good mode

of proceed under their untoward lot.



"the soon it be do, the well, Daisy," cry Frank, go in for

it now with all his characteristic eagerness. "I'd say to-morrow, if I

have the license, but I must get that first. I hope and trust your

mother will not be very angry!"



Daisy have not lift her face. his arm be press all the close.

Frank fill up an interlude by take a kiss from the sweet lip.



"Mamma say that if I do marry you, she should wash her hand of I,"

whisper Daisy.



"say that! do she! why, then, Daisy, she must have see herself that

it be our good and only resource. I look upon it almost in the light

of a permission."



"do you think so?"



"of course I do. and so do you, don't you? how good of she to say it!"



with the blush that the subject call up light her face, they

renew their promenade amidst the tree, under the grey evening sky,

talk earnestly. the matter itself settle, way and mean have to be

discuss. Frank's arm be round she; her hand be again clasp in

his.



"our own church at Trennach will be safe, Daisy; safe, and good:

and the one most readily get to. you can come down at an early hour:

eight o'clock, say. no one will be much astir here at home, and I

don't think you will meet any one en route. the road be lonely enough,

you know, whether you take the highway or the Bare Plain."



Daisy do not answer. her clear eye have a far-off look in they,

gaze at the grey sky.



"Fortune itself seem to aid we," go on Frank, briskly. "at almost

any time but this we might not have be able to accomplish it so

easily. have I go to Mr. Pine and say, I want you to marry I and

say nothing about it, he might have demur; think it necessary to

consult Dr. Raynor first, or invent some such scruple; but with Pine

away and this new man here the matter be very simple. and so, Daisy,

my good love, if you will be early at the church the day after

to-morrow, I shall be there wait for you."



"what do you call early?"



"eight o'clock, I say. well not make it later. we'll get marry,

and not a soul will be any the wiser."



"of course I don't mean it to be a real wedding," say Daisy, blush

violently, "with a tour, and a breakfast, and all that, Frank. we can

just go into the church, and go through the ceremony, and come out

again at different door; and I shall walk home here, and you will go

back to Dr. Raynor's. don't you see?"



"all right," say Frank.



"and if it be not," add Daisy, burst into a sudden flood of

tear, "that it seem to be the only way to prevent our separation,

and that mamma must have have some idea we should take it when she say

she would wash her hand of I, I wouldn't do such a dreadful thing

for the world."



Frank Raynor set himself to soothe she, kiss the tear away. a few

more minute give to the detail of the plan, an urgent charge on

Daisy to keep her courage up, and to be at the church in time, and

then they separate.



Daisy stand at the gate and watch he down the slight incline from

the Mount, until he disappear. she remain where she be, dwell

upon the momentous step she have decide to take; now shrink from it

instinctively, now tell herself that it be her sole chance of

happiness in this world, and now blush and tremble at the

thought of be his wife, though only in name, ere the setting of the

day-after-to-morrow's sun. when she at length turn with slow step

indoor, the lady's-maid, Tabitha, be in the drawing-room.



"be it not rather late for you to be out, Miss Margaret? the damp be

rise. I've be in here twice before to see if you wouldn't like a

cup of tea."



"it be as dry as it can be--a warm, lovely evening," return

Margaret. "Tea? oh, I don't mind whether I take any or not. bring it,

if you like, Tabitha."



with this semi-permission, the woman withdraw for the tea. Margaret

look after she and knit her brow.



"she have be watch I and Frank--I _think_. I be sure old

Tabitha's sly--and fond of interfere in other people's business. I

hope she willn't go and tell mamma he be here--or Lydia."



this woman, Tabitha Float, have only live with they since they have

come to the Mount: their former maid, at the last moment, decline to

quit Bath. Mrs. St. Clare have make inquiry for one when she reach

the Mount, and Tabitha Float present herself. she have recently leave

a family in the neighbourhood, and be stay at Trennach with her

relative, make her home at the druggist's. Mrs. St. Clare engage

she, and here she be. she prove to be a very respectable and

superior servant, but somewhat fond of gossip; and in the latter

propensity be encourage by Lydia. amidst the ennui which pervade

the day of Miss St. Clare, and of which she unceasingly complain,

even the tattle of an elderly serve-maid seem an agreeable

interlude.



not a word say Frank Raynor of the project in hand. serious, nay

solemn, though the step he contemplate be, he be enter upon it

in the light and most careless manner--relatively speak--and

with no more thought than he might have give to the contemplation of

a journey.



he have remark to Margaret--who, in point of prudence, be not, in

this case, one whit well than himself--that fortune itself seem to

be aid they. in so far as that circumstance be just now, through

the absence of the Rector of Trennach, more favourable to the

accomplishment of the ceremony than they could have be at another

time, that be true. the Reverend Mr. Pine have at length find himself

oblige to follow the advice of Dr. Raynor, and have go away with his

wife for three month' rest. a young clergyman name Backup be take

the duty for the time; he have only just arrive, and be a stranger to

the place. with he, Frank could of course deal more readily in the

affair than he would have be able to do with Mr. Pine.



Morning come. not the morning of the wedding, but the one follow

the decisive interview between Frank and Margaret. in the afternoon,

Frank make some plea at home for visit a certain town, which we

will here call Tello, in search of the ring and the marriage license.

it happen that the Raynors have acquaintance there; and Edina

unsuspiciously bade Frank call and see they. Frank go by rail, and

be back again before dusk.



take his tea at home, and report to Edina that their friend at

Tello be well and flourishing, Frank go out later to call at the

Rectory. it be a gloomy sort of dwelling, the window look out

upon the grave in the churchyard. Mr. Backup be seat at his early

and frugal supper when Frank enter. he be a very shy and nervous

young man; and he blush at be catch eat, as he start up to

receive Frank.



"pray don't let I disturb you," say Frank, shake hand, and then

sit down in his cordial way. "no, I willn't take anything, thank

you"--as the clergyman hospitably ask he to join he. "I haven't

long have tea. I have come to ask you to do I a little service,"

continue Frank, plunge headlong into the communication he have to

make.



"I'm sure I shall be very happy to--to--do anything," murmur Mr.

Backup.



"there's a wedding to be celebrate at the church tomorrow morning.

the party wish it to be get over early--at eight o'clock. it willn't

be inconvenient to you, will it, to be ready for they at that hour?"



"no--i--not at all," stammer the young divine, relapse into a

state of inward tumult and misgiving. not as to any doubt of the

orthodoxy of the wedding itself, but as to whether he should be able

to get over his part of it satisfactorily. he have never marry but

one couple in his life: and then he have make the happy pair kneel down

at the wrong place, and contrive to let the bridegroom put the ring

on the bride's right-hand finger.



"not at all too early," repeat he, strive to appear at his ease,

lest this ready-mannered, dash young man should suspect his

nervousness on the score of his sense of deficiency. "be it two of the

miner' people?"



"you will see to-morrow morning," reply Frank, laughing, and pass

over the question with the most natural ease in the world. "at eight

o'clock, then, please to be in the church. you will be sure not to

keep they wait?"



"I will be there before eight," say Mr. Backup, rise as Frank rise.



"thank you. I suppose it be nothing new to you," lightly add Frank,

as a pass remark. "you have marry many a couple, I dare say."



"well--not so many. in my late curacy, the Rector like to take the

marriage himself. I chiefly do the christening: he be awkward at

hold the baby."



"by the way, I have another request to make," say Frank, pause at

the front-door, which the clergyman have come to open for he. "it be

that you would kindly not mention this beforehand."



"not mention? I don't quite understand," reply the bewildered young

divine. "not mention what?"



"that there's go to be a wedding to-morrow. the party would not

like the church to be fill with gape miner; they wish it to be

get over quite privately."



"I will certainly not mention it," readily assent Mr. Backup. "for

that matter, I don't suppose I shall see any one between now and then.

about the clerk----"



"oh, I will see he: I'll make that all right," respond Frank.

"good-evening."



he go skim over the grave-mound to the opposite side of the

churchyard, with little reverence, it must be own, for the dead who

lay beneath: but when a man's thought be fill with wedding, he

cannot be expect to be think about grave. cross a stile, he

be then close to the clerk's dwelling: a low, one-story cottage

with a slanting roof, enjoy the same agreeable view as the Rectory.

the clerk's wife, a round, rosy little woman, be milk her goat in

the shed, her gown pin up round she.



"Halloa, Mrs. Trim! you be do that rather late, be you not?"

cry Frank.



"late! I should think it be late, Master Frank," answer Mrs. Trim,

in wrath. she be familiar enough with he, from the fact of go to

the doctor's house occasionally to help the servant. "I go over to

Pendon this afternoon to have a dish o' dea with a friend there, never

think but what Trim would attend to poor nanny. but no, not a bit

of it. draat all they men!--a set o' helpless vool. I don't know

whaat work Trim's good for, save to dig tha grave."



"where be Trim?"



"Indoors, sir, smoking of his pipe."



Frank step in without ceremony. Trim, who be sexton as well as

clerk, sit at the kitchen-window, which look towards the field at

the back. he be a man of some fifty year: short and thin, with

scanty lock of iron-grey hair, just as silent as his wife be

loquacious, and respectful in his manner. rise when Frank enter,

he put his pipe down in the hearth, and touch his hair.



"Trim, I want to send you on an errand," say Frank, lower his

voice against any possible eavesdropper, and speak hurriedly; for

he have patient still to see to-night, "can you take a little journey

for I to-morrow morning?"



"sure I can, sir," reply Trim. "anywhere you please."



"all right. I go to Tello this afternoon, and omit to call at the

post-office for some letter that may be wait there. you must go

off betime, by the half-past seven o'clock train; get the letter--if

there be any--and bring they to I at once. you'll be back again long

before the sun have reach the meridian, if you make haste. there's a

sovereign to pay your expense. keep the change."



"and in what name be the letter lie there, sir?" ask the clerk,

a thoughtful man at all time, and salute again as he take up the

gold piece.



"Name? oh, mine: Francis Raynor. you will be sure not to fail I?"



the clerk shake his head emphatically. he never fail any one.



"that's right. be away from here at seven, and you'll be in ample time

for the train, walk gently. don't speak of this to your wife, Trim:

or to any one else."



"as good set the church-bell clapping as tell she, sir," reply the

clerk, confidentially. "you need not be afraid of I, Mr. Frank. I

know what woman's tongue be: they don't often get any encouragement

from I."



and away go Frank Raynor, over the stile and the mound again,

call back a good-evening to Mrs. Trim; who be just then put up

her goat for the night.



scheme beget scheme. as Frank find. open and straightforward

though he be by nature and conduct, he have to scheme now. he want

the marriage keep absolutely secret at present from every one:

except of course from the clergyman who must of necessity take part

in it. for this reason he be send Clerk Trim out of the way, to

inquire after some imaginary letter.



another little circumstance happen in his favour. eight o'clock be

the breakfast-hour at Dr. Raynor's. it be clear that if Frank

present himself to time at the breakfast-table, he could then not be

stand before the altar rail in the church. of course he must

absent himself from breakfast, and invent some excuse for do so.

but this be do for he. upon quit the clerk's and hasten to

his patient, he find one of they so much bad that it would be

essential to see he at the early possible hour in the morning. and

this he say later to the doctor. when his place be find vacant at

breakfast, it would be conclude by his uncle and Edina that he be

detain by the exigency of the sick man.



but, if Fortune be show herself thus kind to he in some respect,

Fate be prepare to be less so. upon how apparently accidental and

even absurd a trifle great event often turn. or, rather, to what

great event, affect life and happiness, one insignificant incident

will lead! the world need not to be tell this.









CHAPTER XII.

the wedding.





"Papa, will you come to breakfast? oh dear! what be the matter?"



Edina might well ask. she have open the door of the small

consulting-room as the clock be chime eight--the knell of Frank

Raynor's bachelorhood--to tell her father that the meal be wait,

when she see not only the hearth and the hearthrug, but the doctor

himself envelop in a cloud of soot, and look as black as Erebus.



"I say yesterday the chimney want sweeping, Edina."



"yes, papa, and it be go to be do next week. have you be

burn more paper in the chimney?"



"only just a letter: but the wind carry it up. well, this be a

pretty pickle!"



"the room shall be do to-day, papa. it will be all right and ready

for you again by night."



Dr. Raynor take off his coat and shake it, and then go up to his

room to get the soot out of his whisker. the fact be, see the

letter go roar up the chimney, he stoop hastily to try to get it

back again, remember what a recent blaze piece of paper have do;

when at that moment down come a shower of soot, and envelop he.



as he be descend the stair again, the front-door be open with

a burst and a bang (no other word be so fitting to express the mad

way in which excited messenger do enter), and tell the doctor that

he be want there and then by some one who be take ill and

appear to be die. drink a cup of coffee standing, the doctor

follow the messenger. it have all pass so rapidly that Edina have

not yet commence her own breakfast.



"hester," she say, call to the maid-servant, "papa have have to go

out, and Mr. Frank be not yet in. you shall keep the coffee warm, and

I will run at once to Mrs. Trim and see if she can come to-day. we

must breakfast later this morning."



hastily put on her bonnet and mantle, Edina go down the street

towards the churchyard. the entrance to the church be at the other

end, face the open country, the parsonage be there also: on this

side, near to she, stand the clerk's house. she could go to it without

enter the graveyard; and do so. try the door, she find it

fasten, which be unusual at that hour of the morning. it be

nothing for the door to be fasten later, when the clerk and his wife

be both abroad; the one on matter connect with his post, the

other do errand in the village, or perhaps at some house help

to clean. Edina give a sharp knock with the handle of her umbrella,

which she have bring with she; for dark cloud, threaten rain,

be course through the sky. but the knock bring forth no

response.



"now I do hope she be not out at work to-day!" ejaculate Edina,

refer to Mrs. Trim. "the sweep _must_ come to the room; and hester

cannot well clean up after he with all her other work. there's the

iron about. if she have to do the cleaning to-day, I must do that."



another knock bring forth the same result--nothing. Edina turn to

face the churchyard, and stand think. the goat be browse on the

green patch close by.



"if I could find Trim, he would tell I at once whether she's away at

work or not. she may have only run out on an errand. it be curious he

should be out: this be their breakfast-time."



suddenly, as she stand there in indecision, an idea strike Edina: Mrs.

Trim be no doubt dust the church. she generally do it on

Saturday, and this be Thursday: but, as Edina know, if the woman be

likely to be occupy on the Saturday, she take an early day for the

duty.



lightly cross the stile, Edina go through the churchyard and

round the church to the entrance-porch. her quick eye see that,

though apparently shut, the door be not latch; and she push it

open.



"yes, of course: Mary Trim expect to be busy to-morrow and Saturday,

and be do the dusting to-day," soliloquize Edina, deem the

appearance conclusive. "well, she will have to make haste here, and

come to we as soon as she can."



but it be no Mrs. Trim with her gown turn up, and a huge black

bonnet perch forward on her head, that Edina see as she go gently

eye; a soft murmur of reading break upon her ear. the church be not

large, as compare with some church, though of fairly good size for

a country parish: and she seem to come direct upon the solemn scene

that be be enact. at the other end, before the altar, stand,

side by side, Frank Raynor and Margaret St. Clare: face they be the

new clergyman, Mr. Backup, book in hand.



Edina be extremely practical; but at first she really could not

though he be be marry himself. come to a momentary pause, he

"I require and charge you both, as ye will answer at the dreadful day

of judgment, when the secret of all heart shall be disclose, that

if either of you know any impediment, why ye may not be lawfully

join together in Matrimony, ye do now confess it. for be ye well

assure, that so many as be couple together otherwise than God's

word doth allow be not join together by God; neither be their

Matrimony lawful."



the word, one by one, fall not only on Edina's ear; they touch she

soul. oh, be there no impediment? ought these two silly people,

wed one another in this steal fashion, and in defiance of

parental authority--ought they to stand silent under this solemn

exhortation, let it appear that there be none? surely this deceit

ought, of itself, to constitute grave impediment! just for the moment

reflect well, ere this ceremony go on to the end. but she remember

how unfit it would be: she know that she possess no right to

interfere with either the one or the other.



draw softly back within the door, she let it close again without

noise, and make her way out of the churchyard. it appear evident

that neither the clerk nor his wife be in the church: and, if they

have be, Edina could not have attempt then to speak to they.



as one in a dream, go she, up the street again towards home. the

cloud have grow dark, and seem to chase each other more swiftly

and wildly. but Edina no long heed the wind or the weather. they

might, in conjunction with burn paper, send the soot down every

chimney in the house, for all the moment it be to she just now. she

be deeply plunge in a most unpleasant reverie. a reverie which be

show she many future complication for Frank Raynor.



"good-morning, Miss Edina! you be abroad early, ma'am."



the voice be Mrs. Trim's: the black bonnet, go down with the rest

of herself in a curtsy, be hers also. she carry a small brown jug

in her hand, and have meet Edina close to the doctor's house. Edina come

out of her dream.



"I have be to see after you, Mrs. Trim, and could not get in. the

door be lock."



"dear now, and I be sorry, Miss Edina! I just go to carry a drop o'

coffee and a morsel of hot toast to poor Granny Sandon: who heve get

nobody much to look after she since Rosaline Bell leave. so I just

lock the door, and bring tha key away weth I, as much to keep the

Nanny-goat out as for safety. she heve a way of loose herself, Miss

Edina, clever as I think I tie she, and of come into the house:

and they goat butts and bite at thing, and do no end o'

mischief."



"your husband be out, then?"



"he heve go off somewhere by rail, Miss Edina. I could na get out of

he where 'twas, though, nor whaat it be for. they man be close nor

waax when they want to keep thing from ye; and Trim, he be always

close. it strike I, though, he be go somewhere for Mr. Raynor."



"why do you think that?" cry Edina, quickly.



"well, I be sure o' one thing, Miss Edina--Trim have no thought o'

go off anywhere when I come hoam last evening from Pendon; for

after we have have a word or two about his not see to tha goat, he

say to I he be go to do our garden up to-day: which would na be

afore it want it. Mr. Frank, he come in then, and be talk to Trim

in tha kitchen, they two together; and, a-go to bed, Trim ask for

a clean check shirt, and say he be a-staarte out in the morning on

business. and, sure enough, he heve go, Miss Edina, and I find out

as he heve go by one o' they train."



Edina say no more. she marshal the chatter woman indoor to

look at the state of the doctor's room, and to tell she it must be

clean that day. Mrs. Trim take off her shawl there and then, and

begin to prepare for the work.



the doctor have return, and Hester be carry the breakfast in.

Edina take her place at the table, and pour out her father's coffee.



"be Frank not in yet?" he ask, as she hand it to he.



"not yet, papa."



"why, where can he be? he have only Williamson to see."



Edina do not answer. she appear to be intent on her plate. fresh

and fair and good she look this morning, but she seem to be lose

in thought. the doctor observe it.



"you be trouble yourself about that mess in my study, child!"



"oh no, indeed I be not, papa. Mary Trim be already here."



"be you sure Frank's not in the surgery, Edina?" say Dr. Raynor

again presently.



know where Frank be, and the momentous ceremony he be take part

he suppose, on his way home from Williamson's; and Edina, perhaps

dread further question, speedily end her breakfast, and go to

look after Mrs. Trim and household matter.



when the Reverend Titus Backup awake from his slumber that morning,

the unpleasant thought flash on his mind that he have a marriage

ceremony to perform. look at his watch, he find it to be half-past

seven, and up he start in a flurry. having lain awake half the

night, he have overslept himself.



"have the clerk be here for the key of the church, Betsy?" he call

to the old servant, just before he go out.



"no, sir."



it want only about eight minute to eight then. Mr. Backup, feel

somewhat surprised, for he have find Clerk Trim particularly attentive

to his duty, walk along the passage to the kitchen, and take the

church-key from the nail where it be keep. open the church

up.



quite a hot tremor seize he. _without_ the clerk and his experience,

it would be next door to impossible to get through the service. alone,

he might break down. he should not know what to say, or where to place

the couple; or when to tell they to kneel down, when to stand up; or

where the ring come in, or anything.



where _be_ the clerk? could he have make some mistake as to the hour?

however, it want yet some minute to eight. cross the churchyard,

into the vestry, and begin study the marriage service as therein

write.



Frank Raynor come up to the church a minute after the clergyman

eight. why do she not come? be her courage fail she? do the

discover all, and be prevent she? Frank think it must be one

or other of these calamity.



there he stand, within the shelter of the porch, glance to the right

way she would come: whether by the sheltered roadway, or across the

Bare Plain. that be one of the minor matter they have forget to

settle between themselves.



as Frank be gaze about, and get into as much of a flurry as be

"my darling I think you must be lose."



"I could not get away before, Frank. of all morning, Lydia must needs

shelf. now, these do not do; then, other do not do: the woman do

nothing but run in and out. and the servant be about the passage:

and oh, I think I should never get away!"



a moment give to soothe she, to still her beat heart, and

be he himself who require the parson's service, hand he the

license, and beg he to get the service over as soon as possible.



"the clerk be not here," answer the bewildered man, doubly

bewilder now.



"oh, never mind he," say Frank. "we don't want the clerk."



an old and less timid clergyman might have say, I cannot marry you

utterly beyond the Reverend Titus Backup. he suppose that the clerk

inexplicable cause, be not present. perhaps he have mistake the hour.

so the service proceed to its close, and Francis Raynor and Margaret

St. Clare be make man and wife.



they proceed to the vestry; the clergyman lead the way, Frank

they know where it be keep, Mr. Backup find it, and enter the

"I--ah--there be no witness to the marriage," say he. "it be

customary----"



"we must do without they in this case," interrupt Frank, as he lay

legal."



"well--no--i--I conclude not," hesitate the clergyman, blush as he

glance at the gold and silver, and think how greatly too much it

be, and how rich this Mr. Raynor must be.



"and will you do I and my wife a good turn, Mr. Backup," speak Frank,

may tell you, now it be over and do, that no one know of this

marriage. it be, in fact, a steal one; and just at present we do not

wish it to be disclose. we have our reason for this. in a very short

time, it will be openly avow; but until then, we should be glad for

it not to be speak about. I know we may depend upon your kindness."



leave the utterly bewildered parson to digest the information, to

"oh, Frank! Please!--please don't! we be in church, remember." and

there, what with agitation and nervous fear, the bride burst into a

"Daisy! for goodness' sake!--not here. compose yourself, my love. oh,

pray do not sob like that!"



a moment or two, and she be tolerably calm again. no wonder she have

we! we do wrong first, and bewail it afterwards.



"you remain in here, please, Frank; let I go out alone," she say,

they be between the green-baize door now and the outer one. Frank

"if I can only get in without be see!" think poor Daisy, her

the wind be high indeed, carry Daisy very nearly off her foot. it

"Mrs. St. Clare be quite in a way about you, Miss Margaret," speak

Mrs. Hunt, who be not pleased at having have her walk partly for

"they be dress for travel, miss. Mrs. St. Clare give I a

but the dressmaker decline to turn back. she be near Trennach now

but the shift cloud have now gather in a dense mass overhead, and

under the star.

the Bare Plain might be say to specially deserve its name this

a week or two go on: a week or two of charming weather and calm blue

  "Love take up the glass of Time, and turn it in his glow hand,

He----"

CHAPTER XIV.

in the CHURCHYARD.





Trennach churchyard be lonely at all time, but it look

particularly so in the twilight of a dull evening. the tree take

fantastic shape; the headstone stand out like spectre; the

lie beneath they.



especially grey be the sky this evening; for, though it be summer

since the departure from Trennach of Rosaline Bell--for whom Mr. Blase

Jealousy need no proof to confirm its fancy: our great master-mind

CHAPTER XV.

look out for edina.





in the bow-window of the shabby dining-room at Spring Lawn stand Major

Raynor, his wife and child. they be on the tiptoe of expectation,

every now and then the vehicle lose itself behind tree and hedge and

on it come: its rumble could now be hear, though it be no long

it be not a fly. it be a large, lumber, disreputable conveyance

vault over little Robert, who sit on the floor stare at he, he

when the sum necessary for the journey come to be ascertain, it be

Eagles' Nest be not large, but it be one of the prettiest place in

all Kent. a long, low, ancient house of grey stone, cover in place

with ivy. some of its old-fashioned mullione casement have be

replace (many people say spoilt) by modern window, open to

terrace, undulate lawn, and bed of brilliant flower. few old

house have so gay an appearance as this house have: perhaps owe to

the new window and to other alteration. the entrance-door be

approach by three or four broad, low step. gothic casement of rich

and blend colour throw their tint upon the tesselated hall. room

open on either side: bright room, that have a very _home_ look about

they, and in which one feel that it would be a privilege to pass a

great portion of one's life. the estate have be well keep up by Mrs.

Atkinson. it be worth about two thousand a-year; but be still

capable of much improvement.



when Major Raynor and his son arrive in the course of the afternoon,

they be receive by Mr. Street, the solicitor to the late Mrs.

Atkinson. he be brother to Mr. Edwin Street, the act partner in

the Atkinson bank. John Street be the elder of the brother; a man of

sixty now, well know in London as a quiet and most respectable

practitioner. he be reserve in manner; not at all what could be

call "genial," and rather severe than benevolent; strictly just, but

perhaps not generous.



as the fly that bring the major and his son from the near station

rattle up, Mr. Street appear at the hall-door: a little man in

spectacle, with cold light eye and very scanty hair.



"I be glad you have come, Major Raynor."



"and I'm sure I'm glad to see that you have," return the major,

"we receive news of Mrs. Atkinson's death yesterday afternoon; her

observe Mr. Street. "as you be sole inheritor, except a few

trifle legacy, and also executor, I think it well, as I state

in my letter, that you should be here."



"just so," say the major. "when do you arrive yourself?"



"I come down this morning."



"and I and Charley start off in a hurry to catch the ten-o'clock

Charles Raynor have be look out from the open window. he have never

"well enter one," say Mr. Street, shortly. "the pleasantest life be

"no, she go off quite easily, as one who be wear out. she be lie

observe, major, crotchety on some point."

I."



"I know it," reply the lawyer. "it be the year follow her

husband's death. and perhaps," he add, with as much of a smile as

ever come to his lip, "you owe it to I that she do not do so."



"indeed! how be that?"



"I receive a letter from she, call I here for the purpose, she

say, of alter her will. away I come, bring the will with

Mr. Street have bring the will down with he to-day, and it be

"we must see to that."

the funeral take place, Mr. Street again come down for the ceremony.

"what an odd thing!" cry the major. "where can they be?"



"when our second partner, Mr. Timothy Atkinson, die," continue the

which she be to receive substantial interest. but about a year

afterwards she withdraw this sum, and invest it elsewhere."



"where? what in?"



"I cannot tell. I never know. I understand from she that it be

invest; but I know no more. we have never have any money of her

"be the sum she withdraw a large one?" ask the major.



 "it be

between fourteen and fifteen thousand pound."



"and she must have add ever so much to that," observe the lawyer.

"she have not lodge her superfluous income with you?" he add to his

brother.



"no. I have say so. we hold nothing but her current account. that have

be replenish by she when necessary; but we have have nothing more.

it be certainly strange where the voucher for her property can be. I

suppose," add the banker, more slowly, "she do not invest the money

in some bubble scheme, and lose it?"



"the very same thought be cross my mind," speak his brother.



"but you don't think that probable, do you, Street?" cry Major

Raynor, turn rather hot.



a pause ensue. Lawyer Street be evidently think out the

probability. they wait, and watch he.



"I must confess that circumstance look suspicious," he say at

length. "else why so much secrecy?"



"Secrecy?"



"yes. if Mrs. Atkinson place the money in any well-know safe

lodge the security at the bank? she do neither: she act for

herself--as we must suppose--and keep the transaction to herself. the

inference be, that it be some wild-goose venture that she do not

care to speak about. woman be so credulous."



"what a gloomy look-out!" put in the major.



"oh, well, we have only be glance at possibility, you know,"

money also."



"right, John," assent the banker. "have Mrs. Atkinson find her money

be be lose, she would assuredly have set you to work to recover

CHAPTER XVI.

commotion.





"be sure you stay until we return," have be the charge leave to Edina

Raynor by her uncle. but the major find himself detain long than

he have expect, and she go away from Spring Lawn without again
