during the short period of her absence from Trennach--nine day--her

father have change so much for the bad that she start when she see

he. as he come out of his house to welcome she, all Edina's pulse

stand still for a moment, and then course on with a bound. in a

gradual, waste illness, not very apparent to those around, it be

only on such an occasion as this that its progress can be judge of.



"Papa, you have be ill!"



"true, Edina, but I be mend a little now."



"why do you not send for I?"



"Nay, my dear, there be not any necessity for that."



a substantial tea-table have be spread, and in a very few minute

Edina be preside at it; her travel thing off, her soft brown

hair smooth, her countenance wear its usual cheerful gravity. not

a gravity that repel: one that insensibly attract, for it speak

of its owner's truth, and faith, and earnestness, of her goodwill to

all about she. sit there, dispense cup of tea to the doctor and

Frank, she be ready to hear the news of all that have transpire in

the village during her absence.



almost the first item that greet she be the stir about Josiah Bell,

of which she have previously hear nothing. it have not subside in the

least, but rather increase: the man so long missing be now suppose

to be lie at the bottom of the deep shaft. but the supposition could

only be trace back to a very insecure source indeed: nothing more

than a dream of Mr. Blase Pellet's.



"a dream!" exclaim Edina, in the midst of her wonder.



"so Pellet say," reply Dr. Raynor.



"but, papa, can there be any foundation for it? I mean for the fact,

not the dream."



"the very question we all ask when the rumour arise, Edina. at first

it could not be trace to any source at all; there be the report, but

whence it came seem a mystery. at last, by dint of close and patient

investigation, chiefly on the part of Float the publican, it be

trace to Blase Pellet, and he say he have dream it."



"then, after all, it have no real foundation," cry Edina.



"none but that. I question Pellet myself, ask he how he come to

spread such a report about. he reply that he do not spread the

report that Bell be lie there, only that he dream he be there."



"I should have think Blase Pellet a very unlikely man to have

dream, papa, and to relate they."



"so should I," assent the doctor, significantly. "so unlikely, that

I cannot help suspect he do not have this one."



Frank Raynor, who have rise and cross to the window, as if attract

by something in the street, half turn at this remark, but

immediately turn back again. Edina look inquiringly at her father.



"I could not help fancy, as I listen to he, that Pellet be

say it with a purpose," observe the doctor. "his manner be

peculiar. if I may so describe it--shuffle."



"I scarcely understand you, papa. you think he do not have the dream?

that he only say he have it; and say it to answer some purpose of his

own?"



"just so, Edina."



"but what could be his purpose?"



"ah, there I be at fault. we may discover that later. if he do say it

with a purpose, I conclude it will not end here."



"well, it sound rather strange altogether," observe Edina. "Frank,

do you mean to let your tea get quite cold?"



Frank Raynor return to his place. he drink his tea, but decline to

eat, and begin to speak of Mrs. Atkinson's will.



"do you hear any particular about it, Edina?"



"no," reply Edina. "except the one fact that she do not make a

second will. there be doubt upon the point, you know."



"Uncle Francis never entertain any doubt about it, Edina; and he be

the good judge, I think, of what his sister would or would not do. I

be very glad, though, for his and Charley's sake."



"for all their sake," add Edina.



"I rather wonder we have not hear from he," resume Frank. "the

funeral take place three or four day ago."



"you be not able to go to it, papa?" say Edina.



"no, child. neither could Frank be spare. it would have take three

day, you see, to go and return comfortably."



rise from the tea-table as soon as he could make a decent excuse for

it, for he have no business call on his time this evening, Frank set

off on his usual walk to the Mount. on five evening, since Edina

leave, have he so go; but never with any success: not once have Daisy

come out to he. she be be watch close than ever.



"and I suppose I shall have my walk for nothing this evening also!"

think Frank, as he pluck a wild-rise from a fragrant roadside

hedge. "this shall not go on long: but I should like to present myself

to Mrs. St. Clare with an assured sum to start we in life. I wonder

Uncle Francis do not write! he must know I be anxious--if he think

about it at all. up to his ear in his new interest, he forget other

people's."



Fortune favour Frank this evening. as he approach the outer gate

of the Mount, he see Daisy stand at it, very much to his surprise.



"Mamma's lawyer have come over on business, and she be shut up with

he," begin Daisy, her eye dance with delight. "she tell I to go

up to Lydia, but Lydia be asleep, and I come out here."



"I have want to see you so much, Daisy," say Frank, as he give she

his arm, and they pass under the broad elm-tree. "my aunt, Mrs.

Atkinson, be dead."



"we see it in the paper," answer Daisy.



"it be from she that I expect money, you know. every day, I look for a

letter from my uncle Francis, tell I what sum it be that I

inherit. and then I shall present myself to your mother. I have so

long to tell you this."



"I have long to see you," return Daisy, her pulse beat wildly

with various and very mixed feeling, her face flush and pale.

"I--I--I want to ask you something, Frank."



"ask away, my love," be his reply. but he notice her emotion.



"perhaps you will not answer I?"



"indeed I will, Daisy. why not?"



"it be about--Rosaline Bell." she could scarcely get the word out for

agitation.



Frank be startle. it be quite evident that he be unprepared for

any such topic. it seem to _frighten_ he. else why that sudden

change of countenance, that sudden dropping of Daisy's arm? her heart

fall.



"what of she?" ask Frank, quite sharply. for in truth he believe

Daisy be about to question he, not of Rosaline herself, but of that

mysterious rumour connect with her father and the Bottomless Shaft;

and it grate terribly on all his nerve.



"I see it be true," gasp Daisy. "oh! why do you marry _I?_"



"what be true?" return Frank, unpleasantly agitated.



 "that you--that

you--be fond of Rosaline Bell. you love she all along. before you

love I!"



the charge be so very different from what he have be fear, that

Frank feel for the moment bewilder: bewilder in the midst of his

inexpressible relief. he stand still, turn so that Daisy face he,

and gaze into her eye.



"_what_ be that you say, my dear? I really do not understand."



Daisy shake and shiver, but do not speak.



"that I love Rosaline Bell? I never love she. what in the name of

wonder put such an idea into your head?"



for answer Daisy burst into tear. "she--she be so beautiful!"



"beautiful! of course she be beautiful. and I admire her beauty,

Daisy, if it come to that, as much as other people do. but as to

love Rosaline Bell, that be a mistake. I never feel a spark of love

for she. what a goose you must be, Daisy! and why on earth should you

have take up the fancy just now?"



Daisy sob too much to answer. she almost believe what he say, for

no doubt lie in his earnest tone, and she suffer herself to be

soothe. she would have quite believe it but for Frank's sign of

discomfiture at the introduction of the girl's name. Frank hold she to

he as they walk under the tree, and kiss her tear-stain face

from time to time.



"you need not doubt my love, Daisy. that at least be your."



they part more hopefully than usual, for Frank assure she it could

not be above a day or two ere he claim she openly; and Daisy feel

that she might believe he in all respect; and she resolutely fling

her jealousy to the wind.



"fare you well, my darling. a short time now--we may count it by

hour--and this tantalize life will be over."



he go home by way of the Bare Plain. and by so do--and it be not

very often now that he choose that route--fall into an adventure he have

not bargain for. round and about the Bottomless Shaft have collect

a crowd of man, who be make very much of a commotion.



it appear that the rumour, touch Josiah Bell, have this night

reach what might be call a climax. miner have go off from

various quarter to the alleged scene of Mr. Blase's dream, and be

plunge into the mystery con amore. as many as could press around the

pit's mouth be hold on one to another for safety and bending

dangerously over it: as if by that mean they could solve the problem

of who and what might be lie within its depth. other stand at a

distance, momentarily take their pipe out of their mouth to make

their free comment. Mrs. Bell, hearing of the stir, have tie a yellow

silk square (once Josiah's Sunday-go handkerchief) over her cap,

and come out to make one of the throng. it be a very light, hot

night, daylight scarcely depart, and the western sky bright with a

pale amber. the rugged face of the miner and the red glow from their

pipe, couple with the commotion that stir they, make up a strange

scene.



"be you here, Mrs. Bell?" cry Frank, as he discern she on the

outskirt of the crowd. "what be the matter?"



"there's nothing the matter," interpose Blase Pellet. and Frank

turn on his heel to face the speaker in the moment's impulse, for he

have not know that he be there. "what the plague all the town have

come out for like this, I cann't think. let they mind their own

business."



"but we consider that it be our business, don't you see, blase," put

in Andrew Float, in his civil way. "our poor vanish soe be either

lie there in aal they stone and ashe, or he be not; and we'd like

to make sure which it be."



"well, then, he be _not_ there," return Blase: and he disappear

amidst the throng.



"have anything fresh arisen?" inquire a quiet voice at this

juncture--that of Dr. Raynor--address both Frank and Mrs. Bell, who

be stand side by side. the doctor, observe from his window a

number of people, evidently in excitement, make for the Bare Plain,

have come forth himself to learn what the movement mean.



"I cann't find out that there's anything fresh, sir," be the dame's

answer. "amid such confusion one don't easily get to the bottom of

thing. Andrew Float say 'twa just a thought that take a few of 'em

as they sit talk of Bell at the Golden Shaft--that they'd come off

and have a look down the pit's mouth; and the news spread, and other

collect and follow. but I hardly think anything so simple could

have bring all these."



"they must have some reason for come," remark the doctor, gaze

at the ever-increase crowd.



"Blase Pellet have just say there be no reason," rejoin Frank. "I

should advise you not to stand out here any long," he add, to Mrs.

Bell.



"Blase Pellet's no one to go by: he say one thing to-day, and another

to-morrow," rejoin Dame Bell, as she turn on the path that lead to

her home; they turn with she.



"I think the dream that he say he have, be certainly not very much

to go by," observe Dr. Raynor, quietly.



"oh, but that dream be a good deal," say Dame Bell. "and I've never

have a good night's rest, sir, since I hear it, and that's more than a

week ago. I cann't sleep at night for thinking of it."



"I be sorry to hear you say so, Mrs. Bell: I think you possess

well sense. pellet must have be very foolish to tell you about

it."



"it ben't he that do tell I, Dr. Raynor. leastway, not off-hand.

it be Nancy Tomson. she come into my place one morning, when I be

down on my knee whiten the hearth-flag; and I see how scare she

face look. 'guess what they be say now,' say she: 'they've get a

tale that your husband be lie in the Bottomless Shaft.' well, sir, I

stare at she, sit back, as I knelt, with the stone in my hand:

for you see I think she mean he be lie there asleep; I really

think no worse. 'go along with you, Nancy,' say I; 'as if Bell

would lay himself down to sleep near that shaft!' 'oh, it's not near

it, but in it,' say she; 'and he's not sleep, but dead.' well,

doctor, though I find every soul in the place say the same thing,

for four-and-twenty hour I could not get to learn why they say it.

Andrew Float tell I at last. he say it be through a dream of Blase

Pellet's."



Dr. Raynor, listen attentively, make no comment.



"I have Pellet before I, sir, and he make a clean breast of it. he have

not intend to let I know it, he say--and I don't think he have; but

I do know it, and so it be no use hold out. it be a dreadful

dream, he say. he have see my poor husband lie at the bottom of

that deep shaft, dead: see he as plain as he have ever see anything

in all his life. when he wake up, his hair be stand on end with

horror."



"ah," say the doctor quietly, his tone one of utter disbelief, though

Mrs. Bell do not detect it. "do he intimate, pray, how long Bell have

be lie there?"



"it be just what I ask he, sir, when I could get my breath again.

a good three month, he be sure, he say. which must have bring it

back, sir, you see, to the time of his disappearance."



"yes, I do see," observe the doctor, rather pointedly. "well, I do

not put any faith in dream, Mrs. Bell, and I would advise you not to

put any either. good-night. go in as soon as you can."



Dr. Raynor turn homeward, make a circuit to avoid the throng.

Frank begin whistle softly to himself, as a man sometimes do when

absorb in thought.



"what be your opinion of this, Frank?" ask the doctor, abruptly.



"I can form none, sir. why they should collect----"



"not that," interrupt the doctor. "one fool make many. I speak of

Blase Pellet's allege dream. I, myself, believe he have nothing of the

kind: his manner, when I speak with he about it, be not

satisfactory: but what puzzle I be, his motive for say that he

have the dream. some man be gift with a propensity for astound

their fellow-creature with marvellous tale. to create a sensation

they'd say they have be hang, draw, quarter, and bring to life

again. but Pellet be not one of these; he be quiet, reticent and

practical."



Frank make no reply. they be very close now to the Bottomless Shaft,

and to the crowd surge around it.



"I could almost think that he _know_ Bell be there," resume the

doctor, lower his voice. "if so, he must have be privy to the

accident--if it be an accident--that send poor Bell down. perhaps

take part in it----"



"oh no, no!" incautiously speak Frank. "it be not likely that he would

take part in anything of the sort, Uncle Hugh," he add in quieter

tone.



"if I don't quite think it, it be because there be one or two

stumble-block in the way," go on Dr. Raynor with composure. "have

Pellet be a witness to any accident--any false slip of Bell's, for

instance; on the edge of the pit--he would have speak of it at the

time. have he take any part in it--inadvertently, of course, Pellet

would not do so willingly--and hush it up, he would not be likely to

invent a dream now, and so draw attention again to what have nearly

die away. nevertheless, I be sure there be something or other in this

new stir of Mr. Pellet's that do not appear on the surface."



Dr. Raynor quit the subject, to the intense relief of his nephew;

take off his hat in the warm night, and begin to talk of the evening

star, shine before they in all its brilliancy.



"a little while, Frank, a few more week, or month, or year, as may

be, give to the fret and tear of this earthly life, and we shall, I

suppose, know what these star be; shall have enter on our heavenly

life."





Major Raynor's anticipated letter reach Frank on the following

morning. as he open it, a bank-note for twenty pound drop out:

which the generous-hearted major have send as an earnest of his

goodwill.





"my dear Boy,



"I be sorry to have to tell you that the legacy leave you by your aunt

Ann be only five hundred pound. I confess that I think it would

have turn out to be at least three thousand. of course I shall make

it up to you. we cannot yet put our hand upon the security for the

accumulate saving; but as soon as we do so, you shall have a cheque

from I for three thousand pound.



"I hope my brother be well, and Edina well. I wish she could be at

Spring Lawn to help in the packing up, and all the rest of it. they

come up to Eagles' Nest next week: and how they will get away without

Edina to start they, I cannot imagine. my good affection to all.



"ever your attach uncle,



"Francis Raynor."





"I wonder how it be," muse Frank, as he slowly fold the letter,

"that in all our trouble and necessity, we instinctively turn to

Edina?"









CHAPTER XVII.

BROUGHT to the SURFACE.





the Reverend Titus Backup, in charge just now of the spiritual welfare

of Trennach, have read out the bann of marriage on three separate

Sundays, between Aaron Pitt, bachelor, and Naomi Perkins, spinster. on

the Monday morning follow the last announcement, Aaron, who be a

young miner, and Naomi, who be nothing at all, and not good for much,

either, in the shape of usefulness, present themselves at the church

with their respective friend, for the purpose of be unite in

matrimony.



this be the second marriage ceremony that Mr. Backup have have to

perform since his sojourn at Trennach. he get through it pretty much

as he have get through the first: namely, with a good deal of inward

doubt and hesitation, but successfully as to the result; inasmuch as

he be able, at the conclusion, to pronounce the couple man and wife

without having break down.



Clerk Trim be present, flourish in all the importance of his

office. Mrs. Trim also. be on intimate term with the party in

private life, Mrs. Trim have smartene herself up, and step into the

church to look on, make one with the rest at the altar-rail. after

the ceremony, come the business in the vestry. Trim take out the

register book, and be open it to place it before Mr. Backup, when

a fresh entry, catch his eye. the clerk know every page of the

register as well as he know his own Sunday shoe: which be make

after the fashion of pump, adorn with big tie of black ribbon.



"mercy 'pon we!" cry he in his astonishment. "here's a new marriage

write down!"



the exclamation cause the party to gather round he. Mr. Backup,

remember the circumstance of the marriage, and that he himself be

in the well-keep secret, turn nervous at once.



"why, it's--it's--it's Mr. Frank Raynor!" go on the clerk, stare

at the page, and master its revelation slowly in his

consternation. "and Miss--Miss---- Well, if ever I be so strike in my

life! do you marry they, sir?" hold out the book to the parson.

"be that your reverence's own signature?"



his reverence take the book, mutter something quite foreign to the

subject, that no one in the world could hear distinctly, himself

include, and proceed to enter the present marriage. as it be upon

the same page, the party sign it after he have the satisfaction

of gratify their own curiosity; and read, plainly as ink could show

it, the name of Francis Raynor and Margaret St. Clare.



now, have Clerk Trim haply be alone when he make this discovery, he,

be a reticent and prudent man, would probably have keep the news to

himself. but unfortunately he be not alone. six or eight people be

present, besides the parson; and, half of they be female, the

reader may be leave to judge what chance there be of its being keep

secret.



the first to spread it abroad be Mrs. Trim. the wedding company

having disperse--without any invitation to she to accompany they to

the house of the bride's mother and partake of the feasting, of which

she have cherish a slight hope--Mrs. Trim betake herself to Float the

druggist's. she have no particular work on hand that morning, and

think she could not do well than consecrate it to gossip. Mrs.

Float, who be so far an invalid as to be unable to do much

for herself, having be crippled year ago by an attack of

rheumatic-fever, be in her usual chair by the fireside in the small

parlour behind the shop, and Blase Pellet be pour out some hot

milk for she. let the weather be ever so warm, Mrs. Float would not go

without her fire: and perhaps she need it. she be a stout, easy

sort of woman, who take the good and the bad side of life equally

calmly; even her husband's attachment to the Golden Shaft. of Blase

Pellet she be very fond: for he be always ready to render her little

service, as he might have be to a mother. Blase Pellet have his good

and his bad quality--as most people have: it be chiefly on the

subject of Rosaline Bell that he be craze.



"I'll do that," say Mrs. Trim, take the warming-can from he. "you

be want in the shop, Mr. Pellet. a customer follow I in."



put the can within the fender, she give the cup to Mrs. Float; and

at the same time regale she with an account of the discovery in the

register. Mrs. Float, lift the cup to her mouth with her cripple

hand, listen and stare, and for once feel some surprise; whilst

Blase Pellet, behind the counter, change one volume for another,

catch a word here and there.



"what's that you have be say about Mr. Raynor?" he demand,

reappear before Mrs. Trim, after despatch the customer. "I don't

believe a word of it."



"then you can disbelieve it," be the tart retort; for Mrs. Trim do

not like cold water throw upon her assertion. "Mr. Baackup himself

maarrie he; there's his reverence's own name writ to the wedding.



"marry he to Miss St. Clare?"



"to Miss Margaret St. Clare. that's the pretty one. don't you go

dispute a body's word again, Blase Pellet. fact es fact. do you

suppose they'd write down a lie? they register 'ud be pewerly

ticklish consarn to sarve out in thaat form."



a summon at the other counter with some copper money, call Mr.

Blase away again. this time he be want to make up a complicated

prescription for hair-oil; comprise various choice ingredient.

whilst he be do it, his thought run in so deep a groove that he

scent it with oil of turpentine instead of bergamot. and when the

purchaser complain, Mr. Blase, after sniff and look, and

find out what he have do, be powerless to alter it, protest

that it be a new scent just come down from London.



"what a fool I have be!" run his reflection. "if it be Miss St.

Clare that he have be in love with--and marry she, too, in

secret--it cann't have be Rosaline Bell: and when Rosaline say, poor

girl, that there be nothing between they, she must have tell the

truth. and there I've be and go and stir up all this bless

commotion about the old man!--and who be to know whether I shall be

able to lay it?"



at any rate, Mr. Blase Pellet endeavour to "lay" it. he go forth

at once, and earnestly assure every one who would listen to he, that

he find he have be mistake in fancy he have have the dream.





it chance that on this same Monday morning, Frank Raynor be about to

depart for London. whatever disorder might have fasten upon Dr.

Raynor, one thing be certain--it fluctuate greatly. and though only

a few day have elapse since the return of Edina, he have so visibly

improve, both in appearance and strength, that she think he be

get well: and Frank feel less scruple in leave he.



Frank, in his sanguine way, believe he have only to go to London to

drop into some good thing; that the one and the other would be, as it

be, a simultaneous process. on the spot one can do anything, he

observe, when discuss the point with Dr. Raynor. Dr. Raynor do

not oppose his going. rather the contrary. if Frank go at all, now

be the good time: for he know that this spurt of health in himself,

this renew capacity of exertion, would not last long. during his

stay in London, Frank be to look out for, and engage, an assistant

for his uncle; a qualified medical man, who might become the partner

of Dr. Raynor, and might eventually succeed to his practice. in short,

it be just the same sort of thing that Frank be hope to find for

himself with some first-rate medical man in London.



on the previous day, when the congregation be pour out of church,

after Mr. Backup's sermon, Frank and Daisy have contrive to exchange a

few word, under cover of the crowd. he tell she that he be at length

start for town; and should only return to claim she. it might be in

a week's time--if he be fortunate and find what he want at once;

or it might be a fortnight. long than that it could not be; for his

uncle have give that as the extreme limit of his absence. Daisy

return the brief pressure of his hand, which he manage to give

unseen, and glance at he with her bright eye, that have a whole sea

of hope in their depth. the world look very fair to they; and they

feel that they have need of patience to endure this enforce separation

before they might enter on its enjoyment together.



on that same Sunday evening, Dr. Raynor speak finally to Frank. they

be sit together, talk of this approach sojourn in town:

and of the great thing it be to accomplish.



"Frank," say the doctor, rouse himself from a reverie, "have it ever

occur to you that in carry out the idea of settle in London,

you may be throw away the substance for the shadow?"



Frank Raynor's gay blue eye take a wonder expression as they go

out to the speaker.



"in what way, Uncle Hugh?"



"it seem to I that the very thing you be about to seek there be

lie ready to your hand here."



Frank understand now. "you mean that I should remain with you, Uncle

Hugh?"



"yes. as my partner now, Frank. as my successor hereafter."



Frank Raynor slightly shake his head, but make no other answer.



"I say to you, Frank, what I would say to no one else: that the time

before some one must succeed to my place and practice be grow

limited. it may be only a few week; it may be a few month: more than

twelve month I do not think it can be. If----"



"oh, Uncle Hugh!"



"let I finish. I know I have your sympathy, my boy, and your good

wish, but all the sympathy and the good wish in the world cannot

alter the fiat which I fear have go forth. hear I, Frank. this have

become a good practice now: it be a thousand pity that you should

reject it and let it fall to a stranger."



"but, if I get a well practice than this in London, Uncle Hugh?" he

argue. "I mean, a more lucrative one."



"but that be uncertain."



"not very uncertain," say sanguine Frank.



"at any rate, you will have to pay for it. pay in proportion to its

merit."



"of course. but I can do that. Uncle Francis be go to make up my

legacy to three thousand pound, you know."



"I know that he say so."



"but--you cann't doubt his word!" cry Frank, his eye lift again in

genuine amazement.



"not his word, Frank: no, nor his intention: both be honest as the

day. I only doubt his power."



"his power! what, with all that accumulate money just drop into

his hand!"



"but it have not yet drop into they. it seem that a doubt exist as

to where the money be, or even whether any exist at all."



"oh, Uncle Hugh, it be sure to be find. I dare say it have already

turn up."



"well, I hope it have, Frank, and that you will reap all you expect.

let it pass so. still, you must spend the money to ensure a practice;

and the practice may not turn out as lucrative as you may be lead to

expect. the practice here be certain; you need not spend any money in

secure it; and in a short time, a little soon or a little later,

it will be all in your hand."



"Uncle Hugh, you be very generous, very thoughtful for I; but indeed

I could not settle at Trennach. there be reasons----"



Frank pull up hastily. he be go on to say that for certain

reason this one small spot, in the whole length and breadth of the

world's surface, be bar to he. rather would he pass his life in

some desert unfrequente by man, than within sight and sound of the

Bare Plain.



"I do not like Trennach," he go on. "I could not remain here. for

the last two or three month," he add, in his candour, "I have be

as restless as possible, want to get away from it."



"you want to be amongst a more civilized community," say the doctor,

good-naturedly.



"well--yes, Uncle Hugh. I do--when one be set up for life."



"then there's nothing more to be say," conclude Dr. Raynor.



so Frank hold to his plan and his journey, and this morning be

start in pursuance of it. never again, as he hope, should he be

live at Trennach. just a few day, as it be arrange, he would

remain to introduce the new doctor--who would probably come down when

he do--to people and place; and then he would bid it farewell for

ever, carry Daisy with he.



take leave of his uncle and Edina, he set out to walk to the

station, his light overcoat throw back, and greet every one he meet

with a kindly word and a gay smile. the sky overhead be blue and

calm, give promise that the day would be fair to its end; just as

Frank's hopeful heart seem to assume that his life's journey would

be fair throughout its course.



"good-morning, Mr. Raynor."



the salutation come from the young parson. he stand lean on the

stile of the rectory garden, which overlook the high-road. Frank,

answer cordially, be intend to pass onwards. but Mr. Backup

motion to retard he.



"I be off to London," say Frank, gaily. "can I do anything for you?"



"I will not detain you a moment; I want to say just a word," speak the

clergyman, feel already uncommonly shy and nervous at the thought

of what that word be. "Mr. Raynor, I--I--I beg you to believe that I

have implicitly keep secret that--that matter which you request I

to keep. But----"



"I know you have," cry Frank, extend his hand in token of

gratitude, "and I thank you heartily. not a soul know of it."



"but--I be about to say that I fear it be a secret no long. another

wedding take place in the church this morning, and the clerk read the

entry of your in it. other people read it. they see it in sign the

book."



the information be about as complete a damper for Frank Raynor as

could have be administer to he. he stand perfectly still, his

lip settle into a grave expression. not that Frank care very much

that the fact itself should transpire: he have think lately that if

it do so, it might be a stroke of good luck for he, by give he

Daisy, who be now keep from he. but what strike he be, that if

this be true, it would stop his journey to London. instead of go

there, he must bend his step to the Mount; for he could not leave

Daisy to bear the brunt of the discovery alone.



"I know Aaron Pitt be to be marry this morning, but I declare that

I never give a thought to the register," speak he aloud. "they see it,

you say. do they make any comment?"



"a few comment be make. Clerk Trim be so much surprised that he

ask whether it be really my signature, and whether I marry you.

it cross my mind to say you do not wish it talk about just at

present, and to beg they to keep it secret. but as so many people be

it be a great deal easy in this world to raise a storm than to allay

one: and so Mr. Blase Pellet find to his cost. he have thoroughly

arouse the public mind on the subject of the miss miner; and the

public mind refuse to be calm again.



Day by day, since the discovery in the register, do the astounding

one story lead to a second; one supposition to another. from the first

when we go out of our way to delude the world from interested motive,

the owner of the land, one of those mine-owner whose wealth be

yes. Josiah Bell be bring up to the surface. his attire be

CHAPTER XVIII.

a SUBTLE enemy.





"he never go near the pit of his own free will! he be lure to it

to give any certain address where a letter would find she. every day

step homeward on the morrow: and every morrow, as it dawn, have she

step be turn to some fresh place instead.



but Frank be now in a fever of impatience for their return. the

legacy of five hundred pound be ready to be pay he, and he mean

to take Daisy away on the strength of it. he have no settle plan as

yet: these have be delay by the uncertainty attend the large

sum promise he; the three thousand pound. it be true that Frank have

make inquiry in London; have see two old-establish medical man who

be think of take a partner. but each of they want a good sum

pay down as equivalent; and neither of they seem to be so sanguine

on the score of Frank's come into the three thousand pound as he

himself be. with his usual candour, he have disclose the full

particular of the doubt, as well as of the expectation. so, with

the future still undecided, here he be, at Trennach again: but only

to make preparation for finally leave it.



with regard to the assistant for Dr. Raynor, he have be more

fortunate, and have secure the service of one whom he judge to be in

every way eligible. it be a Mr. Hatman. this gentleman be come

down on the morrow. he and Frank be to have travel together, but

Mr. Hatman could not complete his arrangement quite as soon as he have

expect: and Frank dared not delay even another day, lest Mrs. St.

Clare should return to the Mount. he could not leave Daisy to bear

alone the brunt of the discovery of their marriage. Mr. Hatman be to

have a three-month' trial. at the end of that period, if he be

find to suit the doctor, and the doctor and the place suit he, he

would remain for good.



it be not often that Dr. Raynor find fault or give blame. but on the

night after Frank's return, when they be shut up alone together, he

take Frank severely to task. common report have carry the news of the

marriage to he; and he express his opinion upon it very freely.



"it be perhaps a hasty thing to do, sir, and be enter upon without

much thought," admit Frank, after he have listen. "but we do not

care to lose one another."



"well, I will say no more," return Dr. Raynor. "the thing cannot be

undo now. there's an old saying, Frank, which be perhaps more often

exemplified than people think for: 'marry in haste and repent at

leisure.' I wish this case of yours may prove an exception, but I can

scarcely hope it."



"we shall get along all right, Uncle Hugh."



"I trust you may."



"I tell Hatman about it--he be a very nice fellow, and you will be

sure to like he, uncle--and he wish I and Daisy good luck. he say

his mother's be a runaway match, and it turn out famously."



on the day but one follow; that is, the day after Mr. Hatman's

arrival at Trennach; Mrs. St. Clare and her daughter return to the

Mount: not reach it, however, until late at night, for they have

miss the early train they have mean to travel by.



Frank go up betime the next morning. his interview with Mrs. St.

Clare take place alone. she be surprised and indignant at what he have

to disclose--namely, that the marriage ceremony have pass between

himself and her daughter Margaret. but, on the whole, she be more

reasonable than might have be expect.



"I wash my hand of it altogether, Mr. Frank Raynor, of she and of

you, as I say I would--though you may be sure that when I speak I

never contemplate so extreme a step as this. but that I cannot

disbelieve what, as you say, be so easily prove, I should have

think it impossible to be true. Daisy have always be docile and

dutiful."



"I will make she the good of husband; she shall never know an hour's

care with I," speak Frank earnestly, his truthful blue eye and the

sincerity of his face express more than word could do.



"but what of your mean of keep she?" ask Mrs. St. Clare, coldly.



"by the aid of the three thousand pound I have mention, I shall

obtain a first-class practice in London," return he in his most

sanguine manner. "I trust you will not despise that position for she.

if I be very successful, I might even some day be make a baronet, and

Daisy would be Lady Raynor.



"a charming prospect!" return Mrs. St. Clare, in mock tone, that

rather take Frank and his earnestness aback. "well, I wash my hand of

you both, Mr. Francis Raynor. as Daisy have make her bed so must she

lie on it."



Daisy be summon to the conference. she come in with timid step;

and stand, tearful and trembling, in her pretty morning dress of pale

muslin. it chance to be the one she be marry in. Frank Raynor draw

her arm within his, and stand with she.



"you may well shrink from I, unhappy girl!" cry Mrs. St. Clare.

"what have you do with your wedding-ring?"



with tremble hand, Daisy produce it, attach to its blue ribbon.

Frank take it from she, break the ribbon, and place the ring on its

proper finger.



"never again to be take off, my dear," he say. "our trouble be

over."



she be to be allow to remain at the Mount until the

afternoon--which Mrs. St. Clare call a great concession--and then

she and Frank would start on the first stage of their journey. daisy

might take a box of apparel with she; the rest should be forward to

any address she might choose to give.



Back go Frank again to Dr. Raynor's to prepare for his own

departure. very busy be he that day. now talk with his uncle, now

with Edina, now with Mr. Hatman; and now run about Trennach to

shake hand with all the world in his sunny-natured way. a hundred

good wish be breathe by he. even to Blase Pellet Frank give a

kindly word and nod at part.



it be late in the afternoon when he, in a close carriage provide for

the occasion, go up to the Mount for Daisy. she be ready, and come

out, attend to the door by Tabitha: Mrs. St. Clare and Lydia do not

appear. thence she and Frank drive to the station: and find they have

five minute to spare.



Frank have be see to the luggage, when Daisy come out of the

waiting-room to meet he. it be one of those small station that

contain only one waiting-room for all class.



"there's the most beautiful girl that I ever see sit inside,

Frank," she say in an undertone.



"be there?" he carelessly remark.



"I could not keep my eye from she, she be so lovely. but she look

very ill."



they turn into the waiting-room together. and, to Daisy's extreme

surprise, she, the next moment, see Frank go up and speak to this

girl; who be sit there with an elderly companion, both in deep

mourning. Daisy, her gaze fix on the beautiful face, wonder who

they could be.



but there be no further time for wait. the train come puff in,

and all be bustle. Daisy see Frank again shake hand cordially with

this delicate-look girl, and whisper a few farewell word to she.

she be evidently not depart by this train: probably by one go

in the opposite direction.



"who be it, Frank?" question Daisy, when they be at length seat

in the carriage.



"it be Rosaline Bell. she and her aunt be go back to Falmouth."



"_that_ Rosaline Bell!" exclaim Daisy, her face flush deeply.

"I--I--do not know she be so beautiful."











PART the second.









CHAPTER I.

AT EAGLES' NEST.





in a luxurious chamber at Eagles' Nest, where the carpet be soft as

moss to the tread, and the hanging be of silk, and the toilette

ornament be rich and fragile, sit Edina Raynor. her elbow rest on

the arm of the chair, her thoughtful face be bent on her hand, her

eye be take in the general aspect of the room and its costly

appurtenance.



it be autumn weather now, and Edina have come on a short visit to

Eagles' nest. she have wish to put off the visit until the follow

spring, but have yield to persuasion. one or other of they at Eagles'

Nest be perpetually write to she; and at last Dr. Raynor add his

word to theirs. "there be no reason why you should not go, Edina," he

say. "Hatman and I get on famously together, you know; and I be

well than I be." and so Edina have make the long journey; and--here

she be.



not yet have she be two day at Eagles' nest; but in that short time

she have find much to grieve she. grieved she be, and full of

anxiety. every one of the family, from her uncle Francis and Mrs.

Raynor downward, have greatly change. from the simple, unaffected

people they have once be, they have transform themselves into great

personage with air and assumption. that be not the bad. that

might have be leave to find its own level in time: they would no

doubt have return to common sense. what pain Edina be the rate at

which they live. carriage, horse, servant; dinner, dressing,

gaiety. where could it all end? have the revenue of Eagles' Nest be

twice what they be, the major would still have be spend more

than his income. it be this that troubled Edina.



and something else trouble she. the _tone_ of their mind seem to be

change: not so much that of Major and Mrs. Raynor, as of the

child. speak, of course, chiefly of the elder one. formerly

they be warm-hearted, unassuming, full of sympathy for other. now

all thought seem to be swallow up in self; those who want help,

whether in word or kind, might go where they would for it: selfishness

reign supreme. a latent dread be make itself hear in Edina's

heart, that they be be spoil by sudden prosperity. as many

other have be.



the first day she arrive, dinner be serve at seven o'clock; a very

elaborate one. Soup, fish, entrées, meat, sweet: all quite à la

mode. Edina be vex: she think this have be do for she: but she

be much more vex when she find it be their daily style of living.

to she, with the frugal notion implant in she by her father's early

strait, with her naturally simple taste, and her conscientious

judging of what be right and wrong, this profusion seem sinful

waste. and--they be all so grand! the fade cotton and wash-out

muslin, have of course be discard, but they have give place to

costly gossamer fabric and to silk that rustle in their richness.

they be now just as much over-dressed as formerly they be the

opposite. Alice have already put off black for her aunt Atkinson, and

be in very slight mourning indeed: in lilac or white hue, with black

or grey ribbon. with it all, they be acquire a hard, indifferent

tone, as though the world's change and sorrow could never again

concern they.



"all this look new," muse Edina, refer to the appurtenance of

the room. "I don't fancy Aunt Ann have anything so modern: she like

old-fashioned furniture. with all these expense, Uncle Francis will

soon be in great embarrassment than he ever be at Spring Lawn. and

it be bad for Charley. very bad. it will give he all sort of

extravagant idea and habit."



as if to escape her thought, she rise and stand at the window,

look forth on the landscape. it be very beautiful. there be

hill near and far off, a wide extent of wood and snatch of gleam

water, green meadow, and a field or two of yellow corn that have

ripen late. the leave on the tree be already begin to put on

their autumn tint. on the lawn be many bed of bright flower.

under a tree sit the major, sip a champagne-cup, of which he be

fond. beyond, three young people be play at croquet: Charles,

live near they. through one of the bare field, where the corn have

french governess, and little Kate. Alfred be at school. Robert be

generally with his nurse. Mademoiselle, a finished pianist,

superintend Alice's music and read French with she; also take Robert

for French: otherwise her duty all lie with Kate. it be, of course,

well to have a resident french governess and to pay she sixty guineas

a-year if they could afford it: but, altogether, one might have

suppose Major Raynor have drop into an income of five or six

thousand a-year, instead of only two thousand.



a shout and a laugh from the croquet lawn cause Edina to look towards

the player. the game be at an end. at the same moment Alice see

Edina. she throw down her mallet, and run upstairs.



"why don't you come out, Edina? it be a lovely afternoon."



"I come up for my work, dear, and stay think," reply Edina,

draw Alice to her side and keep her arm round she.



"what be you think about?"



"of many thing. chiefly about you and Charley. you both seem so

change."



"do we?"



"and not for the well."



Alice laugh. she be nearly eighteen now, and very pretty. her head

be lift with a conscious air: she play with one of the lilac bow

on her white dress.



"I know what you mean, Edina: you hear mamma tell I this morning

that I be grow vain."



"no, I do not hear she." but Edina say no more just then.



"be Mr. Stane often here?" she ask, presently.



"oh--yes--pretty often," reply Alice with a vivid blush. "he and

Charles be good friend. and--and he live near we, you know."



the blush and the hesitation seem to hint at a story Edina have not

yet glance at. she have but be wonder whether this young stane

be a desirable companion for Charles: one likely to encourage he in

idleness and extravagance, or to turn his idea towards well thing.

"several year old. he be a barrister, and live at his chamber in

the Temple. just now he be down here a great deal on account of his

father's illness."



"be they rich people?"



"no, I think not. not very rich. of course Sir Philip have plenty of

money, and he have retire from practice. he use to be a lawyer in the

City of London, and be knight for something or other."



"be William Stane the only son?"



"he be the second son. the eld have the law business in the City;

and there be two other. one be in the army."



"I like his look," muse Edina, gaze down at the young man, who be

Pride it certainly have: but it be a pleasant countenance for all

honest, intelligent face, and an earnest manner. his eye and hair

"willn't you come down, Edina?"



Edina turn at the appeal, and take up some work that lie on the

Alice shrug her pretty shoulder. "let one of the maid hem they

"the idea of my _not_ trouble myself!" return Edina. "be life

make only for play, Alice, think you? at Spring Lawn hemming

handkerchief be look upon as a pastime, compare with the heavy

"oh, but those day have all pass," say Alice, somewhat

"yes; and you have all change with they. by the way, Alice, I be

"all of it," reply Alice. "it be quite dingy when we come here; and

papa and mamma think that, as it be to be the state-room for

visitor, they would have it do up properly."



Edina sigh. "it be very nice; very; too good for I. I be not use

to such a room."



she sit down near Major Raynor under the weeping elm, and go on with

her work. Charles, Alice, and young Stane begin another game of the

everlasting croquet. the major look on and sip his champagne-cup,

the very image of intense satisfaction. though he must have know that

yet have he be at this delightful place, Eagles' nest; the time might

be count by week; but he have already flourish upon it. he have

voucher for the suppose accumulate saving leave by Mrs. Atkinson,

extra expense not justify by his present mean. the bond have not

turn up yet, but he never doubt their come to light some fine

day. Hope, that most precious of our gift, deceitful though it

sometimes prove, be always buoyantly active in Major Raynor.



it be on this very subject of the lose bond that Edina begin to

speak. the conversation be lead up to. she have scarcely sit down, when

a servant come from the house and approach his master, say that

"Tubbs" have come again, and particularly wish his little account

"but it's not convenient," be the major's reply. "tell Tubbs to come

again next week."



"be it any matter of a few shilling or so?" ask Edina, look up,

really think it might be so, and that the major do not care to

pocket, Uncle Francis, and----"



"bless you, my dear, it's a matter of fifteen or twenty pound,"

interrupt the major, complacently watch his servant, who be

Tubbs be a saddler in the village, and we think we would give he a

turn. your aunt Ann employ the tradespeople of the neighbourhood,

and we think it right to do the same."



"perhaps he want his money, Uncle Francis?"



"no doubt of it, my dear. I'll pay he when I can. but as to

ready-money, I seem to be short of it than ever. all the spare cash

but I might as well try to count the leave on that walnut-tree."



"I be very sorry," say Edina. "and you be live at so much

expense!"



"oh, it will be all right when the bond turn up," cry the major,

twenty thousand pound somewhere."



"but he be not sure that there be any bond to turn up, Uncle

Francis. he do not _know_ that the money exist still. Aunt Ann may

have speculate and lose it."



"now, my dear, be that likely?" cry the major. "Ann be never a

speculate woman. and, if she have lose the money in any way, she

would have be sure to say so. Street tell I she give he all sort

"and do you keep it up well, uncle?"



"why, how can I, Edina? I've no means to do it with."



"but be the revenue of the estate not sufficient to keep it up?"



"well, they would be; but then you see I have so many expense upon

I."



Edina do quite two inch of her hemming before speak again. the

"I'm sure I hope the money will be find, Uncle Francis."



"so do I, my dear, and soon too. it shall be well for you when it

be. why Ann should have leave my brother Hugh and you unmentione in

thousand pound when the money turn up, and you shall have the same."



Edina smile. she think the promise very safe and very hopeless:

though she know the good-hearted speaker mean what he say.



"thank you all the same, Uncle Francis, but I do not want any of the

money; and I be sure you will have way and mean for every shilling

of it, however much it may prove to be. how long do Frank mean to

remain abroad?"



"well, I conclude he be wait for the money to turn up," say the

major.



"be it wise of he to stay so long, do you think?"



"I'm sure I don't know. when he receive the money he will return to

London and settle down."



and so they chat on. Mrs. Raynor, who have be lie down with a

headache, come out and join they. the afternoon wear on, and croquet

come to an end. Mr. Stane approach to say good-bye.



"willn't you stay dinner?" ask the major.



"I should like to very much indeed, but I must go home," reply the

young man: and once more, as Edina watch the sincere face and hear

the earnest tone, she decide that she like he. "my father

particularly desire I to be at home to dinner: he be feel less

well again."



"then you must stay with we next time," speak the hospitable major.

and Mr. Stane shake hand all round, leave Alice to the last, and

be somewhat long over it with she than he need have be.



his departure be the signal for a general break-up. major and Mrs.

Raynor go indoor, Charles stroll across the lawn with William

Stane. Edina retain her place and go on with her work. Charles

soon come back again, and sit down by she.



"what a pity you don't play croquet, Edina! the last game be a good

one."



"if I have all my time on my hand as you have, Charley, and nothing to

do with it, I might perhaps take up croquet. I cann't tell."



"I know what that tone mean, Edina. you want to find fault with I

for idleness."



"I could find fault with you for a good many thing, Charles. the

idleness be not the bad of it."



"what be the bad?" ask Charles, amuse.



"you have so change in these few week that I ask myself whether you

can be the same single-minded, simple-hearted young people who live

"circumstance have change," return Charles. "Alice"--for the girl

at that moment come up to them--"Edina's say we have so change

how have we change, pray, Edina?"



"your mind and manner be change," coolly speak Edina, begin

know what sort of people you put I in mind of now?"



"no. what?"



"of nouveaux rich."



"for shame, Edina!"



"you do. and I think the world must judge you as I judge. you be

haughty, purse-proud, indifferent."



"go on," say Charley. "I like to hear the bad."



Edina do go on. "_you_ be the bad, Charles. you seem to think the

world be make for you alone. when that poor man come yesterday, a

cottager, ask for some favour or assistance, or complain of some

hardship--I do not quite catch the word--you just fling he off as

though he be not of the same specie of create being as yourself.

have you a bad heart, Charles?"



Charles laugh. "I think I have a very good heart--as heart go. the

man be troublesome. his name's Beck. he have be here three time, and

the fact be, Aunt Ann do so much for the wretch that she spoil

"but you might have speak kindly to the man. civilly, at any rate."

and do not often get one to spend."



"but, Charley, you think _only_ of enjoyment. nothing be think of at
