"well, I shall have enough work to do by-and-by, Edina. I go to Oxford

after the long vacation."



"and you go without any preparation for it," say Edina.



"Preparation! why, I be well up in classic," cry Charley, stare

at Edina.



"I be not think of classic. you have have no experience, Charles;

you be like a child in the way of the world."



"I tell you, Edina, I be a very fair scholar. what else do you want at

Oxford? you don't want experience there."



"well for you, Charley, if it shall prove so," be Edina's answer, as

she fold her work to go indoor; for the evening be draw on, and

the air feel chilly. change they all be, more than she could

express. they see with one set of eye, she with another.



"what a tiresome thing Edina be get!" exclaim Alice to her

brother, as Edina disappear.



"a regular croaker."



"a confirm old maid."



the only one who could not be say to have much change, be Mrs.

Raynor. she be gentle, meek, simple-mannered as ever: but even she

be draw into the vortex of visit and gaiety, of show and expense,

of parade and ceremony that have set in. she seem to have no leisure

to give to anything else. this day be the only quiet day Eagles' Nest

have during Edina's visit. Mrs. Raynor, with she yield will, could

not help herself altogether. but Edina be grieve to see that she

neglect the religious training of her young child. even the

hearing of their evening prayer be give over to the governess.



"Mademoiselle Delrue be a protestant," say Mrs. Raynor; when, on this

same evening, Edina venture to speak a word upon the subject, as Kate

and Robert say good-night and leave the drawing-room.



"I know she be," say Edina. "but none but a mother should, in these

vital matter, train her child. you always use to do it, Mary."



"if you only know how fully my time and thought be occupy!"

return Mrs. Raynor, in a tone of great deprecation. "we live in a

whirl here: and it be rather too much for I. and, to tell you the

truth, Edina, I sometimes wonder whether the old life, with all its

straiten mean, be not the happy; whether we have in all respect

improve matter, in come to Eagles' Nest."









CHAPTER II.

APPREHENSIONS.





the fine old house, Eagles' Nest, lie bury in snow. it be

Christmas-tide, and Christmas weather. all the Raynor family have

assemble within its wall: with the exception of Dr. Raynor and his

daughter Edina. Charles have come home from keep his first term at

Oxford; Alfred from school; Frank Raynor and his wife have return

from their sojourn abroad.



all these past month, during which we have lose sight of they, Frank

and Daisy have be on the continent. almost immediately after their

departure from Trennach, Frank, through his medical friend, Crisp, be

introduce to a lady who be go to Switzerland with her only son; a

sickly lad of fifteen, in whom the doctor at home have hardly be

able to keep life. this lady, Mrs. Berkeley, propose to Frank to

travel with they as medical attendant on her son, and she have not the

least objection to Frank's wife be of the party. so preliminary

be settle, and they start. Frank consider it a most opportune

chance to have fall to he while wait for the missing money to

turn up.



but the engagement do not last long. hardly have they settle in

Switzerland when the lad die, and Mrs. Berkeley return to England.

Frank stay on where he be. the place and the sojourn be alike

pleasant; and, as he remark to his wife, who know but he might pick

up a practice there, amongst the many english resident of the town,

or those who flock to it as bird of passage? Daisy be just as

delight to remain as he: they have fund in hand, and could afford to

throw care to the wind. even have care declare itself: which it do

not. the young be sanguine, rarely gift with much forethought.

Frank and his wife especially lack it. a few odd and end of

practice do drop into Frank, just a small case or so, at long

interval: and they remain stationary for some time in perfect

complacency. but when Christmas approach, and Frank find that his

five hundred pound would not hold out for ever, and that the idea of

a practice in the swiss town be a mere castle in the air, he take his

wife home again. by invitation, they go at once to Eagles' Nest.



Christmas-Day pass merrily, and some of the day immediately

succeed to it. on New-Year's day they be invite to an

entertainment at Sir Philip Stane's; Major and Mrs. Raynor, Charles

and Alice; a later invitation having come in for Frank and his wife.

William Stane be a frequent visitor at Eagles' Nest whenever he be

sojourn at his father's; and, though he have not yet speak, few

could doubt that the chief object to draw he there be Alice Raynor.



yes. Sunshine and merry-making, profusion and reckless expenditure

reign within the door of Eagles' Nest; but little except poverty,

distress and dissatisfaction exist beyond its gate. Mrs. Atkinson

have ever be liberal in her care of the estate; the land have be

enrich and thoroughly well kept; the small tenant and labourer

be care for. one thing she have not do so thoroughly as she might:

and that be, improve the dwelling of the labourer. repair she

have make from time to time; but the place be really beyond repair.

each tenement want one of two thing: to be thoroughly renew and

to have an additional sleeping-room add; or else to be entirely

rebuild. during the last year of Mrs. Atkinson's life, she seem to

awaken suddenly to the necessity of do something. perhaps with the

approach of death--which will often open our eye to many thing they

remain closed to before--she see the supineness she have be guilty

of. Street the lawyer be hastily summon to Eagles' nest: he be

order to procure plan and estimate for new dwelling. a long row

of cottage, some thirty in number, be hastily begin. whilst the

builder be commence their work, Mrs. Atkinson die. with nearly

her last breath she charge Mr. Street to see that the new house be

complete, and that the old one be also repair and make healthy.



Mr. Street could only hand over the charge to the inheritor of the

estate, Major Raynor. the reader may remember that the major speak of

it to Edina. the lawyer could not do more than that, or carry out Mrs.

Atkinson's wish in any other way. and the major do nothing. his

will might have be good enough to carry out the change, but he have

not the mean. so much money be require for his own want and those

of his family, that he have none to spare for other people. the

ready-money he come into have chiefly go in pay back-debt: until

these debt stare he in the face in black and white, he have not

think that he owe a tithe of they. it be a very common experience.

so the new dwelling be summarily stop, and remain as they

be--so many skeleton: and the tumbledown cottage, want space,

drainage, whitewash, and everything else that could render they decent

and healthy, grow bad day by day, and become an eyesore to

spectator and the talk of the neighbourhood.



not only do _they_ suffer from the major's want of money and

foresight; many other necessity be cry out in like manner:

these be only give as a speciman. above all, he be do no good to

the land, spend nothing to enrich it, and spare necessary and

ordinary labour. perhaps have Major Raynor understand the cultivation

and requirement of land, he might have make an effort to improve his

own: as it be, it deteriorate day by day.



this state of thing have cause a certain antagonism to set in between

Eagles' Nest and its dependent. the labourer and their family

grumble; the major, conscious of the state of affair, and feel

some slight shame in consequence, but know at the same time that he

be powerless to remedy it, shun they. when complainer come to the

house he would very rarely see they. a warm-hearted man, he could not

bear to hear they. Mrs. Raynor and the elder child, understand

matter very imperfectly, naturally espouse the major's cause, and

look upon the small tenant as a barbarous, insubordinate set of

wretch, next door to insurgent. when the poor wife or child

fall ill, no succour be send to they from Eagles' Nest. with this

estrangement reigning, Mrs. Raynor do not attempt to help: not from

coldness of heart, but that she consider they do not deserve help,

and, moreover, think it would be fling back on she if she offer

it.



_there_ be where the shoe pinch the poor. the insufficient

dwelling they be use to; though indeed with every winter and every

summer they grow bad than ever; but they be not accustomed to

utter, contemptuous neglect, as they look upon it, in time of need.

Mrs. Atkinson have always be a generous mistress: when sickness or

sorrow or distress in time of little work set in, her hand and purse

be ever open. coal in severe weather, Christmas cheer, warm

garment for the scantily clad, broth for the sick; she have furnish

all: and it be the entire withdrawal of this aid that be so much

feel now. the winter be unusually severe: it frequently be so after a

very hot summer; labour be scarce, food be dear: and a great deal of

illness prevail. so that you perceive all thing be not so

flourish in and about Eagle's Nest as they might have be, and

Major Raynor's bed be not entirely one of rose-leave.



but, unpleasant thing that be out of sight, be, it be say, for the

most part out of mind--Mr. Blase Pellet tell we so much a chapter or

two ago--and the discomfort out-of-door do not disturb the geniality

within. at Eagles' Nest, the day float on in a round of enjoyment;

they seem to be one continuous course of pleasure that would never

end. Daisy Raynor have never be so happy in all her life: eagle'

Nest, she say, be perfection.



the music and wax-light, the flower and evergreen render the

room at Sir Philip Stane's a scene of enchantment. at least it seem

so to Alice Raynor as she enter upon it. William Stane stand near

the door, and catch her hand as she and Charles be follow their

father and mother.



"the first dance be mine, remember, Alice," he whisper. and she

pretty cheek flush and a half-conscious smile part her lip, as

she pass on to Lady Stane.



Lady Stane, a stout and kindly woman in emerald green, receive she

kindly. she suspect that this young lady might some day become her

daughter-in-law, and she look at she more critically than she have

ever look before. Alice could bear the inspection to-night. her new

white dress be beautiful; her face be charming, her manner modest

and graceful. "the most lady-like girl in the room," mentally decide

Lady Stane, "and no doubt will have a fair fortune. William might do

bad."



William Stane think he might do very much bad. without doubt he

be truly attach to Alice. not perhaps in the wild and ardent manner

that some lover own to: all nature be not capable of that: but he

do love she, her only, and he hope that when he marry it be she

who would be his wife. he be not ready to marry at present. he be

progress in his profession, but with the proverbial slowness that

be say to attend the advancement of barrister: and he do not wish

to speak just yet. meanwhile he be quite content to make love

tacitly; and he feel sure that his intention be understand.



his eld brother be not present this evening, and it fall to William

to take his place, and dispense his favour pretty equally amongst the

guest. but every moment that he could snatch for Alice, be give to

she; in every dance that he could possibly spare she, she be his

partner.



"have you enjoy the evening, Alice?" he ask in a whisper, as he

be take she to the carriage at three o'clock in the morning.



"I never enjoy an evening half so much," be the shyly-breathe

answer. and Mr. William Stane take possession of her hand as she

speak, and keep it to the last.



if this light-hearted carelessness never come to an end! if freedom

from trouble could only last for ever! pleasure first, say some wise

old saw, pain afterwards. with the dawn come the pain to Eagles' Nest.



amongst the letter deliver to Major Raynor--who, for a wonder, have

rise betime that morning, and be turn place over in his study

in search of the lose bond--be one from Oxford. it enclose a very

heavy bill for wine supply to his son Charles: heavy, consider

Mr. Charles's year and the duration of his one sojourn at the

University. the major stare at it, with his spectacle, and without

his spectacle; he look at the heading, he gaze at the foot; and

finally when he have master it he go into a passion, and order

Charles before he. so peremptory be the summon, that Charles

appear in haste, half dress. his outburst, when he find out what

the matter be, quite equal his father's.



"I'm sure I think you must be on fire down here, sir," say he.

"what confound sneak they be, to apply to you! I cann't understand

their do it."



"sneak be shoot!" cry the wrathful major. "do you owe all this, or

don't you? that's the question."



"why, the letter be address to _I!_" exclaim Charles, who have

be examine the envelope. "I must say, sir, you might allow I to

open my own letter."



but the major be guiltless of any want of faith. the mistake be the

butler's. he have inadvertently place the letter amongst his master's

letter, and the major open it without glance at the address.



"what do it signify, do you suppose, whether I open it or you?"

demand the major. "not that I do it intentionally. I should have to

know of it: _you_ cann't pay this."



"they can wait," say Charles.



"wait! do you mean to confess that you have have all this wine?"

retort the major, irascible for once. "why, you must be grow

into--into what I don't care to name!"



"you cann't suppose that I drink it, sir. the other undergrad give

wine party, and I have to do the same. they drink the wine; I

don't."



"that be, you drink it amongst you," roar the major; "and a nice

disreputable lot you must all be. I understand that young man go to

college to study; not to drink, and run up bill. what else do you

owe? be this all?"



Charles hesitate in answering. an untruth he would not tell. the

major see what the hesitation mean, and it alarm he. when we

become frightened our wrath cool down. the major drop into a

chair, and lose his fierceness and his voice together.



"Charley," say he in very subdued tone, "I have not the money to pay

with. you know I haven't. if it's much, it will ruin I."



"but it be not much, father," return Charles, his own anger disarm

and contrition take its place. "there may be one or two more

trifling bill; nothing to speak of."



"what on earth make you run they up?"



"I'm sure I don't know; and I be very sorry for it," say Charles.

"these thing accumulate in the most extraordinary manner. when you

fancy that you owe only a few shilling at some place or another, it

turn out to be pound. you have no idea what it be, father!"



"have I not!" return the major, significantly. "it be because I have

rather too much idea of the insidious way in which debt creep upon

one, that I should like to see you keep out of its toil. Charley, my

boy, I have be stave off liability all my life, and haven't

worry myself in do it; but it be begin to tell upon I now.

my constitution's change. I suppose I must be grow fidgety."



"well, don't let this worry you, father. it's not so very much."



"much or little, it must be pay. I don't want my son to get into bad

odour at college; or have 'debtor' attach to his name. you be young

for that, Mr. Charles."



Charles remain silent. the major be evidently in blissful ignorance

of the latitude of opinion current amongst Oxonians.



"go back and dress yourself, Charles; and get your breakfast over; and

then, just sit down and make out a list of what it be you owe, and

I'll see what can be do."



now in the course of this same morning it chance that Frank Raynor

take occasion to speak to his uncle about money matter, as connect

with his own prospect, which he have not previously enter upon

during his present stay. the major be pace his study in a gloomy

mood when Frank enter.



"you look tired, Uncle Francis. just as though you have be dance

all night."



"I leave that to you young man," return the major, draw his

easy-chair to the fire. "as to be tired, Frank, I be so; though I

have not dance."



"tired of what, uncle?"



"of everything, I think. sit down, lad."



"I want to speak to you, Uncle Francis, concern myself and my

plan," say Frank, take a seat near the fire. "it be time I settle

down to something."



"be it?" be the answer. the major's thought be elsewhere.



"why, yes; don't you think it be, sir? the question be, what be it to

be? with regard to the bond for that miss money, uncle? they have

not turn up, I conclude?"



"they have not turn up, my boy, or the money either. if they have,

you'd have be the first to hear of it. I have be search for

they this very morning."



"what be your true opinion about the money, Uncle Francis?" resume

Frank, after a pause. "will it ever be find?"



"yes, Frank, I think it will. I feel assured that the money be lie

somewhere--and that it will come to the surface soon or later. I

should be sorry to think otherwise; for, goodness know, I need it

badly enough."



a piece of blaze wood fall off the grate. Frank catch the tong,

and put it up again.



"and I wish it could be find for your sake, also, Frank. you want

your share of it, you know."



"why, you see, Uncle Francis, without money I don't know what to be

at. if I be single, I'd engage myself out as assistant to-morrow;

but for my wife's sake I wish to take a well position than that."



"naturally you do, Frank, and so you ought."



"it would be easy enough if I have the money in hand; or if I could

with any certainty say when I should have it."



"it's sure to come," say the major. "quite sure."



"well, I hope so. the difficulty be--when?"



"you must wait a bit long, my boy. it may turn up any day. to-night,

even: to-morrow morning. never a day pass but I go ferret into

some corner or other of the old house, think I may put my hand upon

the paper. they be lie in it somewhere, I know, overlook."



"but I don't see my way clear to wait. not to wait long. we must have

a roof over our head, and mean to keep it up----"



"why, you have a roof over your head," interrupt the major. "cann't

you stay here?"



"I should not like to stay too long," avow Frank in his candour. "it

would be abuse your hospitality."



"abuse a fiddlestick!" cry the major, stare at Frank. "what's

come to you? be the house not large enough?--and plenty to eat in it?

I'm sure you may stay here for ever; and the long you stay the more

welcome you'll be. we like to have you."



"thank you greatly, Uncle Francis."



"Daisy do not want to go away; she's as happy as the day's long,"

continue the major. "just make yourself comfortable here, Frank, my

boy, until the money turn up and I can hand you over some of it."



"thank you again, uncle," say Frank, accept the hospitality in the

free-hearted spirit that it be offer. "for a little while at any

rate we will stay with you; but I hope before long to be do

something and to get into a home of my own. I can run up to town once

or twice a week and be look out."



"of course you can."



"have you be a rich man, Uncle Francis, I would have ask you to

lend I a thousand pound, or so, to set I up until the nest-egg be

find; but I know you have not get it to lend."



"got it to lend!" echo the major in dismayed astonishment. "why,

Frank, my boy, I want to borrow such a sum myself. I wish to my heart

I know where to pick it up. here's Charles must have money now: have

come home from Oxford with a pack of debt at his back!"



"Charles have!" exclaim Frank in surprise.



"and would like to make I believe that all the rest of the young

fellow there run up the same bill! every man Jack of 'em! no, no,

Master Charley: you don't get I to take _that_ in. young man can be

steady at college as well as at home if they choose to be. Charley's

just one that's lead any way. he be young, you see, Frank: and he be

throw there, I expect, amongst a few rich blade to whom money be no

object, and must need do as they do. the result be, he have make I

don't know what liability, and I must pay they. oh, it's all worry

and bother together!"



not intentionally, but by chance, Frank, on quit his uncle, come

upon Charles. look into a room in search of his wife, there sit

Charley at a table, pen, ink and paper before he, set down his

debt, as far as he could judge of and recollect they. Frank go in

and close the door.



Charles let off a little of his superfluous discomfort in abuse of the

people who have presume to trouble he with the wine bill. Frank sit

down, and draw the paper towards he.



"I have no idea it could be as much as that, Frank," be the rueful

avowal. "and I wish with all my heart their wine party and their

fast living have be at the bottom of the sea!"



"_be_ it as much, Charley?"



"to tell the truth, I be afraid it's more," say Charles, with

candour. "I've only make a guess at the other amount, and I know I

have not put down too much. that tailor be an awful man for stick

it on: as all the rest of the crew be, for the matter of that. I be

try to recollect how many time I've have horse and trap and

thing; and I cann't."



"do Uncle Francis know it come to all this?"



"no. and I don't care to let he know. thing seem to worry he so

much now. I do wish that lose money could be find!"



"just what your father and I have be wish," cry Frank. "look

here, Charley. I have a little leave out of my five hundred pound. you

shall have half of it: just between ourselves, you know: and then the

sum my uncle must find will not look so formidable to he. Nay, no

thank, lad: would you not all do as much for I--and more? and we be

go to stay on here for a time--and that will save expense."



it be simply impossible for Frank Raynor to see a difficulty of this

kind, or indeed of any kind, and not help to relieve it if he have help

in his power. that he would himself very speedily require the money he

be now give away, be only too probable: but he be content to

forget that in Charley's need.



the one individual person in all the house that Charles would have

keep from the knowledge of his folly--and in his repentance he look

upon it as folly most extreme--be his mother. he love she dearly;

and he have the grace to be ashamed, for her sake, of what he have do,

and to hope that she would never know it. a most fallacious hope, as

he be soon to find, for Major Raynor have take the news up to she

with open mouth.



she be sit on the low sofa in her dressing-room that evening at

dusk, when Charles go in. the firelight play on her face, show

its look of utter weariness, and the trace of tear.



"what's the matter, mother?" he ask, sit down beside she and

take her hand. "be you ill?"



"not ill, Charley," she answer. "only tired and--and out of sort."



"what have tire you? last night, I suppose. but you have be rest

all day."



"not last night particularly. so much fast living do not suit I."



"fast living!" exclaim Charles in wonder accent. "be it the

gravies?--or the plum-pudding?"



Mrs. Raynor could not forbear a smile. "I be not think of the

table, Charles; the gravy and the pudding; but of our fast,

artificial existence. we seem to have no rest at all. it be always

excitement; nothing but excitement. we go out last night; we go out

to dinner to-morrow night; people come here the next night. every day

that we be at home there be something; if it's not luncheon and

afternoon-tea, it's dinner; and if it's not dinner, it's supper. I

have to think of it all; the entertainment and the dress, and

everything; and to go out when you go; and--and I feel it be get

rather too much for I."



"then lie up, mother, for a few day," advise Charles,

affectionately. "keep by your own fire, and turn thing over to Alice

and the servant. you will soon be all right again."



Mrs. Raynor do not answer. she hold Charles's hand in her own, and

be look steadfastly at the flickering blaze. a silence ensue.

Charles lose himself in a train of thought.



"what about this trouble of your, Charley?"



it be a very unpleasant awakening for he. of all thing, this be

what he have want to keep from she. his ingenuous face--and it be an

ingenuous face in spite of the wine bill--flush deeply with

annoyance.



"it's what you need not have hear about, mother. I come away from

Oxford without pay a few pound I owe there; that's all. there need

be no fuss about it."



"I hear of wine bill, and horse, and thing of that kind. oh, my

dear, _need_ you have enter into that fast sort of life?"



"other enter into it," say Charley.



"it be not so much the cost that trouble I," add Mrs. Raynor, in

love tone; "that can be meet somehow. it is----" she stop as if

want word.



"it be what, mother?"



"Charley, my dear, what I think of be this--that you may be fall

into the world's evil way. it be so easy to do it; you young lad be

so inexperienced and confide; you think all be fair that look fair;

that no poison lurk in what have a specious surface. and oh, my boy,

you know that there be a world after this world; and if you be to

fall too deeply into the way of _this_, to get to love it, to be

unable to do without it, you might never gain the other. some young

lad that have fall away from God have not care to find he again;

never have find he.



"there have be no harm," say Charley. "and I assure you I don't

often miss chapel."



"Charley, dear, there's a verse in Ecclesiastes that I often think

of," she resume in low sweet tone. "all mother think of it, I

fancy, when their son begin to go out in the world."



"in Ecclesiastes?" repeat Charley.



"the verse that Edina illuminate for we once, when she be stay at

Spring Lawn. it be she do it, I think, that help to impress it

so much on my memory."



"I remember it, mother mine." and the word run through Charley's

thought as he speak.



"Rejoice, o young man, in thy youth and let thy heart cheer thee in

the day of thy youth, and walk in the way of thine heart, and in the

sight of thine eye: but know thou, that for all these thing God will

bring thee into judgment."









CHAPTER III.

a tiger.





the late spring flower be bloom; the air be soft and balmy.

Easter be rather late; in fact, April be pass; and when Easter

come at that period, it generally bring sunshine with it.



eagle' Nest, amidst other favour spot, seem to be as bright as

the day be long. once more Major Raynor have all his child about

he; also Frank and Daisy. for anything that could be see on the

surface, merry heart reign; none of they seem to have a care in

the world.



Frank decidedly have not. sanguine and light-hearted, he be content as

ever to let the future take care of itself. yield to persuasion, he

still stay on at Eagles' nest. his wife look forward to be lay

up in the course of a month or two: and where, ask the major, could

she be well attend to than at Eagles' Nest? Daisy, of course,

wish to remain; she should feel safe, she say, in the care of Mrs.

Raynor: and who would wish to run away from so pleasant a home? twenty

time at least have Frank go up to town to see if he could pick up

any news, or hear of anything to suit he. delusive dream often

present themselves to his mental vision, of some doctor, rich in

year and philanthropy, who might be willing to take he in for

nothing, to share his first-rate practice. as yet the benevolent old

gentleman have not be discover, but Frank quite believe he exist

somewhere.



another thing have not be discover: the missing money. but Major

Raynor, sanguine as ever be his nephew, do not lose faith in its

existence. it would come to light some time he feel certain; and of

this he never cease to assure Frank. embarrassment decidedly

increase upon the major, chiefly arise from the want of ready cash;

for the great portion of _that_ be sure to be forestall before it

come in. still, a man who enjoy from two to three thousand a-year

cannot be so very badly off: money come to the fore somehow: and on

the whole Major Raynor lead an easy, indolent, and self-satisfy life.

have they decrease their home expense, it would have be all the

well: and they might have do that very materially, and yet not

touch on home comfort. but neither Major nor Mrs. Raynor know how

to set about retrenchment: and so the senseless profusion go on.



"what be there to see, Charley?"



the questioner be Frank. in cross the ground, some little

distance from home, he come upon Charles Raynor. Charles be crane

his neck over a stile, by which the high hedge be divide that

border the large, enclosed, three-cornered tract of land know as

the common. on one side of the common be those miserable dwelling,

the neglect cottage: in a line with they run the row of skeleton,

summarily stop in process of erection. on the other side stand some

pretty detach cottage, inhabit by a somewhat well class of

people; whilst this high hedge--now bud into summer bloom,

and flank with a sloping bank, rich in moss and weed and wild

flower--border the third side. in one corner, between the hedge and

the well house, flourish a small grove of tree. it all belong

to Major Raynor.



"nothing particular," say Charley, in answer to the question. "I be

only look at a fellow."



Frank send his eye over the green space before he. three or four

path traverse it in different direction. a portion of it be rail

off by wooden fencing, and on this some cattle graze; but on most of

it grass be grow, intend for the mower in a month or two's time.

Frank could not see a soul; and say so. some child, indeed, be

play before the hut; but Charles have evidently not allude to

they: his gaze have be direct to the opposite side, near the grove.



"he have disappear amongst those tree," say Charles.



"who be it?" pursue Frank: for there be something in his young

cousin's tone and manner suggestive of uneasiness; and it awake his

own curiosity.



Charles turn and put his back against the stile. he have pluck a

small twig from the hedge, and be twirl it about between his lip.



"Frank, I be in a mess. keep a look-out yonder, and if you see a

stranger, tell I."



"over-run the constable at Oxford this term, as before?" question

Frank, leap to the truth by instinct.



Charles nod. "and I assure you, Frank," he add, attempt to

excuse himself, "that I no more intend to get into debt this last

term than I intend to hang myself. when I go down after Christmas,

I have form the good resolution in the world. I tell the mother she

might trust I. no one could have wish to keep straight than I

wish: and somehow----"



"you don't," put in Frank at the pause.



"I have manage to fall into a fast set, and that's the truth,"

confess Charles. "and I think the very deuce be in the money. it

run away without your know it."



"well, the tradespeople must wait," say Frank, cheerfully; for he be

just as genial over this trouble as he would have be over pleasure.

"they have to wait pretty stiffly for other.



"the bad of it be, I have accept a bill or two," cry Charley,

ruefully. "and--I have a writ serve upon I the last day of term."



"Whew!" whistle Frank. "a writ?"



"one. and I expect another. those horrid bill--there be two of

they--be draw at only a month's date. of course the time's out; and

the fellow wouldn't renew; and I expect there'll be the dicken to

pay. the amount be not much; each fifty pound; but I have not the

ghost of a shilling to meet it with."



"what do you owe besides?"



"as if I know! there's the tailor, and the bootmaker, and the livery

stable, and the wine---- oh, I cann't recollect."



have Frank possess the money, in pocket or prospective, he would have

hand out help to Charles there and then. but he do not possess it.

he be at a nonplus.



"when once a writ's serve, they can take you, cann't they?" ask

Charles, stoop to pluck a pink blossom from the bank, the twig

be bite away to nothing.



"I think so," reply Frank, who have himself contrive to steer clear

of these unpleasant shoal, and know no more of their power than

Charles do. "by the way, though, I don't know. have they get

judgment?"



"Judgment? what's that? sure to have get it if it's anything bad. and

I think I be go to be arrest," continue Charles, drop his

voice, and turn to face the common again. "it's rather a blue

look-out. I should not so _much_ mind it for myself, I think: well

man than I have have to go through the same: but it's the fuss there'll

be at home."



"the idea of call yourself a man, Charley! you be only a boy yet."



"by the way, talk of that, Jones of Corpus tell I a writ could not

be legally serve upon I as I be not of age. Jones say he be sure

of it. what do you think, Frank?"



"I don't know. to tell you the truth, Charley, I be not at home in

these thing. but I should suppose that the very fact of the writ

having be serve upon you be a proof that it can be do, and that

Jones of Corpus be wrong. William Stane could tell you: he must have

all point of law at his finger' end."



"but I don't care to ask William Stane. it may be they take it for

grant that I be of age. any way, I be serve with the writ at

Oxford: and, unless I be mistaken," add Charles, gloomily, "a fellow

have follow I here, and be dodge my heel to arrest I."



"what be your ground for think so, Charley? have you see any

suspicious person about?"



"yes, I have. before you come up just now, I----"



the word be break off suddenly. Charles leap from the corner of

the stile to hide behind the hedge. some individual be emerge from

the grove of tree; and he, it be evident, have cause the movement.



"if he turn his step this way, tell I, Frank, and I'll make a dash

homeward through the oak-coppice," come the hurried whisper.



"all right. no. he be make off across the common."



"that may be only a ruse to throw I off my guard," cry Charley,

from the hedge. "Watch. he will come over here full pelt in a minute.

he look just like a tiger, with that great mass of brown beard. he be

a tiger."



Frank, lean his arm on the stile, scan the movement of the

"Tiger." the Tiger be at some distance, and he could not see he

clearly. a thin tiger of middle height, and apparently approach

middle age, dress in a suit of grey, with a slouch hat on his

brow and a fine brown beard. but the Tiger, whosoever he might be,

appear to entertain no hostile intention for the present moment,

and be stroll leisurely in the direction of the hut. presently

Frank speak.



"he be well away now, Charley: too far to distinguish you, even should

he turn round. there's no danger."



Charley come out from the hedge, and take up his former position at

the extreme corner of the stile, where he be partly hide. every

vestige of colour have forsake his face. he be very young still: not

much more than a boy, as Frank have say: and unfamiliar with these

thing.



"I see he yesterday for the first time," say he to Frank. "I chance

to be stand here, as we be now, and he be walk towards I

across the common. Whilst wonder, in a lazy kind of way, who he be

and what he want here, a rush of fear come over I. it occur to

I that he might be a sheriff's officer. why the idea should flash on

I in that sudden manner--and the fear--I cannot tell; but it do so.

I make the good of my way indoor, and do not stir out again. this

morning I say to myself what a simpleton I have be--that I have no

ground for fear the man, except that he be a stranger, and that

my own mind be full of bother; and I come out, all bravery. the first

person I see, upon cross this stile, be he; just in the same spot,

near the tree, in which I see he yesterday; and the rush of fear

come over I again. it's of no good your laughing, Frank: I cann't help

it: I never be a coward before."



"I be not laugh. do he see you?"



"not to-day, I think. yesterday he do, look at I keenly; and here

he be again in the same spot! I be sure he be look for I. if I

be up in fund, I'd be off somewhere and stay away."



"what about home--and Oxford?"



"there's the bad of it."



"and you could not stay away for ever."



"for ever, no. but, you see, that money may turn up any day, and put

all thing straight."



"well, you may be mistake in the man, Charley: and I hope you be."



William Stane be at home for these Easter holiday, and still the

shadow of Alice Raynor. it chance that this same afternoon he and

Alice encounter the Tiger--as, from that day, Charles and Frank both

call he in private. stroll side by side under the brilliant

afternoon sun, in that silence which be most eloquent of love, with

the bird singe above they, and the very murmur of the tree

speak a sweet language to their heart, they come upon this

stranger in grey, sit on the stump of a tree. the tree, mostly

beech, be thick about there; the path branch off sharply at a

right angle, and they do not see he until they be close up: in

fact, William Stene have to make a hasty stop or two to pass without

touch he. perhaps it be his unexpected appearance in that spot,

or that it be not usual to see stranger there, or else his peculiar

look, with the slouch hat and the bushy beard; but certain it be

that he especially attract their attention; somewhat of their

curiosity.



"what a strange-look man!" exclaim Alice, when they have go on

some distance. "do you not think so, William?"



"Queerish. do he live here? I wonder if he be aware that he be

trespassing?"



"papa let any one come on the ground who like to," reply Alice.

"he be a stranger. I never see he before."



"oh, it must be one of the Easter excursionist. escape from smoky

London to enjoy a day or two of pure air in the Kentish Wolds."



"as you have do," say she.



"as I have do. I only wish, Alice, I could enjoy it oftener."



word and the tone alike bear a precious meaning to her ear. his eye

meet her, and linger there.



"I be get on excellently," he continue. "by the end of this year,

I have no doubt I shall be justify in--in quit my chamber and

take a house. perhaps before that."



"look at that hawthorn!" exclaim Alice, dart to a hedge they be

now pass, for she know too well what the word imply. "have it not

come out early! it be in full bloom."



"shall I gather some for you?"



"no. it would be a pity. it look so well there, and every one who

pass can enjoy it. do you know, I never see the flower hawthorn

but I think of that good old Scotch song, 'ye bank and braes.' I

don't know why."



"let we sit down here," say he, as they come to a rustic seat under

the tree. "and now, Alice, if you would sing that good old song, the

charm would be perfect."



she laugh. "what charm?"



"the charm of--everything. the day and hour, the white and pink may

bud in the hedge, the wild flower we crush with our foot, the

blue sky and the green tree, the sunshine and the shade, the singing

bird and the whispering leave, and--yourself."



not another word from either of they just yet. William Stane have

allow his hand to fall on her. her head be slightly turn from

he, her cheek be glow, her heart be beat: it be again

another interval of that most sweet and eloquent silence.



"willn't you begin, Alice? the bird 'warble through the flowering

thorn' be wait to hear you. so be I."



and as if she have no power to resist his will, she begin at once,

without a dissent murmur, and sing the song to the end. except

the bird above they, there be no listener: no rover be likely to

be near that solitary spot. her voice be sweet, but not loud; every

syllable be speak distinctly. to sit there for ever, side by side,

and not be disturb, would be a very Eden.





     "and my fake lover steal my rose,

      but ah! he leave the thorn wi' I."





Scarcely have the echo melody die away, when the unexpected sound

of footstep be hear approach, and there advance into view a

woman well know to Alice; one Sarah Croft, the wife of a man employ

on the estate. they live in one of the most miserable dwelling on

the common, but be civil and quiet; somewhat independent in manner,

but never join in the semi-rebellion that reign. she look

miserably poor. her blue cotton gown, though clean, be in rag, her

old shawl would hardly hang together, the black bonnet on her head

might have be use for frighten the crow. she drop a curtsy

and be pass onwards, when Alice inquire after her sick child.



"they be no well, Miss Raynor, thank you," she answer, halt in

front of the bench. "the little one, she be take sick now, as well as

the two boy. I've a fine time o't.



"why don't you have a doctor to they?" say Alice.



"More nor a week agone I go up to the parish and tell they I must

have a doctor to my child: but he never come till yesterday."



"what do he say?"



"I'll tell ye what he say, Miss Raynor, if ye like. he say doctor

and doctor' stuff be o' no good, so long as the house remain what

they be--pe-ti-fe-rus. I should not have remember the word,

though, but for Jetty's lodger repeating of the very self-same word to

I a minute or two agone. I've just pass he, a-sit down under

yonder beech."



Alice, as well as William Stane, instantly recall the man in grey

they have see there. "Jetty's lodger!" repeat Alice. "who be he?"



"some stranger stay in the place, Miss Raynor. he come into it one

morning, a week agone, and take Jetty's room which be to let."



"what be he stay here for?"



"to pry into people's business, I think," reply the woman. "he's

always about, here, there, and everywhere; one cann't stir out many

yard but one meet he. Saturday last, he walk right into our place

without as much as knock; and there he turn hisself round and

about, look at the rotten floor and the dripping wall, and

sniff at the bad smell that's always there, just as if he have as

much right inside as a king. 'who be your landlord?' say he, 'and do

he know what a den this be?' so I tell he that our landlord be Major

Raynor at Eagles' Nest, and that he do know, but that nothing be

do for we. he have go, I hear, into some o' the other house as

well."



the woman's tone be quite civil, but there could be no doubt that, in

her independence, she be talk at Alice as the daughter of Major

Raynor.



"as I pass he now he ask I whether my sick child be

well--just as you have, Miss Raynor. I tell he they be bad. 'and

bad they will be, and never well, and all the rest of you too,'

say he, 'as long as you inhabit they pe-ti-fe-rus den!'"



Alice draw up her head in cold disdain, vouchsafe no further word,

and feel very angry at the imply reproach. the woman drop a

slight curtsy again, and go on her way.



"how insolent they all be!" exclaim Alice to Mr. Stane. "that Sarah

Croft would have be abusive in another moment."



"their cottage be bad," return the young man, after a pause.

"could nothing be do, I wonder, to make they a little well?"



"it be papa's business, not mine," remark Alice, in slight

resentment. "and the idea of that stranger presume to interfere!

wonder what he mean by it?"



"I do not suppose he intend it as interference: he be look about

he by way of fill up his time: it must hang rather monotonously on

his hand down here, I presume, away from his book and ledger,"

remark Mr. Stane. "it be the way of the world, Alice; people must be

busy-body and look into what do not concern they, for curiosity's

sake. Nay, just a few moment long," he say, for she have rise to

depart. "to-morrow I shall have no such pleasant and peaceful seat to

linger in; I shall not have you. how delightful it all be!"



and so, the disturbing element forget, they sit on in the balmy

air, under the blue of the sky, the green foliage about they spring

into life and beauty, type of another life that must succeed our own

winter, and listen to the little bird overhead warble their

joyous song. can none of we, grey now with care and work and year,

remember just such an hour spend in our own sweet spring-time?--when

all thing around speak to our heart in one unmixed love-strain of

harmony, and the future look like a charmed scroll that could only

bring intense happiness in the unroll thereof?



"take my arm, Alice," he half whisper, when they at length rise to

return.



she do take it, her face and heart glow. take it timidly and with

much self-consciousness, never having be in the habit of take it,

or he of offer it. her hand tremble as it lie gently upon his arm;

each might have hear the other's heart beat. and so in the bliss

of this, their first love-dream, they saunter home through the

ground, choose pleasant glade and mossy by-way; and arrive to

find Eagles' Nest in a commotion.



Mrs. Frank Raynor have be take seriously and unexpectedly ill.

doctor be send for; servant run about. and William Stane say

farewell, and go home from an afternoon that would ever remain as a

green spot on his memory. it be his last day of holiday.





with the morning, Daisy lie in great danger. the illness, not

anticipate for a month or two, have come on suddenly. in one sense of

the word the event be over, but not the danger; and the baby, not

destine to see the light, be go.



it be perhaps unfortunate that on this same morning Frank should

receive an urgent summon to Trennach. Edina write. her father be

very ill; ill, it be fear, unto death; and he most earnestly beg

Frank to travel to he with all speed, for he have urgent need of

see he. Edina say that, unless her father should rally, three or

four day be the utmost limit of life accord to he by the

doctor: she therefore beg of Frank to lose no time in obey the

summon; and she add that her father desire she to say the journey

should be no cost to he.



"what a distressing thing!" cry Frank, in blank dismay, show the

letter to the major. "I cannot go. it be impossible that I can go

whilst Daisy lie in this state."



"good gracious!" say the major, rub his head, as he always do in

any emergency. "well, I suppose you cann't, my boy. poor Hugh!"



"how can I! suppose I be to go, and--and she die?"



"yes, to be sure. you must wait until she be in less danger. I hope

with all my heart Hugh will rally. and Daisy too."



Frank sit down and write a few word to his uncle, tell he why he

could not start that day, but that he would do so the moment his

wife's state allow it. he write more fully, but to the same effect,

to Edina. perhaps on the morrow, he add. the morrow might bring

well thing.



but on the morrow Daisy be even bad. a high fever have set in. Frank

write again to Trennach, but he could not leave Eagles' Nest. some

day go on; day of peril: Daisy be hover between life and

death. and on the first day that a very faint indication of

improvement be perceptible and the medical man say she might now

live, that there be a bare chance of it, but no certainty; that same

day the final news come from Trennach, and it be too late for Frank

to take the journey. Dr. Raynor be dead.



the tiding come by letter from Edina: write to Frank. it be only a

short note, give a few particular. within this note, however, be a

thick letter, seal and mark "private."  Frank chance to be

alone at the moment, and open it with some curiosity. on a single

sheet of envelop paper, enclose a letter from Dr. Raynor, be

the follow line from Edina.





"my poor father be so anxious to see you, dear Frank, at the last,

that it disturb his peace. of course you could not come, under the

circumstance; he see that; but he say over and over again that your

not come be most unfortunate, and to you might be disastrous. at

the hour of the day and night when a train be due, nothing could

exceed the eagerness with which he look for you, his restlessness

when it grow too late to admit of hope that you have come. the day

before he die, when he know the end be approach and he should not

live to see you, he cause himself to be prop up in bed, and have

pen-and-ink bring that he might write to you. he watch I seal up

the letter when it be finish, and charge I to send it to you when

all be over, but to be sure to enclose it privately, and to tell you

to open and read it when you be alone.--E. R."





send Edina's short note announce the death of her father to Major

Raynor by a servant, Frank carry these line and the doctor's letter

to his chamber: thereby obey injunction, but nevertheless

wonder at they very much. what could his uncle have to say to he

necessitating secrecy? break the seal, he run his eye over the

almost illegible line that the die hand have trace.





"MY dear nephew FRANK,



"I want to see you; I ought not to have put it off so long. but this

closing scene have come upon I somewhat suddenly: and now I cannot

write all I ought to, and should wish: and I must, of necessity, write

abruptly.



"_be you conscious of be in any danger?_ have you commit any

act that could bring you under the arm of the law? if so, take care of

yourself. a terrible rumour be whisper in my ear by Andrew Float,

connect you with the hitherto unexplained fate of Bell the miner. I

charge Float to be silent--and I think he will be, for he be a kind

and good man, and only speak to I that I might put you on your

guard--and I question Blase Pellet, from whom Float have hear it.

Pellet be sullen, obstinate, would not say much; but he do say that

he could hang you, and _would_ do it if you offend he or put

yourself in his way. I could not get anything more from he, and it

be not a subject that I care to inquire into minutely, or could

pursue openly.



"my boy, you good know what ground there may be for this

half-breathe accusation; whether any or none. I have scarcely have a

minute's peace since it reach I, now three week ago: in fact, it

have, I believe, bring on the crisis with I somewhat before it would

otherwise have come. at one moment I say to myself, it be a malicious

invention, an infamous lie; I know my boy Frank too well to believe

this, or anything else against he: the next moment I shudder at the

tale and at the possibility of what may have be enact. perhaps

through passion--or accident--or--I grow confused: I know not what I

would say.



"oh, my boy, my nephew, my dear brother Henry's only child! my heart

be ache with dismay and doubt. I do believe you be innocent of all

intention to do harm; but--my sight be grow dim. _take care of

yourself_. hide yourself if need be (and you well know whether there

be need, or not) from Blase Pellet. it be he who would be your enemy.

I see it; and Andrew Float see it; though we know not why or

wherefore. in any obscure nook of this wide world, shelter yourself

from he. don't let he know where you be. if he do indeed hold

power in his hand, it may be your only chance of safety: _he say it

be so_. I can write no more. God bless and help you! farewell.



"your love and anxious



"UNCLE HUGH."





Frank Raynor may have draw many a deep breath in his life, but never

so deep a one as he draw now. mechanically he fold the letter and

place it in an inner pocket.



"be you there, sir?"



the question come from outside the door, in the voice of one of the

servant. Frank unbolt it.



"Lunch be on the table, sir."



"be it?" return Frank, half bewilder. "I don't want any to-day,

James. just say so. I be go out for a stroll."



the letter from Cornwall be never deliver at Eagles' Nest until

the midday post. Frank take his hat, and go out; bend his step

whithersoever they choose to take he, so that he might be alone.

stroll on mechanically, in deep thought, he plunge into a dark

coppice, and ask himself what he be to do. the letter have disturb

he in no ordinary degree. it have take all his spirit, all his

elasticity out of he: and that be say a great deal for Frank

Raynor.



"I wish I could hang Blase Pellet!" he break forth in his torment and

perplexity. "he deserve it richly. to disturb my poor uncle with his

malicious tongue! villain!"



but Frank be unconsciously unjust. it be not Blase Pellet who have

disturb Dr. Raynor. at least, he have not do it intentionally. to

do Blase justice, he be vex that the doctor should have hear it,

for he hold he in great respect and would not willingly have grieve

he. in an evil moment, when Blase have take rather more than be

quite necessary--an almost unprecedented occurrence with he--he have

drop the dangerous word to Andrew Float.



"yes, I must hide from he, as my uncle say," resume Frank,

refer to the advice in the letter. "there's no help for it. he

could be a dangerous enemy. for my own sake; for--every one's sake, I

must keep myself in some shelter where he cannot find I."



emerge on to the open ground, Frank lift his eye, and see,

stand near he, the man in grey, whom they have christen the

Tiger. he be lean against the tree with bent head and fold arm,

apparently in deep thought. all in a moment, just as a personal fear

of he have rush over Charles, so do it now rush over Frank. his

brain grow dizzy.



for the idea somehow strike he that the man be not want Charles

at all. but that he might be an emissary of Blase Pellet's, come

hither to look after himself and his movement.









CHAPTER IV.

AT JETTY'S.





John Jetty be the local carpenter. a master in a small way. his

workshop be in the village, Grassmere, near to Eagles' Nest; his

dwelling-house be on the common already describe. in this house he

live with his sister, Esther Jetty; a staid woman, more than ten

year old than himself: he be a smart, talkative, active, and

very intelligent man of two or three-and-thirty. the house, which they

rent of Major Raynor, be large than they require, and Esther

Jetty be in the habit of let a sitting and bedroom in it when she

could find a desirable lodger to occupy they.



on the Thursday in Passion Week, when she be in the midst of her

house-cleaning for Easter, and in the act of polish the outside of

the spare sitting-room window, in which hang a card with "Lodgings"

inscribe on it, she notice a man in grey clothe saunter up from

the direction of the railway-station, an overcoat on his arm, and a

good-sized black bag in his hand.



"some traveller from London," decide Esther Jetty, turn to gaze at

he; for a stranger in the quiet place be quite an event. "come down

to spend Easter."



the thought have scarcely cross her mind, when, somewhat to her

surprise, the stranger turn out of the path, walk directly towards

she, and take off his hat while he speak.



"have you lodging to let?" he ask. "I see a card in your window."



"yes, sir; I have two room," say she, respectfully, for the courtesy

of the lift hat have favourably impress she, and the tone of his

voice be courteous also, not at all like those of an individual in

humble station. "what a fine beard!" she think to herself. "how

smooth and silky it be!"



"I want to stay in this place a few day," continue he, "and be

look for lodging. perhaps yours would suit I."



Esther Jetty hasten to show the room. they be small, but clean,

comfortable, and prettily furnish: and the rent be ten shilling

per week.



"it be not too much, sir, at this season of the year, when summer's

come on," she hasten to say, lest the amount should be object

to. "I always try to make my lodger comfortable, and cook for they

and wait on they well. the last I have--a sick young woman and her

little girl--stay here all the winter and spring: they only leave

three week ago."



the stranger's answer be to put down a sovereign. "that's the first

week's rent in advance," say he. "with the change you can get I some

mutton chop for my dinner. I shall not give you much trouble." and he

take possession of the room at once.



as the day have go on, only a few as yet, Esther Jetty find that

his promise of not give much trouble be keep. she have never have a

lodger who give less. he live very simply. his dinner generally

consist of two mutton chop; his other food chiefly of egg and

bread-and-butter. it be glorious weather; and he pass nearly all

his time out-of-door.



not a nook or corner of the immediate neighbourhood escape his keen

eye, his, as it seem, insatiable curiosity. he penetrate into the

small dwelling-house, good and bad, ask question of the inmate,

make friend with they all. he would stand by the half-hour side by

side with the out-door labourer, say the land want this and that

do to it, and demand why it be not do. but, there could be no

doubt that he be even more curious in regard to the Raynor family,

and especially to its eld son, than he be as to the land and its

labourer: and the latter soon notice that if by chance Charles

Raynor come into sight, the stranger would stroll off, apparently

without aim, towards he; and when Charles turn away, as he

invariably do, the man follow in his wake at a distance. in short,

it would seem that his chief business be to look surreptitiously

after some of the inmate of Eagles' Nest; and that his visit to the

land and the cottage, and his disparaging remark thereupon, be

probably only take up to pass the time away. these opinion, however,

grow upon people as time go on, rather than at the beginning of his

stay.



Easter week pass. on the following Sunday the stranger go to

church; and, after the service begin, take up a place whence he have

full view of the large square pew belong to Eagles' nest. on Easter

Sunday he have sit at the back of the church, out of sight. Charles,

Alice, and Frank be in the pew to-day, with the governess and little

Kate: Mrs. Raynor be at home with Frank's wife, then lie

dangerously ill; the major have not come. this be two day before they

receive news of Dr. Raynor's death. Charles be render miserably

uncomfortable during the service by the presence of the Tiger opposite

to he--as might be read by any one in the secret of his fear, and

be read by Frank. never do Charles raise his eye but he see those

of the Tiger fix on he. in fact, the Tiger study the face in

Major Raynor's pew more attentively than he study his book.



"he be take toll of I that he may know I again: I don't suppose he

know I before, or his work would have be do and over," think

Charles. "what a precious idiot I be to come to church! thank Heaven,

he cann't touch I on a Sunday." and when the service be end, the

Tiger coolly stand in the churchyard and watch the family pass he,

look keenly at Charles.



he have in like manner watch they into church. from a shady nook in

the same churchyard, he have stand, himself unseen, look at the

congregation as they file in. when the bell have cease, and the last

person seem to have enter, then the Tiger follow, and put

himself in the good place for see the Raynors. it be, however, the

first and last time Charles be annoy in a similar manner. on

subsequent Sundays, the Tiger, if he go to church at all, be lose

amidst the general congregation.



on this same Sunday evening, John Jetty find himself invite to take

a pipe with his lodger. they sit in the arbour in the back-garden,

amidst the herb, the spring cabbage, and the early flower. Jetty

never want any inducement to talk. he be not of a wary nature by

any mean, and do not observe how skilfully and easily the thread of

his discourse be this evening turn on the Raynors and their

affair. no man in the place could have supply more correct

information to a strange than he. he be often at work in the house,

be particularly intimate with Lamb the butler, who have live with

Mrs. Atkinson; as have two or three of the other head servant; and

they have the family politic at their finger' end. Mrs. Raynor have

bring one servant from Spring Lawn; the nurse; the woman know all

about her branch of the family, Frank include, and have no objection

to relate news for the new people's benefit, who in their turn

repeat it to Jetty. consequently Jetty be as much at home in the

family archive as the Raynors be themselves.



"be the estate entail on the major's son?" question the Tiger, in

a pause of the conversation.



"I don't think it's strictly entail on he, sir, but of course he'll

have it," be Jetty's answer. "indeed, it be no secret that the major

have make a will and leave it to he. Mrs. Atkinson bequeath it

entirely to the major: she don't entail it."



"who be Mrs. Atkinson?" ask the Tiger.



"why, the possessor of the estate before he," cry Jetty, in accent

full of surprise. to he, familiar for many year with Eagles' Nest

and its people, it sound strange to hear any one ask who Mrs.

Atkinson be. "she be an old lady, sir, sister to the major, and it

all belong to she. he only come into it last year when she die."



"have she no son?"



"no, sir; not any. I never hear that she do have any. her husband

be a banker in London; he buy this place a good many year ago.

after his death Mrs. Atkinson entirely live in it."



"then--it be sure to come to the major's eld son?"



"as sure as sure can be," affirm Jetty, replenish his pipe at his

lodger's invitation. "the major would not be likely to will it away to

anybody else."



"I see two young man in the pew to-day: one quite young, scarcely out

of his teen, I should say; the other some year old. which of they

be the son?"



"oh, the young. the other be a nephew; Mr. Frank Raynor. he be very

good-look, he be: such a pleasant face, with nice blue eye and

bright hair. not but what Mr. Charles be good-look, too, in a

different way."



"Mr. Charles look to I like an insolent young puppy," freely

comment the Tiger. "and have a haughty air with it: as though he be

king of the country and all the rest of we be his subject." the

probability be that Charles have honour the stare Tiger with all

the haughty and insolent look he could call up throughout the

service.



"well, he be a bit haughty sometimes," acknowledge the carpenter.

"folk have find he so. he be just home from Oxford, sir, and I

fancy have be spend pretty freely there: Lamb just give I a hint.

but if you want pleasant word and cordial manner, you must go to the

nephew, Mr. Frank.



"what be _he_ do here?" dryly ask the stranger, after a pause.



"he be a doctor, sir."



"a doctor? be he in practice here?"



"oh no. he be wait to set up in London, and stay down here till

he do it."



"what be he wait for?"



"well, sir, for money, I guess. the Raynors be open-natured people

and don't scruple to talk of thing before their servant, so that

there's not much but what's know. when the late Mrs. Atkinson die, a

good deal of stir arise about some money of hers that could not be

find: thousand and thousand of pound, it be say. it could

neither be find, nor the paper relate to it."



"be it not find yet?" ask the Tiger, stroke his silky beard.



"not yet. the major be anxiously wait for it: not a day pass,

Lamb say, but he be sure to remark that it may turn up the next. Mr.

Frank Raynor be to have some of this money to set he up in

practice."



"do Mrs. Atkinson not leave any money to he? he must have be a

relation of her?"



"oh yes, she leave he money. I forget what it be now--a good sum,

though."



"why do he not set up with that?" question the Tiger, wonderingly.



"he have spend it, sir. he and his young wife go abroad, and live

away, I suppose. any way, the money's go, Lamb say. but Mr. Frank's

as nice a fellow as ever live."



"do he----" begin the stranger, and then break off, as if in doubt

whether or not to put the question: but in a moment go on firmly.

"do he ever live at Trennach, in Cornwall?"



"Trennach?" repeat Jetty, consider. "yes, sir, I think that's

where he do live. yes, I'm sure that be the name. he be in practice

there with another uncle, one Dr. Raynor, and might have stop there

and come into the practice after he. a rare good opening for he,

it's say: but he prefer to go elsewhere."



"preferred to travel and see the world," speak the stranger,

cynically. "be Major Raynor's revenue good one?"



"well, sir, I know in Mrs. Atkinson's time this estate be say to

bring in a clear two thousand a-year. and Major Raynor have of course

an income before he come into it: but that, I hear, be only an

annuity, and go from he at his death."



"then, if his revenue amount to that--from two to three thousand

a-year--how be it that he do not do the repair necessary on the

estate, and keep up the land, and help to ameliorate the condition of

the wretched serfs about he?" demand the stranger.



Jetty shake his head. "I don't think it be the will that's want,"

reply he. "the major seem to be thoroughly good-hearted and Lamb

say he be one of the easy master he could ever wish to serve. no,

it be not the will, sir, that be want."



"what be it, then? the money?"



Jetty nod in the affirmative. "they live at such a rate, you see,

sir; and it be say the major have a lot of back-debt to pay when he

come here. altogether, he have nothing to spare."



"then he ought to have," assert the Tiger, tap thoughtfully at

his pipe, that lie on the table. "do he never visit his tenement

and see into thing for himself?"



"no, sir, not he. 'twould be too much exertion for he. he cann't walk

about much; never come beyond his own garden gate; never."



the Tiger pause. "this young Frank Raynor's wife, who be lie ill:

have she no money?"



"no, sir. her family have plenty, I expect, for they live at some

grand place down in Cornwall. but she have none. it be a runaway match

that she and Mr. Frank make, so she couldn't expect any."



the Tiger nod two or three time, as if in self-commune. "I see,"

say he: "these Raynors be an improvident set altogether.

thoughtless, cruel, selfish, upstart and purse-proud. from what little

I have notice during the few day I have be here, that be the

impression they make upon I: and what you say confirm it."



he take his pipe up from the table as he speak, knock the ashe out

of it, and put it into its case. an intimation, John Jetty think,

that their social hour be at an end: and he go away, respectfully

wish his lodger good-evening.





Easter be over; and the time for go back to Oxford for the come

term be past. Charles Raynor have not go up to keep it. he have to

confess to the major that he do not care to go back without a good

sum of money, apart from his allowance; he might have say, dare not

go. it be not convenient to find the sum: so the major decide that

Charles must miss that one term, and keep the next.



the week go on. Charles have in a degree get over his dread of the

Tiger--who still remain on in his lodging--for it be now very

evident that if that mysterious man's mission at Grassmere be to

take he into custody for debt, it might have be accomplish ere

this. nevertheless, so strongly do first impression retain their hold

upon we, his dislike of the man continue in all its force.



but, as Charles's alarm subside, Frank's increase. the more evident

of, but his secret uneasiness be great. neither he nor Charles could

fail to see that the man's daily business appear to be that of

two young man. not watch offensively, but in a quiet, easy,

unobtrusive manner. Frank fully believe that the man be a secret

Major Raynor have never see this man: and Frank and Charles, each for

matter remain pretty stationary: the major indolently nursing

private discomfort; and the Tiger peer into every conceivable spot

open to he, and make himself well acquaint with the general

shortcoming of the Raynors, in regard to the estate and the people on

it, than they be themselves.



it be Saturday evening. Alice sit at the piano in the drawing-room,

singe song in the twilight to the intense gratification of William

home the last day of the week, to remain over the Sunday with his

Eagles' nest. the major sit back in the room, dozing; Charles be

listlessly turn over a pile of music. eagle' Nest have give an

afternoon party that day; a fashionable kettledrum; but the guest have

depart.



"I can scarcely see," say Alice, as her lover place a new song

before she. she be in the dress she have wear in the afternoon: a

black gauze trim with white ribbon, with silver bracelet and

other silver ornament, and look charmingly lovely. they be in

mourning for Dr. Raynor.



"I'll ring for light," say Charles. "I cann't see, either."



the talking have arouse the major. "we don't want light yet," say

he. "it be pleasanter as it be."



"sing the song you know by heart," whisper William Stane. "after

all, they be the good and sweetest."



presently Lamb come in of his own accord, with the wax-light. the

major, wake up again, make no objection now, but forbid the

shutter to be close.



"it's a pity to shut out that moonlight," say he. not that the

moonlight could have interest he much, for in another minute he be

asleep again. he have grow strangely drowsy of late. so the room be

light up, and the moonlight stream in at the window.



Frank enter. he have be sit upstairs with his wife, who be

still very ill. in fact, this have be an unusually prolonged and

critical sickness. take up his position at the window, Frank

listen silently to the song then in progress. Charles come up to

he.



"how be she to-night, Frank?"



"no well. If---- look there!" he suddenly exclaim, his voice sink

to a whisper.



some one have walk deliberately by, outside the window, gaze at

heart beat nineteen to the dozen.



"do you see he, Charley?"



"who be it?" whisper Charley.



"I'm not quite sure; he pass so quickly. the Tiger, I conclude. yes,

I feel sure of it. I know the cut of his hat."



"what consummate impudence, to be trespass here!"



they both leave the room, make their way to a side-door, and look

out. no one be in sight; and yet, whosoever it be that have pass

must have come that way.



"he have turn back," say Charley: and as he speak he advance

cautiously amidst the shrub that skirt that end of the house, and

look round at the front.



no. not a soul be to be see or hear. have he scamper straight

across the lawn and make off? it seem like it.



"I wonder what it's come to!" cry Charley. "could we have he

warn off the estate, I wonder?"



"hardly," speak Frank, in a dreamy tone.



"I _cannot_ think what he do here," exclaim Charles. "if he have

any evil intention, he--he would have act upon they before now."



"you mean as to yourself, Charley. rely upon it, you be out of the

matter altogether."



"who's in it, then?"



"Myself, perhaps."



the answer be give quietly and easily: but there be something in

its tone that keep Charles from regard it as a jest.



"_You_ be not in debt, be you, Frank?" he cry hastily.



"not that I know of."



"I declare for the moment I think you must be in earnest," say

Charles, relieve. "it be uncommonly strange what the fellow can want

here!"



Frank say no more. they pace about for some time, without their

hat, in the bright moonlight, talk of other matter. in cross

the path to the house; they meet Jetty the carpenter come away from

it, a frail in his hand, out of which a saw be stand upright. the

man have be do some repair indoor.



"Jetty," say Charles, accost he, and speak upon impulse, "who

be the man that lodge with you? the fellow with the great brown

beard, who go about in a suit of grey."



"I don't know who he be in particular, sir," reply Jetty. "he be a

very quiet lodger, and pay regular."



"what be he down here for?"



"well, I think for his health," say Jetty. "he tell we he have not

be well for some time before he come to Grassmere."



"what be his name?"



"that I don't know, sir----"



"not know his name?" interrupt Charles, impatiently.



"well, sir, I be go to say that I don't know it from himself. he

be uncommonly close as to his own affair: though he like well enough

to hear about other people's. as to his name, he do not mention it

when he first come in, and my sister say she do not like to ask he.

But----"



"I never know such a thing as not know a lodger's name," go on

"yes, sir. but they don't come to the house; they be leave at the

post-office in Grassmere, and he fetch they himself. the other
