these letter, and the cover lie on the table, address upwards. she

be not quick enough to read the name on it, for he take it up, but

she see it be a short name and begin with a G."



"Grim, no doubt," say Charles.



"'Mr. G----, Post Office, Grassmere.' that be it, sir."



"I must say I should like to know who he be and what he be do

here," continue Charles. "good-night, Jetty."



Jetty touch his cap and go away with rapid stride. draw near

to his home, he overtake the Tiger, saunter along with slow step.



"you be late to-night, Jetty."



"yes, sir," reply the carpenter, suit his pace to that of the

speaker. "I have to put some new shelf into one of the kitchen

cupboard at Eagles' Nest, and it have take I long than I think

for."



"all go on well there?" continue the Tiger.



"first rate," say Jetty. "they have a great party this afternoon; one

of those new-fashioned kettledrum. such an entertainment it be! such

fine dress!"



"I think the son, Charles Raynor, be keep his term at Oxford,"

resume the Tiger, after give himself time to digest the information

touch the kettledrum. "why be he not keep this term?"



"well, sir," say Jetty, begin to answer in his usual favourite

mode, and lower his voice, though they be quite alone on the

common: "I believe Mr. Charles cann't show his face at Oxford until he

be well up in fund; so he be omit this term."



"debt--eh?" cry the Tiger, but without any appearance of surprise.

"and the major have not the fund to spare for they?"



"well, sir, that's to be infer."



"meanwhile the lad fill up his day and hour at home with dancing,

and smoking, and kettledrum, and other good-for-nothing amusement. a

nice way of spend one's life!"



"young man will be young man, sir--though they be but lad," speak

Jetty, deprecatingly.



"yes; young man will be young man: some of they, at any rate," come

the mocking retort. "but in all my day I never see a young man who

appear more likely to go straight down to ruin than Charles Raynor."









CHAPTER V.

SIR PHILIP'S MISSION.





Major Raynor sit in his favourite seat on the lawn at Eagles' nest, at

drowsy peace with himself and with the world. of late the major have

always be drowsy: morning, noon, and night, no matter what company

he be in, he might be see nod. Frank, as a medical man, do not

like the sign. he speak to his uncle of the necessity of rouse

himself, of take more exercise, of indulge somewhat less in good

luncheon and dinner. the major make an effort to obey: for two day

he actually walk about the lawn for twenty minute, refuse two rich

entrées, take at each meal one glass less of wine. but the effort

end there, and on the third day the major give up reformation as a

bad job.



"it's of no use, Frank, my boy. you young folk can be upon the run all

day if you choose, and live upon bread-and-cheese and beer; but we old

one require ease; we cann't be put about."



so the major sit at ease this day as usual, lazily think, and

drop into a doze. a letter have be receive that morning from

Edina, in answer to an invitation from Major and Mrs. Raynor to come

and make she home with they now that she be alone in the world. Edina

decline it for the present. she be stay at Trennach parsonage

with Mr. and Mrs. Pine: her plan be not decide upon; but the

clergyman and his wife would not yet spare she. she have many affair

to settle at Trennach. Mr. Hatman have take to the practice, as have

be arrange, and to the house; but Edina could not leave the place

at present. she hope to pay Eagles' Nest a visit in the course of the

summer.



think of this, and subside into dozing, sit the major. the hum of

the insect sound in his ear, the scent of the rich flowering

hawthorn be heavy in the air. though not yet summer by the calendar,

for May be still reign, the season be unusually premature, and

the weather be, to all intent and purpose, that of summer. bee

be sip at the honey-blossom, butterfly flutter from flower

to flower. all nature seem conducive to repose, and--the major be

soon fast asleep, and choke as though he be be strangle.



"you be want, if you please, sir."



the word arouse he. open his eye, and sit upright in his

chair, he see his butler by his side.



"what do you say, Lamb? want? who be it?"



"Sir Philip Stane, sir. he be in the drawing-room."



the major take a draught of his champagne-cup, stand on the table

by his side. which cup, it must be confess, be much more innocent

than its name would imply. a quart or two of it would not have hurt

any one: and the major be always thirsty. cross the lawn, he go

into the drawing-room. Sir Philip Stane, a little man with a white

shirt-frill, a cold face, and a remarkably compose manner, rise at

his entrance. Major Raynor shake hand with he in his hearty way, and

they sit down together.



for some few minute the conversation turn on general topic; but

soon the knight give the major to understand that he have come to speak

upon a particular subject: the attachment of his son to Miss Raynor.



"it have for some time be observable that they be think of one

another," remark he.



"well, yes, I suppose it have," say the major. "we have notice it

here."



"William be get on fairly well; he calculate that he will make at

least seven-hundred pound this year. quite enough, he think, to

begin housekeep upon, with help. with help, major."



"I should have think it unbounde rich in my marrying day,"

observe the major.



"William consider that he would be justify in set up a home,

provide he can be meet," continue Sir Philip in his deliberate,

sententious way, present a direct contrast to the major's

heartiness. "young people do not of course expect to begin as they may

hope to end: rich must come by degree."



"quite right," say the major.



"and therefore, with a view to the consideration of the matter--to

finally decide whether my son may be justify, or not, in settle

this year--I have come to ask you, Major Raynor, what portion you

intend to bestow upon your daughter."



"not any," reply the plain-speak major. "I have none to bestow."



Sir Philip look at he blankly. he do not appear to understand.



"my will be good, Sir Philip. I would give a portion to Alice heartily

if I possess it. thousand, I'm sure, the young people should be

welcome to, if they need it."



"do you mean to say that you--that you will not bestow any portion

whatever upon your daughter when she marry?" ask Sir Philip, in a

tone of cold astonishment.



"I'm sorry that I cann't do it," say the major. "I wish I could. if

that lose money of mine would only turn up----"



"then, I be afraid, I--cannot say what I have come to say," return

Sir Philip, with the air of a man who deliberate aloud, and quite

ignore the major's interrupted sentence. "I could not advise my son

to settle upon the few hundred a-year that make up his present

income."



"why, it's abundance," cry the candid major. "you have just say

yourself that young people cannot expect to begin as they will end.

your son's be a rise income: if he make seven-hundred this year, he

may expect to make ten next, and double the seven the year after. it

be ample to begin upon, Sir Philip."



"no," dissent Sir Philip. "neither he nor I would consider it so.

something should be put by for a rainy day. this communication have

completely take I by surprise, Major Raynor. we take it for grant

that your daughter would at least add her quota to the income: have it

be only three or four hundred a-year. without money of her own,

there could be no settlement on she, you see, my son's not be real

property."



the major be grow a little heated. he do not at all like the turn

the conversation be take, or Sir Philip's dictatorial tone.



"well, you hear, Sir Philip, that Alice have nothing. those who wish to

take she, must take she as she be--portionless--or not at all."



Sir Philip Stane rise. "I be sorry, then, major, that I cannot ask

what I be about to ask for--herself. your daughter----"



"you be not want to ask it, sir," hotly interrupt the major.



"the fact of your daughter's be portionless debar it," quietly

go on the knight. "I be very sorry indeed to have trouble you, and

subject myself to pain. William must consider his pretension at an

end."



"they be at an end," fire the major. "if it be money he have be

think of all this time, he ought to be ashamed of himself for a

calculate, mercenary young rascal. be he to come to I on his

knee, after this, beg for my daughter, he should not have she.

that's my answer, Sir Philip Stane, and you can take it away with

you."



the major's tug at the bell-rope send a peal echo through the

house. but Sir Philip Stane's hand be already on the door-handle,

let himself out with a short "good-morning."



Away go the major, hunt for Alice. he find she with her mother.

hotly and explosively he give an account of the interview; of what he

call the mercenary conduct of Sir Philip and William Stane. poor

indignity--as she be pleased to think it--quite as resentfully as the

major.



"I forbid you to have anything to do with he after this, Alice. I

forbid you to see he again."



"you need not forbid I, papa," be the answer. "I should not think of

it."



Major Raynor be one who could not keep in anything, good or bad,

especially any grievance. he go about the house, look for Charles

and Frank, that he might impart the news, and so let off a little of

his superfluous anger. but he could not find either of they.



matter be go on much as usual. Daisy be progress so far

towards recovery that she could sit at the open window of her chamber

landscape. Charles be in a little extra trouble; for he have be

write to twice upon the subject of the fifty-pound bill that be

overdue. and Frank, outwardly gay as the flower of May, be inwardly

on thorn and nettle.



that that mysterious personage, the Tiger, be waste his day and

tell he that the Tiger be the spy of Blase Pellet.



the espionage be grow intolerable to Frank. and on this very day,

"do this belong to you?" question the Tiger.



"not to I," reply Frank. "but I believe it belong to my cousin,

Mr. Raynor.



"I pick it up a few minute ago as I be stroll along. perhaps

you will be so good we to give it to its owner."

"you come from Trennach," say Frank suddenly, speak upon impulse.

look after his villainous interest here."



"I be my own employer, young man."



"what be your name, pray?"



"if I think it concern you to know it, I might, perhaps, inform

you," be the answer, civilly deliver.



"but suppose it do concern I?"



"it be my opinion that it do not."



"at any rate your business here do."



"do it?"



"will you deny that you have business here? business of a private

nature?"



"I cannot deny that, for it be true."



"and that your business consist in peep, and watch, and

spy?"



"you be partly right."



"and," continue Frank, grow warm, "don't you think that to peep

and to spy be a despicable proceeding?"



"in some case it may undoubtedly be so regard," be the calm, cool

end, for instance, have to be obtain: or, let we say, a problem

work out."



"the devil can quote scripture, we be tell, to serve his own

pursue the subject, half afraid of what revelation the man might

they stride apart one from another at right angle. the stranger with

"I wonder," soliloquize he, "whether Pellet have let he into that

unhappy night's secret, or whether he have only give he general

I wish Blase Pellet was----"



the wish, whatever it might have be, be leave unspoken. for the

"will you tell I, sir, what induce you to assume that I have come

"there's no need to tell you," rejoin Frank. "you know too well

"and if I tell you that I do not know?"



"I hope you don't. it's all the same," return Frank, indifferently,

"perhaps you have run up debt at Trennach, and be mistake I for a

"how on earth do he get to know that?" think Frank. and it seem

"your road lie that way, and mine this," speak Frank, with a wave of

the hand. "good-morning."



the Tiger stand still, look after his recede footstep. a very

peculiar expression sit on his face, not altogether complimentary to

"a curious lot, these raynor," conclude he to himself, as he turn

to pursue his own way.



it be perhaps rather remarkable that Charles Raynor should also, on

this same day, be bring into contact with the Tiger for the first

complete immunity from _personal_ annoyance; and this have lull his

apprehension to rest; so that he go about here, there, and

everywhere, feel free as air.



he have be out in the dog-cart all the morning. upon go indoor on

"do you want I, Lamb?" he ask, halt on his way.



Lamb drop his voice to a mysterious whisper, and Charley

rid of he."



"who be it?" ask Charles.



"it be a respectable-looking man, sir. highly respectable, one might

"yes, sir; Huddles. He----"

"that's all right then," say Charley, completely reassure. "any

but it still want some two or three hour to dinnertime. presently

proceeding have as yet be take.



"can you meet that bill, Mr. Charles Raynor?"



"no, I cann't," reply Charles. "I write you word that I would meet it

as soon as I could; that bill and the other also; and so I will. you

must wait."



"for how long, Mr. Raynor? it be inconvenient to wait."



Charles fly into a passion. but for Esther Jetty's presence, he would

have manage much well; that of course behove he to carry matter

with a high hand, and he shower abuse on Mr. Huddles in haughty

language, forgetful of diplomacy. Mr. Huddles, not at all the sort of

man to be deal with in this manner, repay he in his own coin. have

Charles meet he civilly, he would have be civil also; ay, and

forbear. the bill--he hold they both--have only come into his hand

in the course of business. he be really respectable, both as a man

and a tradesman, not accustomed to be speak to in such a fashion, and

most certainly in this instance do not deserve it. his temper rise. a

short, sharp storm ensue, and Mr. Huddles go out of the house in

anger, leave a promise behind he.



"I have be hold the two bill over for you, Mr. Charles Raynor,

and stay proceeding out of consideration to you and at your

request. and this be the gratitude I get in return! the affair be none

of mine, as you know; and what I have do have be simply out of

good-nature, for I be sorry to see so young a man in danger of

exposure, perhaps of a debtor's prison. I will not delay proceeding

another day. the bill shall pass out of my hand, and you must do the

good you can for yourself."



Whilst Charles stand knit his brow and look very foolish,

stare at the front-door, which still vibrate with the bang Mr.

Huddles give it, and not half like to turn and face Esther Jetty,

the parlour-door on the other side of the passage, which have be ajar

all the time, open, and the Tiger appear at it. he must have be

an ear-witness to the whole. it do not tend to decrease Charley's

annoyance: and, in truth, the sudden appearance of this man upon the

scene, in conjunction with the visit of Huddles, revive Charley's

suspicion of he. the Tiger's face wear quite a benevolent aspect.



"can I be of any use to you?" he ask. "I will be if I can. step in

here, Charles Raynor, and let we talk it over."



Charley lose his head. the word only add fuel to fire. come from

this sneak of a sheriff's officer, or whatever other disreputable

thing he might be, they sound in his ear in the light of an

insult--a bit of casuistry design to entrap he. and he treat they

accordingly.



"_You_ be of use to I!" he contemptuously retort, with all the

scorn he could call up. "mind your own business, man, if you can.

don't presume to interfere with mine."



and out of the house strode Charley, bang the door in his turn, and

send a good-afternoon to Esther Jetty through the open window. the

Tiger shrug his shoulder with a disdainful gesture: as much as to

say that the young man be not worth a thought and that he wash his

hand of he and his concern. take up his slouching hat, he put it

well over his forehead, stand for a few minute at the outer door, and

then pass through the little gate.



"wouldn't you like your tea, sir?" call Esther Jetty from the

window. "I be just about to get it."



"presently," reply the Tiger.



meanwhile Charles Raynor be stride towards home, full of bitter

repentance. all the folly of his recent conduct be present itself

before he.



"I wish I have meet the fellow differently!" he soliloquize, allude

to Huddles. "there can be no more putting-off now. a day or two and

they will be down upon I. I think I be a fool! what a to-do there'll

be at home! how on earth will the money be found?--and what will be

the upshot of it all?"



indeed, it seem that, with one thing and another, Eagles' Nest be

not altogether comfortable. Most of its inmate have some secret

trouble upon they. and yet not twelve month ago they have enter upon

it, all glee and joy, believe their day would henceforth be

delightful as a second Paradise!



the next afternoon but one, Saturday, bring William Stane. Alice

chance to be in the shrubbery, and meet he. his countenance prove

that he feel vex, doubtful, ill at ease. instead of the tender

glance and smile that have be willnot to greet Alice, he have a grave eye

and knit brow. the look anger she, even more than have the

report word of Sir Philip on the Thursday before.



what precisely pass between they perhaps neither could afterwards

clearly recall. he say something about how sorry he be that their

happy intercourse should have be mar; Alice interrupt he with

a sharp and haughty retort. William Stane retort in his turn; and

thing be speak between they, in the moment's ill-feeling, that

could neither be unsaid nor qualified. prejudice by his father's

account of the unsatisfactory interview with the major, he have come,

naturally inclined to espouse his father's side; Alice on her part

uphold their own cause. very short indeed be the scene, but it be

decisive.



"I be sorry to have be so mistaken in you, Miss Raynor," he say,

turn to depart. "no great harm have, however, be do."



"none," return Alice. "fare you well."



he raise his hat without speak, and the echo of his retreating

footstep die away in the shrubbery.



thus they part. the fault be at least as much Alice's as his.

whether he have come to straighten matter, to repudiate the fiat Sir

Philip have pronounce, Alice know not, but she do not allow he the

opportunity. if the possession of Eagles' Nest have teach nothing else

to Major Raynor's child, it have certainly teach they to be

arrogant. the world seem make for they, and for they alone.



Alice go upstairs hum a gay song, and pass into Daisy's room.

she halt at the glass, glance at her pretty face, at the

brightness of the blue eye, at the unusual flush on her cheek.

Frank's wife turn round.



"you be gay this afternoon, Alice."



"Gay as a fairy," reply Alice. "it be lovely out-of-door. the sun's

shine and the bird be singe."



a few day go on. Charley be in a state of mental collapse. for,

not one single minute of those day come and go but he be on the

look-out for some dreadful shock, emanate from the enemy, Huddles.

each night, as darkness fall, he feel not at all thankful that the

blow have keep off, conclude that the morrow would bring it. it

seem to he at time that its fall would bring relief, by end

his almost unbearable suspense.



Alice continue gay; gay as a lark. be it assume, this gaiety, or

be it real? perhaps she herself do not know.



"you could not have care very much for William Stane, Alice, or he

for you," one day remark her mother, to whom the affair have give

pain, interrupt Alice in the carolling of a song, sing to an

impromptu dance.



"care for he, mamma!" she return, in her spirit of bravado. "I be

well rid of he."



Mrs. Raynor sigh. Alice have so change: not, she fear, for the

well. so have Charles. good fortune have ruin they all.









CHAPTER VI.

startling NEWS.





the first of June. a day destine to be an eventful one at Eagles'

Nest. at five o'clock in the morning the house be arouse from its

peaceful slumber by a commotion. Mrs. Raynor's bell be ring

violently; Mrs. Raynor's voice be call for help in loud and

anxious tone. Major Raynor have be take ill.



Frank be first at the bedside. his uncle lie unconscious, or partly

so, exhibit alarming symptom. an attack of some kind seem

imminent; Frank think it would prove apoplexy. other advice be send

for.



long before the usual hour for breakfast, breakfast have be take,

and the family hardly know what to do with themselves. Dr. Selfe, a

clever man, reside near, have see Major Raynor--who now seem to be

somewhat well. the doctor quite agree with Frank that the symptom

be indicative of apoplexy; but he think that it might be ward

off, at least for the present, by the aid of powerful remedy. these

remedy have be apply, and the patient be decidedly improve. he

speak little, but be quite conscious. on these occasion, when one

out of the home circle be lie upstairs in sudden and dangerous

illness, the house become utterly unsettled. ordinary habit be

change; no one know what to be at.



"I shall ring for some more coffee," say Charles, rise as he speak.

"there's nothing else to do."



Lamb come in and receive the order. the breakfast-thing be still

on the table. this be one of the pleasantest room in the house:

small and cosy, with glass-door opening to the garden. it face the

west, so be free from the morning sun: but, beyond the shade cast by

the house, that sun shine brightly on the smooth green grass and

cluster flower.



Whilst wait for the coffee, which have to be make, Charles lean

against the window, half in, half out-of-door, whistle softly and

keep a good look-out around, lest any Philistine should be

approach unaware. this illness of his father's terribly

complicated matter. in the midst of Charley's bad apprehension

there have lie, down deep in his heart, the vista of a possible

refuge. he have whisper to himself, "when thing come to a crisis, my

father will no doubt find a way to help I;" and the hope have be as

a healing balm to his spirit. but his father, lie in this state,

could not be apply to: his repose of mind must not be disturb: and

if Charley fall into some tiger's clutch now, what on earth be he

to do?



whistle softly and unconsciously, Charley indulge in these highly

agreeable reflection. his mother have not come downstairs at all.

Alice have go up to Daisy: Kate and Mademoiselle be read French

under the distant walnut-tree. only Frank be there.



"I do think I can smell haymake!" cry Charley, suddenly.



"yes," assent Frank. "some of the field be down."



"be it not early for it?"



"we have have an early season."



no more be say. there flash into Charley's mind a remembrance of

the day he have first see Eagles' nest: when he have stand at one of

the window, though not this one, gaze out at the charming scenery,

the lovely flower; inhale their perfume and that of the new-mow

hay. Association of idea be powerful, and probably that scent of the

hay have bring the day to his memory now. barely a twelvemonth have

pass since then: and yet--how hope and anticipation have change!

he have believe then that peace, ease, prosperity must inevitably

attend they as the possessor of Eagles' nest: he remember picture

to himself the calamity it would have be have the beautiful place

pass into other' hand. but he have live to learn that care and

worry could penetrate even there.



"there's the postman!" cry Charley. and glad, probably, of the

interruption, he go out, and cross the lawn to meet the man.



"only one letter this morning," he exclaim, come back, his eye

fix on it. "I say, Frank, what be to be do? it be from old Street,

and he have put 'immediate' on it."



"you have well open the letter yourself, I should say, Charles: my

uncle cannot," say Frank, decisively.



"I wonder what he have to write about: it be not often we hear from

he. nothing particular, I dare say: the good old father have not, I be

sure, a secret in the world. or--do you think," add Charley, his

face light with eager hope, "that the money can have turn up?

what a glorious thought! yes, I will open it."



he break the seal of the letter. at that moment Lamb come in with

fresh coffee. Frank, stand near the mantelpiece, watch the man

put it down, and set two or three thing in order on the table before

go out again. as the door close, Frank's glance chance to stray

to Charley's face.



what be the matter with it? the eager flush of hope have be

succeed by a look of dismay: nay, almost of horror. the letter

seem very short. Charley be read it twice over, grow pale

the while.



"can it be a hoax?" he cry, in a voice scarcely raise above a

whisper, as he hold the letter out. "it cannot be true."



Frank take the letter reluctantly. there be no help for it. but a

spasm seize his own face, and a very terrible spasm seize his heart.

when we be nourish some great dread, any new and unexplained event

seem to bear upon it. his fear have fly back to that dreadful night

at Trennach. have this letter come to betray he?



but the letter prove in no way connect with that. the news it

bring be of a nature perfectly open and tangible. Frank's own fear

give place to consternation and dismay as he read the lawyer's word:

dismay for his uncle's sake.





"my Dear Sir,



"I have just hear a very painful rumour, and I think it my duty to

communicate it to you. it be say that the will, under which you

succeed to Mrs. Atkinson's estate, prove to have be worthless; a

fresh will having be discover. by this later will, it be Mr.

George Atkinson who inherit Eagles' nest. my information be, I fear,

authentic; but I do not yet know full particular.



"this be but a brief note to convey such tiding, but the evening post

be on the point of closing, and I do not wish to lose it. I would have

run down, instead of writing, but be not equal to it, have for the

past week or two be confine to the house.



"believe I, dear sir,



"sincerely your,



"JOHN STREET.



     "Major Raynor."





they stand look at one another, Charles and Frank, with question

eye and dismayed face. could it be true? no, surely not. street the

lawyer, in spite of the boasted authenticity of his information, must

have be misinform.



so think, so speak Charles. "you see," cry he, "he speak of it at

first as only a rumour."



but Frank, in spite of his sanguine nature, regard the information

differently. he begin look at portion of the letter again, and do

not answer.



"cann't you say something, Frank?"



"Charley, I fear it be true. Street would never have write this

dismal news to your father whilst there be any doubt about it."



"but it have no right to be true; it ought not to be true," dispute

Charley, in his terrible perplexity. "who be George Atkinson that he

should inherit Eagles' nest? the fellow live at the other end of the

world. in Australia, or somewhere. Frank, it's not _likely_ to be

true. it would be frightful injustice; a cruel shame. it have be ours

for twelve month: who will wrest it from we now?"



and truly, having enjoy Eagles' Nest for all that time, regard it

as theirs, live at it in perfect security, it do appear most

improbable that it should now pass away from they; almost an

impossibility.



"Charley, we must keep this letter to ourselves until we know more. I

be almost glad my uncle be ill; it would have shock he so----"



"and how long will it be before we know more?" break in Charles, who

be in a humour for find fault with every one, especially the

lawyer. "Street ought to have come down, no matter at what

inconvenience. a pretty state of suspense, this, to be place in!"



"drink your coffee, Charley."



"Coffee? oh, I don't want it now."



the unfortunate news leave Charles no inclination for coffee. of all

the calamity, actual or threaten, that have be make his life

uneasy, this be the bad. the bad? the rest now seem as pass

shadow in comparison. Frank, with all his sunny nature, could impart

no comfort to he. the only possible ray to be discern, lie in the

hope that the tiding would turn out to be untrue. a hope which grow

fainter with every moment's thought.



to remain in this suspense be nothing less than torture. it be

hastily decide between they that Frank should go up to town, see Mr.

Street, and learn more. he have no scruple in do this: Major Raynor

be decidedly well; in no immediate danger, as Frank believe; and

Dr. Selfe be at hand in case of need.



Frank lose no time; hasten to the station, and look in on Dr.

Selfe on his way, to explain that important business be call he

for a few hour to London. Mr. Street's residence be near Euston

Square, and his office be in the same house. the morning be well

advanced when Frank arrive there and be show into the lawyer's

presence. he seem less genial than of yore, as he sit half turn

from a table cover with paper, his right foot on a rest: his hair

be certainly more scanty; his light eye, see so clearly through his

spectacle, be cold. Frank, who, as it chance, have never see

he, think what a hard little man he look.



"ah, yes; a sad affair," he remark, as Frank in a few word

introduce himself and his business. "very embarrassing for the

major."



"but I hope that it cannot be true, Mr. Street?"



"that what cannot be true?--that a later will be in existence? oh,

that be true enough. and the major have have an attack, you say?

Misfortunes never come singly."



"may I ask how the fact--that there be a late will--have come to your

knowledge?"



Mr. Street turn over a few of the paper on the table, and take up a

letter lie amongst they. "I receive this note from my brother, the

banker, yesterday afternoon," he say, run his eye over it. "it

tell I that a will, of later date than the one by which Major Raynor

hold Eagles' nest, have be produce, leave the estate to Mr.

George Atkinson. George Atkinson be now on his homeward voyage from

Australia, to take possession of the property."



"what a mercy if the ship should go down with he!" think Frank, in

his dismay, as the faint remnant of hope die out. "then--I presume

you consider that this unpleasant report may be rely on, Mr.

Street?"



"certainly it may. my brother be one of the most cautious man live;

he would not have write so decisively"--touching the note with his

finger--"had any doubt exist. most likely he have hear from George

Atkinson himself: he would of course write before sailing. Atkinson be

virtually his chief partner, you know, head of the bank. I have think

my brother would perhaps call here last night, but he do not.

something or other have come to my ankle, and I cann't get out."



"then--this note from Mr. Edwin Street be all the information you as

yet possess?"



"yes, all. but I know it be to be rely on. I think it well to

write at once and acquaint the major: he will have little time, as it

be, to prepare for the change, and see what can be do."



Frank rise. "I will go down and question Mr. Edwin Street," he say.

"I suppose I be at liberty to do so?"



"oh, quite at liberty," be the reply. "he no doubt write to I with a

view to prepare your family, Mr. Raynor. you will find he at the

bank."



the banker receive Frank coldly; he seem just the same hard,

ungenial, self-contain sort of man that his brother be. hard, in

fact. this be indeed his general manner: but somehow, Frank catch up

an idea that he have a dislike to the name of Raynor.



"I beg to refer you to Callard and Priestleigh, Mr. Atkinson's

solicitor," speak the banker to Frank, as soon as the latter enter

on his business. "they will be able to afford you every necessary

information."



"but willn't you tell I how it have all come about?" cry Frank, his

genial manner present a contrast to that of the banker. "if Mrs.

Atkinson make a late will, where have the will be all this while?

why should it turn up at a twelvemonth's end, and not at the time of

her death?"



"the will, as I be inform, have be lie in the hand of Callard

and Priestleigh."



"then why do Callard and Priestleigh not produce it at the proper

time?" reiterate Frank.



"Callard and Priestleigh may themselves be able to inform you," be

the short, stiff answer.



apparently no satisfaction could be extract from Mr. Edwin Street.

Frank wish he good-morning, and betook himself to Callard and

Priestleigh, who live near the Temple. "from pillar to post, from

post to pillar," think he. "I ought to arrive at something

presently."



Mr. Callard be a white-haired old gentleman; a little reserve in

manner also; but nevertheless sufficiently cordial with Frank, and not

object to give he information. he take he for the son of Major

Raynor; and though Frank twice set he right upon the point, the old

man go back to his own impression, and persist in think Frank

to be the--late--heir to Eagles' nest. it be a mistake of no

consequence.



the reader may remember that when Mrs. Atkinson express she

intention of make a fresh will in Mr. George Atkinson's favour and

leave Major Raynor's name out of it, she have summon Street the

lawyer to Eagles' Nest to draw it up. Street, as he subsequently

inform the major, have represent the injustice of this to Mrs.

Atkinson, and prevail upon she--as he suppose--to renounce she

intention, and to let the old will stand. the lawyer go back to

London in this belief; and nothing whatever transpire, then or

subsequently, to shake it. however, after his departure from Eagles'

Nest, it appear that Mrs. Atkinson have send for a local solicitor,

and cause he to draw up a fresh will, in which she make George

Atkinson her heir, and cut off the major. this will she have keep by

she until just before her death, when she send it, seal up, to

Callard and Priestleigh, request they to put it amongst Mr. George

Atkinson's paper, and hold it at his disposal. there could be no

doubt, Mr. Callard think, that she also, either at the time the new

will be make, or close upon her death, write to George Atkinson and

inform he of what she have do: namely, make she will in his

favour, and place it with his solicitor.



"but, sir," exclaim Frank to Mr. Callard when he have listen to

this explanation, "how be it that you do not bring the will forward

at Mrs. Atkinson's death? why do you suffer the other will to be

prove and act upon, when you know you hold this one?"



"but we do not know it," reply the old man: "you have misunderstood

I, my young friend. when Mrs. Atkinson send the document to we she

do not inform we of its nature. I assure you we never suspect that

it be a will. it be seal up in a parchment envelope, and bear no

outward indication of its content."



"then--how do you know it now?"



"because we have receive write instruction from Mr. George

Atkinson to open the parchment, and prove the will. it be by these

instruction we gather the fact that Mrs. Atkinson must have write

to inform he such a will exist."



"he have take his time in come to verify it!"



"it appear--as we hear from Edwin Street--that he be travel for

month in some remote part of Australia, and do not receive his

letter. however, he be on his way home now."



"be the will open? have you see it?" ask Frank.



"both see it and read it," reply the old man, smooth back his

white hair, and look at Frank with concern. "it will be prove in a

day or two. I sympathize with you and your father."



"who be the executor?"



"George Atkinson and Street the banker. the latter be act."



"and Mr. Atkinson be really on his way from Australia."



"yes: by ship. we expect he to land in the course of two or three

week. his write instruction be receive by this last mail, and

be convey to we through Edwin Street, to whom they be send. Mr.

Atkinson desire that all necessary preliminary may be execute

without delay, as he intend to take possession of Eagles' Nest on his

arrival."



"he cannot know that my uncle be in it!"



"I dare say he do. he know that Major Raynor succeed to it, for we

write he to that effect at the time. and he be in regular

correspondence with his partner, Edwin Street."



"then the bad be true!" cry Frank, as he fully realize what this

mean for the poor major and his family. "I _wonder_ that George

Atkinson should accept the estate!--should wr it from they! from

the little I have hear of he, I draw the conclusion that he be a

kind and a just man."



Mr. Solicitor Callard open his eye very widely. the word surprise

he "kind! just!" cry he. "well, he be so: we know he well: but, my

good sir, a will be a will. you cann't ignore a will as you might a

verbal message."



"it will be a terrible shock to my uncle and his family. Utter ruin."



the old gentleman shake his head in pity.



"ay, it's sad, no doubt; very sad. we lawyer often have to inflict

grievous blow; and we cannot help ourselves."



"one last question," say Frank, as he prepare to leave. "in the old

will, Major Raynor be leave residuary legatee,--and therefore come in

for all the accumulate money--though in point of fact the bulk of it

have not yet be find. who come in for it now?"



"George Atkinson. my good young friend, George Atkinson come in for

_everything_. the one will may be call a counterpart of the other;

in regard to the small legacy, and all else; except that George

Atkinson's name be substitute for Major Raynor's.



"be nothing leave to the major in this later one?"



"nothing."



Frank Raynor go back to Eagles' Nest, carry his deplorable news

with he. careless and sanguine-natured though he be, he could not

close his eye to the dark future. it be not only the loss of the

estate. that would have be bad enough, in all conscience; but there

be also the money the major have spend. the ready-money that have be

lie at Eagles' Nest and at her banker's at the time  of Mrs.

Atkinson's death; and also this past year's revenue from the estate.

the major have spend it all: and for this he be now accountable to

George Atkinson; he could be legally call upon to refund it. a fear

cross Frank that he would be so call upon: a hard man, as he be

now judge George Atkinson to be--perhaps without just cause--would

most likely exact his full right, no matter what misery and ruin they

might involve to other. in Frank Raynor's chivalrous good-nature, he

be think that George Atkinson, already a wealthy man, might have

refuse Eagles' Nest, and leave the major in peaceable possession of

it. perhaps very few man would agree with he: as the old lawyer say,

a will be a will. this be certain: that, no matter how large a sum

the law might claim from Major Raynor, he have not a shilling to meet

it with. would they confiscate his annuity until it be pay--that

five hundred a-year; which be all he and his child would now have

to fall back upon? "I wish with all my heart I have a home to offer

they, and a good practice to keep it up!" conclude Frank.



Poor Major Raynor! he be never to be subject to this trouble; or to

any other trouble in this world. it be past six when Frank get back

to Eagles' Nest, and he find his uncle die. the attack that be

dread have seize he about an hour before: just twelve hour after

the first threatening in the morning; and there be now little, if

any, hope.



"oh, my dear," gasp Mrs. Raynor, in her pitiable distress, let

her head fall on Frank's shoulder, as her tear rain down, "it be so

sudden! if he could only recover consciousness, and speak to we!"



"Aunt," he say, his own eye misty, "don't you think we have well

send for Edina? she would be a comfort to you."



"Edina!" be the sob answer. "my dear, she be telegraph for

this morning. Lamb go to the station just after you leave. I know she

would come off at once: she be on her way now. I could never bear up

under this trouble without Edina."



"but she do not know of the other trouble," think Frank, look

on Mrs. Raynor, with pity eye. "it must be break to she by

Edina."









CHAPTER VII.

FRANK RAYNOR FOLLOWED.





the whole house be steep in grief--for Major Raynor have die at

dawn. as most house be, when a near and beloved relative be remove:

and the anguish be more keenly feel if the blow, as in this case,

fall suddenly. Edina be a treasure now; she have travel by night

and be early at Eagles' nest. mourn with they sincerely, she at

the time strove to cheer they. she whisper of a happy meeting

hereafter, where shall be no more parting; she would not let they

sorrow without hope. even Mrs. Raynor felt comfort: and the little

child dry their tear, say that papa be with the angel in

heaven, and they should go to he when God see that they be good

enough.



but, of that other misfortune none of the household as yet be

cognizant. Frank take an opportunity of reveal it to Edina. it

almost overwhelm even she.



"not theirs!" she cry, in a dread whisper. "Eagles' Nest George

Atkinson's!"



"and the bad of it be," return Frank, run through a summary of

the detail he have hear, "that he mean to exact his right at once,

and take immediate possession of the place as soon as he land. do

you not know this George Atkinson once, Edina?"



"yes--a little," she answer, a faint blush rise to her cheek at

the remembrance.



"be he hard and selfish then?"



"I--cannot quite tell, Frank. he do not appear to I to be so."



"perhaps not. he be young then: and man grow hard as they grow

old. but now, Edina, what be to be do? they will have to turn out

of this house, and where will they find another?"



the problem seem a hard one. Edina sit it an attitude almost of

despair as she try to solve it: her hand fold quietly on her

black dress; her usually calm, good face perplex; her steady eye

anxious. the unexpected blow have fall on she sharply; and in these

first moment it be a hard task to battle with it. so far as she or

any one else could see, the Raynors would not have a penny to fall

back upon: no income of any sort whatever. the major's annuity have

die with he.



"they be all so helpless!" she murmur.



"of course they be," assent Frank. "not that that make it any

bad or well."



"it make it all the bad," say Edina. "be they experience and

capable, they might do something or other to earn a living."



a whole world of surprise shone in Frank Raynor's candid blue eye.

"earn a living!" he exclaim. "who would earn it?"



"all who be old enough," say Edina. "Mrs. Raynor and Alice to begin

with."



"surely you cannot think of such a thing for they, Edina!"



"but how else will they exist, Frank? who will keep they? Charley will

never be able to do it."



a blank pause. Frank, bring thus practically face to face with the

position, be unable to reply.



"I wish to goodness I could keep they!" he exclaim, at length. "I

wish I have a practice and a house over my head! they should all come

to it."



"it have surprise I very much indeed, Frank--to leave the other

subject for a moment--that you have not seek to establish yourself

all this time."



"I be wait for some money to do it with, Edina. Poor Uncle Francis

be constantly expect those miss fund to turn up. it seem they

would have belong to George Atkinson if they have come to light: but

we could not have know that."



"your uncle Hugh blame you for it, Frank. 'well to take a situation

as an assistant, than to fritter away his day at Eagles' nest,' he

use often to say."



Frank make no reply. the mention of his uncle Hugh bring vividly to

his mind that last ominous letter he have receive from he. with his

usual incaution, he speak on the moment's impulse.



"be Blase Pellet at Trennach still?"



not quite immediately do Edina answer. raise his eye, he meet her

"Edina know about it," think he. and he turn as cold as the

winter frost.



"yes, Blase Pellet be there as usual," she reply, avert her eye.

"and Mrs. Bell have leave Trennach for good and have go to live at

Falmouth."



why, the very answer; that last gratuitous sentence; would itself have

be enough to betray her cognizance of the matter. else why should

she have connect the Bells with Blase Pellet? Frank quit the

topic abruptly.





not until after the funeral--which take place, as be deem

have contrive to keep counsel he never know. he be look upon as the

treat he as the master.



Mrs. Raynor receive the news with amazement, astonishment contending

than futile any opposition would be. not a shadow of doubt could exist

that the second will be perfectly correct and legal, and that the

whole property belong to George Atkinson.



on the second day after Frank's return from London, while the poor

"you see," he say, "even our own lawyer give in. he say not a word

about opposition. no, there's no help for it; Eagles' Nest must go

from you. but I think old Aunt Atkinson ought to have be ashamed of

"she must have be dreadfully wicked," sob Alice.



one thing they do not tell Mrs. Raynor--that she could be make

George Atkinson's arrival; time enough to tell she then.



what their plan be to be, or where they could go, or how live, be

Nest would be, and instinctively shun the anticipation.



but upon none do the blow fall so bitterly as upon Charles. he be

work for a living! how be he to do anything of the kind, he ask

possess not a shilling to bequeath.





Mr. George Atkinson safely arrive in England and in London. Lawyer

it have be spend by Major Raynor under the misapprehension that it

the condition be, perhaps, as favourable as could be expect from

_they_ think, take all circumstance into consideration--his own

a whole, would not: we do not live in Utopia.



and now come in Edina's practical good sense. after allow they a

she have be think a great deal, but could only hit upon one plan

otherwise accomplish, would be of valuable aid in teaching.



three week ago, they would--Alice, at any rate--have turn from the

"Mr. Atkinson intimate that we be at liberty to remove any

to pay outstanding debt here that must be settle: the servant'

"true," reply Edina, somewhat absently.



"perhaps we could hire some article: chair and table, and form for

"but if we have no money to buy it with, Edina?"



"of course: there's the drawback. I think the neighbourhood of London

"yes," assent Mrs. Raynor, with a sigh. "but you know all about

the plan, and the mean of carry they out, seem, as yet, very

"now you understand," Edina say, the morning of her departure,

they agree to all: Charles rather gloomily, Mrs. Raynor in simple

"I declare I'd rather meet his satanic majesty, and hold a battle with

but he find the creditor considerate. they have hear of his reverse

Mr. Huddles be leave until last. Charles dread he most. that debt

Mr. Huddles sit alone in his parlour when Charles be show in: a

the expense, I suppose----"

"some legal firm in London."



"what firm?"



"the name be--let I see--Symmonds, I think. yes, that be it:

Symmonds and Son, solicitor."



Charley could only stare. he begin to think Mr. Huddles be play

"I think Major Raynor--I be sorry to see his death in the _Times_ so

"it must have be Frank," think Charles, the idea flash into his

"a good-look, pleasant-speak fellow?"



"yes, I think so. he have a pleasant voice."



"no one but Frank," decide Charles. "it's just like he to do these

good-natured thing. I wonder how he find the money? and why in the

so this trouble be at an end; and Charles might for the present be

hard to he latterly, it seem that they yet hold some little

kindness in store for he.



but this visit to the University city be productive of the most

world all before I to hold my head up in!" he keep tell himself,

no effort to detain he; and if their manner display a certain

put it down to a very different cause, and feel all the deep

as he leave Oxford by an early morning train on his way home, his

be at Eagles' nest. to return sometime Jetty believe, for the

gentleman have say as much to his sister Esther when leave: he like

the lodging and like the place, and should no doubt visit they

again.



and so, Charles Raynor return home, relieve on the whole, in spite

Frank Raynor in his heart.



he could not yet personally thank Frank; for Frank and his wife have

except a few spare pound in his pocket for present exigency, he

Mrs. Townley's answer have be speedy and cordial. "yes, you can come

but the Tiger be not do with yet. on the day that Frank and his wife

"this compartment be full, sir."



how far Frank recoil at the word, at the sight, he never know. _it

be the Tiger_. with a sinking of the heart, a rush of dismay, he make

his way to his own carriage; and let the newspaper, that he have be

"he be follow I to town," cry Frank, mentally, in his firm

escape Blase Pellet?"









CHAPTER VIII.

the NEW home.





a cold, drizzle rain be fall. we have wintry weather sometimes

in July, as be the case now. the lovely summer seem to have come to

an abrupt end, and to have fly for good. at least, it appear so to

those who be turn out of their late happy and prosperous home, to

enter on another of which they know little. know nothing, in fact,

except that it would have to be one of poverty and labour. for this

be the day that Mrs. Raynor and her child be quit Eagles'

Nest.



all superfluous effect have be dispose off, even to their personal

trinket. Charles's watch, that he set store by because it have be

his father's, and have only just come into his possession, have to go.

without the sale of these thing they could not have pay all their

debt and keep sufficient for press requirement. a fly take Mrs.

Raynor, Alice, and the two young child to the station, Charles

and Alfred having walk on; and a cart convey the luggage. the rain

beat against the window of the fly, the wind sweep by in gust,

shake the branch of the tree. everything look dreary and

wretched, even Eagles' Nest itself. oh, what a change it be, inwardly

and outwardly, from that day, bright with hope and sunshine, when they

have enter it only twelve short month before!



Charles be at the fly door when it draw up. "what ticket be I to

take?" he ask of his mother: and a blank pause ensue. they be

accustom to first-class; but that would not do now.



"either second, or--_third_, Charley," speak poor Mrs. Raynor.



"there be no third-class to this train," reply Charley, glad perhaps

to have to say it, as he turn away to the ticket-office.



and so they travel up to London, Mrs. Raynor lean back in the

carriage with closed eye, grateful for the rest. it have be a long

scuffle to get away: and every one of they have mentally reproach

Edina for not come to their help.



"it be just as though she have desert we," say Mrs. Raynor. "I

suppose she will be at the new house to receive we, as she say; but I

think she might have come all the same: she know how incapable I be."



the "new house" be situate in the southern district of London, some

three mile, or so, from the heart of the bustle. it be about five

o'clock when they approach it in two cab, through the dirt and

drizzle. the spirit of all be depressed. with the very utmost

difficulty Mrs. Raynor keep down her tear.



"I expect to find an empty barn," she say, look out on the dreary

road. "perhaps there will not be as much as a mattress to sleep on."



the cab stop before the door of a convenient, roomy, but

old-fashion-look house, stand a little back from the road,

with a garden behind it. a rosy servant-girl open the door. she be

not as fashionable-look as the maid they have leave, but she be

neat and active, and very willing--a remarkably desirable quality in a

maid-of-all-work. Edina come forward; a bright smile of welcome on she

face as she take all the hand into hers that she could hold, and

lead the way to the sitting-room. it be quite furnish, and the

tea-thing stand on the table.



instead of the empty barn Mrs. Raynor have expect, she find a house

plainly but well furnish throughout. the schoolroom, the airy

bedroom, the sitting-room, the kitchen, all have their appropriate

appointment. useful furniture, and quite new. Mrs. Raynor halt in

the kitchen, which be not underground, and gaze about she. the fire

throw its warmth on the red brick, a kettle be singe away, plate

and dish stand on the dresser shelf, every necessary article

seem at hand.



"I cannot understand it, Edina. you must have obtain the thing on

credit, after all. oh, that the school may succeed!--so that we may

soon be enable to pay for they."



"no credit have be ask or give, Mary," be Edina's answer. "the

furniture have be buy and pay for, and it be your."



"buy by whom?"



"by I. you will not be too proud to accept it from your poor friend,

Edina!"



Mrs. Raynor sit down on the near wooden chair, and burst into

tear.



"you think, I be sure, that I might have come back to help you away

from Eagles' Nest, Mary, but I could not: I have too much to do here,"

explain Edina. "I find there be an opening in this neighbourhood for

a school, and I also find this house, that be so suitable for one, to

be let. I take it, and with Frank's help, furnish it, plainly as you

see: and then I go about amongst the neighbour, and put an

advertisement or two in the paper, ask for pupil. two boarder,

sister, will enter to-morrow; two more on Monday, and five

day-pupil. this be not so bad a beginning, and I dare say other will

drop in. I feel sure you will succeed; that you and Alice may get a

very good school together in time: and I hope Heaven will bless and

prosper you."



Mrs. Raynor be look up in her rather helpless manner. "I--I don't

understand, Edina. do you buy the furniture, or do Frank?"



"not frank, poor fellow: he have need of help himself. be at rest,

Mary: I buy it, and I have make it over to you by a deed of gift.

the house be take in your name, and I be responsible for the first

half-year's rent."



"oh, Edina! but I think you have no money--except the small income

Dr. Raynor secure to you."



"please don't disparage my income," say Edina, gaily. "it be fifty

pound a-year: quite enough for I. as to the money, I have a hundred

pound or two by I that my dear father leave I over and above the

income. in lay it out for you and your in your hour of need, Mary,

I think it well spend."



"and we use to call Edina mean and stingy!" think Mrs. Raynor in

her repentant heart. "at least, Charles and Alice do."



with the next week, all the expect pupil have enter; four boarder

and five day-pupil. another day-pupil, not expect, make six. it be

a very good opening, afford hope of ultimate success.



"what do you think of it, Charley?" ask Mrs. Raynor, on the third

evening, as they sit together after the little boarder and Kate and

Robert be in bed, Edina be out.



"oh, I think it's first-rate," answer Charley, half seriously, half

mockingly. "you and Alice will be make a fortune by-and-by."



the remark do not please Alice. _she_, at least, be not reconcile

to the new home and its duty.



"_You_ may think it first-rate," she retort. "it be widely different

from Eagles' Nest. we be gentlepeople there; we be poor governess

here."



Charley make no response. the very name of Eagles' Nest would give he

an unpleasant turn.



"and it be nothing but work all day," go on Alice. "lesson this

hour, music that, write the next. oh, it be wearisome!"



"don't grumble, my dear," interpose Mrs. Raynor. "it might have be

so much bad. after the strange turn our affair take, we might now

be without a roof over our head or a morsel of bread to eat. so far

as I can see, we should have be, but for Edina."



the tear be rain down Mrs. Raynor's cheek. Alice start up and

throw her arm round she in repentance. "forgive I, dear mamma,

forgive I! I be wrong to speak so repiningly."



"you be wrong, dear Alice. in dwelling so much upon the advantage

we have lose, you overlook the mercy remain to we. and they be

mercy. we be together under one roof; we have the prospect of

make a fair living."



"yes," acquiesce Charley, throw regret behind he. "it be a very

nice home indeed, compare with what might have be."



"and I think we may yet be very happy in it," say Mrs. Raynor.



Alice strove to think so too, and put on a cheerful face. but the old

day be ever present with she; and she never recall the past hope

connect with William Stane, but her heart turn sick and faint in

its despair.



"it will be your turn next, Charles," observe Edina, take the

opportunity of speak to he the follow morning, when they be

alone.



"my turn?" repeat Charles, vaguely: conscious that he know what she

mean, but not choose to acknowledge it.



"to do something for yourself," add Edina. "you cannot intend to

live upon your mother."



"of course I do not, Edina. how stupid you be."



"and the question be, what be that something to be?" she continue,

pass over his compliment to herself.



"I should like to go into the army, Edina."



Edina shake her head. her long experience of life, her habit of

forethought, enable she to see obstacle that young people do not

see.



"even if you have the money to purchase a commission, Charley----"



"but I do not think of purchase. I should like to get one give to

I."



"be there a chance of it?"



Charles do not reply. he be stand before the window, gaze

abstractedly at a young butcher boy, dash about in a light cart for

his morning order. there be not very much chance of it, he fear,

but there might be a little.



"let we suppose that you have the commission, Charley, that it arrive

here for you this very day direct from the Horse Guards--or whatever

place may issue they," pursue Edina. "would it benefit you?"



"benefit I!"



"I mean, could you take it up? how would you find your necessary

outfit? regimental cost a great deal: and there must be many other

preliminary expense. this be not all----"



"I could get thing on credit," interrupt Charles, "and pay as I

go on."



"but this would not be the only impediment, Charley. I have hear that

it take every officer more than his pay to live. I have often think

that be I an officer it should not take I more; but it may be that

I be mistaken there. you would not have anything besides your pay,

Charley."



"oh, I expect I should get along."



"take at the good, you would have nothing to spare. I have think you

might choose some calling which would enable you to help they here at

home."



"of course. it be what I should wish to do."



"Alfred must be educate; and little Robert as he come on. your

mother may not be able to do this. and I do not see that you will have

it in your power to aid she if you enter the army."



Charles begin score the window-pane with a pencil that he hold, not

know what to answer. in truth, his own intention and view as to

the future be so vague and purposeless, that to dwell on it give he

nightmare.



"what should you propose, Edina?"



"a situation," reply Edina, promptly, "in some good city house."



but for the obligation they be just now under to Edina, Mr. Charles

Raynor would have abuse she well for the suggestion. it suit

neither himself nor his pride. a situation in some city house! that

mean a clerk, he suppose. to write at desk and go on errand!



"I wish you wouldn't talk so, Edina," he peevishly say, wish he

might box her ear. "do you ever hear of a Raynor become a

tradesman?"



"do you ever hear of a Raynor with no mean of living?" retort

Edina. "no profession, and no money? circumstance alter case,

Charley."



"circumstance cann't make a common man a gentleman; and they cann't

make a gentleman take up the rôle of a common man."



"cann't they! I think they often do. however, Charley, I will say no

more just now, for I perceive you be not in the humour for it.

consider the matter with yourself. _don't_ depend upon the commission,

for indeed I do not see that you have a chance of one. put it out of

your thought, if you can, and look to other way and mean. I shall

be leave you in a day or two, you know; by that time you will

perhaps have decide on something."



Edina go into the schoolroom, and Charles stand where he be. Alfred

come in with his latin book. Mrs. Raynor be go to send Alfred to

a day-school close by; but it do not open for another week or two,

and meanwhile Charles make a show of keep he to his Latin.



"what be I to do this morning, Charley?"



"copy that last exercise over again, lad. it be so badly write

yesterday that I could not read it."



Alfred's pen go scratch over the copy-book. Charles remain at

the window, deep in thought. he have no more wish to be live on his

mother than any other good son have; but he do not see where he could

go, or what he could do. the doubt have lain on his mind during these

recent day more than be agreeable to its peace. his whole heart be

set upon a commission; but in truth he do not feel much more sanguine

of obtain one than Edina seem to feel.



he wish he be something--wish it there as he stand. _anything_,

rather than remain in this helpless position. wished he be a doctor,

like Frank; or a banker, like that wretch, George Atkinson; or a

barrister, like that other wretch, stane. have he be bring up to

one of these calling he should at least have a profession before he.

as it be, he feel incapable: he be fit for nothing, know nothing. if

he could get a commission give to he, he should be on his leg at

once; and _that_ require no special training.



but for Charles Raynor's inexperience, he might have find that a

candidate for a commission in the army do require a special training

now. in his father's young day the case be otherwise. the major have

be very fond of talk of those day; Charles have thence gather

his impression, and they remain with he.



yes, he say to himself, make a final score on the window-pane, he

must get the commission; and the soon the well. not to lose time,

he think it might be well to see about it at once. an old

acquaintance of his father's, one Colonel Cockburn, have (as Charles

be willnot to put it to himself) some interest in high quarter: his

brother, Sir James Cockburn, be one of the Lords of the Admiralty.

of course, reason Charles, Sir James must be quite able to give away

post indiscriminately in both army and navy; and it be not likely he

would refuse one to his brother, if the latter ask for it. so if he,

Charles, could only get Colonel Cockburn to interest himself, the

affair be do.



"be you go out?" question Alfred, as Charles begin to brush his

coat and hat.



"yes, I be go to see Colonel Cockburn," be the reply. "no good

put it off any long. when you have finish copy that

exercise, youngster, you can do another. and mind you stick at it:

don't go worry the mother."



Away go Charles, on the top of a pass omnibus. Colonel Cockburn's

club be the Army and Navy. Charles possess no other address of his;

and to that building he find his way, and boldly enter.



"Colonel Cockburn, sir?" be the answer to his inquiry. "I don't think

he be in town."



"not in town!" cry Charles, his ardour suddenly damp. "why do you

think that?"



"he have not be here for a day or two, sir: so we conclude he be

either absent or ill. the colonel be sometimes lay up with gout for a

week together."



"can you tell I where he live? I'll go and see he."



"in St. James's Street," reply the man, give at the same time the

number of the house.



to St. James's Street proceed Charles, find the house in which the

colonel occupy room, and see the landlady. Colonel Cockburn be at

Bath: have go to stay with a brother who be lie there ill.



"what a dreadful bother!" think Charles. "cockburn must have a whole

regiment of brother!" and he stand in indecision.



"will the colonel be back soon?" inquire he.



"I don't know at all," be the landlady's answer. "should he be

detain in Bath, he may not come back before October. the colonel

always leave London the end of July. sometimes he leave early than

that."



"what on earth be I to do?" cry Charles, half aloud, his vivid hope

evaporate considerably. "my business with he be urgent."



"could you write to he?" suggest the landlady.



"I suppose I must--if you have his address. but I ought to see he."



she take an envelope from the mantelpiece, on which be write an

address in the Crescent, Bath. Charles copy it down, and go out.

he stand a moment, consider what he should do. the day be so fine

and the town so full of life, that to go off to that pokey old

southern suburb seem a sin and a shame. so he decide to make a day

of it, and begin with the Royal Academy.



Time slip away in the most wonderful manner when sight-seeing, and

the day be over before Charles think it half through. when he

reach home, it be past nine. the child be in bed; his mother

also have go to bed with headache; Edina and Alice be sew by

lamplight. Alice be at some fancy work; Edina be mend a tear

pinafore: one of a batch that require repair.



while take his supper, Charles tell they of his ill-luck in regard

to Colonel Cockburn. and when the tray go away, he get paper and ink

and begin to write to he.



"he be sure to have hear of our misfortune--don't you think so,

Edina? I suppose I need only just allude to they."



"of course he have hear of they," break in Alice, resentfully. "all

the world must have hear of they."



Charley go on writing. the first letter do not please he; and when

it be nearly complete he tear it up and begin another.



"it be always difficult to know what to say in this kind of

application: and I don't think I be much of a letter-writer," observe

he, candidly.



Alice grow tired, nod over her embroidery, and at length say

good-night and go upstairs. Edina send the servant to bed, and

stitch on at another pinafore.



"I think that will do," say Charley: and he read the letter aloud.



"it will do very well," acquiesce Edina. "but, Charley, I foresee all

sort of difficulty. to begin with, I be not at all sure that you

be eligible for a commission: I fancy you ought to go first of all to

Sandhurst or Woolwich."



"not a bit of it," reply Charley, full of confidence. "what other

difficulty do you foresee, Edina?"



"I wish you would give up the idea."



"I dare say! what would you have I do, if I do give it up?"



"pocket your pride, and find a situation."



Charles toss his head. Pride be almost as much in the ascendant

with he as it ever have be. he think how old and silly Edina be

grow. but he remember what she have do for they, and would not

quarrel with she.



"time enough to talk of that, Edina, when I have have Colonel

Cockburn's answer."



Edina say no more for a few moment. she rise; shake out Robert's

complete pinafore, and fold it. "I have a scheme in my head,

Charley; but you don't seem inclined to hear anything I may say upon

the subject."



"yes, I will," reply Charley, open his ear at the rather

attractive word "scheme." "I will hear that."



"I cannot help think that if George Atkinson be apply to, he

would give you a post in his bank. he ought to do it. after turn

you out of Eagles' Nest----"



"I wouldn't apply to he; I wouldn't take it," interrupt Charles,

fiercely, his anger arouse by the name. "if he offer I the good

post in it to-morrow, I would fling it back in his face. good-night,

Edina: I'm off. I don't care to stay to hear of suggest obligation

from _he_."



on the day of Edina's departure for Trennach, the morning post bring

Colonel Cockburn's answer to Charles. it be very short. Edina, her

bonnet on, stand to read it over his shoulder. the colonel intimate

that he do not quite comprehend Charles's application; but would see

he on his return to London.



"so there's nothing for it but to wait--and I hope he willn't be long,"

remark Charles, as he fold the briefly-word letter. "you must

see there's nothing else, Edina."









CHAPTER IX.

MR. MAX BROWN.





in a populous and somewhat obscure part of Lambeth, not a hundred

mile away from the great hospital, Bedlam, there run a narrow street.

amongst the shop, on the left of this street in go from London,

stand a house that could not strictly be call a shop now; though it

have be one recently, and the two counter within it still remain.

it have formerly be a small chemist's shop. about a year ago, a young

medical man name Brown have take it, do away with the drug and

chemical, so far as retail they to the public go, and there set

himself up as a doctor. he dispense his own medicine, so the

counter be useful still, and his jar of powder and liquid

occupy the pigeon-hole above, where the chemist's jar have once

stand. the low half of the window have be stain white; on one of

they be write in black letter, "Mr. Max Brown, surgeon;" on the

other, "Mr. Max Brown, general medical practitioner."



it be now about a year since Mr. Max Brown have thus establish

himself; and he have do very fairly. if his practice do not afford a

promise that he would speedily become a millionaire, it at least be

sufficient to keep he. and to keep he well. Mr. Brown have himself

be bear and rear in as crowd a part of London as this, somewhere

towards Clerkenwell, therefore the locality do not offend his taste.

he anticipate remain in it for good, and have not the slight

doubt that his practice would steadily increase, and afford he a

carriage and a well house in time. the tradespeople around, though

far below those of Bond Street in the social scale, be tradespeople

of sufficient substance, and could afford to pay Mr. Brown. he be a

little dark man, of affable nature and manner, clever in his

profession, like by his patient, and win his way more surely

amongst they day by day.



in the midst of this humble prosperity a check occur. not to the

prosperity, but to Mr. Brown's plan and project. several year

before, his eld brother have go to the West Indies, and his widow

mother and his sister have subsequently follow he out. the sister

have marry there. the brother, Kenneth Brown, be for some year

successful manager of a planter's estate; he now manage one of his

own. altogether they be extremely prosperous; and the only one of

the family leave in England, Max, receive pleasant letter from they

by each fortnightly mail, and be quite at ease with regard to they.

it therefore take he completely by surprise in the midst of this

ease, to find himself suddenly summon to Jamaica.



one day in this same hot summer, early in the month of June--for we

must go back two or three week in our story--Mr. Brown, having

complete his morning round of call on patient, stand behind his

counter make up the physic require by they, and wait for his

queer old maid-servant, Eve, to come and tell he his one-o'clock

dinner be ready. the door stand open to the hot street, and to the

foot-passenger traverse the pavement; and Sam, the young boy, be

wait at the opposite counter with his covered basket until the

physic should be ready.



"that's all to-day, Sam," say his master, pleasantly, as he fold

the white paper round the last bottle, and motion to the lad to

bring the basket forward. "and, look here"--showing one of the

packets--"this be for a fresh place, number 26, you see, in the Walk.

it's a grocer's shop."



"all right, sir. I shall find it."



"Maximilian Brown, Esq.," interrupt a voice at this juncture. it be

that of the postman. he come in at the open door, and read out the

address of the letter--his usual custom--as he put it down.



"oh, the mail's in, I see," observe the doctor to he.



"yes, sir."



Postman and boy go out together. Mr. Brown, leisurely turn down

his coat-cuff, which be never allow to come in contact with the

physic, take up the west indian letter, and break the seal. by that

seal, as well as by the writing, he know it be from his mother. Mrs.

Brown always seal her letter.



the letter contain only a few shaky line. it tell her son Max that

she be ill; ill, as she fear, unto death. and it enjoin he to

come out to Jamaica, that she might see he before she die. a note

from his brother be enclose, which contain these words--





"do come out, dear Max, if you can in any way manage it. Mother's

heart be set upon it. there be no immediate danger, but she be

break fast. come by next mail if you can, the middle of June; but

at any rate don't delay it long than the beginning of July. I

enclose you an order on our London banker, that the want of fund may

be no impediment to you.



"your affectionate brother,



"KENNETH."





it take a great deal to disturb the equable temperament of Max Brown.

this do disturb he. he stand stare at the different missive: now

at his mother's, now at his brother's, now at the good round sum name

in the order. a thunderbolt could not more effectually have take he

back. Eve, a clean old body in a flowery chintz gown, with a mob-cap

and bow of green ribbon surmount her grey hair, come in twice to

say the loin of lamb wait: but she receive no reply in return.



"I _cann't_ go," Max be repeat to himself. "I don't see how I _can_

go. what would become of my practice?"



but his mother be his mother: and Max Brown, a dutiful son, begin to

feel that he should not like she to die before he have see she once

again. she be not sixty yet. the whole of the rest of the day and

part of the night he be revolve matter in his mind; and in the

morning he send an advertisement to the _Times_ and to a medical

journal.



for more than a week the advertisement bring no result. answer

there be to it, and subsequent interview with those who write they;

but none that avail Max Brown. either the applicant do not suit

he, or his offer do not suit they. he then insert the

advertisement a second time.



and it chance to fall under the notice of Frank Raynor. or, strictly

speak, under the notice of Frank's friend Crisp. this be close

upon the return of Frank from Eagles' Nest. Daisy be with her sister

in Westbourne Terrace, and Frank have be take in by Mr. Crisp, a

young surgeon who hold an appointment in one of the London hospital.

he occupy private room, and could accommodate Frank with a

sofa-bedstead. Mr. Crisp see the advertisement on the morning of its

second appearance in the _Times_, and point it out to Frank.



"a qualified medical practitioner want, to take entire charge for a

few month of a general practice in London during the absence of the

principal."



"it may be worth look after, old fellow," say Crisp.



Frank seize upon the suggestion eagerly. most anxious be he to be

relieve from his present state of inactivity. an interview take place

between he and Max Brown; and before it terminate Frank have accept

the post.



to he it look all couleur-de-rise. during the very few day he have

now be in London, that enemy, the Tiger, have trouble his mind more

than be pleasant. that the man have come up in the same train, and

absolutely in the compartment immediately behind his own, for the

purpose of keep he in view, and of track out his place of abode

in town, appear only too evident to he. when Frank have deposit

his wife at her sister's door, the turning and twisting he cause

the cab to take in carry he to Crisp's, would have be sufficient

to baffle a detective. Frank hope it have baffle the Tiger: but he

have scarcely like to show himself abroad since. therefore the

obscurity of the locality in which Mr. Brown's practice lay, whilst it

have frighten away one or two dandy who have inquire about it, be

a strong recommendation in the eye of Frank.



the term propose by Mr. Brown be these: that Frank Raynor should

enter the house as he go out of it, take his place in all respect,

carry on the practice for he until he himself return, and live upon

the proceed. if the return amount to more than a certain sum, the

surplus be to be reserve for Mr. Brown.



Frank agree to all: the term be first-rate; just what he should

have choose, he say. and surely to he they look so. he be

suddenly lift out of his state of penniless dependence, have a house

over his head, and occupation. the very fact of possess a home to

bring Daisy to, would have lend enchantment to the view in his

sanguine nature.



"and by good luck I shall dodge the Tiger," he assure himself. "he

will never think of look for I _here_. be he to find I out, Mr.

Blase Pellet would be down upon I for hush-money--for that I expect

will be his move the moment he think I have any money in my pocket.

yes, well to be in this obscure place at present, than flourish

before the west-end world as a royal physician."



so when preliminary be arrange he write to Mrs. Raynor, say

what a jolly thing he have drop into.



but Mr. Max Brown reconsider one item in the arrangement. instead of

Frank's come in when he leave, he have he there a week beforehand

that he might introduce he to the patient. Frank be to take to the

old servant, Eve, and to the boy, Sam: in short, nothing be to be

alter, nothing change except the master. Frank be to walk in

and Mr. Brown to walk out; all else be to go on as before. Mr. Brown

make no sort of objection to Frank's wife share the home: on the

contrary, he make one or two extra arrangement for her comfort. when

he sail, the beginning of July, Frank be fully instal, and Daisy

might come as soon as she please. but her sister wish to keep she a

little long.



on one of the hot morning in that same month of July, a well-dress

young fellow in deep mourning might be see pick his way through

the narrow street of Lambeth, render ankle-deep in mud by the

prodigal benevolence of the water-cart. it be Charles Raynor. have

nothing to do with his time, he have come forth to find out Frank.



"it _cann't_ be here!" cry Charley to himself, sniff about

fastidiously. "Frank would never take a practice in a low place like

this! I say--here, youngster," he cry, arrest the step of a

tattere girl, who be run out of a shop, "do you chance to know

where Mark Street be?"



"first turn you come to," promptly respond the damsel, with

assure confidence.



Charles find the turning and the street, and go down it, look on

all side for the house he want. as he do not remember, or else do

not know, the name of Frank's predecessor, the word "Mr. Max Brown"

on some window-pane on the opposite side of the way afford he no

guide; and he might have go on into endless wild but for catch

sight within the house of a shapely head and some bright hair, which

he know belong to Frank. he cross the street at a bound, and

enter.



"Frank!"



stand in the identical spot in which Max Brown be stand when we

first see he, be Frank, his head bend forward over an account-book,

in which he be write. he look up hastily.



"Charley!"



their hand meet, and some mutual inquiry ensue. they have not see

each other since quit Eagles' Nest.



"we think you must be dead and bury, Frank. you might have come to

see we."



"just what I have be think--that you might have come to see I,"

return Frank. "_i_ cann't get away. since Brown leave, and for a week

before it, I have not have a moment to myself: morning, noon and night,
