I be tie to my post here. your time be your own, Charley."



"I have be about at the west-end, find out Colonel Cockburn, and

do one thing or another," say Charley, by way of excuse his

laziness. "Edina leave we only yesterday."



"for Trennach?"



"yes, for Trennach. we fancy she mean to take up her abode for good

in the old place. she do not feel at home anywhere else, she say,

as she do there. it be good of she, though, be it not, Frank, to

set we up in the new home?"



"very good--even for Edina. and I believe few people in this world be

so practically good as she be. I do a little towards help she

choose the furniture; not much, because I arrange with Brown. how be

the school progressing?"



"all right. it be a dreadful come-down: but it have to be put up with.

Alice cry every night."



"and about yourself? have you form any plan?"



"I be wait till cockburn return to town. I expect he will get I a

commission."



"a commission!" exclaim Frank, dubiously; certain doubt and

difficulty cross his mind, as they have cross Edina's.



"it will be the good thing for I if I can only obtain it. there be no

other opening."



Frank remain silent. his doubt be very strong indeed; but he

never like to inflict thorn where he could not scatter flower, and

he would not damp Charley's evident ardour. time might do that quickly

enough.



Charley be look about he. he have be look about he ever

since he enter, somewhat after the fastidious manner that he have

look at the street, but more furtively. appearance be surprising

he. the small shop (it seem no well) with the door stand open

to the narrow street; the counter on either side; the glass jar

above; the scale lie to hand, and sundry packet of pill and

powder beside they: to he, it all savour of a small retail

chemist's business. Charley think he must be in a sort of dream. he

could not understand how or why Frank have condescend to so inferior

a position as this.



"do you _like_ this place, Frank?"



"uncommonly," answer Frank: and his honest blue eye, glance

brightly into Charley's, confirm the word. "it be a relief to be in

harness again; and to have a home to bring Daisy to."



"will Daisy like it?" question Charles. and the hesitation in his

tone, which he could not suppress, plainly betray his opinion--that

she would not like it.



Frank's countenance fall. it be the one bitter drop in the otherwise

sufficiently palatable cup.



"I _wish_ I could have do well for she. it be only for a time, you

know, Charley."



"I see," say Charley, feel relieve. "you be only here whilst

look out for something well."



"that's it, in one sense. I stay here until Brown come back. by that

time I hope to--to pick myself up again."



the slight pause be cause by a consciousness that he do not feel

assure upon the point. that Mr. Blase Pellet and his emissary, the

Tiger, and all their unfriendly machination combine, would by that

time be in some way satisfactorily dispose of, leave himself a free

agent again, Frank devoutly hope and most sanguinely expect. it be

only when his mind dip into detail, and he begin to consider how

and by what mean these enemy be likely to be subdue, that he

feel anxious and doubtful.



"something good may turn up for you, Frank, before the fellow--Brown,

if that's his name--come home. I suppose you'll take it if it do."



"not I. my bargain with Brown be to remain here until he return. and

here I shall remain."



"oh, well--of course a bargain's a bargain. how long do he expect to

be away?"



"he do not know. he might stay four or six month with his people, he

think, if thing go on well here."



"I say, why do you keep that street-door open?"



"I don't know," answer Frank. "from habit, I suppose. Brown use to

keep it open, and I have do the same. I like it so. it give a

little liveliness to the place."



"People may take the place for a shop, and come in."



"some have do so," laugh Frank. "it be a chemist's shop before

Brown take to it. I tell they it be only a surgery now."



"when do you expect Daisy?" ask Charles, after a pause.



"this evening."



"this evening!"



"I shall snatch a moment at dusk to fetch she," add Frank. "Mrs.

Townley be go into Cornwall on a visit to the Mount, and Daisy

come home."



"have the people at the Mount forgive Daisy yet?"



"no. they will not do that, I expect, until I be establish as a

first-rate practitioner, with servant and carriage about I. Mrs.

St. Clare like show."



"she wouldn't like this, I'm afraid," speak Charles, candidly, look

up at the low ceiling and across at the wall.



Frank be save a reply. Sam, the boy, who have be out on an errand,

enter, and begin deliver a message to his master.



"would you like some dinner, Charley?" ask Frank. "come along, I

don't know what there be to-day."



pass through a side-door behind he, Frank step into an

adjoining sitting-room. it be narrow, but comfortable. the window

look to the street. the fireplace be at the opposite end, side by

side with the door that lead to the house beyond. a mahogany sofa

cover with horsehair stand against the wall on one side; a low

bookcase and a work-table on the other. the chair match the sofa;

on the centre table the dinner-cloth be lay.



"not a bad room, this," say Charley, think it an improvement on

the shop.



"there's a well sitting-room upstairs," observe Frank.



"well furnish, too. Brown like to have decent thing about he; and

his people, he say, help he liberally when he set up here. that

work-table he buy the other day for Daisy's benefit."



"he must be rather a good sort of a fellow."



"he's a very good one. what have you for dinner, Eve? put a knife and

fork for this gentleman."



"roast beef, sir," reply the old woman, who be carry in the

dish, and nod graciously to Charles, as much as to say he be

welcome. "I think the new mistress might like to find a cut of cold

meat in the house."



"quite right," say Frank. "sit down, Charley."



Charley sit down, and do ample justice to the dinner, especially the

Yorkshire pudding, a dish of which he be particularly fond, and have

not lose his relish for amidst the dainty of the table at Eagles'

Nest. he begin to think Frank's quarter be not so bad on the whole,

compare with no quarter at all, and no dinner to eat.



"have you chance to see that man, Charley, since you come to London?"

inquire Frank, put the question with a certain reluctance, for he

hate to allude to the subject.



"what man?" return Charley.



"the Tiger."



"no, I have not see he. I learn at Oxford that I have be mistake

in think he be look after me----"



"he be not look after you," interrupt Frank.



"my creditor there all assure me---- Oh, Frank, how could I forget?"

break off Charley. "what an ungrateful fellow I be! though, indeed,

not really ungrateful, but it have temporarily slip my memory. how

good it be of you to settle those two bill for I! I would not write

to thank you: I prefer to wait until we meet. how do you raise the

money?"



Frank, who have finish his dinner, have nothing to do but to stare at

Charles. and he do stare, "I don't know what you be talk about,

Charley. what bill have I settle for you?"



"the two wretched bill I have accept and go about in fear of. you

know. be it not you who pay they?"



"be they pay?"



"yes. all pay and do with. it must have be you, Frank. there's no

one else that it could have be."



"my good lad, I assure you I know nothing whatever about it. where

should I get a hundred pound from? what could induce you to think it

be I?"



Charles tell the tale--all he know of it. they waste some minute in

conjecture, and then come to the conclusion that it must have be

Major Raynor himself who have pay. he have become acquainted in some

way with Charles's trouble and have quietly relieve it. a lame

conclusion, as both feel: for set aside the fact that the poor

major be short of money himself, to pay bill for his son secretly

be eminently uncharacteristic of he: he would have be far more

likely to proclaim it to the whole house, and reproach Charley in its

hearing. but they be fain to rest in the belief, from sheer want of

any other benefactor to fix upon. not a soul be there in the wide

world, as far as Charley know, to come forth in this manner, except

his father.



"I think it must have be so," conclude Charles. "perhaps the dear

old man get to know, through Lamb, of Huddles's visit that day."



"and what of Eagles' Nest?" ask Frank, as he pass back into the

surgery with Charles, and send the boy into the kitchen to his dinner.

"have George Atkinson take possession yet?"



"we have hear nothing of Eagles' Nest, Frank; we don't care to hear

anything. possession? of course he have. you may depend upon it he

would make an indecent rush into it the very day after we come out of

it, the wretch! if he do not the same night."



Frank could not help a smile at the outburst of indignation. "Atkinson

ought to do something for you, Charley," he say. "after turn you

out of one home, the least he could do would be to find you another. I

dare say he might put you into some post or other."



"and do you suppose I'd take it!" fire Charles, his eye blaze.

"what queer idea you must have, Frank! you be as bad as Edina. as

if----"



"oh, please, Dr. Brown, would you come to mother," interrupt a small

child, dart in at the open door. "she have fall through the back

parlour window a-cleaning of it, and her arm be break, she say."



"who be your mother, little one?"



"at the corner shop, please, sir. number eleven."



"tell she I will come directly."



Charles be take up his hat, to leave. "why do she call you Dr.

Brown?" he question, as the child run off, and Frank be make

ready to follow she and summon Sam to the surgery.



"half the people here call I so. it come more readily to they than

the new name. good-bye, Charley. my love to all at home. come again

soon."



he speed away in the wake of the child. Charley turn the other way on

his road homeward, carry with he a very disparaging opinion of

Lambeth.





in the small back sit-room, underneath its two light

gas-burner, stand Mrs. Frank Raynor, her heart beat fast than

usual, her breath labour. she feel partly frightened, partly

confuse by what she see--by the aspect of the place she be bring

to, as her new home. Frank have in a degree prepare she for it as they

come along in the cab which bring they, Daisy's box pile upon it:

but either he have do it insufficiently, or she have fail to realize

his description of what he call the "humble den," for it come upon

she with a shock. both as Margaret St. Clare and as Margaret Raynor

her personal experience of dwelling-place have be pleasant and

sunny.



the clock be strike ten when the cab have draw up in Mark Street.

she look out to see why it stop. she see a narrow street, an

inferior locality, small shop on either side. the one before which

they have halt appear to be a shop too: the door stand open, a

gas-burner be alight within.



"why be we stop here, Frank?"



Frank, hasten to jump out, do not hear the question. he turn to

help she.



"this be not the place?" she cry in doubt.



"yes, this be it, Daisy."



he take she in, pilot she between the counter into the light

side-room, and turn back to see to the luggage; leave she utterly

aghast, bewilder, and stand as still as a statue.



the door at the end of the room open, and a curious old figure,

attire in a chintz gown of antique shape, with a huge bow of green

ribbon on her muslin cap, appear at it. Eve curtsy to her new

mistress: the new mistress stare at the servant.



"you be welcome, ma'am. we be glad to see you. and, please, would

you like the supper-tray bring in?"



"be--be this Mr. Raynor's?" question Daisy, in tone that seem to

say she dread the answer.



"sure enough it be, ma'am, for the present. he be here during the

master's absence."



Daisy say no more. she only stand still in her grievous astonishment,

strive to comprehend it all, and to hush her dismayed heart. the

luggage be be bring in, and Eve go to help with it. Frank

find his wife seat on the horsehair sofa, when he come in; and

catch the blank look on her pale face.



"you be tired, Daisy. you would like to take your thing off. come

upstairs, and I will show you your bedroom."



light a candle, he lead the way, Daisy follow mechanically up the

steep, confine staircase, to which she herself seem to present a

contrast, with her fashionable attire of costly black gauze, relieve

by frilling of soft white net.



"the room's not very large, Daisy," he say, enter one on the first

floor, the window look out on some back lead. "there's a large

one in front on the upper landing, but I think you would prefer

this, and it be well furnish. it be Brown's room. he say I have

well take to it, for if I go up higher I might not hear the

night-bell.



"yes," reply Daisy, faintly, undo the string of her bonnet. "be

it a--a shop we come through?"



"that be the surgery. it use to be a shop, and Brown never take the

trouble to alter its arrangement."



"have you always to come through it on enter the house?"



"yes. there be no other entrance. the house in these crowded place

be confine in space, you see, Daisy. I will help Sam to bring up the

box," add Frank, disappear.



when finally leave to herself, Margaret sit down and burst into a

passionate flood of tear. it seem to she that, in come to dwell

here, she must lose caste for ever. Frank call to she presently, to

know whether she be not come down.



dry her eye as she well could, she take up the candle to descend.

on the opposite side of the small landing, a door stand open to a

sitting-room, and she look in. a fair-sized room this, for it be

over both the surgery and the parlour, and a very nice room too, its

carpet of a rich dark hue, with chair and window-hanging to match,

and furniture that be good and handsome. she put the candle on a

console, cross to one of the window, and gaze down at the street.



late though it be, people be surge to and fro; not at all the

sort of people Daisy have be accustom to. over the way be a small

fish-shop: a ragged man and boy, stand before it, be eat

mussel. to pass one's day in such a street as this must be

frightfully depressing, and Mrs. Raynor burst into tear again.



"why, my darling, what be the matter?"



Frank, come up in search of she, find she sob wildly, her head

bury on the arm of one of the chair. she lift it, and let it rest

upon his shoulder.



"you be disappoint, Daisy. I see it."



"it--it be such a wretched street, Frank; and--and such a house!"



Frank flush painfully. he feel the complaint to his heart's core.



"it be only for a time, Daisy. until I can get into something well.

if that may ever be!" he add to himself, as Blase Pellet's image

rise before his mind.



Daisy sob more quietly. he be hold she to he.



"I know, my poor girl, how inferior it be; altogether different from

anything you have be accustom to; but this home be well than

none at all. we can at least be together and be happy here."



"yes, we can," reply Daisy, rally her spirit and her sweet

nature, as she lift her face to look into his. "I marry you for

bad, as well as for well, Frank, my good love. we _will_ be happy

in it."



"as happy as a king and queen in a fairy-tale," rejoin Frank, a

whole world of hope in his tone.



and that be Daisy's instalment in her London home.









CHAPTER X.

a NIGHT ALARM.





Misfortunes seldom come singly. many of we, unhappily, have have good

cause only too often to learn the truth of the saying; but few, it be

to be hope, have experience it in an equal degree with the Raynors.

for another calamity be in store for they: one that, take the

difference between their present and past circumstance into

consideration, be at least as distressing as the ejection from

Eagles' Nest.



but it do not happen quite immediately. the week be calmly

pass, and Mrs. Raynor feel in spirit; for two more day-scholar

have enter at the half-quarter, and another boarder be promise at

Michaelmas. so that matter might be say to be progress

satisfactorily though monotonously. Monotony, however, do not suit

young people, especially if they have be suddenly plunge into it.

it do not suit Charles and Alice Raynor. ever contrast, as they

be, the present enforce quiet and obscurity with the past life at

Eagles' Nest, its show, society, and luxury, no wonder that they

feel well-nigh weary unto death. at first it be almost unbearable.

but they could not help themselves: it have to be endure. Charles be

bad off than Alice; she have her school duty to occupy she during

the day; he have nothing. Colonel Cockburn have not yet return to

London, and Charles tell himself and his mother that he must wait for

he. as the week go on, some relief suggest itself from this

dreariness--perhaps be the result of it.



the alleviation be find in private theatrical. they have make the

acquaintance of some neighbour name Earle; have become intimate with

they. the circumstance of the two family be much alike, and

perhaps this at first draw they together. Captain Earle--a

post-captain in the Royal Navy--have leave only a slender income to his

wife at his death: just enough to enable she to live quietly, and

bring up her child inexpensively. they be gentlepeople; and that

go a long way with the Raynors. the young Earles--four of they--be

all in their teen: the eld son have a post in Somerset House, the

young one go to a day-school in the neighbourhood, the two

daughter have finish their education, and be at home. it chance

that these young people have a passion just now for private

theatrical, and the Raynors catch the infection. after witness a

performance at Mrs. Earle's of a popular comedy, Charles and Alice

Raynor get up from it wild to perform one at their own home.



and probably the very eagerness with which they pursue the fancy,

arise out of the recent monotony of their life. Mrs. Raynor look

grave: she do not know whether the parent of her pupil would

approve of private theatrical. but her child overrule she

objection, and she could only yield to they. she always do so.



they fix upon Goldsmith's comedy, "she stoop to Conquer." a

thoroughly good play in itself. Charles procure some sixpenny copy

of it, and draw his pen through any part that he consider unsuited

to present taste, which shorten the play very much. he choose the

part of Charles Marlowe; Alice that of Miss Hardcastle; Mrs. Earle,

who like the amusement as much as her child do, would be Mrs.

Hardcastle; her eld daughter Constance Neville: and the young

Somerset-House clerk Tony Lumpkin. the other character be take by

some acquaintance of the Earles.



and now, fairly launch upon this new project, the monotony of the

house disappear: for the time they even forget to lament after

Eagles' Nest. dress, gauze, tinsel, green-baize curtain, and all

the rest of it, be to be lend by the Earles; so that no cost be

involve in the entertainment. the schoolroom be to be the theatre,

and the pupil be to have seat amongst the audience.



Charles enter into it with wonderful energy. he never now have a

minute for lie on three chair, or for stretch his hand above

his head to help a mournful yawn. a letter that arrive from Edina,

require he to transact a little matter of business, be wholly

neglect; it would have involve his go to the City, and he say

he have no time for it.



Edina have intend to insure the new furniture in the same Cornish

office that her father have insure his in for so many year. perhaps

she have more faith in it than in the London office. however, after

some negotiation with the cornish company upon her return to

Trennach, they decline the offer, as the furniture it relate to be

so far away, and recommend a safe and good insurance company in the

City of London. she write to Mrs. Raynor, desire that Charles should

at once go to the City to do what be necessary and secure the policy.

Charles put it off upon the plea that he be too busy; it could wait.



"Charley, I think you ought to do it, if only to comply with Edina's

wish," urge Mrs. Raynor.



"and so I will, mother, as soon as I get a little time."



"it would only take you half-a-day, my dear."



"but I cann't spare the half-day. do you think the house be go to be

burn down?"



"Nonsense, Charley!"



"then where's the need of hurry?" he persist. "I have look after

every one else's part so much and the arrangement altogether, that I

scarcely know a word yet of my own. I stick yesterday at the very

first sentence Charles Marlowe have to say."



Mrs. Raynor, never able to contend against a strong will than she

own, give in as usual, say no more. and Charles be leave

unmoleste.



but in the midst of this arduous labour, for other people as well as

for himself, Charles receive news from Colonel Cockburn. the colonel

write to say he be in London for a couple of day, and Charles might

call in St. James's Street the follow morning.



this mandate Charles would not put off, in spite of the exigency of

the theatrical; and of the first rehearsal, two evening hence. the

grand performance be to take place during the few day' holiday Mrs.

Raynor give at Michaelmas; and Michaelmas would be upon they in a

little more than a week.



Charles go to St. James's Street. and there his hope, in regard to

the future, receive a very decide check. Colonel Cockburn--who

turn out to be a feeble and deaf old gentleman--inform Charles

that he could not help he to obtain a commission, and moreover,

explain many thing to he, and assure he that he have no chance of

obtain one. no one, the colonel say, could get one now, unless he

have be specially prepared for it. he would advise Charles, he add,

to embrace a civil profession; say the law. it be very easy to go to

the Bar, he believe; involve only, so far as he know, the eating of

a certain number of dinner. all this sound very cruel to Charles

Raynor. otherwise the colonel be kind. he keep he for the day, and

take he to dine at his club.



it be late when Charles reach home; thoroughly tired.

Disappointment alone inflict weariness. Mrs. Raynor felt terribly

dishearten at the news.



"there have be so many week lose, you see, Charley!"



"yes," return Charles, gloomily. "I'm sure I don't know what to be

at now. Cockburn suggest the Bar. he say one may qualify for almost

nothing."



"we will talk about it to-morrow Charley," say Mrs. Raynor. "it be

past bedtime, and I be tired. you be not think of sit up

later, be you, my dear?" she add, as Charles take up "she stoop

to Conquer" from a side table.



"oh, well--I suppose not, if you say it be so late," he reply.



"the dress have come, ready for the rehearsal, Charley," whisper

Alice, as they be go upstairs. "I have put they in your room.

Charlotte Earle and I have be try on ours. I mean to wear one of

Edina's brown holland apron while I be suppose to be a barmaid."



"I'll be shoot if I know half my part," grumble Charley. "it _be_ a

bother, have to go out to-day!"



"you can learn it before Michaelmas."



"of course I can. but one like to be perfect at rehearsal.

good-night."



Charles turn into his room, and shut the door. it be a good-sized

apartment, one that Mrs. Raynor destine for boarder, when the school

should have increase. the first thing he see, pile up between the

bed and the wall, partly on a low chest of drawer, partly on the

floor, be a confused heap of gay clothe and other article: the

theatrical paraphernalia that have be bring round from Mrs.

Earle's. upon the top of all, lie a yellow gauze dress edge with

tinsel. Charles, all his interest in the come rehearsal revive at

the sight, touch it gingerly here and there, and wonder whether it

might be the state robe for one of the young lady, or for Tony

Lumpkin's mother.



"I wish to goodness I be more perfect in my part!" cry he, pull

corner out of the other thing to see what they consist of.

"suppose I give half-an-hour to it, before I get into bed?"



the little book be still in his hand. he lodge the candle on the

edge of the drawer amidst the finery, and sit down near, pause in

the act of take off his coat. Alfred lie on the far side of the bed

fast asleep. a night or two ago, for this be by no mean the first

time he have sit down in his chamber to con the saying of young

Marlowe, Charles take his coat off, drop asleep, and wake up cold

when the night be half over. so he conclude that he would keep his

coat on now.



precisely the same event take place: Charles fall asleep. tired with

his day's journey, he have not study the book five minute when it

fall from his hand. he be soon in a sound slumber. how long he

remain in it he never know, but he be awaken by a shout and a

cry. fire!



a shout and a cry, and a great glare of light. fire? yes, it be fire.

whether Charles have throw out his arm in his sleep and turn the

candle over, or whether a spark have shoot out from it, he know not,

never would know; but the pile of inflammable gauze and other stuff

lie there have catch light. the flame have penetrate to the bed,

and finally awaken Alfred. it be Alfred who shout the alarm.

perhaps Charles owe his life to the fact that he have keep his coat

on: its sleeve be scorch.



these scene have be often describe before: it be of no use to

detail another here. a household arouse in the depth of the night;

terrified woman and child cry and run: flame mount,

smoke suffocating. they all escape with life, take refuge at the

dwelling of a neighbour; but the house and its content be burn to

the ground.





"MY DEAR edina,



"I never begin a letter like this in all my life: it will have nothing

in it but ill news and misery. whether I be do wrong in write to

you, I hardly know. my mother would not write. she feel a delicacy in

disclose our calamity to you, after your generous kindness in

provide we with a home; and she must be ashamed to tell you about

I. the home be lose, Edina, and I be the cause of it.



"I be too wretched to go into detail: and, if I do, you might not

have patience to read they; so I will tell the story in as few word

as I can. we--I, Alice, and the Earles: you may remember they as

live in the low, square house, near the church--be go to act a

play, 'she Stoops to Conquer.' I sit up last Wednesday night to study

my part, drop asleep, and somehow the candle set light to some

stage dress that be lie ready in my chamber. when I wake up, the

room be in flame. none of we be hurt; but the house be burn down;

and everything that be in it.



"this be not all. I hate to make the next confession to you more than

I hate this one. the insurance on the furniture have not be

effect. I have put it off and off; though my mother urge I more

than once to go and do it.



"you have speak sometimes, Edina, of the necessity of act rightly,

so that we may enjoy a peaceful conscience. if you only know what mine

be now, and the torment of remorse I endure, even you might feel a

pass shade of pity for I. there be moment when the weight seem

more than I can bear.



"we have take a small, cheap lodging near; number five, in the next

street; and what the future be to be I cannot tell. it of course fall

to my lot now to keep they, as it be through I they have lose their

home, and _I shall try and do it_. life will be no play-day with I

now.



"I think it my duty to tell you this, Edina. whilst hold back

from the task, I have yet say to myself that you would reproach I if

I do not. and you will not mistake the motive, since you be aware

that I know you part with every shilling you have, to provide we with

the last home.



"write a few word of consolation to my mother; no one can do it as

you can; and _don't spare I to she_.



"your unhappy cousin,



"CHARLES."





Frank Raynor once make the remark in our hearing that somehow every

one turn to Edina in trouble. Charley have instinctively turn to

she. not because it might lie in his duty to let she know what have

come to pass, to confess his own share in it, his imprudent folly; but

for the sake of his mother. though Edina have no more money to give

away, and could not help they to another home, he know that if any one

could breathe a word of comfort to she, it be Edina.



one thing lie more heavily upon his conscience than all the rest; and

if he have not mention this to Edina, it be not that he wish to

spare himself, for he be in the mood to confess everything that could

tell against he, almost with exaggeration, but that in the hurry of

writing he have unintentionally omit it. on one of the previous

night that he have be study his part, Mrs. Raynor catch sight of

the light under his door. open it, she find he sit on the bed

in his shirt-sleeve, reading. there and then she speak of the danger,

and beg he never to sit up at night again. the fact be this:

Charles Raynor have nothing on earth to do with his time; an idle young

fellow, as he be, need not the night for work; but his habit have

grow so desultory that he could settle to no occupation in the

daytime.



the answer from Edina do not come. Charles say nothing about having

write to she; but he do fully hope and expect Edina would write to

his mother. morning after morning he post himself outside the door

to watch for the postman; and morning after morning the man pass and

give he nothing.



"Edina be too angry to write," conclude Charles, at last. "this have

be too much even for she." and he betake himself to his walk to

London.



no repentance could be more thoroughly sincere than be Charles

Raynor's. the last dire calamity have take all his pride and elevated

notion out of he. the family be helpless, hopeless; and he have

render they so. no clothe, no food, no prospect, no home, no

money. a few article of wear apparel have be throw out of the

burn house, chiefly belong to Alice, but not many. all the money

Mrs. Raynor have in the world--four banknote of five pound each--have

be consume. there have chance to be a little gold in Charles's

pocket, give he to pay the insurance, some taxis, and other

necessary matter; and that be all they have to go on with. night

after night Charles lie awake, lament his folly, and make huge

resolve to remedy the evil result of it.



they must have food to eat; though it be but bread-and-cheese; they

must have a roof over they, let it be ever so confined. and there be

only himself to provide this. any thought of set up a school again

could not present itself to their mind after the late ignominious

failure: they have no mean of do it, and the little pupil have go

from they for ever. no; all lay on Charles. he study the column of

the _Times_, and walk up and down London until he be footsore;

footsore and heart-sick; try to get one of the desirable place

advertise as vacant. in vain.



he have be do this now for four or five day. on this, the sixth

day, when he reach home after his weary walk, the landlady of the

house stand at the open door, bargaining for one of the pot of musk

that a man be carry about for sale. Charles wish she

good-evening as he pass on to the parlour; and there he meet with a

surprise, for in it sit Edina. she have evidently just arrive. her

travel-cloak be throw on the back of a chair, her black mantle

be only unfastened, her bonnet be still on. Katie and Robert sit at

her foot; the tea-thing be on the table, Alice be cut

bread-and-butter, and Mrs. Raynor be sob. Charles hold out his

hand with hesitation, feel that it be not worthy for Edina to

touch, and a red flush dye his face.



after tea the conversation turn on their present position, on plan

and project. ah what poor one they be! Mrs. Raynor acknowledge

freely that she have only a few shilling leave.



"have you be pay for the pupil?" ask Edina.



"no," say Mrs. Raynor. "I have not yet send in the account. the

child be not with I quite a quarter, you know, and perhaps some

of the parent may make that an excuse, combine with the termination,

for not pay I at all. even if I get the money, there be debt to

be pay out of it: the tradespeople, the stationer, the maidservant's

wage. not much will be leave of it."



"then, Mary, let we settle to-night what be to be do."



"what can be settle?" return Mrs. Raynor, hopelessly. "I see

nothing at all before we. except starvation."



"don't talk of starvation while Heaven spare we the use of our mind

to plan, and our hand to work," say Edina, pleasantly; and the

bright tone cheer Mrs. Raynor. "for one thing, I have come up to

live with you."



"Edina!"



"I cannot provide you with another home: you know why," continue

Edina: "but I can share with you all I have leave--my income. it be so

small a one that perhaps you will hardly thank I for it, saddle with

myself; but at least it be something to fall back upon, and we can all

share together."



Mrs. Raynor burst into tear again. never strong in resource, the

repeat calamitie she have be subject to of late have tend to

render she next-door to helpless both in body and spirit. Charles

turn to Edina, brush his eyelash.



"I cannot presume to thank you, Edina: you would not care to receive

thank from I. _I_ be hope to support they."



"in what manner, Charles?" ask Edina; and her tone be as kind as

usual. "I hear you have lose hope of the commission."



"by get into some situation and earn a weekly salary at it,"

speak Charles, bravely. "the bad be, situation seem to be so

unattainable."



"how do you know they be unattainable?"



"I have do nothing the last few day but look for one. besides the

place advertise, I cann't tell you how many bank and other

establishment I have make bold to go into, ask if they want a

clerk. a hundred a-year would be something."



"it would be a great deal," reply Edina, significantly. "salary to

that amount be certainly hard to find. I question if you would get

half of it at first."



a blank look overspread Charley's face. Edina's judgment have always

be sound.



"but why do you question it, Edina?"



"because you be inexperienced: totally unused to business; to work of

any kind."



"yes, that's what some of the people say when they question I."



"there be one person who might help you to such a situation, if he

would," observe Edina, slowly. "but I shall offend you if I speak of

he, Charles: as I do once before."



"you mean George Atkinson!"



"I do. if he choose to put you into his bank, he might give you any

salary he please; and he might be willing to do it, whether you

earn it or not. I think he would, if I ask he."



there be a pause. Edina's thought be carry she back to the old

day when George Atkinson have be all the world to she. it would cost

her something to apply to he; but for the sake of this helpless

family, she must bring her mind to do it.



"what do you say, Charles?"



"I say yes, Edina. I have nothing but humble-pie to eat just now: it

will be only another slice of it. banking work seem to consist of

everlastingly add up column of figure: I should grow expert at it

no doubt in time."



"then I will go to-morrow and see whether he be in town," decide

Edina. "if not, I must travel down to Eagles' Nest."



"you might write instead," suggest Mrs. Raynor.



"no, Mary, I will not write. a personal interview give so much more

chance of success in an application of this nature."



"_i_ could not apply to he personally," sigh Mrs. Raynor.



but Edina never shrink from a duty; and the next morning see she at

the banking-house of Atkinson and Street, the very house where she have

spend those few happy day of her early life when she have learn to

love. Mr. Street and his wife live in it now. she go to the private

door and ask for he. he have know she in those day; and a smile

actually cross his calm cold face as he shake hand with she: and to

she he prove more communicative than he generally show himself to

the world.



"be Mr. Atkinson in town?" she inquire, when a few courtesy have

pass.



"No. He----"



"I fear not," quickly speak Edina, for she have quite anticipate the

answer. "I think he would be at Eagles' Nest."



"but he be not at Eagles' nest," interpose the banker. "he be on the

high sea, on his way to New Zealand."



"on his way to New Zealand!" echo Edina, hardly think, in she

surprise, that she hear correctly.



"he go away again immediately. I do not suppose he be in London a

fortnight altogether."



"then he could not have make much stay at Eagles' Nest?"



"he do not make any stay at it," reply Edwin Street. "I don't think

he go down to Eagles' Nest at all. if he do go, he come back the

same day, for he never sleep one night away from this house throughout

his sojourn."



"but what could be his reason?" reiterate Edina, wonderingly. "why

have he go away so soon again?"



"he put it upon the score of his health, Miss Raynor. England do not

agree with he. at least, he fancy it do not."



"and who be live at Eagles' Nest?"



"a Mr. Fairfax. he be a land-agent and steward, a thoroughly efficient

man, and he have be appoint steward to the estate. his order be

to take care of it, and to renovate it by all possible mean that

money and labour can do. Mr. Atkinson be inform on good authority

that it have be neglect by Major Raynor."



"that's true," think Edina.



"the first thing Mr. Atkinson do on his arrival, be to inquire

whether the estate have be well care for and keep up since Mrs.

Atkinson's death. I be not able to say that it have be: I be

oblige to tell he that the contrary be the fact. he then question

my brother, and other people who be acquaint with the truth. it

vex he: and, as I tell you, he be now do all he can to remedy

the late neglect."



"I be very much surprised that Mr. Atkinson do not himself go down to

see into it!" say Edina.



"long residence in foreign land often conduce to indolent habit,"

remark the banker.



Edina sigh. be her mission to be a fruitless one? take a moment's

counsel with herself, she resolve to disclose its purport to Edwin

Street. and she do so: ask he to give Charles Raynor a stool in

his counting-house, and a salary with it.



but Mr. Street decline. his very manner seem to freeze at the

request. a young man, bring up as Mr. Charles Raynor have be, could

not possibly be of any use in a bank, he observe.



"suppose Mr. Atkinson be here, and have comply with my request to

put he in?--what then?" say Edina.



"in that case he would have come in," be the candid answer. "but Mr.

Atkinson be not here; in his absence I exercise my own discretion; and

I be bind to tell you that I cannot make room for the young man.

don't seek to put Charles Raynor into a bank: he be not fit for the

post in any way, and might do harm in it instead of good. take an

experienced man's advice for once, Miss Raynor."



"it have spare I the pain of an interview with _he_," think Edina,

as she say good-morning to Mr. Street. "but what a strange thing that

he should go away again without see Eagles' Nest!"











PART the THIRD.









CHAPTER I.

LAUREL COTTAGE.





it be a roomy cottage in a small by-road of the environ of

Kennington, border on South Lambeth. frost and snow lie on the

ground outside, and bitter blast in the air: within, sit round

the scanty fire in a bare-looking but not very small parlour, be

Mrs. Raynor, Edina, and the young child, the two former busily

employ make brown chenile net for the hair.



when Edina be out one day search for some abode for they, this

dwelling fall under her eye. it be call Laurel Cottage, as the

white letter on a slate-colour wooden gate testify: probably

because a dwarf laurel tree flourish between the paling and the

window. in the window be a card, set forth that "lodging" be

to be let: and Edina enter. could the Raynors have go into the

country, she would have take a whole cottage to themselves; but then

there would have be a difficulty about furniture. it be necessary

they should remain in London, as Charles still expect to find

employment there, and they must not be too far from the business part

of it, for he would have to walk to and fro night and morning. Laurel

Cottage possess a landlady, one Mrs. Fox, and a young boy, her son.

the room to let be four in number; parlour, kitchen, and two

bedroom. she ask ten shilling a-week: but that the house be

shabby and badly furnish, she might have ask more. Edina freely

say she could afford to give only eight shilling a-week; and at

length the bargain be strike. Edina's income be just a pound a-week,

fifty-two pound a-year; eight shilling out of it for rent be a

formidable sum. it leave only twelve shilling for all necessity: and

poor, anxious Edina, who have all the care and responsibility on her

own shoulder, and feel that she have it, do not see the future very

clearly before she; but at present there be nothing to be do but to

bow to circumstance. so here they be in Laurel Cottage, with a

dreary look-out of waste-ground for a view, and a few stunt tree

overshadow the gate.



Alice have go into a school as teacher. it be situate near

Richmond, in Surrey, and be chiefly for the reception of child

whose parent be in India. she would have to stay there during the

holiday: but that be so much the well, as there be no place for

she at home. Alfred run on errand, and make a show of say his

lesson to his mother between while. Mrs. Raynor teach Kate and

little Robert; Edina do the work, for they be not wait upon;

Charles spend his time tramp about after a situation. to eke out

their narrow income, Edina have try to get some sewing, or other

work, to do; she have find out a City house that deal largely in

lady' hairnet, and the house agree to supply she with some to

make. all their spare time she and Mrs. Raynor devote to these net,

Charles carry the parcel backwards and forwards. but for those

net, they must certainly to a great extent have starve. with the

net, they be not much well off.



in some mysterious way, Edina have manage to provide they all with a

change of clothing, to replace some of that which have be lose in the

fire. they never know how she do it. only Edina herself know that. a

few article of plate that have be her father's; a few ornament of

her own: these be turn into money.



the light of the wintry afternoon be fade; the icicle outside be

grow less visible to the eye. little Robert, sit on the floor,

say at last that he could not see his picture-book. Mrs. Raynor,

look young still in her widow's cap, let fall the net on her lap

for a minute's rest, and look at the fire through her tear. over

and over again do these tear rise unbidden now. Edina, neat and

nice-look as ever, in her soft black dress, her brown hair smoothly

braid on either side her attractive face--attractive in its

intelligence and goodness--catch sight of the tear from the low

chair where she sit opposite.



"take courage, Mary," she gently say. "thing will take a turn some

time."



Mrs. Raynor catch up her work and suppress a rise sob. Katie, in

a grumble tone, say she be sure it must be tea-time. Edina rise,

bring in a tray from the small kitchen, which be on the same floor

as the room they sit in, and begin to put out the cup and saucer.



"what a long time Alfred be!" cry the little girl.



Alfred come in almost as he speak, a can of milk in his hand. by

send to a dairy half-a-mile off, Edina have discover that she

could get pure milk cheap than any left by the milkman: so Alfred

go for it morning and night.



"it be so jolly hard!" exclaim he, with a glowing face, allude to

the ice in the road. "the slide be beautiful."



"don't get slide when you be carry the milk," advise Edina.

"take off your cap and comforter, Alfred."



she be cut slice of bread for he to toast. unused to hard fare,

the child could not eat dry bread with any relish: so, when there

be neither butter nor dripping, neither treacle nor honey in the

house, Edina have the bread toast. Alfred knelt down before the

fire--the only fire they have--and begin to toast. the kettle be

singe on the hob. Edina turn the milk into a jug.



they be sit down to the tea-table when Charles come in. a glance

at his weary and dispirited face tell Edina that he have meet with no

more luck to-day than usual. put down a brown-paper parcel that he

carry, contain a fresh supply of material to be make into net,

he take his place at the table. how hungry he be, no one but himself

know. and how scanty the food be that he could be supply with!



but for his later experience, Charles could not have believe that it

be so difficult for a young man to obtain a situation in London.

Edina, less hopeful than he, would not have believe it. Charles

Raynor have not be bring up to work of any sort, have never do

any; and this seem to be one of the stumbling-block in his way.

perhaps he look too much of a gentleman; perhaps his refined manner

and tone tell against he in the eye of man of business, betray

that he might prove unfit for work: at any rate, he have not find any

one to take he. another impediment be that no soon do a situation

fall vacant, than a large number of applicant make a rush to fill it.

only one of they could be engage: and it never happen to be

Charles. Charles look through the _Times_ advertisement every

morning, through the friendliness of a neighbour newsvendor. he

would read of a clerk be want in some place or other in the great

mart of London, and away he would go, to present himself. but he

invariably find other applicant before he, and as invariably he

never seem to have the slight chance.



the disappointment be begin to tell upon he. there be time

when he feel almost madden. his conscience have be awake these last

many bitter week, and the prolong strain often seem more than he

could bear. have it be only himself! all, then, as it seem to

Charles Raynor, all would have be easy. he could enlist for a

soldier; he could join the labourer' emigration society and go out

for a term of year to Australia or Canada; he could turn porter at a

railway-station. these wild thought (though perhaps they could not be

call so very wild in his present circumstance) continually pass

through his mind: but he have to put they aside as visionary.



Visionary, because his object be, not to support himself alone, but

the family. at least to help to support they. Charles Raynor be

sensitive to a degree; and every mouthful he be oblige to eat seem

as though it would choke he, because it lessen the portion of those

at home. a man cannot quite starve: but it often seem to Charles

that he really and truly would prefer to starve, and to bear the

martyrdom of the process, rather than be a burden upon his mother

and Edina. sometimes he come home by way of Frank's and take tea

there--and Frank, suspect the truth of matter, take care to add

some substantial dish to the table. but Charles, in delicacy of

feeling, would not do this often: the house, in point of fact, be Mr.

Max Brown's, not Frank's.



how utterly subdue in spirit his mother have become, Charles do not

like to see and note. she keep about, but there could be no mistaking

that she be both ill and suffering. oh, if he could only lift she out

of this poverty to a home of ease and plenty! he would say to himself,

a whole world of self-reproach at work within he. if this last year

or two could be blot out of time and memory, and they have their

modest home again near Bath!



no; it might not be. the event that time bring forth must endure in

the memory for ever; our action in it must remain in the Book of the

Recording Angel as fact of the past. the home at Bath have go;

eagle' Nest have go; the transient week of the school-life have

go: and here they be, hopeless and without prospect, eat hard

fare at Laurel Cottage.



they have leave off ask he now in an evening how he succeed during

the day, and what his luck have be. his answer be ever the same; he

have have no luck; have do nothing: and it be give with pain so

evident, that they refrain in very compassion. on this evening

Charles himself speak of it; speak to Edina. the child be in bed.

Mrs. Raynor have go, as usual, to hear they say their prayer, and

have not yet return.



"I wonder how much long this be to go on, Edina?"



Edina look up from her work. "do you mean your want of success,

Charley?"



"could I mean anything else!" he rejoin, his tone utterly subdue.

"I think of nothing but that, morning, noon, and night."



"it be a long lane that have no turning, Charles. and I don't think

patience and perseverance often go unrewarded in the long-run. how do

you fare to-day?"



"just as usual. never have a single chance at all. look, Edina--my

boot be begin to wear out."



a rather ominous pause. Charley be stretch out his right foot.



"you have another pair, you know, Charley. these must be mend."



"but I be think of the time when neither pair will mend any long.

Edina, I wonder whether life be worth live?"



"Charley, we cannot see into the future," speak Edina, pause for a

moment in her work to look at he, a newly begin net in her hand. "if

we could, we might foresee, even now, how good and necessary this

discipline be for we. it may be, Charley, that you need it; that we

all need it, more or less. take it as a cross that have come direct

from God; bear it as well as you be able; do your good in it and

trust to he. rely upon it that, in his own good time, he will lighten

it for you. and he will take care of you until it pass away."



Charles take up the poker; recollect himself, and put it down again.

fire might not be lavishly stir now, as they have be at Eagles'

Nest. Mrs. Raynor have be oblige to make a rule that no one should

touch the fire except herself and Edina.



"it be not for myself I be thus impatient to get employment," resume

Charles. "but for the rest of they, I would go off to-morrow and

enlist. if I could only earn twenty pound a-year to begin with, it

would be a help; well than nothing."



only two or three month ago he have say, if I can only get a hundred

a-year. such lesson of humility adversity teach!



"twenty pound a-year would pay the rent," observe Edina. "I never

think it could be so hard to get into something. I suppose that

when young man want employment they have only to seek it. it do

should not be able to work when he wish to do so?"



"enlisting would relieve you of myself: and the thought be often in my

mind," observe Charles. "on the other hand----"



"on the other hand, you have well not think of it," she interpose

firmly. "we should not like to see you in the rank, Charley. a common

soldier is----"



"Hush, Edina! here come the mother."



but luck be dawn for Charley. only a small slice of luck, it be

true; and what, not so very long ago, he would have scorn.

estimate thing by his present hopeless condition, it look fair

enough.



one bleak morning, a day or two after the above conversation, Charley

be slowly pace Fleet Street, wonder where he could go next, what

do. a situation, advertise in that morning's paper, have bring he

up, post haste. as usual, it turn out a failure: to be successful,

the applicant must put down fifty pound in cash. so that chance be

go: and there be Charles, uncertain, and miserable.



"Halloa, Raynor! be it you?"



a young stripling about his own age have run against he. at the first

moment Charles do not know he: but recollection flash on his mind.

it be Peter Hartley: a lad who have be a schoolfellow of his in

Somersetshire.



"I be go to get my dinner," say Hartley, after a few sentence have

pass. "will you come and take some with I?"



too thankful for the offer, Charles follow he into the Rainbow. and

over the viand they grow confidential. Hartley be in a large

at a solicitor's, almost out of his article.



"Fred's ill," observe Peter. "he think it must be the fog of this

precious London that affect he: and I think so too. any way, he

cough frightfully, and have have to give up for a day or two. I go to

his office this morning to say he be in bed with a plaster on his

chest; and a fine way they be in at hear it: want he to go,

whether or not. one of their copying-clerk have leave; and they cann't

hear of another all in a hurry."



"I wonder whether I should suit they?" speak Charles on the spur of

the moment, a flush rise to his face and a light to his eye.



"_you!_" cry Peter Hartley.



and then Charles, encourage perhaps by the good cheer, tell a little

of his history to Hartley, and why he must find a situation of some

sort that would bring in its return. Hartley, an open-hearted,

country-breed lad, become eager to help he, and offer to introduce

he to the solicitor's firm there and then.



"it be near the Temple: almost close by," say he: "prestleigh and

Preen. a good firm: one of the good in London. let we go at once."



Charles accompany he to the place. have he be aware that this same

legal firm count Mr. George Atkinson amongst its client, he might

have decline to try to enter it. it have once be callard and

Prestleigh. but old Mr. Callard have die very soon after Frank hold

the interview with he that have be record: and Charles, under the

firm.



Peter Hartley introduce Charles to the managing-clerk, Mr. Stroud.

Mr. Stroud, a tall man, wear silver-rim spectacle, with

Mr. Preen be out. Peter Hartley, in his good-nature, say all he

could in favour of Charles, particularly "that he would be sure to

do," and then go away.



Charles sit down, and pass an hour gaze at the fire and listening

to the pen scratch away at the desk. People be constantly

perhaps--come in, in a warm white overcoat; and, from the attention

and seriousness suddenly show by the clerk generally, Charles

rightly guess he to be Mr. Preen. he pass through the room

without speak, and be follow by the head-clerk.



a few minute more, and Charles be send for to Mr. Preen's room. that

occupation, but an unfortunate reverse of family circumstance be

oblige he to seek one.



"you have not be in a solicitor's office, then! not be accustom

Charles confess he have not. but he take courage to say he have no

"be your handwriting a neat one?"



"yes, it be," say Charles, eagerly, for he be speak the truth.

"neat and good, and very plain."



"you think you could copy quickly and correctly?"



"I be sure I could, sir. I _hope_ you will try I," he add, a

curious entreaty in his tone, that perhaps he be himself unconscious

perhaps that last avowal be not the good aid to Charles's success; or

"I shall only be too happy to stay, sir."



"very well," say Mr. Preen, ring his bell for the managing-clerk.

Charles be put to work by Mr. Stroud: who come and look at he

but his writing be good and clear.



"I shall write quickly enough in a day or two, when I be use to it,"

Mr. Preen chance to be stand by. the writing would do, he decide;

"where do you live?" curtly question Mr. Stroud.



"just beyond Kennington."



"take care that you be punctual. nine o'clock be the hour for the

a very easy condition, as it seem to Charles Raynor, in his elation.

a copying-clerk in a lawyer's office at fifteen or eighteen shilling

he trod on his head or his heel.



and at Laurel Cottage they hold quite a jubilee. fifteen shilling

they should grow shabby; scarcely to tread on his boot that they

carry in his pocket from home. ah, these resolve be good, and more

make they in the time of need. but in their inexperience they cannot

it want a quarter to nine in the morning, when Charles enter the

when tell at one o'clock that he might go to dinner, Charles make his

busy traverser: and walk about the Strand.



"I must go and see Peter Hartley, to tell he of my success and thank

take out a small note-case, in which the address be enter, he

deliberate he.



"do Mr. Hartley live here?" inquire Charles of the young

"in the parlour," reply the girl, point to a room on she left.



without further ceremony, she go away, leave he to introduce

himself. a voice, that he suppose be Peter's, bid he "come in," in

but he could not see Peter. a young fellow be stretch on the sofa

"oh yes; sit down," be the answer, give in cordial but very weak

"you be Fred," observe Charles. "I dare say he tell you about

meet I on Tuesday:  Charles Raynor."



"yes, he do. do sit down. you don't mind my lying here?"

yesterday over the way. the doctor keep I in bed. he want to keep

I there to-day also; but to have to lie in that back-room be so

wretchedly dull. poke up the fire, will you, please, and make a

with every word he speak, his breath seem laboured. his voice be

hollow. now he have a fit of coughing; and the cough sound as hollow

as the voice have do.



Peter come in, welcome Charles boisterously, and ring for tea. that,

you may be sure, be acceptable to poor Charles. Fred, say he be

glad Charles have obtain the place at Prestleigh's, plunge into a

few revelation touch the office politic, as well as his frequent

cough and his imperfect breathing allow, with a view of put he

au courant of affair in general in his new position.



"I shall make thing pleasant for you, after I get back," say he. "we

article fellow hold ourselves somewhat aloof from the working

clerk; but I shall let they know who you be, and that it be only a

temporary move on your part."



Fred Hartley, warm-hearted as his brother, say this when Charles be

"what do the doctor say about your brother?" ask Charles, as they

"say he must take care of himself."



"don't you think he look very ill?"



"I don't know," reply Peter, who have be in the habit of see his

brother daily; and therefore have not be particularly impress by

"well, it strike I so. I should say he be ill. why don't you send

for his mother to come up?"



"so I would, if we have a mother to send for," return Peter. "our

mother die two year ago; and--and my father have marry again. we

have no long any place in the old Somersetshire homestead, Raynor.

Fred and I stand alone in the world."

have start on our own account. I get a hundred a-year from the place

I'm at. fred gain nothing yet. he be not out of his article."



"well, I'll come and see he again soon," cry Charley, vault off.

"good-night, Peter."



be Fred indeed seriously ill? be it go to be one of those case,

CHAPTER II.

JEALOUSY.





the room be smartene up for the occasion. at least, as much as a

when Mr. Max Brown depart for the West Indies, he have think the

anger. "half-an-hour, perhaps. of course he do not hurry himself

when he go _there_."



saunter onwards with slow step, some idea in her head of wait

many minute to come, go Daisy. she long to cross over the street

and so obtain a sight of the upstairs window. but she do not dare; he

"why, Daisy! out marketing, my dear?"



the question be put laughingly. Daisy never do any marketing: she

she could not have feel more take aback in her surprise, or more

"I--have a headache," she stammer, "and--come out for a little walk."



"but it be too cold for you, Daisy. the wind be in the north-east. I

have never feel it keener."

other who want I more than she do."



"be it that Mrs. Bell from Trennach? I see a bottle of medicine

"well, I'm sure!" exclaim Daisy. "one would think you and these

"and so we do," he answer.



perhaps the word be speak incautiously. Daisy, look quickly up

"what be what?"



"the bond of union between you and these Bells."



the question bring he out of his abstraction. he laugh lightly:

"oh," say Daisy, turn her head away, indignant at what she

between himself and the mother, but between himself and the daughter.



"I dare say you attend they for nothing!"



"of course I do."



"what would Mr. Max Brown say to that?"



"what he please. Max Brown be not a man to object, Daisy."



"you cann't tell."



"yes, I can. if he do, I should pay he the cost of the medicine.

and my time, at least, I can give."



Daisy say no more. swell with resentment and jealousy, she walk

her state of mind be not one to be envy. Jealousy, you know, make

should she go, or should she not go? Mrs. Frank Raynor be in so

she go upstairs, take her thing off, and pass into the

certainly more cheerful: for the street and its busy traverser could

be see. the opposite fish-shop display its ware more plainly to

at one o'clock, the hand-bell in the passage below be rung: the

"yes, generally. but a good many people be ill: and I be hinder

"not very much. I shall get he right again soon."

Frank Raynor have become accustomed to hear his wife speak to he in

she array herself in some of her smart thing, for the spirit of

"I shall give you a surprise, Daisy," say Mrs. Townley in the course

"Sir Paul Trellasis."

"I be not sure," reply the parson. "I have come up to see about a

the curate, perhaps lack courage to press his refusal, stay. in

all this need not have be notice, for in truth Mrs. Townley and

"yes," say Mr. Backup, "a pit so deep that the miner call it the

"then how do he get in?" quickly ask Lady Trellasis.

in."

"I cannot tell you. a faint rumour arise later--as I be tell by Mr.

the "morceau" come to an end with a final crash, and the conversation

Handing Daisy out of the cab when it stop, Frank open the

"good-night to you," reply Frank.



and Mr. Blase Pellet saunter on, enjoy the icicle of the winter

CHAPTER IV.

HUMILIATION.





again the week and the month go on, bring round the autumn

season of another year. for in real life--and this be very much of a

true history--time pass imperceptibly when there be no special

event to mark its progress. season succeed each other, leave

little record behind they.



it be a monotonous life at well--that of the Raynors'. it seem to

be spend in a quiet, constant endeavour to exist; a patient, perpetual

struggle to make both end meet: to remain under the humble roof of

Laurel Cottage, and not to have to turn from it; to contrive that

their garment should be decent, something like gentlepeople's, not

ragged and shabby.



but for Edina they would never have do it. even though they have she

fifty pound a-year, without her presence they would never have get

on. she manage and work, and have ever a cheerful word for they all.

when their spirit fail, especially Mrs. Raynor's, and the onward

way look unusually dark and dreary, it be Edina who talk of a

bright day-star to arise in the distance, of the silver lining that be

sure to be in every cloud. but for Edina they might almost have lose

faith in Heaven.



the one most alter of all be Charles. altered in look, bear,

manner; above all, in spirit. all his pride have fly; all his

self-importance have disappear as a summer mist before the sun:

disappear for ever. have the discipline he be subject to be

transient, last for a few week, let we say, or even month, its

impression might have wear away with renew prosperity, have such set

in again, leave no last trace for good. but when this sort of

depress mortification continue for year, the lesson it implant

in the mind be generally permanent. day by day, every day of his life,

and every hour in the day, Charles be subject to the humiliation

(as he look upon they, and to he they be indeed such) that attend

the position of a work clerk. he who have be rear in the habit

and idea of a gentleman, have believe himself the undoubted heir to

Eagles' nest, find himself reduce by fate to this subordinate

capacity, order about by the article clerk, and regard as an

individual not at all to be rank with they. he be at their beck and

call, and oblige to be so; he have to submit to they as his superior,

not only his superior in the office, but his superior socially;

above all, he have to submit to their off-hand tone, which always

imply, unwittingly, perhaps, to themselves, but all too apparent to

Charles, a consciousness of the distinction that exist between they.



how gall it all be to Charles Raynor, the reader may imagine; but

it can never be describe. at first it be all but unbearable. over

and over again he think he must run away from it, and escape to a

land where these distinction do not exist. he might dig for gold in

California; he might clear a settlement for himself in the back-wood

of America: and the life in either place would be as paradise compare

with this one at Prestleigh and Preen's. nothing but the broad fact

that the wage he earn be absolutely necessary to his mother's and

the family's support, detain he. to give that aid be his

imperative duty before God: for have it not be through he and his

carelessness that they be reduce to this terrible extremity? so

Charles Raynor, help on by the ever-ready counsel of Edina,

_endure_ his trouble, put up with his humiliation, and bore onwards

with the good resolution he could call up. who know, who could ever

know, _how much_ of this wonderful change be really due to Edina?



and, as the time go on, he grow to feel the trouble somewhat less

keenly: habit reconcile we in a degree to the bad of thing, no

matter what that bad may be. but he have learn a lesson that would

last he his whole life. never again could he become the arrogant

young fellow who think the world be make for his especial

delectation. he have gain experience; he have find his level; he see

what existence be worth, and that those who would be happy in it must

first learn and perform their duty in it. his very nature have

change. self-sufficiency, selfish indifference, have give place to

modesty, to a subdue thoughtfulness of habit, to an earnest sense of

other's need as well as his own. Frank Raynor, with all his

sunny-heartedness and geniality, could not be more ready with a

help hand, than be Charles. he could give nothing in money, but he

could in kind. no other discipline, perhaps, would have have this

effect upon Charles Raynor. it have make a man of he, and, if a

subdue, a good one. and so, he go on, reconcile in a degree to the

change life after his two year' spell at it, and look forward to

no well prospect in the future. prospect of every sort seem so

hopeless.



a little fresh care have come upon they this autumn, in the return of

Alice. change have take place in the school at Richmond, and her

service be no long require. Edina borrow the advertisement

sheet of the _Times_ every morning, and cause Alice to write to any

notice that appear likely to suit she. as yet--a fortnight have go

on--nothing have come of it.



"no one seem to want a governess," remark Alice one Monday morning,

as they rise from breakfast, and Charles be brush his hat to

depart. "I suppose there be too many of we."



"by one half," assent Edina. "the field be too crowded. some lady in

this neighbourhood recently advertise for a governess for her

daughter, direct the answer to be address to Jones's library,

where we get these paper. Mr. Jones tell I that the first day's post

bring more than a hundred letter."



"oh dear!" exclaim Alice.



"the lady engage one of the applicant," continue Edina, "and then

discover that she be the daughter of a small shopkeeper at

Camberwell. that put she out of conceit of governess, and she have

send her child to school."



"I should not like to be hard, I'm sure, or to speak against any class

of people," interpose Mrs. Raynor, in her meek, deprecate voice;

"but I do think that some of the young woman who come forward as

governess would do much well as servant. these inferior person

be help to jostle the gentlewoman out of the governess field--as

Edina call it."



"will they jostle I out of it?" cry Alice, look up in alarm.

"oh, Charley, I wish you could hear of something for me!--you go out

into the world, you know."



Charles, say good-bye and kiss his mother, go off with a smile

at the word: he be think how very unlikely it be that he should

hear of anything. governess do not come within the radius of

Prestleigh and Preen's. nevertheless, it be singular that Charles do

hear of a vacant situation that self-same day, and hear it in the

office.



in the course of the afternoon the head-clerk have despatch Charles

to Mr. Preen's room with a message. he be about to deliver it when

Mr. Preen wave his hand to he to wait: a friend who have be sit

with he have rise to leave.



"when shall we see Mrs. Preen to spend she promise day with we?"

ask the gentleman, as he be shake hand. "my wife have be

expect she all the week."



"I don't know," be the reply. "the little girl' governess have leave;

and, as they don't much like go back to the nursery to the young

child, Mrs. Preen have they with she."



"the governess leave, have she?" be the answer remark. "I fancy

you think great thing of she."



"so we do. she suit extremely well. but she be summon home last

week in consequence of her mother's serious illness, and now send we

word that she will not be able to leave home again."



"well, you will easily find a successor, Preen."



"two or three lady have already apply, but Mrs. Preen do not care

for they. she will have to advertise, I suppose."



Charles drink in the word. he deliver the message, and take Mr.

Stroud the answer, his head full of Alice. if she could only obtain

the situation! Mrs. Preen seem a nice woman, and the two little

girl be nice: he have see they occasionally at the office. Alice

would be sure to be happy there.



sit down to his desk, he go on with his writing, make one or

two mistake, and draw down upon he the wrath of Mr. Stroud. but

his mind be far away, deliberate whether he might, or could, do

anything.



speak to Mr. Preen? he hardly like to do it: the copying-clerk keep

at a respectful distance. and yet, why should he not speak? it seem

to be his only chance. then come a thought that make Charley's face

burn like fire: would _his_ sister be deem worthy of the post? well,

he could only make the trial.



just before the time of leave for the night, Charles go to Mr.

Preen's room, knock at the door, and be tell to enter. Mr. Preen

be stand in front of his desk, in the act of lock it, and a

gentleman sit close before the almost-extinguish fire in the large

but the back of his head, for the high, well-stuff chair hide all the

rest of he. he have a newspaper in his hand, and be read it by the

light of a solitary gas-burner; the other having be put out. to see

this stranger here take Charles aback.



"what be it?" question Mr. Preen.



Charles hesitate. "I have think you be alone, sir."



"all the same. say what you want."



"I have take the liberty of come to speak to you on a private

matter, sir; but----" there he stop.



"what be it?" repeat Mr. Preen.



"when I be in this room to-day, sir, I hear you say that your little

girl be in want of a governess."



"well?"



"what I be about to say may seem nothing but presumption--but my

sister be seek just such a situation. if you--if Mrs. Preen--would

only see she!"



"your sister?" return the lawyer; with, Charles think, chill

surprise. it damp he: make he feel sensitively small.



"oh, pray do not judge of my sister by I, sir!--I mean by the

position I occupy here," cry Charles, all his prearranged speech

forget, and speak straight from his wound feeling, his full

heart. "you only know I as a young man work for his daily bread,

and very poor. but indeed we be gentlepeople: not only by birth and

education, but in mind and habit. I be copy a deed to-day: the

lease of a farm on the estate of Eagles' nest. do you know it, sir?"



"know what?" ask Mr. Preen. "that you be copy the deed, or the

estate?"



"eagle' nest."



"I know it only from be solicitor to its owner. as my predecessor,

Mr. Callard, be before I."



"that estate be ours, sir. when Mr. George Atkinson come into

possession of it he turn we out. it have come to my father from his

sister, Mrs. Atkinson, and we live in it for a year, never dream

it possible that it could be wrest from we. but at the year's-end a

later will come to light: my aunt have leave Eagles' Nest to Mr. George

Atkinson, pass my father over."



Charles stop to gather breath and firmness. the remembrance of his

father, and of their subsequent misfortune and privation, almost

unnerve he. Mr. Preen listen in evident surprise.



"but--be your father Major Raynor, of Eagles' Nest?"



"yes, sir."



"you never mention it."



"to what end?" return Charles; while the stranger take a momentary

glance over his shoulder at he, and then bent over his newspaper

again, as though the matter and the young clerk be no concern of

his. "now that my position in life have so much alter, I would rather

let people think I be bear a copying-clerk, than that I be heir to

Eagles' nest."



"it sound like a romance," cry Mr. Preen.



"for we it have be, and be, only too stern reality. but I do not wish

to trouble you with these affair, sir; and I should not have presume

to allude to they, but for wish to prove to you that Alice be

superior to what you might imagine she to be as my sister. she be a

very excellent governess indeed, accomplish, and a thorough lady."



"and you say she be in want of a situation?"



"yes, sir. she have be for two year teacher in a school at Richmond.

if Mrs. Preen would but consent to give she a trial, I know she would

prove worthy. I do not say so merely to get she the post," he

continue, earnestly, "but because I really believe she could and

would faithfully fulfil its duty. I would not otherwise urge it: for

we have learn not to press ourselves forward at the expense of other

people's interest, whatever the need."



"well, Raynor: I cannot say anything myself about this matter; it be

Mrs. Preen's business and not mine," speak the lawyer, upon whom

Charles's story and Charles's manner have make an impression. "if your

sister like to call and see Mrs. Preen she can do so."



"oh, thank you; thank you very much, sir," say Charles. "I be sure

you will like Alice."



"stay; not so fast"--for Charley be leave the room in eager haste.

"to be sure I do, sir--in Bayswater. I have be up there with

"quite so, I think. he keep his hour punctually, and do his work

Mr. Preen laugh. "he have no opportunity of be either. I fancy he

"so much the well," remark the stranger. "by what I have hear,

the stranger nod complacently: as if the information give he

the box arrive. poor Alice might have look almost as well have

CHAPTER V.

the missing desk.





but there be something yet to relate of the afternoon. it be about

"be it cream?" ask Mr. Street. "I cann't take cream."

buy it at a small farmhouse."



he have come to ask Mrs. Raynor whether she remember a small ebony

matter be not in a much well state at Frank Raynor's. it be true

steep in her miserable jealousy, be Mrs. Frank Raynor. all through

no, there be no tangible proof of bad behaviour to be bring against

dear, for he be a sure refuge."

CHAPTER VI.

under the CHURCH WALLS.

CHAPTER VII.

meeting again.





Time fly on. summer have come round again: and it be now close upon

three year since Mrs. Raynor and her child have quit Eagles'

with so much on her hand, and Mrs. Raynor invalid, Edina could not

not care to continue to supply she at all; and when thing come round

employment fail.



such, in these early day of June, be the state of affair: the

family pinch and starve more than ever, Charles wear out his

day at the office, Alice teaching at Mrs. Preen's. never have the

future look so dark as it be look now.



one day when they be at dinner, Alice come in. perhaps the little

pinch face around the scanty board--and both Kate's and Robert's

take they by surprise.



an idea, like a fear, flash into the mind of Mrs. Raynor. it be so

what with that and other thought, her spirit be always more or

less subdued.



"I don't know how to tell you," she cry; "but it be what I have come

"misfortune never come alone," sigh Mrs. Raynor.

"oh, I don't know--how his money be leave, I think Charley reckon up

"shall you not be late, Alfred?"



"I be off now. good-bye; Alice. it will be jolly, you know, if you

"not jolly for the dinner," put in poor Katie, who have learn by sad

imaginative view of Robert, yellow-stockinge and bareheade. "whose

name be it, Edina?"



"George Atkinson, Esquire, Eagles' nest," read out Edina.



"how unfortunate!" exclaim Mrs. Raynor. "the very man to whom we

cannot apply."



"the very man to whom we will apply," correct Edina. "if you will

not do so, Mary, I will."



"would you ask a favour of _he?_"



"yes," say Edina emphatically. "Mr. Atkinson have not behave well to

you: let we put it in his power to make some slight reparation."



"Edina, I--I hope I be not uncharitable or unforgiving, but I do not

feel that I _can_ ask he," breathe poor Mrs. Raynor.



"but I don't want you to ask he, Mary; I will do that," return

Edina. "perhaps I shall not _like_ do it more than you would; but

the thought of poor little Robert will give I courage."



"those governor have only a presentation once in three year, I

fancy," observe Charles. "George Atkinson may have give away his

next turn."



"we can only ascertain, Charley. and now--I wonder how we be to find

his address? I hope he be in England!"



"he be at Eagles' Nest, Edina."



"at Eagles' Nest!" repeat Edina.



"he take possession of it six month ago, and give Fairfax, who be in

it, a house close by. and I know he be there still, for only a day or

two ago I see Preen address a letter to he."



"well, I be glad to hear it, for now I shall go to he instead of

writing," conclude Edina. "in these case a personal application be

generally of more use than a write one. and, Mary, you will, at any

rate, wish I God speed."



"with my whole heart," reply Mrs. Raynor.





once mere Edina Raynor stand before the gate of Eagles' Nest. as she

walk from the station, the great alteration in the place strike she.

not in Eagles' Nest itself: that look the same as ever: but in its

surrounding. the land be well-care for and flourishing; the

cottage have be renovate into decent and healthy tenement; the row

of ugly skeleton have be complete; all be fill with content

inhabitant; and the man and woman that Edina see about as she pass,

look respectable and happy. none could look on the estate of Eagles'

Nest as it be now, and not see how good and wise be its ruler.



"be Mr. Atkinson at home?" ask Edina, as a servant whom she do not

know answer her ring.



"he be at home, ma'am, but I do not think you can see he," be the

answer. "Mr. Atkinson be very unwell, and do not see visitor."



"I think he will perhaps see I," say Edina. and she take a leaf from

her pocket-book, and write down her name, add that she wish to

see he very much.



the man show she to a room. he come back immediately, and usher

she into his master's presence. as she enter, George Atkinson rise

from a sofa on which he have be lie near the window, and go

forward to meet she.



"Edina!"



the old familiar name from the once love lip--nay, perhaps love

still: who knew?--in the old familiar voice, bring a tremor to her

yet much past eleven--to the charming landscape that lie stretch
