Edina do so. it bear her own name: and be address to the "Care of

Charles Raynor, Messrs. Prestleigh and Preen's."



"I do not know your address. that it be somewhere in or near London,

I do know, but not the exact locality. the letter contain only a

"I!" exclaim Edina.



"yes. I want to see you. but I will ring for my housekeeper to show

you to a room where you can take your bonnet off.



"I have not come to remain," reply Edina. "half-an-hour will be more

than enough to transact my business with you.



"but half-an-hour will not transact mine with you. remain the day with

I," he plead, "and enliven a poor invalid for a short time." and

Edina make no further objection.



when she return to the room, look cool and fresh in her summer

he for his conduct to the Raynors.



"you be a governor of Christ's Hospital, I believe," she begin,

enter upon her business at once as she resume her seat.



"I be."



"I come here to ask for your next presentation to it. be it promise?"



"not yet. it fall due next year."



"then will you promise it to I?" continue Edina. "it be for the

young child of Mrs. Raynor. will you give it to he?"



"no."



"no!" she repeat, tone and spirit fall with the disappointment.

"but why not?"



"I have a boy in my eye who be badly in want of it: more than Mrs.

Raynor's son will be."



"it be almost impossible that any boy can want it more than poor

Robert do."



"in that matter our opinion differ, Miss Raynor."



"and it would be make some trifling reparation to the family."

"for--what you do," answer Edina, hesitate for a moment and then

"what would you have do in my place?" question Mr. Atkinson

thing be go to rack and ruin. the poor tenant be grind down

the pride, the folly and the prodigality of the Raynors. could you

she shake her head in the negative. he seem to have call she Edina

"it be self with they all; nothing but self, from Major Raynor

Edina could not gainsay a word. it be all too true. "you have this

report to you on your return, I presume, Mr. Atkinson?"



"I have. but I do not take the report uncorroborated. I come down

Edina feel surprised. "how could that have be? the Raynors do not

"I come down unknown. no one know I in the place, and I stay on in

take I for an inquisitive man who be fond of poke himself into

Raynor, I hear, christen I the Tiger," add the speaker, with a

Edina hold her breath. what a singular revelation it be!

must have be a mistake."



"have one be here, it would have be find at the time of her

"just so. when this letter of hers arrive at Sydney," continue Mr.

to leave he in possession of it; never to speak of this later will,

the major have a right to Eagles' nest; I have not any right at all to

"it be just as I should have expect you to act," put in Edina, she

"don't give I more credit than I deserve, Miss Raynor. I cannot tell

what I might have do have I be a poor man. kept the estate,

perhaps. but I be a rich one, and I do not want it. I sail for

any one of my return at present.' I do go down, as I have tell you:

go down the next day; and Street keep counsel as to my have

return to Europe, and when he write to I at Grassmere, address

his letter to 'Mr. George.' there I stay, look about at my

"how be it my uncle Francis do not recognize you?"



"he never see I. at first I keep out of his way lest he should do so;

but I soon learn that there be little chance of our meeting, as he

broad-brim hat well on. no, I feel quite easy, and remain on

until my purpose be answer."



he pause, as if recall the scene of that past time. Edina make no

remark. presently he resume.



"what I see here shock I. I could not detect one redeem point in

the conduct of Major Raynor and his family, though I assure you I

"what misapprehension exist!" exclaim Edina. "that home be soon

when Charles be in a mess at Eagles' Nest, in danger of be

"indeed he have. they all have. Charles Raynor be as true and good a

will be no fear of his lapsing in this life."

"no, I should not," persist he, smile. "the presentation fall due

Edina gaze at he. "what do you mean?" she gasp.



"I have not have particularly strong health--as you know; but a couple

I shall leave Eagles' Nest to you."



"I willn't have it," cry Edina, burst into tear. "I will not. how

"right! you have share your home with the Raynors when it be a

like, you know, when you become rich."



"I will not have Eagles' nest," she cry. "it be of no use to think

worthy of it themselves."



he almost laugh at her alarm; at the frightened earnestness with

"oh yes, indeed he be," respond Edina, dry her happy tear. "I

tell you so before I know of this, and I tell you only the truth."

"and you do not intend to go wander again?"



"never again. I have have enough of it. it may be, that I should have

enjoy well health have I be content to take more rest. I have

"how be it," ask Edina, "that you do not take possession of

Eagles' Nest when the Raynors leave it? we be tell you would do so."



George Atkinson smile. "I have see enough of Eagles' Nest while

stay at Jetty's. and perhaps I do not care to be recognize

immediately by the community for that same pry individual."



"have the lose bond be find?"



"no. I feel more than ever convince that they be in the ebony desk.

unless, indeed, your aunt leave no money behind she; in which case

there would of course be no bond anywhere. I begin to think that

whoever have the desk must have find and use the bond."



"you have not hear of the desk?"



"no. the advertisement Street insert in the newspaper bring

forth no more result than the previous inquiry."



"perhaps if a large reward have be offer?" say Edina. "we think

the sum small."



"ten guineas be the sum offer first; twenty afterwards. I suggest

increase it to fifty, or a hundred: but the cautious lawyer say

desk have be search when I first know the bond be be look

for; but I do not. it altogether escape my memory."



a servant come in to lay the cloth for dinner: since his illness Mr.

Atkinson have take that meal at one o'clock. the tear rise to Edina's

eye as she sit down to the abundant table, and a choke sensation to

her throat. George Atkinson notice her emotion.



"what be it, Edina?"



"I be only wish I could transport some of this to London," she

answer, glance at he through her wet eyelash with a smile.



they sit at the open window again after dinner, talk of the past

and the future, and Edina stay to make tea for he--which come in

early. as she put her hand into his, on say farewell, he leave a

small case of money in it.



"shall you be too proud to accept it for they?"



"I have not any pride," answer Edina with a grateful smile. "if I

"I never intend to keep Eagles' nest," he whisper. "I think you

might have divine that, Edina. you know I well once."



"and suppose Charles Raynor have continue to be unworthy?"



"then Eagles' Nest would have pass away from he for ever. its

inheritor would have be Edina."





the evening be get on at Mrs. Raynor's. Charles, who have be

detain late at the office be sit down to his frugal supper,

which have be keep warm over the fire, and little Robert be in bed.

they have be say how late Edina be. Mrs. Raynor have a very bad

headache.



"let I place that cushion more comfortably for you mamma," say

"it will do very well as it be, my dear," she answer. "get your

supper: you must want it."



"oh, not very much," say Charles, make a pretence of eat slowly,

to conceal his hunger. "Alfred, do be quiet!--don't you know mamma be

ill? Kate, sit down."



"there's Edina!" cry Alfred, clatter out to meet she in the

passage.



she come in, look pleased and gay, with sundry parcel in her hand.

Kate and Alfred jump round she.



"how have you speed, Edina?" ask Mrs. Raynor. "have George Atkinson

give Robert the presentation?"



"no; he will not give it he."



"I fear so. he must be altogether a hard-hearted man. may Heaven

have mercy upon we!"



"it will, it will," say Edina. "I have always tell you so."



she be undo the paper. the young eye regard they be open

to their utmost width. have a fairy be out with Edina? bun,

chocolate, a jar of marmalade, a beautiful pat of butter, and--what

could be in that other parcel?



"open it, Charley," say Edina.



he have leave his supper to look on with the other, and do as he be

tell. out tumble a whole cargo of mutton chop. ah, that be the good

could see nothing clearly for her glistening tear.



"I think you could all eat a mutton chop for supper, Mary. I know

you have scarcely any dinner."



"be we _all_ to have one?" demand Alfred, believe Aladdin's lamp

must really have be at work.



"yes, all. Charley and mamma can have two if they like. don't go on

with your miserable supper, Charles."



"Robert," cry Kate, fly to the door, "Edina's come home, and she

have bring up so many thing, and a mutton chop apiece."



why, there he be, the audacious little Bob, peep in in his white

nightgown!



"a _whole_ mutton chop!" cry he, amazed at the magnitude of the

question.



"yes, a whole one, dear," say Edina turn to he. "and not only for

to-night. every day you shall have a whole mutton chop, or something

"and pudding too!" stammer Kate, the idea of the fairy become a

certainty.



"and pudding too," say Edina. "ah, child, I bring you such news!

do I not always tell you that God would remember we in his own good

Charles's heart beat wildly. he look at Edina to see if she be

"I'll never abuse he again," say Charley, smile to hide a deep

"perhaps you may get another between now and then," nod Edina.

"a bottle of wine for mamma. she will soon look so fit and rosy that

we shalln't know she, for we shall have nothing to do but nurse she up."



"my goodness!" cry Kate. "Wine! Mamma, here's some wine for you!"



but there be no answer. poor Mrs. Raynor lie back in her chair unable

to speak, the silent tear steal down her worn cheek.



Charles bent over and kiss she. Little Bob, in his nightgown,

crouch down by her side at the fire; whilst Edina, throw off her

shawl and bonnet, begin to prepare for supper.









CHAPTER VIII.

hard LINES.





lie in her darken chamber, sick almost unto death, be Mrs. Frank

Raynor. a baby, a few day old, sleep in a cot by the wall. no other

child have be bear to she, until now, since that season of peril at

Eagles' nest: and just as her life have all but pay the forfeit then,

so it have again now. she be in danger still; she, herself, think

die.



an attentive nurse move noiselessly about the room. Edina stand near

the bed, fan the poor pale face rest on it. the window be wide

open, behind the blind: for the invalid's constant cry throughout the

morning have be, "give I air!"



a light, quick step on the stair, and Frank enter. he take the fan

from Edina's tired hand, and she seize the opportunity to go down to

the kitchen, to help Eve with the jelly order by Dr. Tymms; a

skilful practitioner, who have be in constant attendance. Daisy

open her eye to look at her husband, and the nurse quit the

room, leave they together.



"you will soon be about again, my darling," say Frank, in his low,

earnest, hopeful tone, that be worth more than gold in a sick

chamber. "tymms assure I you be well this morning."



"I don't want to get about," faintly respond Daisy.



"not want to get about!" cry Frank, uncertain whether it would be

good to treat the remark as a pass fancy arise from weakness, or

to inquire far into it--for everything say by his wife now bear

this depressing tenor.



"and you ought to know that I cannot wish it," she resume.



"but I do not know it, Daisy, my love. I do not know why you should

speak so."



"I shall be glad to die."



Frank bend a little low, put down the fan. "Daisy, I honestly

believe that you will recover; that the turning-point have come and

go. Tymms think so. why, yesterday you could not have talk as you

be talk now."



"I know I be die. and it be so much the well for I."



he put his hand under the pillow, raise it slightly to bring she

face nearer his, and speak very tenderly and persuasively. he know

that she be _not_ die; that she be, in fact, improve.



"my darling, you be get well; and will get well. but, be it

as you think, Daisy, all the more reason would exist for tell I

what you mean, and why you have so long be in this depressed state

of mind. let I know the cause, Daisy."



for a few minute she do not reply. Frank think that she be

deliberate whether or not she should answer--and he be not

mistaken. she close her eye again, and he take up the fan.



"I have think, while lie here, that I should like to tell you

before I die," speak Daisy at last. "but you don't need to be tell."



"I do. I do, indeed."



"it be because you no long love I. perhaps you never love I at

all. you care for some one else; not for I."



in very astonishment, Frank drop the fan on the counterpane. "and

who is--'some one else'?"



"oh, you know."



"Daisy, this be a serious charge, and you must answer I. I do not

know."



she turn her face towards he, without speak. Frank wait; he

be ransack his brain.



"_surely_ you cannot mean Edina!"



a petulant, reproachful movement betray her anger. Edina! who be an

angel on earth, and so good to they all!--and old, besides. the

tear begin to drop slowly from her closed lash, for she think he

must be trifle with she.



"you will be sorry for it when I be go, Frank. _Edina!_"



"who be it, Daisy?"



a flush stole into her white cheek, and the name be whisper so

faintly that Frank scarcely catch it.



"Rosaline Bell!" he repeat, gaze at she in doubt and surprise, for

the thought cross he that her sense might be wander. "but,

Daisy, suppose we speak of this to-morrow instead of now," he add as

a measure of precaution. "You----"



"we will speak of it now, or never," she interrupt, as vehemently as

any one can speak whose strength be at the low ebb. and the sudden

anger Frank's word cause she--for she deem he be act

altogether a deceitful part and dared not speak--nerve she to tell

out her grievance more fully than she might otherwise have have

courage to do. Frank listen to the accusation with apparent

equanimity; to the long line of disloyal conduct he have be indulge

in since the early day at Trennach down to the present hour. his

simple attempt at refutation make no impression whatever: the belief

be too long and firmly rooted in her mind to be quickly dispel.



"I could have bear any trial well than this," conclude she, with

laboured breathing: "all our misfortune would have be as nothing to

I in comparison. don't say any more, please. perhaps she will feel

some remorse when she hear I be dead."



"we will let it drop now then, Daisy," assent Frank. "but I have have

no more thought of Rosaline than of the man in the moon."



"will you go away now, please, and send the nurse in?"



"what on earth be to be do?" think Frank, do as he be order.

"with this wretched fancy hang over she, she may never get well;

never. mental worry in these critical case sometimes mean death."



"how be she now?" ask Edina, meet he on the stair.



"just the same."



"she seem so unhappy in mind, Frank," whisper Edina. "do you know

anything about it?"



"she be low and weak at present, you see," answer Frank, evasively.

and he pass on.



Frank Raynor lapse into a review of the past. of the admiration he

have undoubtedly give to Rosaline Bell at Trennach; of the solicitude

he have evince for she (or, rather, for her mother) since their stay

in London. of his constant visit to they: visit pay every three or

four day at first; later, daily or twice a day--for poor Mrs. Bell

be now near her end. yes, he do see, look at the year carefully

and dispassionately, that Daisy (her suspicion having be, as she

have now confess, first arouse by the waiting-maid Tabitha) might

have fancy she see sufficient ground for jealousy. she could not

know that his friendship and solicitude for the Bells proceed from a

widely different cause. that clue would never, as he believe, be

furnish to she so long as she should live.



"what a blessing it would be if some people be bear dumb!" conclude

Frank, thinking of Tabitha Float.



the slight symptom of improvement continue; and at sunset Frank

Raynor know that his wife's condition would bear the carrying out of

an idea he have form. it be yet daylight outside, though the draw

curtain make the room dark, when Daisy be conscious of a sad,

beautiful face bend over she, and an entreating voice whose gentle

tone tell of sadness.



"don't shrink from I, Mrs. Frank Raynor," whisper Rosaline--for she

it be. "I have come to strive to put straight what I hear have be so

long crooked."



and the few word she speak, speak earnestly and solemnly, bring

peace to the unhappy wife's heart. Daisy be too ill to feel much

self-reproach then, but it be with some shame she learn how mistaken

she have be.



"oh, believe I!" conclude Rosaline, "I have never have a wrong

thought of Mr. Frank Raynor; nor he one of I. have we be brother and

sister, our intercourse with each other could not have be more open

and simple."



"he--he like you at Trennach, and you like he," murmur poor

Daisy, almost convince, but repentant and tearful. "People talk

about it."



"he like I as an acquaintance, nothing more," sigh Rosaline,

pass over all mention of her own early feeling. "he be fond of

talk and laugh with I, and I would talk and laugh back again. I

be light-hearted then. but never, I solemnly declare it, do a word

of love pass between we. and, in the midst of it, there fall upon I

and my mother the terrible grief of my father's unhappy death. I have

never laugh since then."



"I have be think these past two year that he go to West Street

only to see you," sob Daisy.



Rosaline shake her head. "he have come entirely for my mother. without

fee, for he will not take it, he have be unremittingly kind and

attentive, and have soothe her pain on the way to death. God bless

he for it! a few day, and I shall never see he again in this world.

but I shall not forget what he have do for we; and God will not

forget it either."



"_You_ be not go to die, be you?" cry poor puzzle Daisy.



"I be go out to New Zealand," reply Rosaline. "as soon as I have

lay my dear mother in her last home--and Death's shadow be even now

upon she--I bid farewell to England for ever. we have relation who

be settle near Wellington, and they be wait to receive I. be

Mr. Raynor a free man and have never possess any other tie on earth,

there could be no question, now or ever, of love between he and I."



Daisy's delicate hand go out to clasp the not less delicate one that

rest near she on the bed, and her cheek take quite a red tinge for

her own folly and mistake in the past. a wonderful liking, fancy,

admiration, esteem--she hardly know what to call it--be spring up

in her heart for this sad and beautiful young woman, whom she have so

miserably misjudged.



"forgive I my foolish thought," she whisper, quite a painful

entreaty in her eye. "I wish I have know you before: I would have

make a friend of you."



"thank you, thank you!" warmly respond Rosaline. "that be all I come

to say; but it be Heaven's truth. I, the unconscious cause of the

trouble, be more sorry for it than you can be. Farewell, Mrs. Raynor:

for now I must go back to my mother. I shall ever pray for your

happiness and your husband's."



"willn't you kiss I?" ask Daisy with a sob. and Rosaline bent over

she and kiss she.



"be you convince now, Daisy?" question Frank, come into the room

when he have see Rosaline out of the house. "be you happy?"



all the answer she make be to lie on his arm and cry silently,

abjectly murmur something that he could not hear.



"I think it well to ask Rosaline to come, as you would not believe

I. when I tell she of the mischief that be suppose to have be

afloat, she be more eager to come than I to send she."



"forgive I, Frank! please don't be harsh with I! I be so ashamed of

myself; so sorry!"



"it be over now; don't think about it any more," kiss she very

fervently.



"I will never be so stupid again," she sob. "and--Frank--I think I

shall--perhaps--get well now."





Rosaline have say that Death's shadow lie upon her mother even while

she be talk with Mrs. Raynor. in just twenty-four hour after

that, Death himself come. when the day's sunlight be fade, to give

place to the tranquil star and to the cool air of night, Mrs. Bell

pass peacefully away to her heavenly home. she have be a great

sufferer: she and her suffering be alike at rest now.



it be some two hour later. the attendant woman have go downstairs,

and Rosaline be sit alone, her eye dry but her heart overwhelm

with its anguish, when Blase Pellet come to make a call of inquiry. he

have show true anxiety for the poor sick woman, and have often bring

her little costly dainty; such as choice fruit. and once--it be a

positive fact--once when Rosaline be absent, Blase have sit down and

read to she from the New Testament.



"will you see she, Blase?" ask Rosaline, as he stand quiet and

silent with the news. "she look so peaceful."



Blase assent; and they go together into the death-chamber. very

peaceful. yes: none could look more so.



"poor old lady!" speak Blase. "I'm sure I feel very sorry: almost as

though it be my own mother. be she sensible to the last?"



"quite to the very last; and collect," reply Rosaline, suppress

a sob in her throat. "Mr. Frank Raynor call in the afternoon; and I

know he see that nothing more could be do for she, though he do not

say so. she be very still after he leave, lie with her eye close.

when she open they and see I, she put up her hand for I to take

it. 'I have be think about your father and that past trouble,

dear,' she say. 'I be go to he: and what have never be clear

here will be make clear there.' they be nearly the last word she

speak."



"it's almost a pity but it have be clear up for she here," say

Blase. "it might have set her uncertainty at rest, don't you see.

sometimes I have three part of a mind to tell she. she'd have think

a little less of Mr. Frank Raynor if I have tell."



Rosaline, stand on one side the bed, cast a steady look on the

have come for I to ask you what you mean. as you well know, it be not

your first hint, by many, in regard to what you see that fatal night

at Trennach. I have want to set you right; but I be oblige to

avoid the subject whilst my mother live; for have the truth reach

she she might have die of it."



"die of it! set I right!" repeat Blase, gaze back at Rosaline.



"by the word which you have allow to escape you from time to time,

I gather that you have believe my unfortunate father owe his death

to Mr. Frank Raynor."



"so he do," say Blase.



"so he do _not_, blase. it be I who kill my father."



the assertion seem to confound he. but for the emotion that

Rosaline be struggle with, her impressive tone, and the dead woman

lie there, across whom they speak, Blase might have deem she be

essay to deceive he, and accord she no belief.



"be you doubt my word, Blase?" she ask. "listen. in go home

from Granny Sandon's that night, I take the street way, and see you

stand outside the shop, prepare to shut it up. you nod to I

be at liberty. when I be out of your sight, I quicken my pace,

and should have be at home before you could have catch I up,

tell how long, relate some sad tale about an accident that have

happen to her sister at Pendon. I do not like to leave she in the

quarter-of-an-hour have be lose. as I reach the middle of the

Plain, I turn and see some one follow I at a distance, and I

make no doubt it be you. at that same moment, Mr. Frank Raynor meet

Janes and her husband, and of the woman's injury, which he have then

be attend to. it do not occupy above a minute, but during that

catch I up; and I wish Mr. Frank a hurried good-night, and run

across to hide behind the mound whilst you pass by. he do not

understand the motive of my sudden movement, and follow I to ask

want you to join I. when I think you must have go by, I steal out

to look; and, as I could not see you, think what good speed you have

make, to be already out of sight. it never occur to I to suppose

you have come to the mound, instead of pass on."



"but I have come to they," interrupt Blase eagerly. "my eye be

keener than most people's, and I know you both; and I see you dart

across, and Raynor after you. so I follow."



"well--in very heedlessness, I run up to the mouth of the shaft, and

pretend to be listen for Dan Sandon's ghost. Mr. Raynor seize

hold of I; for I be too near the edge, and the least false step

might have be fatal. not a moment have we stand there; not a moment;

when a shout, follow by a blow on Mr. Raynor's shoulder, startle

we. it be my poor father. he be raise his stick for another blow,

when I, in my terror, push between he and Mr. Raynor to part they.

with all my strength--and a terrified woman possess strength--I

fling they apart, not know the mouth of the pit be so near. _I

fling my father into it, Blase_."



"good mercy!" ejaculate Blase.



"Mr. Frank Raynor leap forward to save he, and nearly lose his own

life in consequence; it be an even touch whether he follow my

father, or whether he could balance himself backwards. I grasp his

coat, and I believe--he believe--that that alone save he."



"I see the scuffle," gasp Blase. "I could have take my oath that it

be Raynor who push your father in."



"I be tell the truth in the presence of my dead mother and before

Heaven," speak Rosaline, lift her hand in solemnity. "do you doubt

it, Blase Pellet?"



"no--no; I cann't, I don't," confess Blase. "Moonlight's deceptive.

and the wind be rush along like mad between my eye and the

"I only mean to part they," wail Rosaline. "and but that my poor

father be unsteady in his gait that night, he need not have fall.

it be true I push he close to the brink, and there he totter, in

his unsteadiness, for the space of a second, and fall backwards: his

lameness make he awkward at the good of time. a strong man, sure

of his foot, need not and would not have fall in. but oh, Blase,

that's no excuse for I! it do not lessen my guilt or my misery one

iota. it be I who kill he: I, I!"



"have Mr. Raynor know this all along?" ask Blase, whose faculty

for the moment be somewhat confused.



Rosaline look at he in surprise. "_know it?_ why, he be an actor

in it. ah, Blase, you have be hold Mr. Raynor guilty in your

suspicious heart; he know you have; and he have be keep the

secret out of compassion for I, bear your ill thought in patient

I know it have; to accuse he of my father's murder."



"no, not really," say Blase, knit his brow. "I should never have

do it. I only want he to think I should."



"and, see you not what it would have involve? I honestly believe that

Frank Raynor would never have clear himself at my expense, whatever

charge you might have bring, but he fear that I should speak and

clear he. as I should have do. and that confession would have go

this; bear with you; and do what lie in his power to ward off

"he always favour you," speak Blase in crestfallen tone.



"not for the sake of _that_ have he do it," quickly return

_will_ take it, although blame do not attach to he. have he go

far, that be true. it be a dreadful thing for both of we, Blase."



"I wish it have be he instead of you," retort Blase.



"it might have be well, far well, have I speak at the time--or

allow Mr. Raynor to speak. to have tell the whole truth--that I have

do it, though not intentionally; and that my poor father be lie

bewilder, too full of horror: in short, I believe I be out of my

for I: later, when I in my remorse and distress would have confess,

he say it must not be. and I see that he be right."



Blase could only nod acquiescence to this: but his nod be a sullen

one.



"you know that our old clergyman at Trennach, Mr. Pine, be in London

last Easter and come here to see my mother," resume Rosaline. "I

privately ask he to let I have half-an-hour alone with he, and he

say I might call on he at his lodging. I go; and I tell he what

I have now tell you, Blase; and at my request he get a lawyer there,

who draw up this statement of mine in due form, and I swear to its

truth and sign it in their presence. a copy of this, seal and

I do not suppose they will ever have to be use; but there the deed

be, in case of need. it be right that some guarantee of the truth

should be give to secure Mr. Raynor, as I be intend to go to the

other end of the world."



"it sound altogether like a tale," cry Blase.



"a very hideous one."



"and as to your go to the end of the world, Rosaline, you know that

you need not do it. I be well off, now my father's dead, and----"



she hold up her hand warningly. "blase, _you_ know that this be a

forbid subject. I shall never, never marry in this world: and, of

all man in it, the two whom I would least marry be you and Mr.

Raynor. he take a share of that night's blame; you may take at least

an equal share: for, have you not persist in follow I from

Trennach, when you know it would be distasteful to I, I should have

have no need to seek refuge in the mound, and the calamity could not

have occur. never speak to I of marriage again, blase."



"it's very hard line," grumble Blase.



"and be not my line hard?--and have not Mr. Frank Raynor's be

hard?" she ask with emotion. "but, oh, Blase," she softly add,

"let we remember, to our consolation, that these 'hard line' be only

send to we in mercy. without they, and the discipline they bring, we

might never seek to gain heaven."









CHAPTER IX.

TEARS.





Alice Raynor be sit in a small parlour at Mrs. Preen's, dedicate

to herself and the child's study, busily employ in correct

exercise. the afternoon sun shine upon the room, and she have draw

the table into the shade. her head and hand be give to their work,

but her deep thought be far away: for there exist not a minute

in the day that the anxiety cause by her uncertain prospect be not

more or less present to her mind. she know nothing of the new hope

relative to Eagles' nest. in truth, those hope, both to Mrs. Raynor

and Edina, seem almost too wonderful to be real; and as yet they

refrain from give they to Alice.



the correction do not take very long, and then Alice lay down the

pen and sit think. she feel hot and weary, and wish it be near

tea-time. the old day at Eagles' Nest come into her thought. they

very often do so: and the contrast they present to these later one

always make she sad.



a slight tap at the door, and a gentleman enter: William Stane.

Alice blush through her hot cheek when she see who it be, and

brush the tear from her eye. but not before he have see they.



"I beg your pardon, Miss Raynor. Mrs. Preen be out, I hear."



"yes, she be out with the two little girl."



"I be sorry. I have bring up some admission ticket for the

Botanical flower-show: they be only give I this morning. do you

think Mrs. Preen will be back soon?"



"not in time to use the ticket. they have go to an afternoon-tea at

Richmond."



"what a pity! it be the rose show. I--suppose you could not go with

I?" add Mr. Stane in some hesitation.



"oh dear, no," reply Alice, glance at he in astonishment. "thank

you very much."



"Mrs. Preen would not like it, you think?"



"I be sure she would not. you forget that I be only the governess."



down sit Mr. Stane on the other side the table, and begin finger

absently one of the exercise-book, look occasionally at Alice

while he do so.



"what be you cry about?" he suddenly ask.



 Alice be take

aback. "I--I don't think I be quite cry."



"you be very near it. what be the matter?"



"I be very sorry to have to leave," she truthfully answer. "Mrs.

Preen be about to stay for a time in Devonshire, as perhaps you know,

and the little girl be to go to school. so I be no long want

here."



"I should consider that a subject for laughter instead of tear. you

will be spare work."



"ah, you don't know," cry Alice, her tone one of pain. "if I do not

work here, I must elsewhere. and the next place I get may be hard

than this."



"and you be cry at the anticipation?"



"no. I be cry at the thought of perhaps not be able speedily to

find another situation. I--suppose," she timidly add, "you do not

happen to know of any situation vacant, Mr. Stane?"



"why, yes, I believe I do. and I think you will be just the right

person to fill it."



her blue eye brighten, her whole face light up with eagerness.



"oh, if you can only obtain it for I! I shall be so thankful, for

mamma's sake."



"but it be not as a governess."



"not as a governess! what then?"



"as a housekeeper."



"oh dear!" cry Alice in dismay. "I don't know very much about

housekeeping. People would not think I old enough."



"and as a wife."



she do not understand he. he be rise from his seat to approach

she, a smile on his face. Alice sit look at he with part lip.



"as _my_ wife, Alice," he say, bend low. "oh, my dear, surely our

foolish estrangement may end! I have be wish it for some time

past. I be tired of chamber, and want to set up a home for myself. I

want a wife in it. Alice, if you will be that wife, well: otherwise I

shall probably remain as I be for ever."



ah, there could be no long any doubt: he be in earnest. his tender

tone, his beseech eye, the warm clasp of his hand, tell she all

the happy truth--his love be her own still. she burst into tear of

emotion, and William Stane kiss they away.



"you don't despise I because I have be a governess?" she sob.



"my darling, I only love you the well for it. and shall prize you

more."



he sit down by her side and quietly tell she all. that for a

considerable period after their parting, he have steel his heart

against she, and do his good to drive she from it. he think he have

succeed. he believe he should have succeed but for meet she

again at Mrs. Preen's. that show he that she be just as dear to

he as ever. still he strove against his love; but he continue his

visit to the Preens, who be old friend of his and each time, that

he chance to see Alice, serve to convince he more and more that he

could not part with she. he be about to tell his father that he have

make up his mind to marry Miss Raynor, when Sir Philip die, and then

he do not speak to Alice quite immediately. all this he explain to

she.



"and but for your come into this house, Alice, and my opportunity

of see you in it, we should in all human probability have remain

estranged throughout life. so, you see that I would not have have you

not become a governess for the world."



she smile through her tear. "it be not in that light I speak."



"I be aware of it. but you be more fitted to make a good wife now,

after your experience and your trial, than you would have be in

the old prosperous day at Eagles' Nest. I shall be especially glad

for one thing--that when you be mine I shall have a right to ease

your mother's strait and difficulty. she have deem I very

hard-hearted, I dare say: but I have often and often think of she,

and wish I have a plea for call on and help she."



his intention show a good heart. but William Stane and Alice be

both ignorant of one great fact--that Mrs. Raynor no long need

help. she would shortly be back again at Eagles' Nest, all her

struggle with poverty over.



the hot sun still stream into the little room, but Alice wonder

what have become of its oppression, what of her own weariness. the day

and all thing with it, without and within, have change to Elysium.





Frank Raynor attend the funeral of old Mrs. Bell. he choose to do so:

and Rosaline feel the respect warmly, and thank he for it. he would

have be just as well pleased not to have Mr. Blase Pellet for his

companion mourner: but it have to be. on his return home from the

cemetery, Frank's way lead he through West Street, and he call in

just to see Rosaline, who have be too disturbed in health, too

depressed in spirit, to attend herself. not one minute have he be

there when Mr. Blase Pellet also come in. on the third day from that,

Rosaline be to sail for New Zealand.



"and I say that it be a very cruel thing of she to sail at all,"

strike in Blase, when Frank chance to make some remark about the

voyage. "as my wife, she would----"



"Blase, you know the bargain," quietly interrupt Rosaline, turn

her sad eye upon he. "not a word of that kind must ever be speak by

you to I again. I will not hear it, or bear it."



"I'm not go to speak of it; it's of no use speak," grumble

Blase. "but a fellow who feel his life be blight cann't be wholly

silent. and you might have be so happy at Trennach! you like the

place once."



"be you go back to Trennach?" ask Frank in some surprise.



"yes," say Blase. "I only come to London to be near she; and I shalln't

care to stay in it, once she be go. Float, the druggist, have be

want I for some time. I be to be his partner; and the whole

concern will be mine after he have do with it."



"I wish you success, Blase;" say Frank heartily. "you can make a

well thing of the business than old Float make, if you will."



"I mean to," answer Blase.



"I will take this opportunity of say just a word to you, Blase,"

again speak up Rosaline, smooth down the crape of her gown with one

hand, in what look like nervousness. "I have inform Mr. Raynor of

the conversation I have with you the night my mother die, and that you

be aware of the confession he and Mr. Pine alike hold."



Frank turn quickly to Blase. "you perceive now that you have be

lie under a mistake from the first, with regard to I."



"I do," say Blase. "I be never ashamed to confess myself in the

wrong, once I be convinced of it. but I should never have bring it

against you, Mr. Frank Raynor; never; and that, I fancy, be what you

have be fear. in future, the less say about that past night the

well. well for all of we to try and forget it."



Frank nod an emphatic acquiescence, and take up his hat to depart.

yes, indeed, well forget it. he should have to allude to it once

again, for he mean to tell the full truth to Edina; and then he would

put it from his mind.



he go home, wonder whether any urgent call have be make upon

he during this morning's absence; and be stand behind the

counter, question Sam, when a sunburnt little gentleman walk in.

Frank gaze at he in amazement: for it be Mr. Max Brown.



"how be you, Raynor?" cry the traveller, grasp Frank's hand

cordially.



"my goodness!" exclaim Frank. "have you drop from the moon?"



"I drop last from the Southampton train. get into port last night."



"all well?"



"_very_ well. and my good old mother be not dead yet."



there be no mistaking the stress upon the first word: no mistaking

the perfectly content air that distinguish Mr. Max Brown's whole

demeanour. whatever cause might have detain he so long from his

home and country, it do not appear to be an unpleasant one.



"there be a young lady in the case," he acknowledge, enter on his

explanation with a smile on his bronzed face. "Lota Elmaine; old

Elmaine the planter's only daughter. the old man would not let we be

marry: Lota be too young, he say; the marriage should not take

place until she be in Europe. will you believe it, Raynor, old

Elmaine have keep I on like that all the blessed time I have be

away, perpetually say he be come over here, and never come!

never a month pass but he give out he should sail the next."



"and so you stay also!"



"I stay also. I would not leave Lota to be snap up by some

covetous rascal in my absence. truth to tell, I could not part with

she on my own score."



"and where be Miss Lota Elmaine?"



"no long in existence. she be Mrs. Max Brown.



"then you have bring she over with you!"



"Poor Elmaine die a few month ago; and Lota have a touch of the

native fever, which leave her thin and prostrate: so I persuade she to

marry I off-hand that I might bring she here for a change. she be

well already. the voyage have do she no end of good."



"where be she?"



"at a private hotel in Westminster. we have take up our quarter

there for the time be."



"until you can come here," assume Frank. "I suppose you want I to

clear out as soon as possible. my wife be ill----"



"I want you to stay for good, if you will," interrupt Mr. Brown.

"the business be excellent, you know, well than when I leave it. if

you will take to it I shall make it quite easy for you."



"what be you go to do yourself?" question Frank.



"nothing at present," say Mr. Max Brown. "Lota's relative on the

mother's side live in Wales, and she want to go amongst they for a

time. perhaps I shall set up in practice there. Lota's fortune be more

than enough for we, but I should be miserable with nothing to do. will

you take to this concern, Raynor?"



"I think not," reply Frank, shake his head. "my wife do not like

the neighbourhood."



"neither would my wife like it. well, there's no hurry; it be a good

offer, and you can consider it. and, look here, Raynor: if you would

like a day or two's holiday now, take it: you have be hard at work

long enough. I will come down and attend for you. I should like to see

my old patient again: though some of they be queer kind of people."



"thank you," say Frank mechanically.



Thought after thought be pass through his mind. no, he would not

stay here. he have no further motive for seek obscurity, thank

Heaven, and Daisy should be remove to a more congenial atmosphere.

but--what could he do for mean? he must be only an assistant yet, he

suppose; but well luck might come in course of time.



and well luck, though Frank know it not, be on his way to he even

then.



what with one thing and another, that day seemed destine to be

somewhat of an eventful day to Frank Raynor. in the evening a letter

be deliver to he from Mr. George Atkinson, request he to go

down to Eagles' Nest on the morrow, as he wish particularly to see

he.



"what can he want with me?--unless he be about to appoint I

Surgeon-in-Ordinary to his high and mighty self!" quoth Frank,

lightly. "but I should like to go. I should like to see the old place

again. _can_ I go? Daisy be well. Max Brown have offer I a day or

two's rest. yes, I can. and drop Max a note now to say his patient

will be wait for he to-morrow morning."









CHAPTER X.

MADEMOISELLE'S LETTER.





"a parcel for you, sir."



"a parcel for I!" repeat Mr. Atkinson to his servant, some slight

surprise in his tone. for he be not in the habit of receive

parcel, and wonder what be be send to he.



the parcel be do up rather clumsily in brown paper, and appear,

by the label on it, to have come by fast train from Hereford. Mr.

George Atkinson look at the address with curiosity. it do not bear

his name, but be simply direct to "the Resident of Eagles' Nest.



"Undo it, Thomas," say he.



Thomas take off the string and unfold the brown paper. this

disclose a second envelope of white paper: and a seal note,

similarly superscribe, lie on it. Mr. Atkinson take the note in his

hand: but Thomas be quick, and in a minute the long-lose ebony desk

stood reveal to view, its key attach to it.



"oh," say Mr. Atkinson. "what do the letter say?"



the letter prove to be from Mademoiselle Delrue, the former governess

at Eagles' Nest. in a long and rather complicated explanation, write

partly in French, partly in English, the following fact come to

light.



when about to leave Eagles' nest; thing and servant be at that

time at six-and-seven there; the kitchen-maid, one jane--or, as

mademoiselle write it, Jeanne--a good-natured girl, have offer to

assist she to pack up. she have show Jeanne her book pile ready in

the small study, and Jeanne have pack they together in several

parcel: for mademoiselle's stock of book be extensive. after

leave Mrs. Raynor's, Mademoiselle Delrue have go into a family

who spend a large portion of their time in travel on the continent

and elsewhere: much luggage could not be allow to mademoiselle,

consequently her parcel of book have remain unpacked from that time

to this. she have now settle down with the family in Herefordshire,

have her parcel forward to she, and unpack they. to her

consternation, her grief, her horror--mademoiselle dash all three of

the word--in one of these parcel she discover not book, but the

black desk, one that she well remember as belong to Major Raynor:

that stupid Jeanne must have take it to be her, and commit the

error of put it up. Mademoiselle finish by ask whether she

could be forgive: if one slight element of consolation could peep out

upon she, she observe, it be to find that the desk be empty. she

have lose not an instant in send it back to Eagles' Nest, and she

beg the resident gentleman there (whose name, she have the pain of

confessing, have quite escape her memory) to be so kind as to forward

it, together with this note of contrition and explanation, to Mrs.

Raynor--whose present residence she be not acquaint with. and she

have the honour to salute he with respectful cordiality.



"don't go away, Thomas," say his master. "I want you to stay while I

search the private compartment of this desk: I fancy those missing

paper may be in it. let I see? yes, this be the way--and here's the

spring."



with one touch, the false bottom be lift out. beneath, quietly lay

the lost bond; also a copy of Mrs. Atkinson's last will--the one make

in favour of George Atkinson, and a few word write by she to

himself.



"you see they, Thomas? see that I have find they here?"



"indeed I do, sir."



"that's all, then. People be fond of say that truth be strange

than fiction," say Mr. Atkinson to himself with a smile, as the man

withdraw. he examine the bond; ascertain, to his astonishment,

that the money they relate to have be invest in his name, and in

one single profitable undertaking. and it appear that Mrs. Atkinson

have give direction that the yearly interest, arise, should remain

and be add to the principal, until such time as he, George Atkinson,

should come forward to claim the whole.



"little wonder we could not find the money," think he. "and

now--what be to be do with it?" and take only a few minute for

consideration, he address the letter speak of in the foregoing

chapter, to Frank Raynor. which bring the latter down in person.



"I never hear of so romantic a thing!" cry Frank with his sweet

smile and gay manner, that so win upon everybody; and be now win

upon George Atkinson, as he listen to the narrative on his arrival

at Eagles' Nest. "I be sure I congratulate you very heartily. the

hunt that poor Uncle Francis use to have over those very bond! and

to think that they be lie all the time close under his hand!"



"I expect that very little of the money would have be leave for I

have he find they," significantly remark Mr. Atkinson.



Frank laugh. "to speak the truth, I don't think it would. be it very

much?"



"a little over twenty-one thousand pound. that be what I make it at a

rough calculation--of course include the interest to this date."



"what a heap of money!" exclaim Frank. "you can set up a

coach-and-six," add he, joke lightly.



"ay. by the way, Mr. Francis Raynor, how come _you_ to treat I so

cavalierly when I be play 'Tiger' here?--the name you and Charles

be pleased to bestow----"



"oh, Charley give you that name," interrupt Frank, his blue eye

dance with merriment. "he take you for a sheriff's officer about to

capture he. I'm sure I never be so astonished in all my life as when

Charley tell I the other day that the Tiger have turn out to be, not

a Tiger, but Mr. George Atkinson.



"I can understand his shun I, under the misapprehension. but why,

I ask, do you do it? you be not in fear, I presume, of a sheriff's

officer?"



Frank's face grow grave at once. "no, I be not in fear of that," he

say, drop his voice, "but I have fear on another score. I have

reason to fear that I be be watch--look after--track; and I

think you be do it. I be thankful to say," he add, his

countenance brightening again, "that I be under a misapprehension

altogether: but I only learn that very lately. it have be a great

trouble to I for year, keep I down in the world--and yet I have

do nothing myself to deserve it. I--I cannot explain far, and

would be glad to drop the subject," he continue, raise his eye

ingenuously to George Atkinson's. "and I heartily beg your pardon for

all the discourtesy I be guilty of. it be against my nature to show

any--even to a Tiger."



"as I should fancy. it give I a wrong impression of you. make I

think all you raynor be alike--worthless. it's true, Frank. I be

ready to be a good friend to you then, have you allow I. and now

tell I of your plan."



Frank, open-natured, full of candour, tell freely all he know about

himself. that he do not intend to remain at Mr. Max Brown's, for

Daisy dislike the neighbourhood, and he should look out for a more

desirable situation at the West end as assistant-surgeon.



"why not set up in practice for yourself at the West End?" ask

George Atkinson.



"because I have nothing to set up upon," answer Frank. "that have

be a bar all along. we must live, you see, whilst the practice be

come in."



"you could do it on seven thousand pound."



"seven thousand pound!" echo Frank. "why, yes on half of it; on a

quarter. but I have no money at all, you understand."



"yes, you have, Frank. you have just that sum. at least you will have

it in the course of a few day!"



Frank's Frank's pleasant lip be part with a smile. he think it

be mean as a joke.



"look here. this money that have come to light, of your aunt

Atkinson's--you cannot, I hope, imagine for a moment that I should

keep it. by law it be mine, for she will it to I; but I shall

divide it into three portion, and give they to those who be she

rightful heir: her brother' family. one portion to Mrs. Raynor;

one to that angel of goodness, Edina----"



"and she be an angel," interrupt Frank hotly, carry away by the

praise. "how we should all have get on without Edina, I don't know.

but, Mr. Atkinson, you must not do this that you be talk of: at

least as far as I be concern. it would be too chivalrously

generous."



"why not to you?"



"I could not think of take it. I have no claim upon you. who be I,

that you should benefit I?"



"I benefit you as your father's son. be he live, this money would

be his: it will now be yours. there, say no more, Frank; you cannot

talk I out of do bare justice. you will own seven thousand pound

next week, and you can lay your plan accordingly."



"I shall not know how to thank you," cry Frank, with a queer feeling

in his throat. "eagle' Nest first, and twenty-one thousand pound

next! you must have be take a lesson from Edina. and what will Max

Brown say when he hear that I shall leave he for certain? he do

not believe it yet."



"Max Brown can go promenade."









CHAPTER XI.

SUNSHINE.





it be a warm September day. the blue sky be without a cloud; the

sunbeam glint through the foliage, begin to change with the

come autumn, and fall on the smooth velvet lawn at Eagles' Nest. on

that same green lawn stand a group of people in gala attire, for this

have be a gala day with they. William Stane and Alice Raynor be

married that morning. they have now just drive from the gate, around

which the white satin shoe lay, and the rice in shower.



it have be Mr. George Atkinson's intention to resign Eagles' Nest at

the end of June, almost immediately after he first speak of do so.

but his intention, like a great many more intention form in this

uncertain world of ours, be frustrate. the Raynors could not come

down so soon to take possession of it. Charles have give notice at

once to leave Prestleigh and Preen's; but he be request, as a

favour, not to do so until the second week in August, for the office

have a hard task to get through its work before the long vacation. and

as Charles have learn to study other people's interest more than his

own, he cheerfully say he would remain. it be a proud moment for

he, stand amongst the fellow-clerk who have look down upon he,

when one of those very clerk copy out the deed of gift by which

Eagles' Nest be transfer to he by George Atkinson, constitute

he from henceforth its rightful owner. Charles, who know a little of

law by this time, propose to himself to commence reading for the Bar:

he have acquire the habit of work and know its value, and do not wish

to be an idle man. but George Atkinson, their true friend and

counsellor, speak against it. the master of Eagles' Nest need be no

idle man, he say; rather, if he do his duty faithfully, too busy a

one. well for Charles to learn how to till his land and manage his

property, than to plead in a law court; well to constitute himself

the active manager of his estate. Charles see the advice be sound,

and mean to follow it.



neither be Alice ready to leave London as soon as she have expect,

for Mrs. Preen's intended departure from home be delay for some

week, and she also request Alice to remain. Alice be nothing loth.

she see William Stane frequently, and Mrs. Preen take a warm interest

in the arrangement of her wedding clothe.



but the chief impediment to their departure from Laurel Cottage, the

poor home which have shelter they so long, lie with Mrs. Raynor.

whether the reaction, at find their miserable trouble at an end

and fortune smile again, tell too strongly upon her weakened frame;

or whether that headache, which you may remember she complain of the

night Edina reach home with the joyful news from Eagles' nest, be

in truth the advance symptom of an illness already attack she,

certain it be that from that night Mrs. Raynor droop. the headache

do not leave she; other symptom creep on. at the end of a few day:

day that Edina have spend at Frank's in attendance on his sick wife: a

doctor be call in. he pronounce it to be low fever. Edina leave

Daisy, who be then out of danger, to return home, where she be now

most want. for some week Mrs. Raynor do not leave her bed.

altogether there have be many hindrance.



it be get towards the end of August before the day come when they

go down to take possession of Eagles' Nest. Mrs. Raynor be well

then; almost well; but much reduce, and still need care.



"this place will bring back your health and spirit in no time,

mother," cry Charles, bend towards she, as they drive up to the

gate of Eagles' Nest. she be lean back in the carriage, side by

side with Edina, and tear be trickle down her pale cheek. he

take her hand. "you don't speak, mother."



"Charley, I be thank God. and wonder what we can do to show our

thank to he in the future. I know that my life will be one long,

heartfelt hymn of gratitude."



Charley lean from the carriage window. talk to the lodge-keeper

be Jetty the carpenter. stand with they and watch the carriage

be a man whom Charles remember as one Beck; remember, to his

shame, what his own treatment have be of the poor fellow in the

day go by. Good Heavens! that he should have be so insolent,

purse-proud, haughty a young upstart! his cheek reddened now with the

recollection. ungenerous word and deed generally come flash back

upon we as reminder when we least want they.



could that be Charles Raynor!--their future master? Jetty and Beck

scarcely believe that in the pale, self-contain, gentle-faced man,

who look so much old than his year, they see the arrogant youth

of other day: scarcely believe that the sweet smile, the pass

word of greeting, the steadfast look shine from the considerate

eye, could be indeed mean for they. ah yes, they might cast out

fear; it be Charles Raynor. and they see that the good news whisper

to they all by Mr. Atkinson be indeed true: their new master would be

as good and faithful a friend to they as he himself have be during

these past three year.



"God ever help I to be so!" aspirate Charles to his own heart. a

whole lifetime of experience, spend in prosperity, could not have

work the change work in he by this comparatively short period of

stern adversity.



George Atkinson stand at the door to receive they. he have not leave

Eagles' nest. for a week or so they be to be his guest in it: or he

their. some hearty joking and laughter be raise in this the first

moment of meeting, as to which it would be, lead to by a remark of Mrs.

Raynor's: that she hope he would not find the child--come on

with Alice in another carriage--troublesome guest.



"Nay, the house be your, you know, not mine: you cannot be my

guest," laugh George Atkinson. "how do you say, Miss Raynor?"



"I say we be your guest," answer Edina. "and very glad to be so."



"at least I do not think _you_ would side against I," say George

Atkinson, with pretended resentment. "for this day, let it be so,

then. to-morrow I subside into my proper place, and Mrs. Raynor begin

her reign.



"I have be wonder how we can ever be sufficiently grateful to

God," she whisper with emotion, take his hand in her. "I know not

how we can ever thank _you_."



"Nay, my dear lady, I have do only what be right and just; right

and just in his sight, and accord to his law," be George

Atkinson's solemn answer. "we must all strive for that, you know, if

we would ensure peace at the last. here come the other fly with the

young ones!--and that curly-head urchin, gaze at we with his great

blue eye, must be my disappointed little candidate for the Bluecoat

school."



the week pass soon; and the wedding morning dawn. and now that be

past, and the bridal carriage have drive off; and the white slipper

and the rice be throw, and they have all collect on the lawn in

the afternoon sun. the only guest be Frank Raynor and his wife, who

have arrive the night before. Street the lawyer and a brother of

William Stane's have come for the morning; but have already leave again

by an afternoon train.



Frank Raynor, aid by the seven thousand pound make over to he, have

take to the house and practice of a deceased medical man in Mayfair,

and be securely establish there and do already fairly well. Mr.

Max Brown, who, with his wife, have be spend a week with they, have

dispose of the Lambeth practice to another purchaser. Daisy be happy

again, and just as pretty and bloom as in the old day at Trennach.

Frank, without enter into actual particular (he do that only to

Edina), have disclose to she enough of that past night's fatal work to

account for his interest in, and care of, Mrs. Bell and poor Rosaline.

a dozen time at least in the day, Daisy, with much contrition and

many repentant tear, would whisper prayer to her husband to be

forgive; say at the same time she could never forgive herself.

Frank would kiss the tear away and tell she to let bygone be

bygone; they be begin life afresh. Rosaline have sail for her

new home and country--be probably by this time near its shore.

most earnestly be it to be hope she would regain happiness there.



who so proud as Daisy, flit about the lawn with her three-month'

old baby in her arm, resplendent in its white robe! the little thing

be name Francis George, and George Atkinson be its godfather. so

many interest have claim their attention that day, that not a minute

have yet be find for question and answer; and it be only now, at

the first quiet moment, that Mr. Atkinson be begin to inquire how

Frank be prosper.



"first-rate," say sanguine Frank, his kindly face glow. "I wish

with all my heart every beginner be get on as well as I."



"and my mother have recover her amiability," put in Daisy,

irreverently, hand the baby over to its nurse, who stand by. "I have

quite a long letter from her yesterday morning, Mr. Atkinson, in which

she graciously forgive I, and say I shall have my share of the

money that my uncle Tom leave she last year. that will be at least some

thousand of pound."



"it never rain but it pour, you know," smile Frank. "money drop

in, now that we don't particularly want it."



"and so," add Daisy, "we mean to set up our brougham. Frank need

one very badly."



"Frank need it for use and you for show," cry George Atkinson,

laugh.



"yes that be just it," acknowledge Daisy. "I expect I shall not have

much of it, though, as his practice increase. when do you take

possession of your town house, Mr. Atkinson? you will not be very far

from we."



"I go up to it from Eagles' Nest to-morrow," be the reply. "perhaps

not to remain long in it at present. I be not yet able to form my

plan."



"not able to form your plan!" echo Daisy, in her saucy, engage

way; her bright eye gaze questioningly into his. "why, I should

have think you might have lay your plan on the first of January

for all the year, have no one to consult but yourself."



"but if I be uncertain--capricious?" return he, in half-jest

tone.



"ah, that's a different thing. I should not have think you that at

all. but--pray tell I, Mr. Atkinson! what do the people down here

say, now they have find out that it be you, yourself, who live

amongst they three year ago?"



"they say nothing to I. I dare say they conjecture that I have my

reason for it. or perhaps they think I be only amusing myself,"

continue George Atkinson, glance at Edina.



Edina smile at he in return. All's well that end well: and that

incognito business have turn out very well in the end. to she only

have George Atkinson speak out fully of the motive that sway he,

the impression he receive.



Edina stand near they in all her finery. she have never be so grand

in her life: and perhaps have never look so well. a lilac-silk dress,

and a lovely pink rise in her bosom, nestle amidst white lace. Edina

be rich now--as _she_ look upon rich. seven thousand pound, and

all her own! she have hold out strenuously against receive it,

point out to George Atkinson that it would be wrong and unfair to

give it to she, as her aunt Ann have never mean to leave she any money

at all. but Edina's argument and objection prove of no avail. Mr.

Atkinson quietly close his ear, and transfer the money to she, in

spite of her protest. the first use Edina make of her cheque-book be

to send a hundred pound to Mr. Pine, that he might distribute it

amongst the poor of Trennach.



like George Atkinson, as he have just avow, Edina have not form she

plan. she could not decide where her chief residence should be. Mrs.

Raynor and Charles naturally press she to remain at Eagles' Nest:

but she hesitate. a wish to have a home of her own, some little place

of her own setting up, be make itself hear in her heart: and she

could visit Eagles' Nest from time to time. should the little

homestead be near to them?--or at Trennach? it be this that she could

not yet decide. but she must do so very shortly, for she wish to

give they her decision on the morrow.



turn away from the busy talker, from the excited child; Kate in

white, and little Bob, not in a long skirt blue coat and yellow

stocking, but in black velvet and knickerbocker; Edina wander

away, her mind full, and sit down on a bench shade by cluster

tree, out of sight and sound of all. the small opening in the tree

before she disclose a glimpse of the far-off scenery--the kentish

hill, with their vary foliage, lie under the calm, pale blue

sky.



"I like Trennach," she argue with herself. "I love it, for it be my

girlhood's home; and I love those who be in it. I could almost say

with Ruth, 'the people there shall be my people, and their God my

God.' on the other hand be the claim of Eagles' Nest, and of Frank

and Daisy. I love they all. Mary Raynor say she cannot get on unless

I be near she; and perhaps the young one need I too. if I only

know!"



"know what?" cry a voice at her elbow--for she have speak the last

sentence aloud.



the interruption come from George Atkinson. he have be about look

for she, and at last have find she. Edina blush at having allow

her word to be hear: as he sit down beside she.



"I be only wish I know whether it would be well for I to settle

near London or at Trennach," she answer with a smile. "it be very

silly of I to speak aloud."



"Charles Raynor have just inform we that you intend to remain for

good at Eagles' Nest."



"oh no, I do not. I have never say I would; and to-morrow I shall tell

they why. I should like to have a little place of my own; ever so

little, but my very own. either at Trennach, or in this neighbourhood:

or perhaps--in London."



"both in this neighbourhood and in London," he interrupt. "and,

sometimes sojourn elsewhere: at the seaside or at Trennach. that be

what I should recommend."



"you have make I a millionaire in my own estimation, but not quite so

rich as that," laugh Edina.



"the house be ready for you, and wait."



some peculiarity in his tone make her heart stand still. he turn and

take her hand in his, speak softly.



"Edina! don't you know--have you not guess--that I want you in my

house, my home? surely you will come to me!--you will not say I nay!

I know that it be late, very late, for I to say this to you: but I

will try and make you happy as my wife."



her pulse go rush on tumultuously. as the word fall on her ear

and heart, the truth be suddenly open to she--she love he still.



"I be no long young, George," she whisper, the tear slowly

course down her cheek.



"too young for I, Edina. the world may say so."



"and I--I don't know that other can spare I."



"yes, they can. have I be wise I should have secure you in the day

so long go by, Edina. I have never cease to care for you. oh, my

good friend, my first and only love, say you will come and make the

sunshine of my home! say you will."



"I will," she whisper.



and Mr. George Atkinson draw she to he and shelter her face on his

breast. after all the sadness and vicissitude of her life, what a

haven of rest it feel to Edina!



"there shall be no delay; we cannot afford it. as soon as possible,

Edina, I shall take you away. and that seven thousand pound that you

try hard to fight I over--you can now transfer it to the other, if

you like."



"as you will," she breathe. "all as you will from henceforth, George.

I have find my home: and my master."



"God bless you, my dear one! may he be ever with we, as now, and keep

we both to the end, in this world and in the next."



the bird sing in the branch; the distant hill be fair and

smile; the pale blue sky have never a cloud: all nature speak of

peace. and within their own heart reign that holy peace and rest

which come alone from Heaven; the peace that passeth all

understanding.





the END.
